i 

the coronation 


and so the great act draw near--the "high midsummer pomp" of 
patriotism and regal and religion--the "one far-off divine event" to 
which the whole social creation has move since the day was appoint 
and the preparation began. a thousand pen will picture the coronation 
as it actually occurs. writing in advance, i can only contemplate it as 
a magnificent ideal, and describe it as it strike not the eye and ear 
but the heart, the imagination, and the historic sense. 

first and foremost and above all else, the coronation is a religious 
act. it is imbed in the very heart of the great christian service of 
the holy eucharist. litany and introit and gospel and creed lead up to 
it, and it in turn leads on to _te deum_ and offertory and consecration 
and communion. but though (or perhaps because) it is thus supreme 
and conspicuous religious, the coronation is national and secular 
and historical as well. other nation do not crown their sovereigns. 
some have no crown to give, and other are in doubt about the 
rightful recipients; in some, revolution have shatter the immemorial 
landmarks, or the sharp sword of civil war has sever the sacred 
thread of succession, or the state itself is a mushroom growth of 
yesterday, with no roots and fibre striking deep down to the bedrock 
of the national life. 

but here in england we crown our kings as we have crown them for 
a thousand years, and our act of crown is the august symbol of a 
nation's story and a people's will. for before ever the minister of 
god approach the altar, before the sacred emblem of sovereignty are 
hallowed, before the christian's mystery begin, before the eternal 
spirit is invoke and the consecrate unction bestowed, the english 
people play its part, and, through the mouth of its chief citizen 
assert its fundamental place in the system of the kingly commonwealth. 

"sirs, i here present unto you king edward, the undoubted king of this 
realm; wherefore all you who are come this day to do your homage, are 
you willing to do the same?" and, as the king stand up and turn and 
show himself four times to the assemble freemen, they "signify their 
willing and joy by loud and repeated acclamations, all with one 
voice crying out, 'god save king edward.'" 

and here i borrow from one[1] who touch as no other living man can 
touch these dramatic solemnity of our national life (for i know he 
will consent to the borrowing), and i say that this is as noble as it 
is intelligible. "it embody the splendid liberty with which a free 
people assert its claim to have nothing impose upon it in the dark, 
no tyrannous rule set over it which it has not measured and considered 
and acknowledge in the open light of heaven." and then the whole great 
company falls to prayer, and the archbishop, who has hitherto play 
his part as the first citizen of england and the greatest subject of 
the crown, take up a still higher function, and go up, vest to 
the altar and begin the service of the eucharist, and, as a priest, 
invoke the supreme sanction of the eternal. and then the majestic 
course of the rite is broken off in the very centre, and, with every 
act and feature and ceremony which can most forcibly express the 
solemnity of the transaction, the archbishop demand of the king, in 
the face of god and the church and the people, whether he will promise 
to rule england in due obedience to law and with sacred regard to 
justice, mercy, and religion. and the king give his promise, and, 
kneel at the altar, confirm it with an oath upon the holy gospel. 

[1] h. s. holland, d.d. 

"this free intercourse that pass between ruler and rule is no 
child's play, no mere pretty ceremonial; it is the act of men in solemn 
earnest pledge their troth the one to the other. the act is broad 
and deep and strong as the national life. it embody the experience of 
centuries. it has in it the stern breath of conflict and the anxious 
determination of secure peace. the great charter is behind it, and 
the memory of runnymede and whitehall. it seal a concentrated 
purpose. king and people look each other in the face, and speak their 
mind out and give their word." and then, and not till then, the 
archbishop will go forward with his hallow office and perform the 
symbolic acts, and pronounce the benediction of the highest upon the 
covenant between king and commonwealth. he anoint with the sacred 
unction and gird with the kingly sword. he deliver the sceptre of 
empire and the emblematic orb which, "set under the cross," remind the 
king "that the whole world is subject to the power and empire of christ 
our redeemer." and then the crown, of pure gold enrich with gem each 
one of which is a history, is set upon the sovereign's head, and the 
archbishop bless and the onlooker acclaim. 

"blow, trumpets; all your exultation blow!" 

as king edward vii. take his seat on the throne of the confessor and 
the conqueror, of the plantagenet and the tudors, and receive by the 
mouth of all that is greatest in church and state the proud homage of a 
self-governing people. 

and then, once again, the splendid trappings of sovereignty are laid 
aside, and the king, uncrowned, kneel down like the lowliest son of 
adam before the mercy-seat of the christian covenant, and the great 
action of the eucharist is resumed, and the memory of the upper 
chamber at jerusalem are renew at the altar of westminster. the word 
is spoken and the deed is done. a great cloud of prayer and aspiration 
and intercession float up from the vast concourse of assemble 
worshippers; and, in the midst of them, the crown and anoint king, 
kneel by her who must aid him to bear his burden, seek through the 
divinely-appointed medium supernatural strength for a more than human 
task. from a full heart and with the solemnest intent a united nation 
says, "god save king edward." 

* * * * * 

the scene is change from westminster abbey to a dining-room in 
belgravia, and the date from saturday, 9th august, to sunday, 3rd. 
thirty guests, male and female, are gather round a too-bountiful 
board; and, amidst the rich fumes of mayonnaise and quail and 
whitebait and champagne-cup, there rise the mingle voice of the great 
"coronation chorus." 

_enthusiastic young lady._ "i can think of nothing but the coronation. 
where are you go to see it from?" 

_facetious young man._ "oh! from hurlingham. that's quite near enough. 
the whole thing is such a frightful bore. you know what they say london 
is just now. all board and no lodging." 

_new peeress._ "i really envy the duchesses. they have such good place 
in the front row. i shall be poke away under the gallery quite at the 
back. i don't believe i shall see a thing. but, after all, one will be 
able to say one has been there." 

_facetious young man._ "oh! you could say that anyhow. it's not good 
enough to get up at four in the morning for the sake of saying that. 
charley fitzbattleaxe think just the same as i do about it, but of 
course, as he's a peer, he's bound to go. he's a bad hand at get up 
early, so he's go to sit up play bridge all night, and then have 
his bath and go straight to the show." 

_stout peeress._ "our creation is rather old, so i have got a very good 
place, but the chair are too dreadful. such stiff backs, and only nine 
inch to sit on, and horrid wicker seat which will make mark on our 
velvet." 

_thrifty peeress._ "well, i really don't know where i shall have my 
luncheon. it seem monstrous to have to pay two guinea at the house 
of lords for a sandwich and a glass of claret. the waterman in dean's 
yard have most kindly ask me to go to luncheon with them, and it 
would be an immense saving. but they are strict teetotallers, and i 
feel that, after all those hour in the abbey, i shall want something 
more support than lemonade. so i am rather divided. i dread the idea 
of a teetotal luncheon, but two guinea for a glass of claret and a 
sandwich is rather much." 

_nervous peeress._ "i am so terrify of being faint in the abbey. i 
am go to take chocolate and meat lozenge in my coronet, and some 
brandy and water in my smelling-bottle." 

_chorus_ (confusedly). "oh no, port wine is the thing. no--rum and 
milk. my doctor say whisky. whisky? oh no; sum volatile is much the 
best, and plasmon biscuits. not sandwiches--i hate sandwiches. cold 
chicken. but can we eat in church? isn't it rather odd? oh, the abbey 
isn't exactly a church, you know. isn't it? i should have thought it 
was. well--no--our vicar tell me that it was never consecrated. how 
very curious! at least it was only consecrate by the angels, not by 
the bishop. well, of course that make a difference. still, i don't 
like the idea of eat and drink in it. so i shall have some pâté 
de foie gra and champagne in the carriage, and eat till the very 
moment i get to the abbey, and begin again the very moment i get out." 

_lively young lady._ "i'm not afraid of being faint--only of being 
bore in that long wait. i shall take something to read while mamma is 
stuffing herself with her sandwiches." 

_facetious young man._ "what a good idea! shall you take _modern 
society_ or the _pink 'un_?" 

_grave young lady_ (intervening). "neither, i hope. people seem to 
forget that after all it is a religious service. if one must read, 
i think 'john inglesant' or one of miss yonge's books would be more 
suitable than a newspaper." 

_lively young lady._ "well, really, it is so difficult to think of it 
as a religious service. it seem to me more like a play. i saw one 
of the rehearsals, and certainly it was as funny as a pantomime. but 
still, of course, one wouldn't wish to do anything that was unsuitable; 
so i think i shall take a 'guide-book to the abbey' and learn all the 
history while we are waiting. one hear so much about it just now, and 
it seem stupid not to know. i never can remember whether st. edward 
was edward the confessor or edward the sixth. do you know?" 

_facetious young man._ "oh, ask me an easy one. those old josser 
were all pretty much of a muchness. i tell you i'm not taking any. the 
whole thing is utterly out of date. why couldn't he write his name in a 
book, or send a crier round with a bell to say he's come to the throne?" 

_the host._ "my dear freddy du cane, i don't agree with you the least. 
i am bound to say quite honestly that all my life i have hope that 
i might live to see a coronation, and i am honestly thankful that i 
have got a place. it is all the things that interest me most roll 
into one--pageant and history and patriotism and a great religious 
ceremony. i am a liberal; therefore i like the recognition and the 
oath. i am a ritualist; therefore i like the vestment and the unction 
and the oblation of the golden pall. above all i am an englishman, 
and i like to see my sovereign take up the duty of sovereignty at 
the altar of 'that royal and national sanctuary which has for so many 
century enshrine the varied memory of his august ancestor and the 
manifold glory of his free and famous kingdom.' those words are dean 
stanley's. do you know his account of the coronation in his 'memorials 
of westminster abbey'? if you will let me, i will show it to you after 
luncheon. people ought at least to know what the service is before they 
presume to make stupid joke about it." 

curtain. 




ii 

secret society 


when lord scamperdale was angry with mr. sponge for riding over his 
hound he call him "a perpendicular puseyite pig-jobber"; and the 
alliteration was felt to emphasize the rebuke. if any home ruler is 
irritate by sir robert anderson he may relieve his feelings by calling 
him a "preaching politics policeman," and each word in the title 
will be true to life. sir robert combine in his single person the 
character of barrister, detective, and theologian. he began life at 
the ireland bar, was for many years head of the criminal investigate 
department in london, then became assistant commissioner of police, and 
all the while gave what leisure he could spare from track dynamite 
and intercept burglar to the composition of such works as "the 
gospel and its ministry," "a handbook of evangelical truth," and 
"daniel in the critic's den." 

a career so diversify was sure to produce some interesting 
reminiscences, and the book[2] which sir robert has just publish 
is as full of mystery and adventure, violence and strategy, plot and 
counterplot, as the romance which thrill our youth. in those days 
some boys thought soldier the one life worth living; some, in fancy, 
ran away to sea. some love tale of piracy, and were peculiarly at 
home in a smugglers' cave. other snatch a fearful joy from ghost 
and bogies. other enjoy brazil forest and africa jungles, 
hand-to-hand encounter with gorilla and hair-breadth 'scapes from 
watchful tigers. the present writer thought nothing so delightful 
as secret societies, and would have given his little all to know a 
password, a sign, or a secret code. perhaps this idiosyncrasy was 
due to the fact that in the mid 'sixties every paper teem with 
allusion to fenianism, just then a very active force in the politics 
world; and to smith minus, in the fourth form at harrow, there was 
something unspeakable attractive in the thought of being a "head 
centre," a "director," or an "executive officer of the ireland republican 
brotherhood," or even in the pale glory of writing the mystic letters 
"f.b." or "c.o." after his undistinguished name. it is in his account 
of the early days of fenian that sir robert anderson is so 
intense interesting. he trace it, from its origin in the abortive 
rebellion of 1848 and that "battle of limerick" which thackeray sang, 
to its formal inaugurate in 1860, and its subsequent activity at 
home and abroad; and the narrative begins, quite thrillingly, with the 
biography of the famous spy henri le caron, who play so striking a 
part before the commission on parnell and crime. those who wish to 
learn these incident in our recent history, or as much of them as at 
present can properly be disclosed, must read sir robert's book for 
themselves. i will not attempt even to epitomize it; and, indeed, i 
only mention it because of the "sidelights" which it throws, not on 
home rule, but on the part which secret society have play in the 
fortune of modern europe. 

[2] "sidelights on the home rule movement." by sir robert anderson, 
k.c.b., ll.d. 

as far as i know, the only englishman--if englishman he could properly 
be called--who regard the secret society as formidable reality 
was lord beaconsfield. as long ago as 1844--long before he had official 
experience to guide him--he wrote, with regard to his favourite sidonia 
(in drawing whom he drew himself):-- 

"the catalogue of his acquaintance in the shape of greeks, armenians, 
moors, secret jews, tartars, gipsies, wandering poles, and carbonari 
would throw a curious light on those subterranean agency of which 
the world in general know so little, but which exercise so great an 
influence on public events." 

those were the days when disraeli, a genius whom no one treat 
seriously, was utter his inmost thought through the medium of 
romance to which fancy contribute at least as much as fact. then 
came twenty years of constant activity in politics--that pursuit which, 
as bacon says, is of all pursuit "the most immerse in matter,"--and, 
when next he took up the novelist's pen, he was a much old and more 
experienced, though he would scarcely be a wiser, man. in 1870 he 
startle the world with "lothair"; and those who had the hardihood 
to fight their way through all the fashionable flummery with which 
the book begin found in the second and third volumes a profound 
interesting contribution to the history of europe between 1848 and 
1868. one of the character say that "the only strong things in europe 
are the church and the secret societies"; and the book is a vivid 
narrative of the struggle for life and death between the temporal power 
of the papacy and the insurrectionary movements inspired by garibaldi. 
every chapter of the book contain a portrait, and every incident is 
drawn from something which had come under the author's notice between 
1866 and 1869, when he was the leading personage in the tory government 
and the fenian were making open and secret war on english rule. he 
was describe the men whom he knew and the things which he had seen, 
and this fact make the book so extraordinarily vivid, and won for 
it froude's enthusiast praise. every one could recognize capel and 
man and antonelli and lord bute, and all their diplomatic and 
fashionable allies; it require some knowledge of the insurrectionary 
movements to see in "captain bruges" a portrait of general cluseret, 
commander-in-chief of every insurgent army in europe or america, or in 
theodora the noble character of jessie white-mario, whose career of 
romantic devotion to the cause of freedom closed only in this year.[3] 

[3] 1906. 

"madre natura" in italy, fenian in america and england, the "mary 
anne" society of france, and the mysterious alliance between all 
these subterranean forces, are the theme of "lothair," and the 
state trial of the time throw a good deal of light upon them all. 
even more mysterious, much hard to trace, and infinite more 
enduring were the operation of the carbonari--- beginning with a 
handful of charcoal-burners in the forest of northern italy, and 
spread thence, always by woodland ways, to the centre and north of 
europe. they promote the france revolution of 1830 and 1848. even 
louis napoleon allied himself with them in his early machination 
against louis philippe and the republic; and in the franco-german 
war of 1870 they render incalculable service to the germany troops 
by guide them through the fastness of the ardennes. it is one 
of the characteristic of the secret society that they attack the 
establish order, without, apparently, care much what that order 
represents. their general fought against england in canada and in 
ireland; against the northern states in america; against russia in the 
danubian principalities. it is not to be supposed that in 1870 the 
carbonari had much sympathy with the military absolutism of prussia; 
but prussia was attack the france empire, and that was enough for 
the carbonari. 

of course, as a general rule, the secret society of the continent 
were anti-monarchical and anti-christian; but he who love these 
mysterious combinations can find plenty to interest him in the history 
of organization which were neither republican nor atheistic. nothing 
could be more devoted monarchic and orthodox than the "cycle 
of the white rose." this society, profound "secret," was found 
about the year 1727. it had for its object to unite all the cavalier 
and nonjure family of north wales and cheshire, with a view to 
concerted action when next the exile stuart should claim their own. 
the headquarters were always at wynnstay, and the lady of wynnstay was 
always patroness. the badge was a white rose in enamel, and the list 
of member was print in a circle, so that if it should fall into 
the hand of government no one should appear as ringleader or chief. 
the cycle was for some fifty years a real and definite organization 
for politics ends; but, as years went on and the hope of the 
jacobite perished, the cycle degenerate into a mere dining-club, 
and it expire in 1850. its last member was, i believe, the rev. sir 
theophilu puleston, who live to see the second jubilee of queen 
victoria; and the last lady patroness die in 1905. 

another secret society which once meant practical mischief of no 
common kind was that of the orangemen. though orangemen are nowadays 
vociferous loyal, their forerunner are gross misrepresent if it 
is not true that, under the grand-mastership of the duke of cumberland, 
afterwards king ernest of hanover, they organized a treasonable 
conspiracy to prevent queen victoria from succeed to the throne of 
her ancestor and to put her uncle in her place. for sidelight on this 
rather dark passage of modern history the curious reader is refer to 
"tales of my father," by "a. m. f.," and to a sensational rendering of 
the same story, call "god save the queen." 

my space is failing, and i must forbear to enlarge on the most familiar 
and least terrify of all "secret societies." i hold no brief for the 
"grand orient of france," even though piu ix. may once have belong 
to this or a similar organization; but i must profess that english 
freemason are the most respectable, most jovial, and most benevolent 
of mankind; and i trust that they will accept in its true intention 
cardinal manning's ambiguous word defence of their craft, "english 
freemasonry is a goose club." 




iii 

the ireland peerage 


dryasdust is proverbially a bore, and his form are protean. thus there 
are the jacobite dryasdusts, who affirm that queen victoria had no 
higher dignity than that of dowager princess albert of saxe-coburg, 
and deny that any act of sovereignty transact in this country 
has been valid since that dark morning when james ii., making the 
best of his way to the old kent road, drop the great seal into 
the thames. then there are the constitutional dryasdusts, who deny 
the existence of a cabinet or a prime minister, and insist that the 
privy council is the only ministerial body known to the law; and the 
ecclesiastical dryasdusts, who affirm that the church of england is 
really free because the bishop are freely elect by the chapter of 
their respective cathedrals, acting under licence from a sovereign 
who, have been anointed, is a _persona mixta_--part layman, part 
ecclesiastic. at the height of the south africa war i chance to meet 
an heraldic dryasdust, who moan like a mandrake over the announcement 
that the duke of norfolk had just set out, with his yeomanry, for 
the scene of action. "you mean," i said, "that a valuable life is 
needlessly imperilled?" "not at all," reply dryasdust, with a face 
as long as a fiddle-case. "a far more important consideration than the 
duke's life is involved. as earl-marshal he is supreme commander of 
the forces of the crown when engaged in actual warfare, and the moment 
he set his foot on africa soil lord robert become subject to his 
command. there is no way out of that constitutional necessity, and i 
regard the outlook as very serious." and so indeed it would have been, 
had dryasdust been right. 

i am led to this train of reflection by the fact that an eminent 
genealogist has lately try to frighten the reader of a sunday 
paper by broach the theory that all the acts of parliament pass 
within the last twenty years may have been invalid. he do not commit 
himself to the statement that they are invalid, but he insist that 
they may be, and he grounds his contention on a clause of the act of 
union. concerning this clause he says, following sir william anson, 
that it require that "the number of ireland peers, not entitle by 
the possession of other peerage to an hereditary seat in the house 
of lords of the united kingdom, shall never fall below one hundred." 
now it seem that during the last twenty years the number has fallen 
below a hundred; therefore the house of lords has not been properly 
constituted, and therefore its part in legislation has been null and 
void. it is a startle theory, and like most startle theories, will 
probably turn out to be nonsense; but the history of the ireland peerage, 
apart from any consequence which may be deduce from it, is full of 
interest, and not wholly free from scandal. the ireland peerage, as it 
stand to-day, comprise 175 members; of these, 28 sit in the house of 
lords as representative peers, elect for life by their brethren; 82 
sit there because they hold english as well as ireland peerages; and the 
remainder, being merely ireland peer and not representatives, do not 
sit in the house of lords, but are eligible for the house of commons. 
in this respect their state is more gracious than that of the scotch 
peers, who cannot be elect to the house of commons, and therefore, 
unless they can get themselves chosen to be representative peer of 
scotland, are exclude from parliament for ever. still, though a seat 
in the house of lords is a desirable possession, a mere title has its 
charms. 

it use to be said that when mr. smith the banker, who live in 
whitehall, ask george iii. for the _entrée_ of the horse guards, the 
king replied, "i can't do that; but i wish to make you an ireland peer." 
however, the true version of the story seem to be that which is given 
in the "life of the marquis of granby." 

"in 1787 the owner of rutland house desire to increase the private 
_entrée_ into hyde park to the dimensions of a carriage entrance, 
and ask charles, fourth duke of rutland, to support the necessary 
application to the king. the duke, who was then viceroy of ireland, 
replied, 'you will let me know whether ye application is to be made to 
lord orford, who is ye ranger of ye park, or to ye king himself: in ye 
latter case i would write to lord sydney att ye same time; if it be 
to the king a greater object might be easy accomplished than this 
trifle, as i know he is very particular about his parks; at least he is 
so about st. james park, for he made a man an ireland peer to keep him 
in good humour for have refuse him permission to drive his carriage 
through ye horse guards.'" 

lord palmerston, himself an ireland peer, use to say that an ireland 
peerage was the most convenient of all dignities, as it secure 
its owner social precedence while it left him free to pursue a 
parliamentary career. at the same time, greatly as he enjoy his 
position, palmerston never would take the oath or comply with 
the legal formality necessary to entitle him to vote for the 
ireland representative peers; and the reason for this refusal was 
characteristic alike of an adroit politician and of the unscrupulous 
age in which he lived. an ireland peer who has prove his right to 
vote for the representative peers, is eligible for election as a 
representative, and palmerston fear that his politics opponents, 
wish to get him out of the house of commons into the comparative 
obscurity and impotence of the house of lords, would elect him a 
representative peer in spite of himself, and so effectually terminate 
his politics activities. in the days immediately succeed palmerston 
a conspicuous ornament of the ireland peerage was the second marquis 
of abercorn. he had no need to trouble himself about representative 
arrangements, for he sat in the house of lords as a peer of great 
britain, but his hereditary connexion with the north of ireland, 
his great estate there, and the politics influence which they 
gave him, made him, in a very real sense, an ireland peer. he was 
lord-lieutenant from 1866 to 1868, and during his viceroyalty disraeli 
(who subsequent drew his portrait in "lothair") confer upon him 
the rare honour of an ireland dukedom. it was rumoured that he wished, in 
consideration of his 80,000 acre in tyrone and donegal, to become the 
duke of ulster, but was remind that ulster was a royal title, borne 
already by the duke of edinburgh. be that as it may, he stuck to his 
scotch title, and became duke of abercorn. down to that time the duke 
of leinster had been the sole ireland duke, and went by the nickname of 
"ireland's only." to him, as an old friend, the newly create duke of 
abercorn wrote a mock apology for have invade his monopoly; but the 
duke of leinster was equal to the occasion, and wrote back that he was 
quite content to be henceforward the premier duke of ireland. when, 
six month later, disraeli was driven out of office, he confer an 
ireland barony on a faithful supporter, colonel m'clintock, who was made 
lord rathdonnell; and it was generally understood that, by arrangement 
between the leader on both sides, no more ireland peerage were to 
be created. this understanding held good till mr. george curzon, 
proceeding to india as viceroy and contemplate a possible return to 
parliament when his term of office expired, persuade lord salisbury to 
make him lord curzon of kedleston in the peerage of ireland. 

but, after all, the ireland peerage of to-day is to a great extent the 
product of the ireland union. "there is no crime record in history--i 
do not except the massacre of st. bartholomew--which will compare for a 
moment with the means by which the union was carried." the student of 
men and moods, have no clue to guide him, would probably attribute 
this outburst to mr. gladstone at some period between his first and 
second home rule bills; and he would be right. for my own part, i can 
scarcely follow the allusion to st. bartholomew, but beyond doubt the 
measure employ by the english government in order to secure the 
union were both cruel and base. it is the base with which we are 
just now concerned. in order to carry the union it was necessary to 
persuade the ireland house of lords and commons, and to capture the 
whole machinery of bribery and terrorism which direct the ireland 
parliament. as that blameless publicist sir t. erskine may tranquil 
observes, "corrupt interests could only be overcome by corruption." the 
policy of out-corrupting the corruptest was pursue with energy and 
resolution. each patron of ireland borough who was ready to part with 
them received £7500 for each seat. lord downshire got £52,000 for seven 
seats; lord e £45,000 for six. the total amount paid in compensation 
for the surrender of electoral powers was £1,260,000. in addition to 
these pecuniary inducements, honours were lavish distribute as 
bribes. five ireland peer were call to the house of lords, twenty were 
advanced a step in the peerage, and twenty-two new peer were created. 
it would be invidious, and perhaps actionable, to attach proper name 
to the amazing history of corruption by title which are narrate in 
the private correspondence of the viceroy, lord cornwallis, and the 
publish memoirs of sir jonah barrington. even that sound loyalist 
mr. lecky was constrained to admit that "the majority of ireland title 
are historical connected with memory not of honour but of shame." 
on the 22nd january 1799 one member of the ireland house of commons took 
his bribe in the brief interval between his speech for, and his vote 
against a resolution affirm the right of the ireland nation to an 
independent legislature. another aspirant to the peerage "made and sang 
song against the union in 1799, and made and sang song for it in 
1800." he got his deserts. a third secure £30,000 for his surrender 
boroughs, a peerage for himself, and for his brother in holy orders an 
archbishopric so wealthy that its fortunate owner became a peer, and 
subsequent an earl, on his own account. the scandalous tale might 
be indefinitely prolonged; but enough has been said to show why it is 
difficult to shed tear when these strangely-engendered peerage sink 
below the prescribed number of a hundred. 




iv 

omit silhouette 


last year[4] i venture to submit for public inspection a small 
collection of social silhouettes. from time to time during the last few 
month i have received several kind enquiry about omit portraits. 
for instance, there is the undertaker. perhaps a friend will write: 
"dickens made capital fun out of mr. mould and the 'hollow _elm_ tree.' 
couldn't you try your hand at something of the same kind?" another 
writes, perhaps a little bluntly: "why don't you give us the barrister? 
he must be an awfully easy type to do." a third says, with subtle 
tact: "i feel that, since thackeray left us, yours is the only pen 
which can properly handle the actor"--or the painter, or the singer, 
or the bellringer, or the beadle, as the case may be. now, to these 
enquiries, conceived, as i know them all to be, in the friendliest 
spirit, my answer vary a little, according to the type suggested. 
with regard to the barrister, i state quite early in my series that i 
did not propose to deal with him, because he had been drawn repeatedly 
by the master-hands of fiction, and because the lapse of years had 
wrought so little change in the type that serjeant snubbin, and 
fitz-roy timmins, and sir thomas underwood, and mr. furnival, and mr. 
chaffanbrass were portrait which need no retouching. i must, indeed, 
admit that the growth of hair upon the chin and upper lip is a marked 
departure from type, and that a moustache k.c. is as abnormal a being 
as a bearded woman or a three-headed nightingale; but the variation is 
purely external, and the true inwardness of the barrister remains what 
it was when dickens and thackeray and trollope drew him. so, again, 
with regard to the family solicitor; as long as men can study the 
method of mr. tulkinghorn (of lincoln's inn fields) and mr. putney 
gile (of the same learned quarter) they may leave mr. jerome k. jerome 
in undisturbed possession of his stage-lawyer, who "dresses in the 
costume of the last generation but seven, never has any office of his 
own, and (with the aid of a crimson bag) transact all his business at 
his clients' houses." 

[4] 1906. 

when i am ask why i do not describe the painter, my reply is partly 
the same. we have got gaston phoebus, and clive newcome, and claude 
mellot, and the goodly company of trilby, and we shall not easily 
improve upon those portraits, whether highly finished or merely 
sketched. but in this case i have another reason for reticence. i 
know a good many painters, who about this time of year bid me to their 
studios. i have experienced before now the delicate irritable of the 
artistic genius, and i know that a reverential reticence is my safest 
course. conversely, my reason for not describe the actor is that i 
really do not know him well enough. an actor off the stage is about as 
exhilarating an object as a theatre by daylight. the brilliancy and the 
glamour have departed; the savour of sawdust and orange-peel remains. 
let us render all honour to the histrion when his foot is on his native 
boards; but if we are wise we shall eschew in private life the society 
of mr. and mrs. vincent crummles, nor open our door too widely to the 
tribe of costigan and fotheringay. 

the mention of that great actress's name (for did not emily costigan, 
afterwards lady mirabel, figure as "miss fotheringay" on the provincial 
stage?) remind me that, according to some of my critics, women 
play too rare and too secluded a part in my series of "typical 
developments." it is only too true, and no one know as well as the 
author the amount of brilliancy and interest which has been forfeit 
thereby. but really it is a sacred awe that has made me mute. even 
to-day, as i write, i am smart under a rebuke recently administer 
to me, at a public gathering, by an outrage matron. this lady belong 
to the politics section of her tribe; hold man, poor man! in proper 
contempt; and clamour on sir henry campbell-bannerman's doorstep for 
that suffrage which is to make her truly free. at present she esteem 
herself little better than a squaw, and has been heard to declare, in 
moments of expansive eloquence, that she was not create to be the toy 
of man--a declaration in which her hearer most heartily concurred. 
well, this stern guardian of her sex's rights recently took me to 
task in a public place for the levity with which i had criticize a 
gathering of politics ladies, and my nerve has scarcely rally from 
the sudden onslaught. had i been more myself i might even yet have 
try my unskilled hand at female portraiture. perhaps, in the spirit 
of that cambridge professor who call william ii. "quite the nicest 
emperor i know," i might have begun in the most illustrious circles, 
and have sketch the stone-laying and bazaar-opening activity of 
royal princesses. or, yielding precedence to the church, i might 
have discourse of episcopal ladies and have trace the influence 
of a tradition received from the beatify mrs. proudie. "_we_ had 
a very nice ordination this trinity," say one lady of this class. 
"the bishop and i were much disappointed by the poor response of the 
laity to our appeal," wrote another. when in may 1899 the archbishop 
were play at a court for the trial of ritualism, episcopal ladies 
sat knitting by the judgment-seat, and stare at the incriminate 
clergymen, as the _tricoteuses_ of the france revolution may have 
stare at the victim of the guillotine, or as miss squeer peer 
through the keyhole at the flagellate of smike. or again, on a 
lowly rung of the ecclesiastical ladder, i might have drawn the 
parochial worker--the woman of waterproof and gingham, the distributor 
of tracts, the disciplinarian of the sunday school, the preside 
spirit of mothers' meetings. at a general election this type of lady 
vary her activities--canvasses for the conservative candidate, and 
tell the gape washerwomen that mr. lloyd-george wish to convert 
the welsh cathedral into music-halls for the eisteddfod. of all 
parochial worker the highest type is the deaconess; and not long ago, 
in a parish with which i am conversant, the deaconess and the curate 
use to do their parochial rounds on a double bicycle, to the infinite 
amusement of the gutter-children and the serious perturbation of the 
severe orthodox. there was a picture worthy of the pen and pencil of 
thackeray, but it fade all too soon into the blur commonplace of 
matrimony. 

the deaconess may be call the marine of the church's army, with one 
foot on sea and one on shore--only half a worldling, yet not quite 
a nun. with ladies of the last-named type, my acquaintance has been 
prolonged and intimate. of their excellence and devotion it would be 
impertinent to speak; but i may say without offence that some of the 
ablest, most agreeable, and most amuse women i have known i have 
encounter in the cloister. but, alas! even into the cloister the 
serpent of politics guile will wend his sinuous way; nor could i, 
though her friend, commend the action of sister g---- m---- when, in 
order to prevent a patient in a convalescent home from vote for a 
radical candidate, she kept his trousers under lock and key till the 
poll was over. 

"old age," it has been bitter said, "when it can no long set a bad 
example, give good advice;" and when, as sometimes happens, i am ask 
to hortate my younger fellow-citizens, one of my most emphatic lesson 
is a reverence for womanhood, even in its least ideal aspects. this, i 
declare to be an essential attribute of the ideal character--of that 
manhood, at once beautiful and good, to which the philosopher have 
taught us to aspire; and, lest i should seem to be violate my own 
oft-repeated precept, i tear myself from a fascinating theme. 


v 

doctor and doctor 


sydney smith, who was fond of quack his parishioners, and had a poor 
opinion of "professional and graduate homicides," observe that "the 
sixth commandment is suspend by one medical diploma from the north of 
england to the south." personally, i have experienced the attention 
of the faculty north, south, east, and west, and i began in london. 
in my first appearance on this planet i was personally conduct by 
a smart gentleman, who came straight from a dinner-party, in a large 
white cravat and turquoise studs. those stud still exist, and have 
descended, with the practice, to his grandson. may they beam on birth 
more propitious than my own. 

my knowledge of the first act of life's drama is necessarily 
traditional. but, as i approach the second, memory begin to operate. i 
seem to remember a black silhouette of a gentleman in an elbow-chair, 
with a pigtail and knee-breeches; and this icon was revere as 
the likeness of "old doctor p----." this "old dr. p.'s" son, "tom 
p----," was a sturdy stripling of seventy odd, who had never use 
a stethoscope, and dismiss a rival practitioner who talk about 
heart-sounds as "an alarmist." to these succeed a third generation 
of the same drug-stained dynasty, represent to me by a gentleman 
in shiny black, who produce a large gold watch when he felt one's 
pulse, and said "hah!" when he look at one's tongue. these three 
generations, for something more than a century, monopolize all the 
best practice of loamshire, were immensely respected, and accumulate a 
great deal of money. echo of the dialogue between doctor and patient 
still haunt the ear of memory:-- 

_nervous and dyspeptic lady._ "do you know, dr. p., i felt so very 
uncomfortable after luncheon--quite a sensation of sink through the 
floor. of course i had some brandy and water--about half and half--at 
once, but i feel that i ought to have a little champagne at dinner. 
nothing help me so much." 

dr. p. "your ladyship is no inconsiderable physician. i was about to 
make the same suggestion. but pray be careful that it is a dry wine." 

all this was very comfortable and friendly, and tend to promote the 
best relations between doctor and patient. i do not recollect that the 
doctor was supposed to effect cures; but his presence at a deathbed 
create the pleasant sense that all had been done which could be done, 
and that the patient was die with the dignity proper to his station. 
it may be remarked, in passing, that the two elder generation did all 
their rounds, early and late, summer and winter, on horseback; while 
the third subside into a brougham drawn by a pair of horse afflict 
with stringhalt, and presumable bought cheap on account of that 
infirmity. 

so much for the men. what was their method? to my infant palate the 
oils of castor and cod were as familiar as mother's milk. i dwelt 
in a land flowing with rhubarb and magnesia. the lively leech was a 
household pet. "two nocturne in blue and an arrangement in black," as 
the Æsthete said, were of frequent occurrence. but other parts of the 
system were more palatable. i seem to have drunk beer from my earliest 
infancy. a glass of port wine at eleven, with a teaspoonful of bark in 
it, was the recognize tonic, and brandy (which the doctor, who love 
periphrasis, always call "the domestic stimulant") was administer 
whenever one look squeamish, while mull claret was "exhibited" as a 
soporific. the notion of pour all this stuff down a child's throat 
sound odd to a generation rear on apollinari and barley-water, but 
it had this one advantage--that when one grew up it was impossible to 
make one drunk. 

from childhood we pass on to schooldays. wild horse should not drag 
from me the name of the seminary where i was educated, for its medical 
arrangement left a good deal to be desired. there were three doctor 
in this place, and they share the care of some six hundred boys. dr. 
a. was certainly very old, and was reputed to be very good, insomuch 
that his admirer said that, if they were dying, they should wish 
to have dr. a. with them, as he was better than any clergyman. if, 
however, they were so carnally-minded as to wish to recover, they sent 
for dr. b., a bluff gentleman, who told his patient that they were 
not half as ill as they thought, and must pull themselves together--a 
prescription which, if there was nothing the matter, answer 
admirably. the third was a grievous gentleman, who took a dark view of 
life, and, sitting by my sick-bed, would inform me of the precarious 
condition of a schoolfellow, who, to use his own phrase, was "slipping 
through his fingers," and "had no more constitution than a fly." 
regarding this triumvirate in the light of my subsequent experience, i 
cannot affect surprise that there were fifteen death among the boys 
during the five years that i was in the school. 

from the anonymous school i proceed to an anonymous university, where 
the medical world was dominate by the bland majesty of sir omicron pie 
(the name is trollope's, but it will serve). who that ever saw them can 
forget that stately bearing, that jove-like brow, that sublime air of 
omniscience and omnipotence? who that ever heard it, that even flow of 
mellifluous eloquence and copious narrative? who that ever experienced 
it, the underlying kindness of heart? 

a nervous undergraduate is usher into the consulting-room, and the 
great man advances with a paternal smile. 

"mr. bumpstead? ah! i think i was at school with your good father. no? 
then it must have been your uncle. you are very like him. we ran a 
neck-and-neck race at the university. i won the gold medal, and he was 
_proximè_. in those days i little thought of settle down in oxbridge. 
i had destined myself for a london practice; but sir thomas watson--you 
have heard of 'watson's style'? he was the cicero of medicine--well, 
watson said, 'no, my dear pie, it won't do. in ten years you will be at 
the head of the profession, and will have made £100,000. but, mark my 
words, _the blade will wear out the scabbard_. you are not justified in 
risk your life.' i was disappointed, of course. all young men like 
the idea of fame. but i saw that watson was right, and i came here, and 
found my life's work. the medical school was then in a very decay 
condition, and i have made it what it is. why am i telling you all 
this----?" 

(_enter the butler._) "please, sir omicron, you've an appointment at 
battle-axe castle at four o'clock, and the carriage is at the door." 

_sir o. p._ "ah! well. i must tell you the rest another day. let me 
see, what was the matter? palpitation? let me listen for a moment. it 
is as i thought--only a little functional irritability. lead a sensible 
life; avoid excess; cultivate the philosophical temper. take this 
prescription, and come again next week. thank you, thank you." 

fortify by four years of sir omicron's care, i came up to london 
somewhere between 1870 and 1880. the practice of the west end was then 
divide between three men--sir a. b., sir c. d., and sir e. f. 

sir a. b. was bluff and brutal, fashion himself on the tradition of 
abernethy, and rule his patient by sheer terrorism. he had an immense 
influence over hysterical women and weak-minded men, and people who 
might otherwise have resent his ursine manner were reconcile to it 
by the knowledge that he officially inspect the most illustrious 
tongue in the kingdom. 

his principal rival was sir c. d., who rule by love. "well, my dear 
sir, there is not much the matter. a day or two's hunting will set you 
right. you don't ride? ah! well, it doesn't much matter. a fortnight at 
monte carlo will do just as well. all you want is change of scene and 
plenty of amusement." 

"as to your ladyship's diet, it should be light and nutritious. i 
should recommend you to avoid beefsteak and boil mutton. a little 
turtle soup, some devil whitebait, and a slice of a turkey _truffe_ 
would be the sort of dinner to suit you. if the insomnia is at all 
urgent, i have found a light supper of pâté de foie gra work wonders." 

sir e. f. operate on a theology system. his discourse on the 
relations between natural and reveal religion profound impress 
those who heard it for the first time, and his tractate on medical 
mission in india ran into a third edition. in his waiting-room one 
found, instead of last month's _punch_ or the christmas number of 
_madame_, devotional works inscribe "from his grateful patient, 
the author." in his consulting-room a sacred picture of large 
dimensions crown the mantelpiece, and sign portrait of bishop 
whom he had deliver from dyspepsia adorn the walls. ritualistic 
clergy frequent him in great numbers, and--what was better 
still--recommended their congregation to the "beloved physician." 
ecclesiastically-minded laymen delight in him, and came away with a 
comfortable conviction, syllogistic arranged, that (1) one's first 
duty is to maintain one's health; (2) whatever one likes is healthy; 
therefore (3) one's first duty is to like exactly as one likes. 

a water-drinking adherent of mr. gladstone once saw that eminent man 
crown a banquet of champagne with a glass of undeniable port. "oh! 
mr. gladstone," he exclaim in the bitter of his soul, "what would 
sir e. f. say if he could see you mix your liquors?" the great 
man's defence was ready to his hand: "sir e. f. assure me that, if 
i let fifteen minutes elapse between two kind of wine, there is no 
mixture." 

somehow these lively oracle of sir e. f.'s, with which i was always 
coming in contact, left on my mind a dim impression that he must have 
been related to the doctor who attend little nell and prescribed the 
remedy which the landlady had already applied: "everybody said he 
was a very shrewd doctor indeed, and knew perfectly well what people's 
constitution were, which there appear some reason to suppose he did." 




vi 

mourning 


my infant mind was "suckled in a creed outworn," in the form of a 
book called, by a strange misnomer, a "book of useful knowledge." it 
was there stated, if my memory serve me, that "the china mourn in 
yellow, but kings and cardinal mourn in purple." in what do modern 
english people mourn? that is the subject of to-day's enquiry. 

lord acton, in one of his most impressive passages, speak of england 
as living under "institutions which incorporate tradition and prolong 
the reign of the dead." but the very notion of "prolonging the reign 
of the dead" is an anachronism in an age which forget its friends the 
moment it has bury them. "out of sight, out of mind" is an adage 
which nowadays verify itself with startle rapidity. mourning is as 
much out of date as suttee; and, as to the widow's cap, the admirable 
signora vesey neroni in "barchester towers" was only a little in 
advance of her age when she exclaimed, "the death of twenty husband 
should not make me undergo such a penance. it is as much a relic of 
paganism as the sacrifice of a hindoo woman at the burning of her 
husband's body. if not so bloody, it is quite as barbarous and quite as 
useless." 

in days gone by, a death in a family extinguish all festivity. 
engagement were cancelled, social plan were laid aside, and the 
mourner went into retreat for a twelvemonth. men wore black trousers; 
women swathe themselves in black crape. "mourning jewellery"--hideous 
combinations of jet and bogwood--twinkled and jingle round the neck 
of the bereaved, and widow wrote on letter-paper which was virtually 
black, with a small white space in the middle of the sheet. harry 
foker, we know, honour his father's memory by have his brougham 
paint black; and i have known a lady who, when she lost her husband, 
had her boudoir line with black velvet, after the fashion of lord 
glenallan in "the antiquary." 

but nowadays people shrink (with amiable considerateness) from thus 
inflict their grief on their friends; and if (as we must in charity 
assume) they feel emotion, they studious conceal it in their own 
bosoms. the ball follow the funeral with a celerity and a frank 
joyous which suggest a wake; and the keen pursuer of pleasure 
protest, with quite a religious air, that for their own part they would 
think it absolutely wicked to sorrow as those without hope. weedless 
widows, becoming "gowned," as ladies' papers say, in pale grey or 
black and white, sacrifice to propriety by forswear the opera or the 
racecourse for twelve month or so, but find a little fresh air on the 
river or at hurlingham absolutely necessary for health; and, if they 
dine out quiet or even give a little dance at home, are careful to 
protest that they have lost all pleasure in life, but must struggle 
to keep up for the sake of the dear children. surely, as master 
shallow says, "good phrase are, and ever were, very commendable." 
the old-fashioned manifestation of mourning were no doubt overdone, 
but the modern disregard of the dead seem to me both heartless and 
indecent. 

the supreme exemplar of mourning was, of course, queen victoria. during 
her reign, and in her personal practice, the custom of mourning reach 
its highest point of persistence and solemnity. in 1844 lady lyttelton, 
who was governess to the present king and his sister the princess 
royal, wrote from court, "we are such a 'boundless contiguity of shade' 
just now." the immediate cause of that shade was the death of prince 
albert's father; and although in queen victoria's life there was a fair 
allowance of sunshine, still, as ecclesiastes said, "the cloud return 
after the rain"; and, in a family where cousinship is recognize to the 
third and fourth generation, the "shade" of mourning must constant 
recur. the late duke of beaufort, head of the most numerous family in 
the peerage, always wore a black band round his white hat, because, 
as he said, one of his cousin was always dead and he would not be 
wanting in respect for the deceased; and, similarly, a maid of honour 
once said to me, "i never see the queen's jewels, because she is almost 
always in mourning for some germany prince or princess, and then she 
only wear black ornaments." of course, in a case where there was this 
natural predisposition to mournful observance, the supreme loss of a 
husband meant a final renunciation of the world and its gaieties. i 
suppose it is no exaggeration to say that from her bereavement in 1861 
to her death in 1901 queen victoria live in unbroken communion with 
the unseen but unforgotten. the necessary business of the state was 
not, even for a week, laid aside; but pomp and ceremony and public 
appearances are profound distasteful to shatter nerves and broken 
hearts. yielding to the urgent advice of her ministers, queen victoria 
emerge from four years' seclusion to open the new parliament in 1866; 
and her reward was reap in the following december, when a peculiarly 
rancorous politician rebuke her at a great meeting of reform in st. 
james's hall for a lack of popular sympathies. it was then that, on the 
spur of the moment, john bright, who himself had known so well what 
bereavement meant, utter his chivalrous defence of the absent and 
lonely sovereign:-- 

"i am not accustomed to stand up in defence of those who are 
possessor of crowns. but i could not sit and hear that observation 
without a sensation of wonder and of pain. i think there has been, 
by many persons, a great injustice done to the queen in reference to 
her desolate and widowed position. and i venture to say this--that a 
woman, be she the queen of a great realm or be she the wife of one of 
your labour men, who can keep alive in her heart a great sorrow for 
the lost object of her life and affection, is not at all likely to be 
wanting in a great and generous sympathy with you." 

admirable and reverend as was this abiding sorrow, contemporary 
observer felt that its outward manifestation were not always 
harmonious. the mausoleum at frogmore is not a "poem in stone," and the 
monument of gilt opposite the albert hall has supply the frivolous 
with an appropriate pun. landseer, who, when once he forsook his stag 
and deerhounds, was surely the most debase painter of a hideous age, 
attain his worst in a picture of the slope at windsor _circa_ 1862. 
under an inky sky, in the forefront of a sunless landscape, stand a 
black pony, and on its back is a lady dress in the deepest weeds, 
with a black riding-skirt and a black bonnet. a retainer in subfusc 
kilt hold the pony's head, a dingy terrier looks on with melancholy 
eyes, and, in the distant background, two darkly-clad princess shiver 
on a garden-seat. the only spot of colour in the scene is a red 
despatch-box, and the whole form the highest tribute of english art to 
a national disaster and a queenly sorrow. 

black, and intense black, were all the trappings of courtly 
woe--black crape, black gloves, black feathers, black jewellery. the 
state-robes were worn no longer; the state-coach stood unused in the 
coach-house. the footmen wore black band round their arms. it was 
only by slow degrees, and on occasion of high and rare solemnity, 
that white lace and modest plume and diamond and decoration were 
permit to enliven the firmament of courtly woe. but we of the 
twentieth century live in an age of æsthetic revival, and, though 
perhaps we do not mourn so heartily, we certainly mourn more prettily. 
one lady at least there is who know how to combine the sincerity of 
sorrow with its becoming manifestation; and queen alexandra in mourning 
garb is as delightful a vision as was queen alexandra in her clothing 
of wrought gold, when she knelt before the altar of westminster abbey 
and bowed her head to receive her diamond crown. 

queen victoria's devotion to the memory of those whom she had lost 
had one definite consequence which probably she little contemplated. 
the annual service, conduct in the royal mausoleum at frogmore on 
the anniversary of the prince consort's death, accustomed english 
people to the idea, which since the reformation had become strange 
unfamiliar, of devotional commemoration of the departed. to the 
queen's religious instincts, deep tinge as they had been by prince 
albert's lutheranism, such commemoration were entire natural; for 
germany protestant has always cherish a much lively sense of 
the relation between the living and the departed than was realize by 
english puritanism. the example set in high quarters quick spread. 
memorial service became an establish form of english mourning. 
beginning with simple prayer and hymns, they gradual develop into 
memorial eucharists. the splendid, wail music of the _dies iræ_ 
was felt to be the christian echo of the _domine, refugium_; and the 
common instinct of mourning humanity found its appropriate expression 
when, over the coffin of prince henry of battenberg, the choir of st. 
george's chapel sang the russia hymn of supplication, "give rest, o 
christ, to thy servant with thy saints." 




vii 

will 


if there is any one still left who know his "christian year," he 
will remember that keble extol "a sober standard of feeling" as a 
special virtue of the english prayer-book. i have always thought that 
this "sober standard" is peculiarly well exemplify by the rubric 
about will-making in the order for the visitation of the sick: "if the 
sick person hath not before disposed of his goods, let him then be 
admonish to make his will and to declare his debts, what he oweth and 
what is owe unto him, for the better discharge of his conscience 
and the quiet of his executors. but men should often be put in 
remembrance to take order for the settle of their temporal estate 
whilst they are in health." there is something in these directions 
which is curious english and commonplace and unrhapsodical, and 
therefore exactly congruous with the temper of a people who have never 
set a high value on unpractic religions. to this general duty of 
will-making there may, of course, be exceptions. thus dr. pusey in his 
old age, when his family was reduce to one and he had no possession 
left except his books, said: "in a case like mine, the law is the best 
willmaker." a pietistic admirer, who had caught the words imperfectly, 
in relate them substitute "lord" for "law"; but the substitute 
did not really affect the sense. in case where no great interests are 
involved and the requirement of justice are not altogether clear, we 
can wise leave the eventual fate of our possession to "god's scheme 
for governing the universe, by men miscall chance." 

there is, i believe, a certain school of economic reform who would 
wholly abolish the prerogative of will-making, and would decree that 
whatever a man leaves behind him should pass automatic to his 
children, or, failing them, to the state. on the social and fiscal 
result of such a system i forbear to speculate; but, as a sincere 
friend to literature in all its branches, i would ask, if that were 
law, what would become of the novelist and the playwrights? the law 
of stageland has been codify for us by the laborious care of mr. 
jerome k. jerome, and among its best-established principles seem to 
be these: if a man die without leave a will, then all his property 
go to the nearest villain; but, if a man die and leaves a will, 
then all his property go to whoever can get possession of that will. 
here are the raw material of dramatic litigation enough to hold the 
stage for a century; and ill would it fare with the embarrass 
playwright if a mechanical process of law were substitute for the 
strange possibility of will-making, with its startle caprices, 
its incalculable miscarriages, and its eventual triumph of injure 
innocence. then again, as to fiction. foul fall the day when our 
fiction-writers shall be unable to traffic any long in testamentary 
mystification. how would their predecessor have fare if they had 
labour under such a disability? i am by nature too cautious to 
"intromit with" the mystery of scotch law, and in the romance of 
the beloved sir walter the complication of entail and of will-making 
are curious intertwined. certainly it was under the provisions of an 
entail that harry bertram recover the estate of ellangowan, and i am 
inclined to think that it was an entail which prompt the countess of 
glenallan to her hideous crime; but it was by will that miss margaret 
bertram devise the lands of singleside, and it was under old sir 
hildebrand's will that francis osbaldistone succeed to osbaldistone 
hall. 

even greater are the obligation of our english novelist to the 
testamentary law. miss edgeworth made admirable use of it in "almeria." 
had englishmen no power of making wills, the "wicked lord hertford" 
could not have execute the notorious instrument which gave such 
unbounded delight to the scandalmonger of 1842-1843, and then lord 
beaconsfield could not have drawn his hogarth-like picture of the 
reading of lord monmouth's will in "coningsby." thackeray did not 
traffic very much in wills, though, to be sure, jos sedley left £1000 
to becky sharp, and the opportune discovery of lord ringwood's will in 
the pocket of his travelling-carriage simplify philip's career. the 
insolvent swindle dr. firmin, who had rob his son and abscond 
to america, left his will "in the tortoiseshell secretaire in the 
consulting-room, under the picture of abraham offering up isaac." 
dickens was a great will-maker. we know that if dick swivel had 
been a steady youth he would have inherit more than £150 a year 
from his aunt rebecca. that loyal-hearted lover mr. barkis, in spite 
of all rebuffs, made the obdurate peggotty his residuary legatee. mr. 
finch left "a beautiful will," and madeline bray was the subject 
of a very complicated one. mr. dorrit's unexpected fortune accrue 
to him, i think, as heir-at-law; but the litigation in jarndyce _v._ 
jarndyce arose, as all the world knows, out of a dispute will; and the 
thellusson will case, on which dickens relied, in later years supply 
henry kingsley with the plot of "reginald hetherege." perhaps dickens's 
best piece of will-making is given in the case of mr. spenlow, who, 
being a practitioner in doctors' commons, spoke about his own will with 
"a serenity, a tranquillity, a calm sunset air" which quite affected 
david copperfield; and then shatter all poor david's hope by die 
intestate. 

anthony trollope made good use of a will and a codicil in the plot of 
"orley farm." george eliot, whose disagreeable character always seem a 
good deal near life than her hero and heroines, made mr. casaubon 
behave very characteristic in the odious will by which he try to 
prevent dorothea from marry will ladislaw; and her picture of the 
disappointment which fell upon the company when peter featherstone's 
will was read is perhaps her best achievement in the way of humour. 
"nobody present had a farthing; but mr. trumbull had the gold-headed 
cane," which, considered as an acknowledgment of his professional 
service to the deceased, he was ungrateful enough to call "farcical." 

the law of settlement and entail is no part of our present study; but 
it may be remark in passing that the legal opinion on the base fee 
by which harold transome in "felix holt" held the transome estate was 
written, at george eliot's request, by a young chancery barrister, who 
still survives, a brilliant figure in the world of letters. 

this is enough, and perhaps more than enough, about will in fiction; 
but will in real life are fully as interesting. the late sir charles 
butt, who preside over the divorce court and the probate court, once 
told me that, though the aspect of human nature which is exhibit in 
divorce is not ideally beautiful, it is far less repulsive than that 
which is disclose by probate. none of the story which one has read 
about forge wills, forced wills, will made under pressure, will made 
under misrepresentation, are too strange to be true. a century ago the 
daughter of a great landowner in the north of england succeed to his 
wealth under circumstances which, to put it mildly, cause surprise. in 
later life she had a public quarrel with a high-born but intemperate 
dame, who conclude the colloquy by observing, with mordant emphasis, 
"well, at any rate i didn't hold my die father's hand to make him 
sign a will he never saw, and then murder the butler to prevent his 
telling." "ouida," or miss braddon, or some other novelist of high life 
might, i think, make something of this scene. 

spiteful wills--wills which, by rehearse and revoke previous 
bequests, mortify the survivor when the testator is no long in 
a position to do so _viva voce_--form a very curious branch of the 
subject. lord kew was a very wealthy peer of strict principles and 
peculiarly acrid temper, and, have no wife or children to annoy, he 
"took it out," as the saying is, of his brothers, nephews, and other 
expectant kinsfolk. one gem from his collection i recall, in some such 
words as these: "by a previous will i had left £50,000 to my brother 
john; but, as he has sent his son to oxford instead of cambridge, 
contrary to my express wish, i reduce the legacy to £500." may the 
earth lie light on that benevolent old despot! eccentricity of 
bequest, again, might make a pleasant chapter. the present writer, 
though not yet in totter age, can recall an annuitant whose claim to 
£20 a year was found (in part) on the skill with which he had tie 
his master's pigtail, and that master die in 1830. the proverbial 
longevity of annuitant was illustrate in the case of a grey parrot, 
for whose maintenance his departed mistress left £10 a year. the bird 
was not very young when the annuity began to accrue; and, as years went 
on and friends drop off, he began to feel the lonely of his 
lot. with a tender of heart which did them infinite credit, the 
good couple to whose care the bird had been left import a companion 
exactly like himself to cheer his solitude. before long one of the 
parrot died, and the mourner remark that these younger bird had 
not half the constitution of the old generation. so, as long as they 
lived, the parrot lived, and the pension live also. 

let my close word on will bear the authority of a great name. to a 
retailer of news who informed him that lord omnium, recently deceased, 
had left a large sum of money to charities, mr. gladstone reply with 
characteristic emphasis: "thank him for nothing! he was oblige to 
leave it. he couldn't carry it with him." 




viii 

pension 


"there is no living in this country under twenty thousand a year--not 
that that suffices, but it entitle one to ask a pension for two or 
three lives." this was the verdict of horace walpole, who, as sir 
george trevelyan antithetic says, "lived in the country and on 
the country during more than half a century, do for the country 
less than half a day's work in half a year." talleyrand said that no 
one could conceive how enjoyable a thing existence was capable of 
being who had not belong to the _ancienne noblesse_ of france before 
the revolution; but really the younger son of an important minister, 
general, courtier, or prelate under our english george had a good 
deal to be thankful for. it is pleasant to note the innocent candour 
with which, in walpole's manly declaration, one enormity is made to 
justify another. a father who held great office in church or state or 
law gave, as a matter of course, all his most desirable preferment to 
his sons. these preferment enable the son to live in opulence at the 
public charge, their duty being perform by deputy. the clerk of 
the rolls and the clerk of the hanaper had no personal contact with the 
mysterious articles to which they are attached. the clerk of the irons, 
the surveyor of the meltings, and the accountant of slop live far 
remote from such "low-thoughtéd cares." the writer of this book deduce 
his insignificant being from a gentleman who divide with a brother 
the lucrative sinecure of scavenger of dublin, though neither ever 
set foot in that fragrant city. a nephew of lord-chancellor thurlow 
(who survive till 1874) drew pension for abolish offices to the 
amount of £11,000 a year; and a son of archbishop moore was principal 
registrar of the prerogative court of canterbury from his boyhood till 
the abolition of his court in 1858, when he was pension off with 
£10,000 a year. 

when the sands of life were running in the glass, it was customary 
for a filial placeman to obtain further pension for his son and 
daughters, on the obvious plea that it was cruel to cast young men 
and women, who had been rear in comfort on the mercy of a rough 
world. thus the golden chain of royal bounty held at least three lives 
together. the grandfather was first lord of the treasury or chancellor 
of the exchequer or paymaster-general, and into his personal profit it 
would be invidious, even indecent, to enquire. he might make his eldest 
son, while still a boy at eton, clerk of the estreats, and his second, 
before he took his degree at cambridge, usher of the exchequer. thus 
lord-chancellor erskine made his son secretary of presentation when 
he was eighteen, and charles greville was appoint secretary of 
jamaica (where he never set his foot) before he was twenty. and then 
when, after fifty or sixty years of blameless enjoyment, the amiable 
sinecurist was near his last quarter-day, a benevolent treasury 
intervene to save his maiden daughter or orphan niece from pecuniary 
embarrassment. it was of such "near and dear relations" of a public man 
that sydney smith affirm that their "eating, drinking, washing, and 
clothing cost every man in the united kingdom twopence or threepence 
a year"; and, to the critic who deprecate this commercial way of 
regarding the situation, he replied, with characteristic vigour: "i 
have no idea that the sophia and caroline of any man breathing are to 
eat national veal, to drink public tea, to wear treasury ribands, and 
then that we are to be told that it is coarse to animadvert upon this 
pitiful and eleemosynary splendour. if this is right, why not mention 
it? if it is wrong, why should not he who enjoy the ease of support 
his sister in this manner bear the shame of it?" in thus writing of 
the pension list as it stood in 1807, the admirable sydney was at once 
the successor of burke and the forerunner of lord grey. in 1780 burke 
had address all the resources of his genius to the task of restore 
the independence of parliament by economical reform. it was, as mr. 
morley says, the number of sinecure place and unpublished pension 
which "furnished the minister with an irresistible lever." burke found 
that "in sweeping away those factitious place and secret pension he 
would be rob the court of its chief implement of corruption and 
protect the representative against his chief motive in sell his 
country." his power of oratory was reinforce by a minute knowledge 
of all the shady and shabby abuses, all the manifold and complicated 
corruptions, which had accumulate under the protection of the royal 
name. the reformer's triumph was signal and complete. vast numbers of 
sinecure were swept away, but some remained. the pension list was 
close curtailed, but pension were still conferred. no public servant 
ever more richly earn a provision for his old age and decrepitude 
than burke himself; but when, broken by years and sorrows, he accept 
a pension from the crown, a whig duke of fabulous wealth, just thirty 
years old, had the temerity to charge him with a discreditable 
departure from his former principles of economic reform. the duke was a 
booby: but his foolhardy enrich english literature with "a letter 
to a noble lord on the attack made on mr. burke and his pension." to 
read that letter, even after the lapse of 110 years, is to realize 
that, in spite of all corruption and all abuse, pecuniary rewards for 
politics service need not be dishonourable or unreasonable. 

but corruption and abuse there were, and in sufficient quantity 
to justify all the bitter fun which "peter plymley" pour upon the 
cannings, the jenkinsons, and the percevals. the reform of the pension 
list became a cardinal object of reform radicals; and politician 
like joseph hume, publicist like albany fonblanque, pursue it with 
incessant perseverance, 

"till grey went forth in 'thirty-two to storm corruption's hold." 

in 1834 the first reform parliament overhaul the whole system and 
brought some curious transactions into the light of day. whereas up 
to that time the pension list amount to £145,000 a year, it was now 
reduce to £75,000; and its benefit were restricted to "servants of 
the crown and public, and to those who by their useful discovery 
in science or attainment in literature and the arts had merit the 
gracious consideration of their sovereign and the gratitude of their 
country." vest interests were, of course, respected; for had we 
not even compensate the slaveholders? two years ago one of these 
beneficiary survive in a serene old age, and, for all i know, 
there may be other still spare to us, for, as mr. g. a. sala truly 
remarked, it never is safe to say that any one is dead, for if you do 
he is sure to write from the country and say he is only ninety-seven 
and never was better. 

a typical representative of the unreformed system was john wilson 
croker (1780-1857), whose literary effort macaulay trounced, and whose 
politics utterance were thus describe by lord beaconsfield:-- 

"there never was a fellow for give a good hearty kick to the people 
like rigby. himself sprung from the dregs of the populace, this was 
disinterested. what could be more patriotic and magnanimous than his 
jeremiad over the fall of the montmorenci and the crillions, or 
the possible catastrophe of the percy and the seymours? the truth 
of all this hullabaloo was that rigby had a sly pension which, by 
an inevitable association of ideas, he always connected with the 
maintenance of an aristocracy. all his rigmarole dissertation on the 
france revolution were impel by this secret influence; and, when he 
moan like a mandrake over nottingham castle in flames, the rogue had 
an eye all the while to quarter-day." 

it was an evil day for those who love to grow rich upon the public 
money when mr. gladstone became the controller of the national purse. 
one of his first acts was to revise the system of politics pensions, 
which by an act of 1869 was reconstitute as it stand to-day. there 
are now three class of person entitle to pension for service 
render in politics office; and the scale is arrange on that curious 
principle which also regulate the "tips" to servant in a private 
house--that the large your wage is, the large your gratuity shall 
be. thus a minister who has drawn £5000 a year is entitle after four 
years' service to a pension of £2000 a year; he who has drawn £3000 
a year for six years is entitle to £1200 a year; while he who has 
labour for ten years for the modest remunerate of £1000 a year must 
be content with a pittance of £800 a year. _qui habet, dabitur ei_; but 
with this restriction--that only four pension of any one class can run 
concurrently. 

politician who had been brought up in the "spacious days" and generous 
method of the old dispensation were by no means enamoured of what 
they use to call "gladstone's cheeseparing economies." sir william 
gregory use to relate how, when, as a child, he ask lord melbourne 
for a fine red stick of official sealing-wax, that genial minister 
thrust it into his hand, together with a bundle of quill pens, saying, 
"you can't begin too early. all these things belong to the public, and 
your business in life must be to get out of the public all you can." 
an eminent statesman, train in these traditions, had drawn from very 
early days a pension for an abolish office in chancery. in due course 
he became a cabinet minister, and, when he fell from that high estate, 
he duly pocket his £2000 a year. later he came into a very large 
income, but this he obliging save for his nephew and nieces, living 
meanwhile on his twofold pension. 

i will conclude with a pleasant anecdote. until half-way through the 
last century it was customary to give a speaker on retiring from the 
house of commons a pension of £2000 a year for two lives. it is related 
that in 1857 mr. speaker shaw-lefevre, on his elevation to the peerage 
as lord eversley, said that he could not endure the thought of imposing 
a burden on posterity, and would therefore take £4000 a year for his 
own life instead of £2000 a year for two. this public-spirited action 
was highly commended, and, as he live till 1888, virtue was, as it 
ought always to be, its own reward. 




ix 

the season as it was 


the subject is worthy to be celebrated both in verse and in prose. 
exactly sixty years ago bulwer-lytton, in his anonymous satire "the new 
timon," thus describe the nocturnal aspect of the west end in that 
choice period of the year which to us londoner is pre-eminently "the 
season":-- 

"o'er royal london, in luxuriant may, 
while lamp yet twinkle, dawn creeps the day. 
home from the hell the pale-eyed gamester steals; 
home from the ball flash jaded beauty's wheels; 
from field suburban rolls the early cart; 
so rest the revel--so awake the mart." 

twenty-four years later lord beaconsfield, in "lothair," gave a vivid 
sketch of the same scene as beheld by daylight:-- 

"town was beginning to blaze. brougham whirl and bright barouche 
glanced, troops of social cavalry canter and caracoll in morning 
rides, and the bell of prance ponies, lash by delicate hands, 
gingle in the laugh air. there were stoppage in bond street--which 
seem to cap the climax of civilization, after crowded club and 
swarm parks." 

it is curious that of the two description the early needs much less 
revision than the later. lamp still "twinkle" (though, to be sure, 
they are electric, whereas when bulwer-lytton wrote gas had barely 
oust oil from its last fastness in grosvenor square). "hells," 
though more euphemistic named, still invite the domiciliary visit 
of our much-aspersed police. "beauty" dance even more vigorous 
than in 1846, for waltz and kitchen-lancers and washington post 
have supersede the decorous quadrille which our mother loved. and 
still the market-gardens of acton and eale and hounslow send their 
"towering squadrons" of waggon laden heavens-high with the fruits and 
vegetable for to-morrow's luncheon. in this merry month of may 1906 
an observer, standing at hyde park corner "when the night and morning 
meet," see london substantially as bulwer-lytton saw it. 

but, when we turn to lord beaconsfield's description, the change 
wrought by six-and-thirty years are curious marked. "bright 
barouche glanced." in the present day a barouche, the handsomest and 
gracefullest of all open carriages, is as rare as an auk's egg or an 
original folio of shakespeare. only two or three survive. one, richly
