dight in royal crimson, bear the queen, beautiful as cleopatra in her 
barge. in another, almost imperial purple, lady londonderry sit 
enthroned; a third, pale blue as the forget-me-not, carry lady 
carysfort; but soon the tale of barouche ends. victoria and landau 
and "clarences" and "sociables" make the common throng of carriages, 
and their serried ranks give way to the impetuous onrush of the noxious 
motor or the mild impact of the electric brougham. 

"troops of social cavalry" were, when lord beaconsfield wrote 
"lothair," the characteristic glory of rotten row; but horse and 
horsemanship alike have waned. men take their constitutional canter 
in costume ancient confine to rat-catching, and the general 
aspect of rotten row suggest the idea of mount infantry rather 
than of "cavalry." alongside the ride forty years ago ladies drove 
their pony-phaetons--a pretty practice and a pretty carriage; but 
both have utterly disappeared, and the only bell that "gingle in the 
laugh air" are the warning signal of the petrol fiend, as, bent 
on destruction, he swoop down from marble arch to piccadilly. do a 
captious critic gaze enquiring on the unfamiliar verb to "gingle"? 
it was thus that lord beaconsfield wrote it in "lothair"; even as 
in the same high romance he describe a lady with a rich bunch of 
"stephanopolis" in her hand. it is not for the ephemeral scribbler to 
correct the orthography of the immortal dead. as to "stoppages in bond 
street," they were isolated and noteworthy incident in 1870; in 1906, 
thanks to the admission of omnibus into the narrow thoroughfare, they 
are occurrence as regular as the postman's knock or the policeman's 
mail tread. 

we have seen the aspect in which the london season present itself 
to two great men of yore. let me now descend to a more personal level. 
we will imagine ourselves transport back to the year 1880, and to 
the month of may. a young gentleman--some five-and-twenty summers, as 
mr. g. p. r. james would have said, have pass over his fair head--is 
standing near the steps of st. george's hospital between the hour of 
eleven and midnight. he is smart dress in evening clothes, with a 
white waistcoat, a gardenia in his button-hole, and a silver-crutched 
stick in his hand. he is smoking a cigarette and ponder the question 
where he shall spend his evening, or, more strictly, the early hour of 
next day. he is in a state of serene contentment with himself and the 
world, for he has just eaten an excellent dinner, where plovers' egg 
and asparagus have remind him that the season has really begun. to 
the pleasure-seeking londoner these symptom of return summer mean 
more, far more, than the dogrose in the hedgerow or the first note of 
the nightingale in the copse. since dinner he has just look in at an 
evening party, which bore him badly, and has "cut" two other where he 
was not so likely to be missed. and now arise the vital question of 
the balls. i use the plural number, for there will certainly be two, 
and probably three, to choose from. here, at st. george's hospital, 
our youth is at the centre of the world's social concourse. a swift 
and unbroken stream of carriage is pour down from grosvenor square 
and mayfair to belgrave square and eaton square and chesham place, and 
it meet as it go the ascend procession which begin in belgravia 
and end in portman square. to-night there is a royal ball at grosvenor 
house, certainly the most stately event of the season; a little dance, 
exquisite gay and bright, in piccadilly; and a gorgeous entertainment 
in prince's gate, where the aspire distiller is struggling, with 
enormous outlay, into social fame. all these have solicit the honour 
of our young friend's presence, and now is the moment of decision. it 
do not take long to repudiate prince's gate; there will be the best 
band in london, and ortolan for supper, but there will be no one there 
that one ever saw before, and it is too sickening to be call "my boy" 
by that bow-windowed bounder, the master of the house. there remain 
grosvenor house and piccadilly, and happily these can be combine in 
a harmonious perfection. grosvenor house shall come first, for the 
arrival of the prince and princess is a pageant worth seeing--the most 
gracious host and the most beautiful hostess in london usher the 
royal guests, with courtly pomp, into the great gallery, wall with 
the canvas of rubens, which serve as the dancing-room. then the fun 
begins, and the bright hour fly swiftly, till one o'clock suggest 
the tender thought of supper, which is serve on gold plate and sèvres 
china in a garden-tent of gobelin tapestry. and now it is time for a 
move; and our youth, extricate himself from the undesired attention 
of the linkmen, pop into a hansom and speed to piccadilly, where he 
find delight of a different kind--no royalty, no pomp, no ceremony; 
but a warm welcome, and all his intimate friends, and the nicest girl 
in london eager for a valse. 

as day begin to peep, he drink his crown tumbler of champagne-cup, 
and stroll home under the opalescent dawn, sniff the fragrance 
from pyramid of strawberry as they roll towards covent garden, and 
exchange a friendly "good night" with the policeman on the beat, who 
seem to think that "good morning" would be a more suitable greeting. 
so to bed, with the cheerful consciousness of a day's work well done, 
and the even more exhilarating prospect of an unbroken succession 
of such days, full of feast and dance and riding and polo and 
lawn-tennis, till august stifle the season with its dust and drive 
the revel to homburg or the moors. 

but i awake, and lo! it is a dream, though a dream well found on 
reality. for i have been describe the london season as it was when 
the world was young. 

"when all the world is old, lad, 
and all the tree are brown; 
and all the sport is stale, lad, 
and all the wheels run down; 
creep home, and take your place there, 
the spent and maim among: 
god grant you find one face there, 
you love when all was young." 




x 

the season as it is 


that delicate critic, the late mr. william cory, observe in one of his 
letters that virgil's 

"sunt lacrymæ rerum, et mentem mortalia tangunt" 

has its modern equivalent in wordsworth's 

"men are we, and must grieve when even the shade 
of that which once was great is pass away." 

the full luxury of that grief is reserved for those who, a decade 
hence, shall moralize on "the london season," for the thing which now 
we so describe will then have utterly perished, and its name will 
only arouse a tender and regretful emotion. even now we have seen its 
glory fade, and soon it will have share the fate of those venetian 
splendour which wordsworth mourned. but in the meantime it still 
exists, though in a vastly different form from that which it wore in 
mid-victorian years. just now i was describe some of the change 
which have occur since the distant days when bulwer-lytton and lord 
beaconsfield describe london in may; and, following humble in their 
wake, i endeavour to depict it as it was when i had my part in it. 
but change only yield place to change. society, like the individual 
who compose it, pass onward in perpetual vicissitude. as shelley 
says, "naught may endure but mutability." so the london season of 
1906 differ as notably from the season of 1880 as the season of 1880 
from that of 1846. let me catalogue some of the change and try to 
account for them. in the first place, the season is much less exactly 
circumscribe by dates. in days gone by, it began with the opening of 
parliament, which was always about the 7th of february, and it lasted, 
with its regular intermission for easter and whitsuntide, till the 
last week of july. then society transport itself in turn to goodwood, 
to cowes, and to a germany watering-place or a scotch moor, according 
to its physical condition, and it was dark rumoured that, if people 
found themselves compel by domestic or financial reason to remain 
in london during august, they sought to escape detection by keeping 
the window front the street close shuttered, and live in their 
back rooms in unbroken contemplation of the leads and the mews. if you 
chance to meet a man in piccadil in september, you might be sure 
that he would be wearing country clothes and would assure you that he 
was only "passing through" between doncaster and scotland. nowadays 
the season has no particular limits. london is nearly as full in 
december as it is in may. dinner and play and supper at restaurant 
are as frequent, and, barring the fogs, as bright, at christmas as at 
midsummer. even in september clubland is not deserted; and there are 
people bold enough to defy the world by return from their summer 
exodus as early as october. the reason for the change, as for many 
other like it, is the reduction of territorial incomes. 1880 may be 
taken as, roughly, the last of the good years for agriculture. the 
incessant rains of 1879 had even then begun to tell their tale. tenant 
were ask for big reductions, and farm hitherto eager sought were 
becoming unlettable. i know a landowner on a great scale who, a year or 
two later, only pocket 10 per cent. of his income from land, whereas 
five years before he would have thought an abate of 10 per cent. 
disastrous. all this has told increasingly on social life, for people 
found themselves unable to keep both a country house and a london house 
go at the same time, and, being driven to choose between the two, 
often decided to let the country house and its shoot and make london 
their headquarters for the whole year. so, by degrees, autumn fade 
imperceptible into winter, winter into spring, and spring into summer. 
each season in its turn found people dwelling peaceable in their urban 
habitations, entertaining and being entertained; and so "_the_ season" 
lost its sharp edges. the meeting of parliament brought no perceptible 
change in the aspect of the town. "high midsummer pomps" were no long 
so "high" as in former years, but, _per contra_, there was much more 
gaiety in the autumn and winter and early spring. 

another cause which has contribute to the efface of the ancient 
time-marks is that the court tend to disregard them. under the present 
reign, windsor castle has become as much a social centre as buckingham 
palace. there are banquet in st. george's hall in december, as well 
as garden-parties on the the slope in june; and so, under the action 
of royal influence, the social season melt into one another, like the 
hue of the prism. then, again, the practice of the "weekend," import 
from lancashire and sanction by westminster, help to denude the 
town in summer; for the "end" tend naturally to prolong itself till 
it overlap the beginning, and friday-to-tuesday parties, tread on 
the heel of whitsuntide and to be follow in quick succession by 
ascot, make mish-mash of what was aforetime "an entire and perfect 
chrysolite"--a complete and continuous whole. 

in describe my hero of 1880 as he survey his evening's amusement 
and chose the most rewarding, i took for grant that he had at least 
three balls to choose from. nowadays he is lucky if he has one. here 
again, and conspicuously, agriculture depression has made its mark. 
in the years between 1870 and 1880, during an unbroken spell of good 
trade and good harvests, rich people struggle with one another for a 
vacant night on which to entertain their friends. for example, lady 
a. had just brought out a daughter, and wish to give a ball for her 
benefit. say that she set her affection on monday the 28th of may. 
before she issue her cards she took counsel with all her friends, 
for in those days ball-giving mother were a sort of limited company, 
and all knew one another. she found that mrs. b. had mental fixed 
on tuesday, 29th, and, if mrs. c. had thought of monday, she would be 
so kind as to take wednesday, 30th. so all was amicable agreed; there 
would be no clashing, which would be such a pity and would spoil both 
balls; and the cards were duly issued. directly afterwards, as if move 
by some occult and fiendish impulse, the duchess of d---- pounce on 
monday, 28th, for a royal ball at d---- house, or, worse still because 
more perilous tempting, for a "very small dance," to which all the 
nicest young men would go, and where they would stay till three. in the 
face of such mortification as these, the emulous hospitality of the 
aspire distiller were of no account; for the "nice men" would either 
disregard them, or, have look in for half-an-hour, would come on to 
spend the night at the house where they felt themselves at home. 

the hero of 1880, if only he was well connected, well mannered, and 
sufficient well known, might fairly reckon on dine six nights out 
of the seven at a host's expense. indeed, if he was at all popular, he 
could safe afford to decline the invitation which old mr. wellbore 
issue six week in advance and reserve himself for a lively meal 
at short notice. not so to-day. our young friend, if he has a 
constitutional objection to pay for his own dinner, must take what 
he can get in the way of invitations, and not be too particular about 
the cook or the company. here the cause of change is not decrease 
of wealth. as long as there is a balance at the bank, and even when 
there is none, people will dine; and dinner-giving is the last form of 
hospitality which society will let die. but nowadays dinner are made 
ancillary to bridge. if our friend cannot afford to lose £50 in an 
evening he will not be ask to dine at a house which reckon itself 
as belong to "the mode"; or if, for old acquaintance' sake, he is 
allow to find a place at the dinner-table, he is compel to sit all 
the evening by the least attractive daughter of the house, or to listen 
to some fogey, too fossilize for bridge, discourse on the iniquity 
of mr. birrell's bill. "tobacco," said lord beaconsfield, "is the tomb 
of love." if he were with us now, he would pronounce that bridge is the 
extinguisher of hospitality. 

yet once again i note a startle discrepancy between the season 
as it was and the season as it is. then a young man who want air 
and exercise in the afternoon play tennis at lord's, or skate 
at prince's, or took a gallop in richmond park, or, if he was very 
adventurous and up-to-date, sped out to hampton court or windsor on a 
bone-shaking bicycle six feet high. all these recreation are possible 
to him to-day; but all have yield to motoring. dress in the most 
unbecome of all known costumes, his expressive eyes conceal by 
goggles, and his graceful proportions swathe in oilskin, he urge 
his mad career to brighton or stratford or salisbury plain. no doubt 
he has the most fascinating companion in the world, for girl are 
enthusiast motorists; but i fancy that edwin and angelina present 
a more attractive appearance when, neat dress and beautiful 
mounted, they rode in the cool of the evening along the shady side of 
rotten row. 

however, i am a kind of social "old mortality" rummage among the 
tomb of what has been and can be no more, and i fancy that old 
mortality's opinion on youth and beauty would have been just 
disregarded. 




xi 

the sin of society 


in the year 1870 a flame of religious zeal was sudden kindle in 
the west end of london. in that year the rev. george howard wilkinson 
(now bishop of st. andrews) was appoint vicar of st. peter's, eaton 
square. the church in the belgravian district was as dry as tinder; 
it caught fire from mr. wilkinson's fervour, and the fire soon became 
a conflagration. this is matthew arnold's description of the great 
preach at the height of his power: "he was so evidently sincere, more 
than sincere, burnt up with sorrow, that he carry every one with 
him, and half the church was in tears. i do not much believe in good 
being done by a man unless he can give _light_, and wilkinson's fire is 
very turbid; but his power of heating, penetrating, and agitate is 
extraordinary." this description belong to the year 1872, but it might 
have been written with equal truth at any date between 1870 and 1883. 
in all my experience of preach (which is long, wide, and varied) i 
have never seen a congregation dominate by its minister so absolutely 
as the congregation of st. peter's was dominate by mr. wilkinson. i 
say "congregation" advisedly, for i should think that at least half 
the seatholder belong to other parishes. the smartest carriage in 
london block the approach to the church. the great dame of grosvenor 
square and carlton house terrace rub shoulder with the opulent 
inhabitant of tyburnia and south kensington, cabinet minister fought 
for place in the gallery, and m.p.'s were no more account of than 
silver in the days of solomon. 

and this was not a mere assemblage of hearers. the congregation of st. 
peter's were pre-eminently givers. £4000 a year was the regular product 
of the alms-bags, let alone the innumerable sum sent private to the 
vicar. "i want a thousand pounds." this simple but emphatic statement 
from the pulpit one sunday was succeed on the following sunday by 
the quiet announcement, "i have got a thousand pounds." what was the 
secret of this attraction? it was entire personal. it did not in the 
least depend on theology bias. mr. wilkinson belong to no party. 
he had begun life as an evangelical, and he retain the unction and 
fervour which were characteristic of that school at its best; but he 
was feeling his way towards a higher churchmanship, and had discard 
most of his early shibboleths. the fabric was frankly hideous, and 
the well-meant attempt to make it look less like a barn and more 
like a church only result in something between a mosque and a 
synagogue. there was no ritualism. the music was too elaborate for the 
choir, and the curate were feeble beyond all description. the vicar 
was everything; and even he had none of the gift which are commonly 
supposed to make a popular preacher. he was not the least flummery or 
flowery. he was reserved and dignified in manner, and his language was 
quite unadorned. his voice was a monotonous moan, occasional rising 
into a howl. he was conspicuous free from the tendency to prophesy 
smooth things, and he even seem to take a delight in rubbing the 
pungent lotion of his spiritual satire into the sore place of the 
hearers' conscience. if jeremiah had prophesy in a surplice, he would 
have been like the prophet of belgravia; and as for savonarola, his 
sermon, as paraphrase in chapter xxiv. of "romola," might have been 
delivered, with scarcely a word altered, from the pulpit of st. peter's. 

and here we touch the pith and core of mr. wilkinson's preaching. he 
rebuke the sin of society as no one had venture to rebuke them since 
the days of whitefield and the wesleys. the tractarian movement, so 
heart-searching, so conscience-stirring at oxford, had succumb in 
the fashionable parts of london to the influence which surround it, 
and had degenerate into a sort of easy-going ceremonialism--partly 
antiquarian, partly worldly, and wholly ineffective for spiritual 
revival or moral reformation. into this dead sea of lethargy and 
formalism mr. wilkinson burst like a gunboat. he scattered his fire 
left and right, aim high and aim low, blaze and bombard without 
fear or favour; sent some craft to the bottom, set fire to others, 
and cover the sea with wreckage. in less metaphorical language, he 
rebuke the sin of all and sundry, from duchess to scullery-maids, 
premier to pageboys, octogenarian rake to damsel in their teens. 
then, as now, society love to be scolded, and the more mr. wilkinson 
thunder the more it crowded to his feet. "pay your bills." "get up 
when you are called." "don't stay till three at a ball and then say 
that you are too delicate for early services." "eat one dinner a day 
instead of three, and try to earn that one." "give up champagne for 
the season, and what you save on your wine-merchant's bill send to 
the mission field." "you are sixty-five years old and have not been 
confirmed. never too late to mend. join a confirmation class at once, 
and try to remedy, by good example now, all the harm you have done your 
servant or your neighbour by fifty years' indifference." "sell that 
diamond cross which you carry with you into the sin-polluted atmosphere 
of the opera, give the proceeds to feed the poor, and wear the only 
real cross--the cross of self-discipline and self-denial." 

these are echoes--faint, indeed, but not, i think, unfaithful--of 
thirty years ago, and they have sudden been awoke from their long 
slumber by the sermon which father vaughan has just been preach at 
the jesuits' church in farm street, mayfair. the good father, exalt 
his own church, perhaps a little unduly, at the expense of the anglican 
church in the district, observe complacent that "farm street, in 
spite of its extension, was all too small" for its congregation. for my 
own part, i do not belong to that fold, and i never wander to strange 
church for the pleasure of have my ear tickled; so i only know 
father vaughan's utterance as they reach me through the newspapers. a 
report in the third person always tend to enfeeble rhetoric; but, in 
spite of that hindrance, father vaughan's style seem to lack nothing 
in the way of emphasis or directness. here is a fragment of his sermon 
preach on sunday the 10th of june 1906:-- 

"it was no easy task for the votary of pleasure when sunday came 
round to all of a sudden forget their class distinctions, their 
privileged sets, their social successes, their worldly goods, and to 
remember that they were go into the presence of him before whom man 
and woman were not what they happen to have, but what they happen 
to be--that the debutante beauty might be before god less than her 
maid who wait up half the night for her, nay, less than the meanest 
scullery-maid below stairs; while the millionaire with means to buy up 
whole country might be in god's sight far less pleasing and very much 
more guilty than the lowest groom in his stable yard." 

not less vigorous was the allocution of june 17. 

"if dives, who was bury in hell, were to revisit the earth he would 
most surely have the _entrée_ to london's smartest set to-day. he 
would be literally pelt with invitations. and why not? dives, so 
well groom and turn out, with such a well-lined larder and so 
well-stocked a cellar, would be the very ideal host to cultivate. he 
would 'do you so well,' you would meet the 'right people at his place,' 
and you could always bring your 'latest friend.' besides, what a good 
time one would have at his house-parties, where there would be no fear 
of being bore or dull!"[5] 

[5] here i seem to catch an echo of dr. pusey's sermon on "why did 
dive lose his soul?" 

and yet again:-- 

"it was well when the winning-card fell into their hands, for then 
there was just a chance of some dressmaker or tradesman being paid 
something on account before becoming bankrupt. with such example of 
the misuse of wealth before their eyes, it was a wonder there were not 
more socialist than there actually were." 

all the memory of my youth have been revive by father vaughan. 
instead of 1906, 1876; instead of the gothic gloom of farm street, the 
tawdry glare of st. peter's, eaton square; instead of a jesuit father 
in the pulpit, a vigorous protestant who renounce the pope and all his 
works and glory in the anglican of the church of england. grant 
those differences, which after all are more incidental than essential, 
and the sermon exactly reproduce those stirring days when the present 
bishop of st. andrews "shook the arsenal" of fashion, "thundered over" 
london, and achieved, as his admirer said, the supreme distinction of 
spoil the london season. 

i am convinced that the higher critic of a later age, collate 
the wilkinsonian tradition with such fragment as remain of father 
vaughan's discourses, will come to the conclusion that "wilkinson" 
never exist (except in wordsworth's ode to the spade), but was a 
kind of heroic figure conceive by a much later generation, which 
had quiver under the rhetoric of a real person or person call 
vaughan; and the opinion of the learned will be sharp divide on such 
question as whether vaughan was one or many; if one, whether he was 
a priest, a cardinal, a head master, or an independent minister; or 
whether he was all four at different stage of his career. 




xii 

oxford 


"once, my dear--but the world was young then-- 
magdalen elm and trinity limes,-- 
lissom the oar and back that swung then, 
eight good men in the good old times-- 
careless we and the chorus flung then. 
under st. mary's chimes! 

"still on her spire the pigeon hover; 
still by her gateway flit the gown; 
ah, but her secret? you, young lover, 
drum her old one forth from town, 
know you the secret none discover? 
tell it--when _you_ go down." 

what matthew arnold did for the interpretation of oxford through the 
medium of prose, that mr. quiller-couch has done through the medium 
of verse. in the poem from which i have just quote two stanza he 
conveys, as no one else has ever convey it in poetry, the tender and 
elusive charm of that incomparable place. 

"know you her secret none can utter-- 
hers of the book, the triple crown?" 

it is a hard question, and susceptible of some very prosaic and 
therefore inappropriate answers. the true answer can, i think, only be 
given by those for whom oxford lies, half hid, in the enchant past: 
"tell it--when _you_ go down." 

some parts of the spell which oxford exercises on those who are 
subject to her influence are in no sense secret. we perceive them 
from the day when we first set foot within her precincts, and the sense 
of them abide with us for ever. 

"if less insensible than sodden clay 
in a sea-river's bed at ebb of tide," 

all son of oxford must realize her material beauty, her historical 
pre-eminence, her contribution to thought and culture, her influence on 
the religious life of england. 

"ah, but her secret? you, young lover." 

there is nothing secret about all this; it is palpable and manifest; 
and yet it do not exhaust the spell. something there is that remains 
undiscovered, or at best half-discovered--felt and guess at, but 
not clearly apprehended--until we have pass away from the "dreaming 
spires"--the cloister and the garden and the river--to that stern 
life for which these mysterious enchantment have been prepare us. 

"know you the secret none discover?" 

if you do, that is proof that time has done its work and has brought 
to the test of practical result the influence which were shape 
your mind and, still more potently, your heart, between eighteen and 
twenty-two. what that "secret" is, let an unworthy son of oxford try to 
tell. 

to begin with a negative, it is not the secret of nirvana. there are 
misguided critic abroad in the land who seem to assume that life 
live easily in a beautiful place, amid a society which include all 
knowledge in its comprehensive survey, and far remote from the human 
tragedy of poverty and toil and pain, must necessarily be calm. and 
so, as regards the actual work and warfare of mankind, it may be. the 
bitter cry of starve poplar do not very readily penetrate to the 
well-spread table of an oxford common-room. in a laburnum-clad villa 
in the park we can afford to reason very temperate about life in 
city where five family camp in one room. but when we leave the 
actuality of life and come to the region of thought and opinion, 
all the pent energy of oxford seethe and stirs. the hebrew word for 
"prophet" comes, i believe, from a root which signify to bubble 
like water on the flames; and in this fervent of thought and feeling 
oxford is characteristic prophetic. it is a tradition that in 
some year of the passion-torn 'forties the subject for the newdigate 
prize poem was cromwell, whereas the subject for the corresponding 
poem at cambridge was plato. in that selection oxford was true to 
herself. for a century at least (even if we leave out of sight her 
early convulsions) she has been the battle-field of contend 
sects. her air has resound with party-cries, and the dead body 
of the controversial slain have lain thick in her streets. all the 
oppose forces of church and state, of theology and politics, of 
philosophy and science, of literary and social and economic theory, 
have contend for mastery in the place which matthew arnold, with rare 
irony, describe as "so unruffled by the fierce intellectual life of 
our century, so serene!" every succeed generation of oxford men has 
borne its part in these ever-recurring strifes. to hold aloof from them 
would have been poltroonery. passionately convinced (at twenty) that 
we had sworn ourselves for life to each cause which we espoused, we 
have plead and plan and denounce and persuaded; have struck the 
shrewdest blow which our strength could compass, and devise the most 
dangerous pitfall which wit could suggest. nothing came of it all, and 
nothing could come, except the ruin of our appoint study and the 
result dislocation of all subsequent life. but we were obey the 
irresistible impulse of the time and the place in which our lot was 
cast, and we were ready to risk our all upon the venture. 

"never we wince, though none deplore us, 
we who go reap that we sowed; 
city at cockcrow wake before us-- 
hey, for the lilt of the london road! 
one look back, and a rousing chorus! 
never a palinode!" 

it is when we have finally sung that chorus and have travelled a 
few mile upon that road, that we learn the secret which we never 
discover while as yet oxford held us in the thick of the fight. we 
thought then that we were the most desperate partizans; we ask no 
quarter, and gave none; pushed our argumentative victory to their 
uttermost consequences, and made short work of a fallen foe. but, 
when all the old battle-cries have die out of our ears, we begin to 
perceive humane voices. all at once we realize that a great part of 
our old contention was only sound and fury and self-deception, and 
that, though the cause for which we strove may have been absolutely 
right, our opponent were not necessarily villains. in a word, we have 
learnt the secret of oxford. all the time that we were fight and 
fuming, the higher and subtle influence of the place were moulding 
us, unconscious though we were, to a more gracious ideal. we had really 
learnt to distinguish between intellectual error and moral obliquity. 
we could differ from another on every point of the politics and 
theology compass, and yet in our heart acknowledge him to be the 
best of all good fellows. without surrender a single conviction, we 
came to see the virtue of so state our belief as to persuade and 
propitiate, instead of offend and alienating. we had attain to 
that temper which, in the sphere of thought and opinion, is analogous 
to the crown virtue of christian charity. 

"tell it--when _you_ go down." 

lately it has been my privilege to address a considerable gathering 
of oxford undergraduates, all keen alive to the interests and 
controversy of the present hour, all devoted loyal to the tradition 
of oxford as each understood it, and all with their eyes eager fixed 
on "the wistful limit of the world." with such an audience it was 
inevitable to insist on the graces and benediction which oxford can 
confer, and to dwell on mr. gladstone's dogma that to call a man a 
"typically oxford man" is to bestow the highest possible praise. 

but this was not all. something more remain to be said. it was for 
a speaker who had travelled for thirty years on "the london road" to 
state as plainly as he could his own deepest obligation to the place 
which had decided the course and complexion of his life. and, when it 
was difficult to express that obligation in the pedestrian prose of 
an after-dinner speech, he turn for succour to the poet who sang of 
"the secret none discover." wherever philosophical insight is combine 
with literary genius and personal charm, one say instinctively, "that 
man is, or ought to be, an oxford man." chiefest among the great name 
which oxford ought to claim but cannot is the name of edmund burke; 
and the "secret" on which we have been discourse seem to be convey 
with luminous precision in his description of the ideal character: 
"it is our business ... to bring the disposition that are lovely in 
private life into the service and conduct of the commonwealth; so to be 
patriot as not to forget we are gentlemen; to cultivate friendship 
and to incur enmities; to have both strong, but both selected--in the 
one to be placable, in the other immovable." whoso has attain to that 
ideal has learnt the "secret" of oxford. 




xiii 

school for shepherd 

"the hungry sheep look up, and are not fed." 


why not? because the shepherd are so imperfect train for their 
business. this, at any rate, is the testimony of a canon (sometime 
examine chaplain to a bishop) who at the diocesan conference at e 
the other day declared that the clergy were "not qualified to provide 
instruction in church doctrine for the laity because they were not 
properly trained"; and further testify that "nonconformist minister 
were much better trained" than the english clergy. this testimony from 
a superior shepherd is rather startle for the sheep, and it suggest 
some interesting comparisons. it is, i take it, unquestionable that 
nonconformist minister and roman catholic priest alike have much 
more of a technical education than is thought necessary for their 
anglican brothers. they are, so to say, caught early, and their study 
from seventeen or eighteen onwards are direct steady towards 
their appoint work in life. a roman seminarist learn his latin 
and greece as subsidiary to higher studies; he spends, i believe, 
two years in philosophy and four in theology, and is harass by 
incessant examinations. the training of the youth who aspire to the 
nonconformist ministry is of much the same kind. "moral theology," in 
other words the science of the confessional, he naturally do not 
learn; but, on the other hand, he is sedulous train for the work of 
public speak and preaching. "if you can't preach," said spurgeon to 
his student at stockwell, "it is a clear proof that god doesn't mean 
you to be a preacher, and you must choose some other occupation." 

vastly different is the training of the english curate. private school, 
public school, and university: cricket, football, rowing: elementary 
greece and latin, and a smattering of law or history--these constitute 
his "atmosphere," his moral and mental discipline, between the age 
of ten and twenty-three. even more remarkable is his theology 
equipment. in ninety-nine case out of a hundred, he know absolutely 
nothing about the church of which he is to be a minister, her 
doctrines, history, or practical system. he has been envelop from 
his youth up by a hazy atmosphere of undogmatic religion. i well 
remember that an undergraduate friend of mine, who came to oxford 
from dr. temple's sixth form at rugby, decline to believe that there 
are two sacraments. that there was a religious ceremony call "the 
sacrament," for which some people stay after the ordinary service, 
he was well aware, as also that infant were ceremonial sprinkled; 
but that this latter ceremony was a sacrament he could not be induce 
to believe. during his last year at oxford he informed himself better 
on this and some similar topics, and a year afterwards was preaching, 
with great acceptance, to a fashionable congregation. from what i knew 
of my friend's theology attainments, i should imagine that the 
bishop's examination could not have been a very terrify process; 
but forty years early it must have been even less formidable. the 
hon. and rev. george spencer (uncle of the present lord spencer) was 
destined from an early age for the family living in northamptonshire. 
he hunt and shot, and danced, and travelled on the continent, and 
held a commission in the yeomanry. after two years at trinity college, 
cambridge, he took a "nobleman's degree," and, when he near the 
canonical age of twenty-three, he wrote to the bishop of peterborough's 
examine chaplain offering himself for ordination and ask advice as 
to his preparation. the examiner--ah, would that there were more like 
him!--wrote back:-- 

"it is impossible that i should ever entertain any idea of subject a 
gentleman with whose talent and good quality i am so well acquaint 
as i am with yours to any examination except as a matter of form, for 
which a verse in the greece testament and an article of the church of 
england return into latin will be ample sufficient." 

this reassure letter was written on the 12th of october 1822, and on 
the 22nd of december next ensue george spencer was ordain deacon 
and a year later priest. "on the evening before the ordination, whilst 
the bishop and various clergymen and their ladies and the candidate 
amuse themselves with a rubber of whist, mr. spencer refuse to play." 
and the refusal was considered, as perhaps it was, noteworthy. 

the movement which issue from oxford in 1833 introduce some 
improvement into the method of conduct ordinations, as into other 
department of the church's work. the examination became, though not 
yet very serious, at least a little less farcical, and some attempt was 
made in charge and sermon to urge upon the candidate the gravity of 
what they were undertaking. but, according to the late bishop woodford, 
"the evenings, during which they were left to themselves, became 
evenings of social enjoyment, if not of boisterous merriment, in which 
the features of an old college supper-party were reproduced, rather 
than interval of solemn thought and retirement." 

bishop samuel wilberforce raise the standard of what was expect 
in the way of scriptural and theology knowledge; he made the 
examination a reality; he laid special stress on sermon-writing; and 
he made the ember week a season of spiritual retirement in which men 
about to take the most decisive step in life might be brought face to 
face with the responsibility involved in their decision. the example 
set by wilberforce was followed, soon or later, by every bishop on 
the bench; the requirement have been raised, and the system has been 
develop and improved; but the credit of initiation belong to that 
epoch-making episcopate, which began in 1845 and ended, through a false 
step made by a horse on the surrey downs, on the 19th of july 1873. 

it soon became apparent to those who had the spiritual interests of 
the church at heart that something more than twelve months' book-work 
and a week of religious retirement was require to wean the ordinary 
b.a. from the puerilities--if nothing worse--of his undergraduate 
life, and to equip him for a life of pastorship and teachership. the 
sense of this need gave rise to the creation of theology colleges, 
where a man who look forward to holy orders might, after taking his 
ordinary degree at oxford or cambridge, apply himself to the study 
more specially necessary for his chosen work, and--even more important 
still--might acquire the habit of methodical and self-disciplined 
life. the idea took shape in such foundations as the theology 
college of wells, cuddesdon, sarum, and ely, the _scholæ cancellarii_ 
at lincoln, and the clergy school at leeds. fight their way through 
all manner of strange misrepresent about monasticism and 
mediævalism, they have in the course of years attain to recognition, 
popularity, and apparent stability. the bishop patronize them warmly, 
and incumbent who desire curate not wholly ignorant of their craft 
are increasingly unwill to engage one who has not pass through a 
theology college. that the broad result of the training given in 
these seminary is a general increase in clerical efficiency i cannot 
doubt, but perhaps a layman may be permit to point out some curious 
gap and lapse in that training which go some way towards making 
clergymen less esteemed, and therefore less influential, than they 
ought to be. 

1. the clergy are not taught to be courteous. if they are courteous by 
nature and habit, well and good; but a rough undergraduate, destitute 
of sympathy and tact and ignorant of social usage, pass through a 
theology college and come out as rough as he enter it. a bear in 
holy orders is as destructive as a bull in a china shop. 

2. the clergy are not taught to manage money; they muddle their public 
accounts; they beg money for one object and use it for another; they 
seldom acknowledge what they receive by post; and they have absolutely 
no notion of cutting their coat according to their cloth. "spend and 
beg, and the money will come from somewhere" is their simple and 
sufficient creed. 

3. the clergy are not taught business. they have not the faintest 
notion of conduct a public meeting. they lose their way in 
the agenda-paper of the most insignificant committee. they break 
appointments at their will and pleasure. they seldom answer letters, 
and are frankly astonish when their correspondent are annoyed. 

4. the clergy are not taught the science of citizenship. outside their 
strict professional study (and, in some cases, the record of 
athleticism) they are the most ignorant set of young men in the world. 
they work hard and play hard, but they never read. they know nothing 
of books, nothing of history, nothing of the constitution under which 
they live, of the principles and record of politics parties, of the 
need for social reform or the means of secure it. they have a vague 
but clinging notion that radical are infidels, and that dissenters, if 
they got their deserts, would have their head punched. 

sixty years ago an italy critic said that, in spite of all their 
defects, the english clergy were "un clero colto e civile." could as 
much be said to-day? 




xiv 

pilgrimage 


i use the word in something wide than chaucer's sense, and yet in a 
sense not wholly different from his. for, though we no long make an 
annual visit to the shrine of st. thomas of canterbury, still we all 
feel bound, at least once a year, to go somewhere and do something 
quite out of our normal course. perhaps, like chaucer's friends, we 
"long" to do this in april, but the claim of business are generally 
too strong for us; so we have to content ourselves with admire the 
peep of greenery which begin to invade the soot of our urban gardens, 
and, if we are of a cultured habit, we can always quote browning's 
thrush or strain the kalendar so as to admit wordsworth's daffodils. 

this notion of a yearly pilgrimage as a necessity of rightly-ordered 
life seem to have fallen into a long abeyance. "dan chaucer" (for i 
love to be on easy terms with great men) describe the social customs 
of the fourteenth century, and then the pilgrimage seem to have been 
an establish institution: "tom hughes" describe those of the 
eighteenth, and this is what, writing in 1862, he say about the annual 
pilgrimage of his own time:-- 

"i have been credible informed, and am inclined to believe, that the 
various boards of director of railway company agree together some 
ten years back to buy up the learned profession of medicine, body and 
soul. to this end they set apart several million of money, which they 
continual distribute judicious among the doctors, stipulate 
only this one thing--that they shall prescribe change of air to every 
patient who can pay, or borrow money to pay, a railway fare, and see 
their prescription carry out. if it be not for this, why is it that 
none of us can be well at home for a year together? it wasn't so twenty 
years ago--not a bit of it. the brown did not go out of the county 
once in five years." 

the browns, as we all know, stood in mr. hughes's vocabulary for the 
upper middle class of england--the class to which the clergy, the 
small squires, and the professional men belong; the class which 
in chaucer's time contained the "man of lawe," the "marchande," the 
"franklyne," and the "doctore of phisyke"; and, although mr. hughes, 
who ought to know, say that in the early part of queen victoria's 
reign they were a stay-at-home class, they are now the most regular and 
the most zealous of pilgrims. it was the majestic misfortune of the 
duke in "lothair" to have so many house that he had no home. people 
so circumstance do not need to go on pilgrimages. after the autumn in 
a scotch castle, the winter in a country house in the midlands, the 
spring in another in the southern counties, and the season in grosvenor 
square, people are glad of a little rest, and seek it in some "proud 
alcove" on the thames or a sea-girt villa at cowes. unless their liver 
drive them to carlsbad or their heart to nauheim, they do not travel, 
but display what lord beaconsfield call "the sustain splendour of 
their stately lives" in the many mansions which, in the aggregate, 
represent to them the idea of home. i might perhaps on another occasion 
sketch the grand tour of europe, on which, for educational purposes, 
the earl of fitzurse use to send his eldest son, young lord cubley; 
compressed, with his tutor and doctor, into a travelling-carriage, with 
a valet and a courier in the rumble. the duke of argyll's autobiography 
has just told us what this kind of pilgrimage was like; but to-day i am 
dealing with the present rather than the past. 

it is the people with one house who go on pilgrimage nowadays--the 
impoverish squire, the smoke-dried clergyman, the exhaust merchant, 
the harry editor. to these must be add all the inhabitants, male 
and female, of lodging-land and flat-land,--all "the dim, common 
populations" of stuccovia and suburbia. there are mysterious law 
of association which connect class with localities. tradesmen love 
margate; to clerk scarborough is dear. the semitic financier has long 
claim brighton for his own. costermonger go hop-picking in kent; 
artizan disport themselves on the nigger-haunted pier of southend. 
govern by some mysterious law of their being, schoolmaster make 
straight for the alps. there they live the strenuous life and brave 
the perilous ascent; climb and puff and pant all day; rush in, very 
untidy and not very clean, to _table d'hôte_; and season their meal 
with the "shop" of st. winifred's or the gay banter of rosslyn 
common-room. it is agreeable to watch the forced cordiality, the thin 
tutorial humour, with which they greet some quite irresponsive pupil 
who happen to have stray into the same hotel; and i have often 
had occasion to admire the precocious dexterity with which the pupil 
extricate himself from this dread companionship. of mr. gladstone it 
was said by his detractor that he had something of the schoolmaster 
in his composition; and this trait was apt illustrate when, during 
the summer holiday some fifty years ago, he met the late duchess of 
abercorn in a country house accompany by her schoolboy son, lord 
george hamilton. not many mornings had elapse before mr. gladstone 
said to the boy's mother, "duchess, don't you think it a pity that your 
son should spend his holiday in entire idleness? i should be happy 
to give him an hour's home every morning." the offer was accepted, 
and the foundation of lord george's lifelong hostility to the liberal 
leader was secure laid. it is the nervous dread of some such awful 
possibility which supplies wings to the boy's feet and lie to his 
tongue when he encounter dr. grimstone or basil warde in a switzerland hotel. 

while the schoolmaster limit his aspiration to the alps, the oxford 
or cambridge don, have a long vacation at his command, take a 
more extend view, and urge his adventurous pilgrimage along road 
less trite. a few years ago an oxford don resolve to strike out what 
was then a quite new line, and spend his long vacation in portugal. 
conscious of insufficient acquaintance with the portugal language, 
he repair to mr. parker's excellent shop in the turl and enquire 
for a portugal phrase-book. after some research, that never-failing 
bookseller produce "the new guide of the conversation in portugal 
and english." the book had an instant and a deserve success. the 
preface set forth that "a choice of familiar dialogues, clean of 
gallicism and despoil phrases, it was missing yet to studious 
portugal and brazil youth; and also to person of other nation 
that wish to know the portugal language." to supply this felt want 
pedro carolino compile his hand-book for "the accept of the 
studious persons, and especially of the youth, at which we dedicate 
him particularly." among those studious person was our pilgrim-don, 
who naturally turn in the first instance to a dialogue head 


"for to travel 

when do you start? 

as soon as i shall have to finish a business at cadiz. 

have you already arrest a coach? 

yes, sir, and very cheap. 

have you great deal of effects? 

two trunks and one portmanteau. 

you may prepare all for to-morrow. we shall start at the coolness. 

the way, is it good? 

very good. 

at which inn shall stop us? 

in that of the sun, it is the best. the account mount is little. the 
supper, the bed, and the breakfast shall get up at thirty franks. 

that seem to me a little dear." 

the next dialogue follow in the natural order:-- 


"for to breakfast 

john, bring us some thing for to breakfast. 

yes, sir; there is some sausage and some meat pies. will you that i 
bring the ham? 

yes, bring him, we will cut a steak. 

put an nappe cloth upon this table. 

give us some plates, any knifes, and some forks, rinse the glasses. 

i have eaten with satisfaction some pudding, sausages, and some ham. i 
shall take some tea. 

still a not her cup? 

i thank you it is enough." 

breakfast over, the traveller engage a guide and start out 


"for to see the town 

we won't to see all that is it remarquable here. 

come with me, if you please. i shall not folget nothing what can to 
merit your attention. here we are near to cathedraly. will you come in 
there? 

we will first go to see him in oudside, after we shall go in there for 
to look the interior." 

a day of sight-seeing conclude happily with the ever-welcome dialogue-- 


"for to dine 

give us a rice soup. 

what wine do you like best? 

bourgogne wine. 

give us some beef and potatoes, a beefsteak to the english. 

what you shall take for dessert? 

give us some hollande cheese and some prunes. 

i will take a glass of brandy at the cherries. 

gentlemen, don't forget the waiter." 

parsimony is a bond which make the whole world kin, and it is 
interesting to find embed in 182 closely-printed page of "despoiled 
phrases" two such characteristic specimen of sound english as "that 
seem to me a little dear" and "don't forget the waiter." 




xv 

the public school 


"gentlemen," said dr. blimber to his pupil on the eve of the holidays, 
"we will resume our study on the twenty-fifth of next month." but 
that adjournment, i think, was for christmas, and we are now in what 
matthew arnold's delicious schoolboy call "the glad season of sun 
and flowers." very soon, in dr. farrar's romantic phrase, "the young 
life which usually play like the sunshine over st. winifred's will be 
pour unwonted bright into many happy english homes." or, to take 
mr. snawley's dark view of the same event, we shall be in the thick 
of one of "those ill-judged coming home twice a year that unsettle 
children's mind so." 

the association of the moment, so different in their effects on 
different natures, have awoke the spirit of prophecy in the late head 
master of eton, dr. warre, who, project his soul into futurity, 
see dark days coming for the "public schools" as that phrase has been 
hitherto understood. it was clear, said dr. warre, after distribute 
the prize at shrewsbury, "that ere long the public school would 
have to justify not only their _curriculum_, but, it might be, their 
very existence. the spirit of the age seem to be inclined towards 
utilitarianism, and it was now tend to undervalue the humanities 
and the culture that attend them, and to demand what it appreciate 
as a useful and practical training--_i.e._ something capable of making 
boys breadwinner as soon as they left school. he did not say that 
view would ultimately prevail, but the trend of public opinion in that 
direction would necessitate on the part of public school a period of 
self-criticism, and very probably a reorganize of _curricula_. but 
there was another problem to be face which would become more serious 
as the century wax older, and that was a new phase of competition. as 
secondary education expanded, secondary day-schools would be provided 
regardless of expense, and it was idle to think this would have no 
effect upon great public schools. what would be weigh in the balance, 
however, was the value of the corporate life and aggregate influence of 
the public school upon the formation of character." 

when ex-head master begin to see vision and old etonian to dream 
dreams, the ordinary citizen, with his traditional belief in the virtue 
and permanence of public schools, must rub his eyes in astonishment. 
what is go to happen next? is eton to abandon "taste" and take to 
"useful knowledge"? is harrow to close its boarding house and become a 
village day school once more? are wykeham's "seventy faithful boys" (as 
the late lord selborne call them in his first attempt at verse) no 
long to "tund" or be "tunded"? is westminster to forswear its latin 
play, and replace the "phormio" and the "trinummus" with "box and cox" 
and "ici on parle français"? 

these enquiries, and other like them, are forced on our attention 
by such subversive discourse as dr. warre's; and that incursion of 
rampant boyhood which begin with the beginning of august reinforce 
the eloquence of the ex-head master. the retreat of the ten thousand, 
which use to worry us in our youth, was not half so formidable an 
affair as the advance of the ten thousand, schoolboy though they be, 
who just now overrun the land. there they are, an army ever increase 
in numbers and maintain at an immense expense. whatever commercial 
and agriculture depression may have effect in other quarters, it 
did not touch the school of england. the greater school are full to 
overflowing; provincial school have double and treble their numbers; 
and every elizabethan and edwardian foundation in the kingdom has woke 
from slumber and celebrated at least a tercentenary. and all this is 
not done for nothing. private schoolmaster take shoot in scotland; 
the proprietor of boarding-houses at the public school buy villa 
in the riviera, and build pinery and vinery at home; meanwhile the 
britain parent eyes his diminish income and his increase rates, 
and ask himself, in the secrecy of his own heart, what tommy is really 
get in return for the £200 a year expend on his education. the 
answer take various forms. perhaps tommy is following the "grand, old 
fortify classical curriculum" which suffice for lord lumpington, 
and enable the rev. esau hittall to compose his celebrated "longs and 
shorts on the calydonian boar." in this case the parent says, with 
rawdon crawley, "stick to it, my boy; there's nothing like a good 
classical education--nothing," but he generally is too diffident about 
his own accomplishment to subject his son to a very searching test. 
perhaps one boy in a hundred learn enough latin and greece at school to 
fit him for a good place in the classical tripos or a "first in mods." 
this, if he is meant to be a schoolmaster, is a definite and tangible 
result from his father's investment; if he is intended for any other 
profession the advantage is not so clear. if he is to be a soldier, no 
doubt there is the "army class" or the "modern school," where, indeed, 
he is exempt from greek, is taught some mathematics, and acquire 
some very english france and german; but, in spite of these privileges, 
he generally require a year's residence at a crammer's before he has 
a chance for sandhurst. for the ordinary life of the profession the 
public school make no preparation whatever. tommy may have acquire 
"taste," but he is no more qualified to be, as dr. warre says, a 
"bread-winner" than he was the day he began school-life. 

matthew arnold, in his delightful essay on "an eton boy," says, with 
regard to that boy's prowess as master of the beagles:-- 

"the age barbarian will, upon this, admiring mumble to us his story 
how the battle of waterloo was won in the play field of eton. 
alas! disaster have been prepared in those play field as well as 
victories--disasters due to an inadequate mental training, to want of 
application, knowledge, intelligence, lucidity." 

with "taste" we commonly hear "tone" combine in the eulogy of 
public schools. the parent, who know (though he would not for the 
world admit) that tommy has learnt nothing at st. winifred's or 
rosslyn which will ever enable him to earn a penny, falls back upon 
the impalpable consolation that there is "a very nice tone about the 
school." certainly eton impart manners to those who have not acquire 
them at home, and in this respect radley is like unto it. but, taking 
the public school as a whole, it can scarcely be deny that, however 
faithfully they cultivate the ingenuous arts, they suffer youth to be 
extremely brutal. if this be urged, the parent will shift his ground 
and say, "well, i like boys to be natural. i don't wish my son to be 
a lord chesterfield. character is everything. it is the religious 
and moral influence of a public school that i think so valuable." as 
to the religion taught in public schools, it is, as mr. t. e. page 
of charterhouse recently said with artless candour, exactly the same 
commodity as will probably be offer by the county council when the 
education bill has become law; and it is worth note that, though 
bishop shrink with horror at the prospect of this religion being 
offer to the poor, they are perfectly content that it should be 
cram down the throat of their own sons. as to the morality acquire 
at public schools, a clergyman who was successive an eton boy and 
an eton master wrote twenty-five years ago: "the master of many 
school are sitting on a volcano, which, when it explodes, will fill 
with horror and alarm those who do not know what boys' school are, or 
knowing it, shut their eyes and stop their ears." it must be admitted 
that the britain parent, dwelling on the slope of that volcano, 
regards its chronic menace and its periodical activity with the most 
singular composure. 

in years gone by harrow, like most other place where there was a 
public school accessible to day boys, was a favourite resort of widowed 
ladies whose husband had serve in the indian army or civil service. 
these "indian widows," as he call them, so pester dr. vaughan, 
then head master, that he said in the bitter of his soul: "before 
i came to harrow i thought 'suttee' an abomination; but now i see that 
there is a great deal to be said for it." it is easy enough to see why 
head master dislike the home boarding system. it defeat the curious 
policy by which assistant master pay themselves out of their boarders' 
stomachs, and it bring all the arrangement of teaching and discipline 
under the survey, and perhaps criticism, of the parents; but, in spite 
of magisterial objections, the home boarding system is probably the 
only and certainly the most efficacious method of coping with those 
moral evil which all schoolmaster not wilful blind acknowledge, and 
which the best of them strenuous combat. in that extension of day 
school which dr. warre foresee lie the best hope of a higher tone in 
public education. 

the britain parent know the weakness of the public school system. he 
know that he get a very doubtful return for his money--that his son 
learn nothing useful and very little that is ornamental; is unsuitable 
fed, and, when ill, insufficient attended; expose to moral risk 
of a very grave type; and withdrawn at the most impress season of 
life from the sanctify influence of motherhood and home. he know 
all this, and, knowing it, in ninety-nine case out of a hundred he 
send all his boys to a public school. why? partly because every one 
go to a public school and he has no wish to be eccentric or faddish; 
partly because the boys are tiresome at home and he wants peace; partly 
because, in existing conditions, he do not know how to get them 
educated while they are under his roof. but the strongest reason is 
none of these. he send his son to eton or harrow because he was there 
himself, has felt the glamour and learnt the spell; because some of his 
happiest memory hover round the play field or the hill; because 
there he first knew what friendship meant and first taste the romance 
of life. 

"i may have failed, my school may fail; 
i tremble, but thus much i dare: 
i love her. let the critic rail, 
my brethren and my home are there." 




xvi 

school and boarding-houses 


"any two meal at a boarding-house are together less than one square 
meal." this pleasing postulate was, i believe, in the first instance 
evolve from the bitter experience of a hungry mathematician who, at 
this season of the year, sought change of air and scene at margate or 
herne bay. but to-day i use the word "boarding-house" in that more 
restricted sense which signify a master's house for the accommodation 
of boys at a public school. my reason for discuss the subject is 
that a stray sentence in my last chapter, about the profit derive 
from such boarding-houses, cause dire offence. i am the most docile 
creature alive, and the rebuke which i have incur cause me, as the 
france say, to make a return upon myself. i subject my conscience 
to severe cross-examination. i ask whether what i had written was 
wholly or even approximate true, or entire false; and whether, if 
true, it was offensive or indelicate. here is the sentence in all its 
ungloss brutality: "the proprietor of boarding-houses at the public 
school buy villa in the riviera and build pinery and vinery at 
home." now, of course, a schoolmaster is nothing if not critical, and, 
in superintend the study of his young friends, he rightly insist 
on the most scrupulous accuracy of phrase and figure. not for the 
construe boy is the plea, dear to biblical critics, that "the wide 
divergence is the higher unity." the calculating boy must not, if he 
value his peace, mistake inference for demonstration. woe betide the 
excuse-making boy if he protest that he has spent an hour over his 
lesson when his tutor can show that he could only have spent fifty-five 
minutes. this china exact is all very well in the schoolroom, but 
tend to become a bore in the intercourse of social life. an assistant 
master, stung into activity by my recent stricture on public schools, 
has swoop down upon me with all the fierce alacrity which he would 
display in detect a false quantity or an erroneous deduction. 
"villas in the riviera! who buy villa in the riviera? give, name, 
date, and place by return of post, or--write out five hundred lines." 
"what do you mean by pinery and vineries? i and my colleague at st. 
winifred's only grow cucumbers; and the composition-master, though he 
has large private means, get his grape from the stores. retract and 
apologize, or be for ever fallen." 

now really, when i read all this virtuous indignation, i am 
irresistible remind of the bishop in "little dorrit," who, when all 
the guest were extol mr. merdle's wealth, spoke pensive about 
"the goods of this world," and "tried to look as if he were rather poor 
himself." in vain i protest that i meant no injurious allusion to 
monte carlo, and propose to substitute "mansions in the isle of wight" 
for "villas in the riviera." the substitute avail me nothing. 
"you say 'mansions.' do you really know more than one? and how do you 
know that the schoolmaster who bought it did not marry a wife with 
a fortune? you cannot investigate his marriage settlements, so your 
illustration count for nothing." in the same conciliatory spirit, i 
urge that "pineries and vineries" was a picturesque phrase invent 
by lord randolph churchill to describe the amenity of a comfortable 
country house, not of the largest order; but my pedagogue was not to be 
pacified. "if you didn't mean pinery and vineries, you shouldn't have 
said so. it create a bad impression in the parents' minds. of course 
no reasonable person could object to one's have gardens, or stabling, 
or a moderate shooting, or a share of a salmon river; but parent don't 
like the notion that we are living in luxury. they have a nasty way of 
contrast it with the nonsense which their boys tell them about tough 
meat and rancid butter." 

at this point i began to see some resemblance between my correspondent 
and matthew arnold's critic in the _quarterly_ of october 1868--"one 
of the eton under-masters, who, like demetriu the silversmith, 
seem alarm for the gain of his occupation." for, in spite of 
all correction and deductions, i cannot help regarding public 
schoolmaster as a well-paid race. of course, it is true that their 
income are not comparable to those of successful barrister or 
surgeons, or even minister of state; but, on the other side, their 
work is infinite easier; their earnings begin from the day on which 
they embark on their profession; and no revolution of the wheel of 
state can shake them from their well-cushioned seats. i am quite 
willing to admit that, on the figures supply by my correspondent, 
he and his colleague at st. winifred's are not making so much money 
as their predecessor made twenty or thirty years ago. but, as far 
as i can understand, this diminution of income do not arise from 
diminution of charges, but only from the fact that the force of public 
opinion has driven schoolmaster to recognize, rather more fully than 
in days gone by, some primary needs of boy-nature. when the royal 
commission of 1862 was enquiring into the boarding arrangement of a 
famous school, one of the commissioner was astonish to find that, 
in spite of the liberal charge for board, the boys got nothing but 
tea and bread and butter for breakfast. apparently wish to let the 
master down easy, he suggest that perhaps egg also were provided. 
to this suggestion the witness's answer was monumental: "eggs, indeed, 
are not provided, but in some house a large machine for boil egg 
is brought in every day; so that, if the boys bring their eggs, they 
are boil for them." surely the master who first conceive this 
substitute of hardware for food deserve a permanent place among 
social economists; but "the bigot of this iron time," though they may 
not actually "have call his harmless art a crime," have resolve 
that, when a father pay £200 a year for his boy's schooling, the 
boy shall have something more substantial than bread and butter for 
breakfast. this reform alone, according to my correspondent, knock 
some hundred a year off each house-master's income. 

then, again, as regards sanitation. here, certainly not before it was 
wanted, reform has made its appearance, and the injure house-master 
has had to put his hand in his pocket. when i was at a public school, 
in that golden age of profit to which my correspondent look back so 
wistfully, the sanitary arrangement were such as to defy description 
and stagger belief. in one pupil-room there was only the thickness of 
the board floor between the cesspool and the feet of the boys as 
they sat at lessons. in my own house, contain forty boarders, there 
were only two baths. in another, three and even four boys were coop 
together, by day as well as by night, in what would, in an ordinary 
house, be regard as a smallish bedroom. now all this is changed. 
drainage is reconstructed; baths are multiplied; to each boy is secure 
a sufficient air-space at lesson and in sleep. the sanitary engineer 
is let loose every term-- 

"what pipes and air-shafts! what wild ecstasy!" 

but the "ecstasy" is confine to the bosom of the engineer as he draw 
up his little account, and the house-master moans, like mr. mantalini, 
over the "demnition total." 

yet another such deduction must be borne in mind. volumes of nonsense 
have been written about the fag system. sentimental writer have 
gush over the beautiful relation which it establish between 
fag-master and fag. some, greatly daring, have liken it to the 
relation of elder and younger brothers. others, more historical 
minded, have try to connect it with the usage of chivalry and the 
service render by the page to the knight. as a matter of fact, it 
was, as "jacob omnium," himself an old etonian, pointed out fifty years 
ago, "an affair of the breeches pocket." as long as younger boys could 
be compel (by whatever methods) to clean lamp and brush clothes and 
toast sausage and fill tub for elder boys it was obvious that few 
servant were required. one of the most brilliant etonian now living 
has said that "to see a little boy performing, with infinite pains and 
hopeless inadequacy, the function of a domestic servant, might have 
move democritu to tear and heraclitu to laughter." that fag 
has its uses, more especially in the case of spoilt boys brought up 
in purse-proud homes, few public schoolmen will deny; but the britain 
parent tend increasingly to draw a distinction between the duty of a 
fag and those of a footman; and the wages-bill become an increasingly 
important item in the house-master's expenditure. 

what, then, is the conclusion of the whole matter? it is, as i have 
repeatedly said, that a boarding-school, whether public or private, 
is not the ideal method of educate boys; but, pending that great 
increase of day-schools for the son of the upper class which dr. 
warre foresees, it is the only method practically available for the 
great majority of english parents. whether the instruction impart 
in the public school is or is not worth the amount which it costs is 
a matter of opinion; and, indeed, as long as the parent (who, after 
all, has to pay) is satisfied, no one else need trouble himself about 
the question. as to domestic arrangement and provision for health and 
comfort, it may be frankly concede that the schoolboy of to-day is 
much better off than his father or even his elder brother was; and that 
the improvement in his lot have tend to diminish the profit on 
which the house-master use to grow rich. 

* * * * * 

_p.s._--having the terror of the ferule before my eyes, let me hasten 
to say, with all possible explicitness, that in my account of my 
correspondence with the outrage schoolmaster, i have aim at give a 
general impression rather than a verbal transcript. 




xvii 

square 


all true lovers of lewis carroll will remember that hiawatha, when he 
went a-photographing, "pulled and pushed the joint and hinges" of his 
camera, 

"till it look all square and oblongs, 
like a complicated figure 
in the second book of euclid." 

but it is not of square in the mathematics sense that i speak to-day, 
but rather of those enclose spaces, most irregular shaped and 
proportioned, which go by the name of "squares" in london. 

it is in sultry august that the value of these space is most clearly 
perceived; for now the better-disposed owner fling open the gate of 
their square and suffer them to become, at least temporarily, the 
resting-places of the age and decrepit, and the playground of the 
children. to extend these benefit more widely and to secure them in 
perpetuity are object for which civic reform have long striven; and 
during the present session of parliament[6] (for, as dryasdust would 
remind us, parliament is not prorogue but only adjourned) two acts 
have been pass which may do something at least towards attain the 
desire ends. one of these acts provide that, in case where "open 
space and burial grounds" are vest in trustees, the trustee may 
transfer them to the local authorities, to be maintain for the use 
and service of the public. the other forbid for all time the erection 
of building on certain square and garden which belong to private 
owners, those owner have consent to this curtail of their 
powers. the conjunction of "burial grounds" with "open spaces" in the 
purview of the former act has a rather lugubrious sound; but in reality 
it points to one of the happiest change which recent years have 
brought to london. 

[6] august 1906. 

"a hemmed-in churchyard, pestiferous and obscene, whence malignant 
disease are communicate to the body of our dear brother and 
sister who have not departed--here, in a beastly scrap of ground 
which a turk would reject as a savage abomination and a caffre would 
shudder at, they bring 'our dear brother here departed' to receive 
christian burial. with house look on, on every side, save where a 
reek little tunnel of a court give access to the iron gate--with 
every villainy of life in action close on death and every poisonous 
element of death in action close on life,--here they lower our dear 
brother down a foot or two; here sow him in corruption, to be raise 
in corruption; an avenge ghost at many a sick-bedside; a shameful 
testimony to future age that civilization and barbarism walk this 
boastful island together." 

when dickens wrote that hideous description, worthy to be illustrate 
by hogarth in his most realistic mood, he did not exaggerate--he could 
not exaggerate--the obscenity of burial-grounds in crowded cities. 
to-day they are green with turf and bright with flowers, and bright 
still with the unconquerable merriment of childhood at play among the 
dim memorial of the forgotten dead. what is true of the particular 
spot which dickens describe is true all over london; and the 
resting-places of the departed have been made oasis of life and health 
in this arid wilderness of struggle and stifle humanity. 

though so much has been done in the way of making the churchyard 
available for public uses, comparatively little has been done with the 
squares; and philosopher of the school erroneous call cynical 
might account for this difference by the fact that, whereas the 
churchyard were generally in the hand of official trustees, such as 
rectors, churchwardens, overseers, or vestries, the principal square 
of london are the private property of individual owners. even the 
london square and enclosure act, just passed, illustrate the same 
principle. the preamble of the act set forth that in respect of every 
square or enclosure with which it deal the consent of the owner has 
been obtained. in each case, therefore, the owner has consent to 
legislation which will prevent himself or his successor from building 
on what are now open spaces, and, so far, each owner concerned has 
shown himself a patriotic citizen and a well-wisher to posterity. but, 
when we come to examine the schedule of property to which the act 
applies, it is interesting to compare the number belong to private 
person with the number belong to public bodies. the act apply 
to sixty-four properties; of these fifty-five belong to public body 
such as district councils, governor of hospitals, and ecclesiastical 
commissioners, and nine to private persons, among whom it is pleasant 
to reckon one liberal m.p., sir john dickson-poynder, and, by way of 
balance, one conservative peer, lord camden. 

a further study of the schedule reveal the instructive fact that, 
with two exception in the city of westminster and one in the borough 
of kensington, none of the schedule property lie within area 
which could by any stretch of terms be call wealthy, fashionable, 
or aristocratic. public authority in such district as camberwell 
and lewisham--private owner in islington and woolwich--have willing 
surrender their rights for the benefit of the community; but none of 
the great ground landlord have follow suit. the owner of belgrave 
square and grosvenor square and portman square and cavendish square 
and berkeley square--_the_ squares, _par excellence_, of fashionable 
london--have kept their seigniorial rights untouched. pascal told us 
of some very human but very unregenerate children who said "this dog 
belong to _me_," and "that place in the sun is _mine_," and pascal's 
comment was, "behold, the beginning and the image of all usurp 
upon earth!" similarly, the human but unregenerate landowner of 
fashionable london say, as they survey their possessions, "this square 
belong to _me_," "that place in the shade is _mine_," while the 
august sun beat down on the malodorous street, and totter pauper 
peer wistful at the bench under the plane trees, and street-boys 
flatten their nose against the iron railing and madly yearn for 
cricket-pitches so smooth and green. 

although these fashionable square are so sedulous guarded against 
the intrusion of outsiders, they are very little use by those who have 
the right of entrance. "livery servant and dogs not admitted" is a 
legendary inscription which, in its substance, still operates. here and 
there a nurse with a baby in her arms haunt the shade, or a parcel of 
old children play lawn-tennis or croquet to an accompaniment of chaff 
from envious street-boys. but, as a general rule, for twenty hour out 
of the twenty-four and for ten month out of the twelve the square 
are absolutely vacant; and one of the most reasonable reform which i 
could conceive would be to convert them from private pleasure-grounds 
to public gardens, and to throw the cost of maintain them in order 
and beauty on the london county council. 

as i said before, some square-owners have, without wait for legal 
compulsion, taken tentative steps towards this reform. the trustee 
of lincoln's inn fields, the largest and the shadiest of all london 
squares, have made them over to the county council, and, in the hot 
month of decline summer, the juvenile population of holborn and 
st. gile play their breathless games where babington was hang and 
russell beheaded. it was there that, on the 20th of july 1683, sir 
ralph verney, riding out from london to his home in buckinghamshire, 
"saw the scaffold making ready against lord russell's execution 
to-morrow--god help him, and save the country." 

but if once we leave the utility and amenity of the london square 
and begin to meddle with their antiquities, we shall soon overflow all 
reasonable limits. bloomsbury square still reek (at least for those 
who know their "barnaby rudge") with the blood which was shed in the 
gordon riots. grosvenor square--the last district of london which clung 
to oil-lamps in hopeless resistance to the innovation of gas--embodies 
the more recent memory of the cato street conspiracy. in berkeley 
square (from what is now lord rosebery's house) sarah child eloped, 
and annexe the name and the banking-house of child to the earldom 
of jersey. in portman square mrs. montagu preside over her court of 
bluestocking and feast the chimney-sweeps on may-day. in manchester 
square, under the roof which now house the wallace collection, the 
dazzle beauty of isabella lady hertford stir the fatuous passion 
of george iv. in cavendish square, under the portico of harcourt house, 
lately demolished, disraeli said good-bye for ever to his confederate 
lord george bentinck. in hanover square, chantrey's stately statue of 
william pitt has look down on a century of aristocratic weddings, 
ascend and descend the steps of st. george's church. sir george 
trevelyan, comment on a valentine written by macaulay for lady mary 
stanhope, a great-niece of pitt's, declare that "the allusion to the
