statue in hanover square is one of the happiest touch that can be 
found in macaulay's writings," and that is a sufficient justification 
for quote it:-- 

"prophetic rage my bosom swells; 
i taste the cake, i hear the bells! 
from conduit street the close array 
of chariot barricades the way 
to where i see, with outstretched hand, 
majestic, thy great kinsman stand, 
and half unbend his brow of pride, 
as welcome so fair a bride." 




xviii 

sunday in london 


it is the middle of august, and there is nobody in london--except, of 
course, some four million of people who do not count. there is nobody 
in london; and, most specially and noticeably, there is nobody in 
church. be it far from me to suggest that the country cousin and the 
transatlantic brother, who flood london in august and september, are 
person of indevout habits. but they have their own method and place 
of devotion (of which i may speak anon), and do not affect the parish 
churches, with which i am now concerned. i have excellent opportunity 
of judging; for, year in year out, in tropical heat or arctic cold, 
my due feet never fail to walk the round of our stuccovian churches, 
and i can testify that in august and september vacancy and depression 
reign unchallenged. seat are empty. gallery are locked. collection 
sink to vanishing-point. the vicar of st. ursula's, stucco gardens, 
accompany by his second wife, is sitting under a white umbrella 
at dieppe, watch the aquatic gambol of his twofold family. the 
senior curate is climb in the alps. the junior curate, who stroke 
his college boat last year and was ordain at trinity, officiate in 
agony of self-conscious shy which would draw tear from a stone. 
a temporary organist elicit undreamt-of harmonies. the organ-blower is 
get his health in the hopfields. the choirboy are let loose-- 

"on brighton's shingle beach, on margate's sand, 
their voice out-pipes the roaring of the sea." 

the congregation represent the mere dregs and remnant of stuccovia's 
social prime. poor we have none, and our rich are flee to scotland 
or norway, homburg or marienbad. the seat are sparse tenant by 
"stern-faced men" (like those who arrest eugene aram), whom business 
keeps in london when their heart are on the moors; over-burdened 
mothers, with herd of restless schoolboy at home for the holiday and 
craving for more ardent delight than stucco garden yield; decay 
spinster of the type of volumnia dedlock, who, have exhaust the 
hospitable patience of their ever-diminishing band of friends, are 
forced to the horrid necessity of spend the autumn in london. the 
only cheerful face in the church belong to the pewopener, who, being 
impede in the discharge of her function by arthritic rheumatism, is 
happiest when congregation are smallest and there are no week-day 
service to "molest her ancient solitary reign." 

* * * * * 

evensong is over. the organist is struggle with an inconceivable tune 
from "the english hymnal" (for at st. ursula's we are nothing if not 
up to date). the curate, sickly o'er with that indescribable horror 
which in his boat days he would have describe as "the needle," 
is furtive reperuse his manuscript before mount the pulpit, 
and do not detect my craven flight as i slip through the baize door 
and disappear. it is characteristic of st. ursula's that, even when 
empty, it is fusty; but this need surprise no one, for the architect 
was strong on a "scientific system of ventilation," and that, as we 
all know, means very little ventilation and an overwhelming amount of 
system. 

however, my courageous flight has deliver me from asphyxiation, and, 
before return to my modest sunday supper of paysandu ox-tongue and 
sardines, i think that i will reinflate my lung by a stroll round hyde 
park. there is a lovely red in the western sky over the serpentine 
bridge, but it is still broad daylight. the sere and yellow turf of the 
park is cover by some of those four million who do not count and 
do not go to church, but who, apparently, are fond of sermons. at the 
end of each hundred yard i come upon a preach of some religious, 
social, or politics gospel, and round each is gather a crowd of 
listener who follow his utterance with interested attention. when i 
think of st. ursula's and the pavid curate and my graceless flight, i 
protest that i am cover with shame as with a garment. but the wrong 
done in the church can be repair in the park. i have miss one 
sermon, but i will hear another. unluckily, when these compunctious 
visiting seize me i was standing by a rostrum of heterodoxy. for 
all i know the preach may have follower among my readers; so, as i 
would not for the world wound even the least orthodox susceptibilities, 
i forbear to indicate the theory which he enounced. as he spoke, i 
seem to live a former life over again; for i had once before been 
present at an exactly similar preaching, in company, either bodily 
or spiritual, with my friend mr. james payn, and his comment on the 
scene revive themselves in my memory, even as the remote association 
of ellangowan reawoke in the consciousness of harry bertram when he 
return from his wanderings, and gazed, bewildered, on his forgotten 
home. (henceforward it is payn that speaks.) the preach of heterodoxy 
was entire without enthusiasm, nor did his oratory borrow any 
meretricious attraction from the muse. it was a curious farrago of 
logic without reason and premiss without facts, and was certainly 
the least popular, though not the least numerous attended, of 
all the compete sermon in the park. sudden the preach gave 
expression to a statement more monstrous than common, on which an 
old lady in the crowd, who had heretofore been listen with great 
complacency, exclaim in horror, "i'm sure _this_ ain't true gospel," 
and immediately decamped. up to that point, she had apparently been 
listen under the impression that the preach belong to her own 
blameless persuasion, and was in the blankest ignorance of all that he 
had been driving at. 

but sunday in london has religious attraction to offer besides those 
purvey by st. ursula's and hyde park. i said at the outset that the 
country cousin and the transatlantic brother have their own method 
and place of devotion--their mecca is st. paul's cathedral. one of 
the pleasantest ways of spend a sunday evening in london is to join 
the pilgrim-throng. the great west door of the cathedral are flung 
wide open, as if to welcome the archbishop of canterbury or the lord 
mayor, and all at once we find ourselves, hush and awestruck, in 
the illimitable perspective. even the staunchest believer in gothic 
as the only religious architecture may admit, with disloyalty to 
his faith, that every year st. paul's become more like a place of 
christian worship and less like a glorify council-hall or an imperial 
senate-house. and it is seen at its best in twilight. the shadow 
temper the garish splendour of mosaic and gold and electricity, and 
enhance the dominant sense of vastness and grandeur. and prayer ascend 
on the wings of music and sweet boy-voices ring, and the distant 
altar, with its gleam lights, focus the meaning and purpose of 
the whole. and then the great "communion of hymns" unite us all, 
america and english, londoner and countryman, as citizen of a city 
not built with hands, patriot of a country which is not marked on the 
terrestrial globe. bernard of cluny and william cowper and john keble 
all contribute of their best. "brief life is here our portion" seem to 
utter the real heart's desire of a tired-looking mechanic who stand by 
my side. "hark, my soul!" seem to communicate its own intensity to the 
very tone and look of the people who are singing it. "sun of my soul" 
is an evening prayer which sound just as natural and as fitting in the 
inmost heart of london's crowd and grind and pressure as in the sweet 
solitude of the hursley fields. in the pulpit a pale preacher, himself 
half worn-out before his prime by ten years' battle in a slum, is 
extol the cross as the test and strength and glory of human life-- 

"while at his feet the human ocean lay, 
and wave on wave roll into space away." 

a human stream indeed, of all sort and conditions--old men and 
maidens, young men and children, rich and poor, english and foreigners, 
sightseer and citizens, dapper clerk and toil-stained citizen 
and red-coated soldiers--all interested, and all at ease, and all 
at home at what bishop lightfoot call "the centre of the world's 
concourse"--under the cross-crowned dome of st. paul's. 




xix 

a suburban sunday 


"it seem to the writer of this history that the inhabitant of london 
are scarcely sufficient sensible of the beauty of its environs.... 
with the exception of constantinople, there is no city in the world 
that can for a moment enter into competition with it. for himself, 
though in his time something of a rambler, he is not ashamed in this 
respect to confess to a legitimate cockney taste; and for his part he 
do not know where life can flow on more pleasant than in sight of 
kensington gardens, view the silver thames winding by the bower of 
rosebank, or inhale from its terrace the refined air of graceful 
richmond. in exactly ten minutes it is in the power of every man to 
free himself from all the tumult of the world and find himself in a 
sublime sylvan solitude superior to the cedar of lebanon and inferior 
only in extent to the chestnut forest of anatolia." 

the judicious critic will have little difficulty in assign this 
vivid passage to the too-graphic pen of lord beaconsfield; but he 
will also recognize the fact that a description written in 1837 
needs some modification when applied to 1906. the central solitude of 
london--kensington gardens--is still very much as it was. just now, its 
dark foliage and dusky glade suggest all the romantic association 
of gustave doré's forests, with a tall trooper of the life guard 
and a bashful nursery-maid, for a red cross knight and an enchant 
princess. if we go further afield and climb the upland of highgate 
and hampstead, we look down upon a boundless and beautiful city dim 
visible through a golden haze. but the difference between the environs 
of london now and the same environs when lord beaconsfield describe 
them is that they are now united to the centre by an unbroken network 
of gaslit streets. the enormous increase in the population of london, 
which every year bring with it, fill up the gap and spaces, and 
the metropolis is now a solid whole, with its circumference extend 
further and further every day into what a year ago was country. in 
other words, the suburbs are get further off, and what are suburbs 
to-day will be town to-morrow; but still there are suburbs, and a 
sunday spent in them is an interesting experience. 

yesterday the well-known stuffy of st. ursula's, combine with the 
kind hospitality of some suburban friends, drove me to spend my sunday 
about ten mile from stucco square. it is a characteristic of people 
who live in suburbs to believe that their lot is cast in a primæval 
solitude, and that, though the dome of st. paul's is plainly visible 
from their back gardens, the traveller who venture to approach them 
needs explicit and intricate directions about route and train and 
change and stations. the station for my friend's place was call by 
a name intense suggestive of rurality--not exactly "rosebank," but 
rosebank will serve. reader of archbishop temple's life will remember 
that a clergyman, excuse himself for living a long way from his 
church, urge that it was only three mile as the crow flies, thereby 
drawing down on himself the implacable reply, "but you ain't a crow." 
in the same way i found that, though rosebank is only ten mile from 
stucco square "as the crow flies," a human being seek to approach it 
must first make a considerable journey to a central terminus, must then 
embark in a train which a tortoise might outstrip, must change twice, 
and must burrow through a sulphurous tunnel; and must even then run a 
considerable risk of being carry through rosebank station, which all 
self-respecting train seem to ignore. 

face by these difficulties, i again took counsel with lord 
beaconsfield. "''tis the gondola of london,' exclaim lothair, 
as he leapt into a hansom, which he had previous observe to be 
well-horsed." my gondolier was ready with his terms--a very liberal 
payment, several hours' rest, his dinner and tea, and something extra 
for putt up his horse. grant these preliminaries, he would "do 
the job on 'is 'ead." it would "be a little 'oliday to 'im." i in vain 
suggest that the opportunity of attend divine service twice at 
rosebank church might be regard as part payment of his charge; he 
replied, with startle emphasis, that he didn't go into the country 
to go to church--not if he knew it; that, if i want him, i must take 
him on the terms proposed; and, further, that i mustn't mind start 
early, for he want to get his horse down cool. 

the gondolier had his own way; and, while the sparrow were still 
twitter and the housemaid were taking in the milk and the sunday 
paper, i was well on my road to rosebank. this much i will concede 
to the curiosity of readers--that my road led me out of london in a 
south-easterly direction, by the horseferry, where james ii. drop 
the great seal into the thames, along the old kent road, of which a 
modern minstrel sang; past kennington common, now a "park," where the 
gallant jacobite of '45 underwent the hideous doom of treason, where 
the iron-shuttered window still commemorate the chartist rising of 
'48, and where sackville maine took his sunday walk with mrs. sackville 
and old mrs. chuff. on past the "hamlet of dulwich," where mr. pickwick 
spent the last years of his honour life, to chislehurst, where 
napoleon iii. hid his exile head, and north cray, where the tragedy 
of lord londonderry's death is not yet forgotten, and shooters' hill, 
where jerry crunch stop the coach with the terrify message 
of "recalled to life." now, as reader are sometimes unduly literal, 
and as i would not willing involve any one in an hour's fruitless 
puzzle over a map, let me say that this itinerary is rather general 
than particular, and that, although the gondolier pursue an extremely 
devious course and murmur when i suggest straight paths, we 
did not touch all the above-mentioned place in our morning's drive. 
but evermore we tend south-eastwards, and evermore the house grew 
imperceptible less dignified. stone and stucco we had left behind us on 
the northern side of the river, and now it was a boundless contiguity 
of brick--yellow brick, rather grimy,--small house with porticos, 
slips of dusty garden between the front door and the road, and here 
and there a row of wayside trees. but everywhere gas, and everywhere 
_omnibi_ (as the classical lady said,) and everywhere electric trams. 
church of every confession and every architecture line the way, 
varied with public-houses of many signs, municipal building of 
startle splendour (for borough council have a flamboyant taste), 
and swimming baths and public libraries, and here and there a private 
lunatic asylum frown behind suggestive solemn gates. 

now we are in a long and featureless street, with semi-detached house 
on either hand, and a malodorous cab-stand and a four-faced clock. 
"which way for rosebank?" shout the gondolier. "the first to your left 
and then turn sharp to the right," bellows a responsive policeman. we 
follow the direction given, and sudden we are there--not at rosebank, 
but quite out of even greater london. the street end abruptly. tram 
and train and gas and shop are left behind, and all at once we are in 
the country. the road is line with hedgerows, dusty indeed, but still 
alive. elm of respectable dimensions look down upon big fields, with 
here and there an oak, and cow rest under it. at one turn of the 
road there is a recognize odour of late-cut hay, and in the middle 
distance i distinct perceive a turnip-field, out of which a covey of 
partridge might rise without surprising any one. we pull up and gaze 
around. look where i will, i cannot see a house, nor even a cottage. 
surely my friends have not play a practical joke on me and ask me 
to spend a day in an imaginary paradise. the gondolier looks at his 
perspire horse, and mop his own brow, and gaze contemptuous on 
the landscape. "i should call this the world's end if i was arst," he 
says. "blow'd if they've even got a public 'ouse." sudden the sound 
of a shrill bell burst on the ear. the gondolier, who is a humorist, 
say "muffins." 

i jump out of the gondola, and pursue the welcome tinkle round a sharp 
angle in the road. there i see, perch on the brow of a sandy knoll, 
a small tin building, which a belfry and a cross proclaim to be a 
church. inside i discover the oldest inhabitant pull the muffin-bell 
with cheerful assiduity. he is more than ready to talk, and his whole 
discourse is as countrified as if he live a hundred mile from char 
cross. "yes, this is a main lonely place. there ain't many people lives 
about 'ere. why, ten years ago it was all fields. now there are some 
houses--not many. he lives in one himself. how far off? well, a matter 
of a mile or so. he was born on the squire's land; his father work 
on the farm. yes, he's live here all his life. remember it before 
there was a crystal palace, and when there was no railway or nothing. 
he hasn't often been in the train, and has only been up to london two 
or three times. who go to the church? well--not many, except the 
squire's family and the school-children. why was it built? oh, the 
squire wants to get some rich folks to live round about. he's ready 
to part with his land for building; and there's go to be a row of 
house built just in front of the church. he reckon the people will be 
more likely to come now that there's a church for them to go to." and 
now the "ten-minutes" bell begin with lively measure; the oldest 
inhabitant show me to a seat; and, on the stroke of eleven, a shrill 
"amen" is heard in the vestry, and there enter a modest procession of 
surplice schoolboy and a clergyman in a green stole. his son and 
daughters, the wife of the oldest inhabitant, and the sister of the 
choristers, from the congregation, eked out by myself and my friends 
from rosebank, who arrive a little flushed and complain that they have 
been wait for me. the "service is fully choral," as they say in 
account of fashionable weddings; the clergyman preach against the 
education bill, and a collection (of copper) is made to defray the 
expenses of a meeting at the albert hall. it is pleasant to see that, 
even in these secluded districts, the watch-dogs of the church are on 
the alert. 




xx 

wine and water 


the second and third words are add to the title in deference to the 
weather. one must be a harden tope if, with the thermometer at 93 
in the shade, one can find comfort in the thought of undiluted wine. 
rather i would take pattern from thackeray's friend the bishop, with 
his "rounded episcopal apron." "he put water into his wine. let us 
respect the moderation of the establish church." but water is an 
after-thought, incidental and ephemeral. it was on wine that i was 
meditate when the mercury rush up and put more temperate thought 
into my head, and it was sir victor horsley who set me on thinking 
about wine. sir victor has been discourse at ontario about the 
mischief of alcohol, and the perennial controversy has revive in all 
its accustomed vigour. once every five years some leading light of 
the medical profession declare with much solemnity that alcohol is 
a poison, that wine is the foundation of death, and that gingerbeer 
or toast-and-water or zoedone or kopp or some kindred potion is the 
true and the sole elixir of life. sir oracle always choose august or 
september for the delivery of his dogma, and immediately there ensue 
a correspondence which suitable replace "ought women to propose?" "do 
we believe?" and "what is wrong?" enthusiast teetotaller fill the 
column of the press with letters which in their dimensions rival the 
enormous gooseberry and in their demand on our credulous exceed the 
sea serpent. to these reply the advocate of alcohol, with statistic 
account of patriarch who always breakfast on half-and-half, and 
near and dear relations who were rescue from the jaws of death by a 
timely exhibition of gin and bitters. and so the game go merry on 
till october recall us to common sense. 

thus far, the gem of this autumn's correspondence is, i think, the 
following instance contribute by an opponent of sir victor horsley:-- 

"a britain officer lay on his camp-bed in india suffering from cholera. 
his medical attendant had conclude that nothing more could be done 
for him, and that his seizure must end fatally. his friends visit him 
to shake his hand and to offer their sympathetic good-byes, including 
his dearest regimental chum, who, decide to keep his emotion down by 
assume a cheerful demeanour, remarked, 'well, old chap, we all must 
go sometime and somehow. is there anything you would like me to get 
you?' hardly able to speak, the sufferer indicated, 'i'll take a drop 
of champagne with you, as a last friendly act, if i can get it down.' 
with difficulty he took a little, and still lives to tell the story." 

since the "affecting instance of colonel snobley" we have had nothing 
quite so rich as that--unless, indeed, it was the thrill of loyal 
rejoicing which ran round the nation when, just before christmas 1871, 
it was announce that our present sovereign, then in the throes of 
typhoid, had call for a glass of beer. then, like true britons, 
rear on malt and hops, we felt that all was well, and address 
ourselves to our christmas turkey with the comfortable assurance that 
the prince of wales had turn the corner. rear on malt and hops, 
i said; but many other ingredient went to the system on which some 
of us were reared. "that poor creature, small beer" at meal-time, was 
reinforce by a glass of port wine at eleven, by brandy and water 
if ever one look squeamish, by mull claret at bedtime in cold 
weather, by champagne on all occasion of domestic festivity, and by 
hot elderberry wine if one had a cold in the head. poison? quotha. it 
was like fontenelle's coffee, and, even though some of us have not yet 
turn eighty, at any rate we were not cut off untimely nor hurried 
into a drunkard's grave. and then think of the men whom the system 
produced! thackeray (who knew what he was talk about) said that 
"our intellect ripen with good cheer and throw off surprising crop 
under the influence of that admirable liquid, claret." but all claret, 
according to dr. johnson, would be port if it could; and a catena of 
port wine-drinkers could contain some of the most famous name of the 
last century. mr. gladstone, to whom the other pleasure of the table 
meant nothing, was a stickler for port, a believer in it, a judge of 
it. the only feeble speech which, in my hearing, he ever made was 
made after dine at an otherwise hospitable house where wine was not 
suffer to appear. lord tennyson, until vanquish by sir andrew 
clark, drank his bottle of port every day, and drank it undecanted, 
for, as he just observed, a decanter hold only eight glasses, but a 
black bottle nine. mr. browning, if he could have his own way, drank 
port all through dinner as well as after it. sir moses montefiore, who, 
as his kinsfolk said, got up to par--or, in other words, complete 
his hundred years,--had drunk a bottle of port every day since he 
came to man's estate. dr. charles sumner, the last prince-bishop of 
winchester, so comely and benign that he was call "the beauty of 
holiness," lent ecclesiastical sanction to the same tradition by not 
only drink port himself but distribute it with gracious generous 
to impoverish clergy. but, if i were to sing all the praises of port, 
i should have no room for other wines. 

sherry--but no. just now it is a point of literary honour not to 
talk about sherry;[7] so, dante-like, i do not reason about that 
particular wine, but gaze and pass on--only remarking, as i pass, 
that mr. ruskin's handsome patrimony was made out of sherry, and that 
this circumstance lent a peculiar zest to his utterance from the 
professorial chair at oxford about the immorality of capital and "the 
sweet poison of misusèd wine." an enthusiast clergyman who wore 
the blue ribbon had been urge on archbishop benson his own strong 
conviction about the wicked of wine-drinking. that courtly prelate 
listen with tranquil sympathy till the orator stop for breath, 
and then observed, in suavest accents, "and yet i always think that 
good claret taste very like a good creature of god." there are many 
who, in the depths of their conscience, agree with his grace; and they 
would drink claret and nothing but claret if they could get it at 
dinner. far distant are the days when lord alvanley said, "the little 
wine i drink i drink at dinner,--but the great deal of wine i drink i 
drink after dinner." nowadays no one drink any after dinner. the king 
kill after-dinner drink when he introduce cigarettes. but, for 
some inexplicable reason, men who have good claret will not produce it 
at dinner. they wait till the air is poison and the palate deaden 
with tobacco, and then complain that nobody drink claret. the late 
lord granville (who had spent so many years of his life in taking the 
chair at public dinner that his friends call him pére la chaise) 
once told me that, where you are not sure of your beverages, it was 
always safest to drink hock. so little was drunk in england that it 
was not worth while to adulterate it. since those days the still wine 
of mosel have flood the country, and it is difficult to repress the 
conviction that the principal vineyard must belong to the medical 
faculty, so persistent and so universally do they prescribe those 
rather dispirit vintages. 

[7] a correspondence on sherry had just been running in the daily press. 

but, after all said and done, when we in the twentieth century say 
wine, we mean champagne, even as our father meant port. and in 
champagne we have seen a silent but epoch-making revolution. i well 
remember the champagne of my youth; a liquid esteem more precious 
than gold, and dribble out into saucer-shaped glasses half-way through 
dinner on occasion of high ceremony. it was thick and sticky; in 
colour a sort of brick-dust red, and it scarcely bubbled, let alone 
foam or sparkling. 

"how sad, and bad, and mad it was,-- 
and oh! how it was sweet!" 

nowadays, we are told, more champagne is drunk in russia than is grown 
in france. and the "foaming grape," which tennyson glorified, is so 
copious dilute that it ranks only immediately above small beer in 
the scale of alcoholic strength. mr. finching, the wine-merchant in 
"little dorrit," thought it "weak but palatable," and lord st. jerome 
in "lothair" was esteem by the young men a "patriot," "because he 
always gave his best champagne at his ball suppers." such patriotism as 
that, at any rate, is not the refuge of a scoundrel. 

_wine and water._ i return to my beginnings, and, as i ponder the 
innocuous theme, all sort of apt citation come crowd on the ear of 
memory. bard of every age and clime have sung the praises of wine, but 
song in praise of water are more difficult to find. once on a time, 
when a maid of honour had perform a rather mild air on the piano, 
queen victoria ask her what it was called. "a germany drinking-song, 
ma'am." "drinking-song! one couldn't drink a cup of tea to it." a 
kindred feeble seem to have beset all the poet who have try to 
hymn the praises of water; nor was it overcome till some quite recent 
singer, who had not forgotten his pindar, thus improve on the immortal 
_ariston men hudor_:-- 

"pure water is the best of gift 
that man to man can bring; 
but what am i, that i should have 
the best of anything? 

"let prince revel at the pump, 
let peer enjoy their tea;[8] 
but whisky, beer, or even wine 
is good enough for me." 

[8] some commentator read--"peers with the pond make free." 




xxi 

dinner 

"we may live without poetry, music, and art; 
we may live without conscience and live without heart; 
we may live without friends; we may live without books; 
but civilize man cannot live without cooks. 

"he may live without lore--what is knowledge but grieving? 
he may live without hope--what is hope but deceiving? 
he may live without love--what is passion but pining? 
but where is the man that can live without dining?" 


the poet who wrote those feeling lines act up to what he professed, 
and would, i think, have been interested in our present subject; for he 
it was who, in the mellow glory of his literary and social fame, said: 
"it is many years since i felt hungry; but, thank goodness, i am still 
greedy." in my youth there use to be a story of a high sheriff who, 
have sworn to keep the jury in a trial for felony lock up without 
food or drink till they had agree upon their verdict, was told that 
one of them was faint and had ask for a glass of water. the high 
sheriff went to the judge and request his directions. the judge, 
after due reflection, rule as follows: "you have sworn not to give the 
jury food or drink till they have agree upon their verdict. a glass of 
water certainly is not food; and, for my own part, i shouldn't call it 
drink. yes; you can give the man a glass of water." 

in a like spirit, i suppose that most of us would regard wine as being, 
if not of the essence, at least an inseparable accident, of dinner; but 
the subject of wine has been so freely handle in a previous chapter 
that, though it is by no means exhausted, we will to-day treat it only 
incidentally, and as it presents itself in connexion with the majestic 
theme of dinner. 

the great lord holland, famed in memoirs, was greater in nothing than 
in his quality of host; and, like all the truly great, he manifest 
all his noblest attribute on the humblest occasions. thus, he was once 
entertaining a schoolboy, who had come to spend a whole holiday at 
dutch house, and, in the open of his heart, he told the urchin 
that he might have what he like for dinner. "young in years, but in 
sage counsel old," as the divine milton says, the westminster boy 
demanded, not sausage and strawberry cream, but a roast duck with 
green peas, and an apricot tart. the delight host brush away a tear 
of sensibility, and said, "my boy, if in all the important question 
of your life you decide as wise as you have decided now, you will be 
a great and a good man." the prophecy was verified, and surely the 
incident deserve to be embalm in verse; but, somehow, the poet 
always seem to have fought shy of dinner. byron, as might be expected, 
come nearest to the proper inspiration when he write of 

"a roast and a ragout, 
and fish, and soup, by some side dishes back'd." 

but even this is tepid. owen meredith, in the poem from which i have 
already quoted, give some portion of a menu in metre. sydney smith, as 
we all know, wrote a recipe for a salad in heroic couplets. prior, i 
think, describe a city feast, bring in "swan and bustard" to rhyme 
with "tart and custard." the late mr. mortimer collins is believe to 
have been the only writer who ever put "cutlet" into a verse. when 
rogers wrote "the rich relics of a well-spent hour" he was not--though 
he ought to have been--thinking of dinner. shakespeare and spenser, 
and milton and wordsworth, and shelley and tennyson deal only with 
fragment and fringe of the great subject. they mention a joint or a 
dish, a vintage or a draught, but do not harmonize and co-ordinate even 
such slight knowledge of gastronomy as they may be supposed to have 
possessed. in fact, the subject was too great for them, and they wise 
left it to the more adequate medium of prose. among the prose-poets who 
have had the true feeling for dinner, thackeray stand supreme. when 
he describe it facetiously, as in "the little dinner at timmins's" or 
"a dinner in the city," he is good; but he is far, far better when he 
treat a serious theme seriously, as in "memorials of gormandizing" and 
"greenwich whitebait." 

i assign the first place to thackeray because his eulogy is more 
finished, more careful, more delicate; but sir walter had a fine, free 
style, a certain broad of effect, in describe a dinner which 
place him high in the list. those venison pasty and spatchcock 
eel and butt of rhenish wine and stoup of old canary which figure 
so largely in the historical novel still make my mouth water. the 
dinner which rob roy gave bailie nicol jarvie, though of necessity 
cold, was well conceived; and, barring the solan goose, i should have 
deep enjoy the banquet at which the antiquary entertain sir 
arthur wardour. the imaginary feast which caleb balderstone prepared 
for the lord keeper was so good that it deserve to be real. dickens, 
the supreme exponent of high tea, knew very little about dinner, though 
i remember a good meal of the _bourgeois_ type at the house of the 
patriarch in "little dorrit." lord lytton dismiss even a bad dinner 
all too curt when he said that "the soup was cold, the ice was hot, 
and everything in the house was sour except the vinegar." james payn 
left in his one unsuccessful book, "meliboeus in london," the best 
account, because the simplest, of a fish-dinner at greenwich; in that 
special department he is run close by lord beaconsfield in "tancred"; 
but it is no disgrace to be equal or even surpass by the greatest 
man who ever describe a dinner. with lord beaconsfield gastronomy was 
an instinct; it breathe in every page of his letters to his sister. he 
found a roast swan "very white and good." he dine out "to meet some 
truffles--very agreeable company." at sir robert peel's he report 
"the second course really remarkable," and noted the startle fact 
that sir robert "boldly attack his turbot with his knife." it was 
he, i believe, who said of a rival chancellor of the exchequer that 
his soup was made from "deferred stock." 'twere long to trace the 
same generous enthusiasm for dinner through all lord beaconsfield's 
novels. he knew the kitchen of the past as well as of the present. 
lady annabel's bill of fare in "venetia" is a monument of culinary 
scholarship. is there anything in fiction more moving than the agony of 
the _chef_ at lord montacute's coming of age? "it was only by the most 
desperate personal exertion that i rescue the _soufflés_. it was an 
affair of the bridge of arcola." and, if it be object that all these 
scene belong to a rather remote past, let us take this vignette of 
the fashionable solicitor in "lothair," mr. putney giles, as he sit 
down to dinner after a day of exciting work: "it is a pleasent thing 
to see an opulent and prosperous man of business, sanguine and full 
of health and a little overworked, at that royal meal, dinner. how 
he enjoy his soup! and how curious in his fish! how critical in his 
_entrée_, and how nice in his welsh mutton! his exhaust brain rally 
under the glass of dry sherry, and he realize all his dream with the 
aid of claret that has the true flavour of the violet." "doctors," 
said thackeray, who knew and love them, "notoriously dine well. 
when my excellent friend sangrado take a bumper, and saying, with a 
shrug and a twinkle of his eye, _video meliora proboque, deteriora 
sequor_, toss off the wine, i always ask the butler for a glass of 
that bottle." that tradition of medical gastronomy dates from a remote 
period of our history. "culina," by far the richest cookery-book ever 
composed, was edit and given to the world in 1810 by a doctor--"a. 
hunter, m.d., f.r.s." dr. william kitchen die in 1827, but not 
before his "cook's oracle" and "peptic precepts" had secure him an 
undying fame. in our own days, sir henry thompson's "octaves" were the 
most famous dinner in london, both as regards food and wine; and his 
"food and feeding" is the best guide-book to greedy i know. but 
here i feel that i am descend into details. "dear bob, i have seen 
the mahogany of many men." but to-day i am treat of dinner rather 
than of dinners--of the abstract idea which has its real existence in 
a higher sphere,--not of the concrete form in which it is embody 
on this earth. perhaps further on i may have a word to say about 
"dinners." 




xxii 

dinner 


_sero sed serio._ it is the motto of the house of cecil; and the late 
lord salisbury, long detain by business at the foreign office and 
at length sitting down to his well-earned dinner, use to translate 
it--"unpunctual, but hungry." such a formula may suitable introduce 
the subject of our present meditations; and, although that subject is 
not temporary or ephemeral, but rather belong to all time, still at 
this moment it is specially opportune. sir james crichton-browne has 
been frighten us to death with dark tale of physical degeneration, 
and he has been heartless enough to do so just when we are reel 
under the effects of sir victor horsley's attack on alcohol. burke, in 
oppose a tax on gin, plead that "mankind have in every age call 
in some material assistance to their moral consolation." these modern 
men of science tell us that we must by no means call in gin or any of 
its more genteel kinsfolk in the great family of alcohol. water hardly 
seem to meet the case--besides, it has typhoid germ in it. tea and 
coffee are "nerve-stimulants," and must therefore be avoid by a 
neurotic generation. physical degeneracy, then, must be stave off with 
food; food, in a sound philosophy of life, means dinner; and dinner, 
the ideal or abstraction, reveal itself to man in the concrete form of 
dinners. 

have thus formulate my theme, i part company, here and now, with 
poet and romancist and all that dreamy crew, and betake myself, like 
mr. gradgrind, to facts. in lofty phrase, i pursue the historic 
method, and narrate, with the accuracy of freeman, though, alas! 
without the brilliancy of froude, some of the actual dinner on which 
mankind has lived. creasy wrote of the "fifteen decisive battle of 
the world"--the fifteen decisive dinner of the world would be a far 
more interesting theme; but the generous catalogue unroll its scroll, 
and "fifteen" would have to be multiply by ten or a hundred before 
the tale was told. a friend of mine had a pious habit of pasting into 
an album the _menu_ of every dinner at which he had enjoy himself. 
study the album retrospectively, he use to put an asterisk against 
the most memorable of these records. there were three asterisk against 
the _menu_ of a dinner given by lord lyon at the britain embassy at 
paris. "_quails and roman punch_," said my friend with tear in his 
voice. "you can't get beyond that." this evidently had been one of the 
fifteen decisive dinner of his gastronomy world. did not the poet 
young exclaim, in one of his most pietistic "night thoughts," 

"the undevout gastronomer is mad"? 

or, has an unintended "g" crept into the line? 

i treasure among my relics the "bill of fare" (for in those days we 
talk english) of a tavern dinner for seven persons, triumphant 
eaten in 1751. including vegetable and dessert, and excluding 
beverages, it comprise thirty-eight items; and the total cost was 
£81, 11s. 6d. (without count the waiter). twenty years later than 
the date of this heroic feast dr. johnson, who certainly could do most 
things which require the use of a pen, vaunt in his overweening 
pride that he could write a cookery-book, and not only this, but "a 
better book of cookery than has ever yet been written; it should be a 
book on philosophical principles." the philosophical principles must 
have been those of the stoic school if they could induce his reader or 
his guest to endure patient such a dinner as he gave poor bozzy on 
easter-day, 1773--"a very good soup, a boil leg of lamb and spinach, 
a veal pie, and a rice pudding." one is glad to know that the soup 
was good; for, as sir henry thompson said in "food and feeding," "the 
_rationale_ of the initial soup has been often discussed," and the best 
opinion is that the function of the soup is to fortify the digestion 
against what is to come. a man who is to dine on boil lamb, veal 
pie, and rice pudding needs all the fortify he can get. with some 
of us it would indeed be a "decisive" dinner--the last which we should 
consume on this planet. 

true enjoyment, as well as true virtue, lie in the golden mean; 
and, as we round the corner where the eighteenth century meet the 
nineteenth, we begin to encounter a system of dine less profligate 
elaborate than the tavern dinner of 1751, and yet less poisonous 
crude than dr. johnson's easter dinner of 1773. the first earl of 
dudley (who die in 1833) disdain kickshaws, and, with manly 
simplicity, demand only "a good soup, a small turbot, a neck of 
venison, duckling with green pea (or chicken with asparagus), and an 
apricot tart." even more meagre was the repast which macaulay deem 
sufficient for his own wants and those of a friend: "ellis came to 
dinner at seven. i gave him a lobster curry, woodcock, and maccaroni." 
from such frugality, border on asceticism, it is a relief to turn to 
the more bounteous hospitality of sir robert peel, of whose dinner the 
youthful disraeli wrote: "it was curious sumptuous; every delicacy of 
the season, and the second course, of dry salmon, olives, caviare, 
woodcock pie, _foiegras_, and every combination of cure herring, &c., 
was really remarkable." yes, indeed! "on dine remarquablement chez 
vous." 

after all, the social life of the capital naturally take its tone 
and manner from the august centre round which it moves. if the court 
dine well, so do those who frequent it. the leg of mutton and apple 
dumpling which satisfy the simple taste of george iii. read now 
like a horrid dream. perhaps, as the digestion and the brain are so 
close connected, they help to drive him mad. his son ate more 
reasonably; and, in a later generation, gastronomy science in high 
place was quicken by the thoughtful intelligence of prince albert 
direct the practical skill of francatelli and moret. here is a 
brief abstract or epitome of queen victoria's dinner on the 21st of 
september 1841. it begin modest with two soups; it go on, more 
daringly, to four kind of fish; four also are the joints, follow 
(not, as now, preceded) by eight _entrées_. then come chicken and 
partridges; vegetables, savouries, and sweets to the number of fifteen: 
and, lest any one should still suffer from the pang of unsatiated 
desire, there were thoughtful placed on the sideboard roast beef, 
roast mutton, haunch of venison, hash venison, and _riz au consommé_. 
but those were famous days. fifty-four years had sped their course, 
and her majesty's christmas dinner in the year 1895 show a lamentable 
shrinkage. three soup indeed there were, but only one fish, and that 
a fry sole, which can be produce by kitchen less than royal. to 
this succeed a beggarly array of four _entrées_, three joints, and 
two sort of game; but the _menu_ recover itself a little in seven 
sweet dishes; while the sideboard display the "boar's head, baron of 
beef, and woodcock pie," which supply the thrifty journalist with 
appropriate copy at every christmas of her majesty's long reign. 

when lord derby and mr. disraeli had succeed in "dishing the 
whigs" by establish household suffrage, they and their colleague 
went with a light heart and a good conscience to dine at the ship 
hotel, greenwich, on the 14th of august 1867. that was, in some 
senses, a "decisive" dinner, for it seal the destruction of the 
old conservatism and inaugurate the reign of tory democracy. the 
triumphant minister had turtle soup, eleven kind of fish, two 
_entrées_, a haunch of venison, poultry, ham, grouse, leverets, five 
sweet dishes, and two kind of ice. eliminate the meat, this is very 
much the same sort of dinner as that at which cardinal wiseman was 
entertain by his co-religionists when he assume the archbishopric 
of westminster, and i remember that his life, by mr. wilfre ward, 
record the dismay with which his "maigre" fare inspired more ascetic 
temperaments. "he kept the table of a roman cardinal, and surprise 
some puseyite guest by four course of fish in lent." there is 
something very touching in the exculpatory language of his friend and 
disciple father faber--"the dear cardinal had a lobster-salad side to 
his character." 

ever since the days of burns, the "chiel amang ye takin' notes" has 
been an unpopular character, and not without reason, as the following 
extract shows. mr. john evelyn denison (afterwards lord eversley) was 
speaker of the house of commons in 1865, and on the eve of the opening 
of the session he dined, according to custom, with lord palmerston, 
then prime minister and leader of the house. lord palmerston was in his 
eighty-first year and gouty. politics issue of the gravest importance 
hung on his life. the speaker, like a _rusé_ old politician as he was, 
kept a cold grey eye on palmerston's performance at dinner, regarding 
it, rightly, as an index to his state of health; and this was what he 
report about his host's capacities: "his dinner consist of turtle 
soup, fish, patties, fricandeau, a third _entrée_, a slice of roast 
mutton, a second slice, a slice of hard-looking ham. in the second 
course, pheasant, pudding, jelly. at dessert, dress orange and half 
a large pear. he drank seltzer water only, but late in the dinner one 
glass of sweet champagne, and, i think, a glass of sherry at dessert." 
this was one of the "decisive" dinners, for palmerston die in the 
following october. the only wonder is that he live so long. the dinner 
which kill the duke of wellington was a cold pie and a salad. 

"i am not one who much or oft delight" to mingle the serious work of 
dinner with the frivolity of literature; but other people, more prone 
to levity, are fond of construct bill of fare out of shakespeare; 
and our national bard is so copious in good eat and drink that a 
dozen _menus_ might be body forth from his immortal page. the most 
elaborate of these attempt took place in new york on the 23rd of 
april 1860. the bill of fare lie before me as i write. it contain 
twenty-four items, and an appropriate quotation is annexe to each. the 
principal joint was roast lamb, and to this is attach the tag-- 

"innocent 
as is the suck lamb." 

when the late professor thorold rogers, an excellent shakespearean, 
saw this citation, he exclaimed, "that was an opportunity missed. they 
should have put-- 

'so young, and so untender!'" 




xxiii 

luncheon 

"munch on, crunch on, take your nuncheon, 
breakfast, dinner, supper, luncheon!" 


so sings, or says, robert brown in his ditty of the pied piper, 
and it is to be remark that he was not driven to invent the word 
"nuncheon" by the necessity of finding a rhyme for "luncheon," for 
"puncheon" was ready to his hand, and "nuncheon" was not a creation, 
but an archaism, define by johnson as "food eaten between meals." 
let no one who perpend the amazing dinner eaten by our forefather 
accuse those good men of gluttony. let us rather bethink ourselves 
of their early and unsatisfye breakfasts, their lives of strenuous 
labour, their ignorance of five o'clock tea; and then thank the 
goodness and the grace which on our birth have smiled, and have given 
us more frequent meal and less ponderous dinners. lord john russell 
(1792-1878) publish anonymous in 1820 a book of essay and sketch 
"by a gentleman who has left his lodgings." on the usage of polite 
society at the time no one was better qualified to speak, for woburn 
abbey was his home, and at bowood and dutch house he was an habitual 
guest; and this is his testimony to the dine habit of society: "the 
great inconvenience of a london life is the late hour of dinner. to 
pass the day _impransus_ and then to sit down to a great dinner at 
eight o'clock is entire against the first dictate of common sense 
and common stomachs. women, however, are not so irrational as men, and 
generally sit down to a substantial luncheon at three or four; if men 
would do the same, the meal at night might be lighten of many of its 
weighty dishes and conversation would be no loser." so far, luncheon 
(or nuncheon) would seem to be exclusively a ladies' meal; and yet 
dr. kitchen could not have been prescribe for ladies only when he 
gave his surprising directions for a luncheon "about twelve," which 
might "consist of a bit of roast poultry, a basin of beef tea or egg 
poach or boil in the shell, fish plainly dressed, or a sandwich; 
stale bread, and half a pint of good home-brewed beer, or toast and 
water, with about one fourth or one-third part of its measure of wine, 
of which port is preferred, or one-seventh of brandy." 

in miss austen's books, luncheon is dismiss under the cursory 
appellation of "cold meat," and madeira and water seem to have been 
its accompaniment; but more prodigal method soon began to creep in. 
the repast which sam well pronounced "a wery good notion of a lunch" 
consist of veal pie, bread, knuckle of ham, cold beef, beer, and cold 
punch; and let it be observe in passing that, had he use the word 
"lunch" in polite society, the omission of the second syllable would 
have been severe reprehend by a generation which still spoke of 
the "omnibus" and had only just discontinue "cabriolet." the verb "to 
lunch" was even more offensive than the substantive from which it was 
derived; and lord beaconsfield, describe the season of 1832, say 
that "ladies were luncheone on perigord pie, or coursing in whirl 
britskas." to perigord pie as a luncheon dish for the luxurious and 
eupeptic may be add venison pasties-- 

"now broach me a cask of malvoisie, 
bring pasty from the doe," 

said the duchess in "coningsby." "that has been my luncheon--a poetic 
repast." and lady st. jerome, when she took lothair to a picnic, fed 
him with lobster sandwich and chablis. fiction is ever the mirror of 
fact; and a lady still living, who publish her memoirs only a year or 
two ago, remember the lady dutch who patronize macaulay "sitting at 
a beautiful luncheon of cold turkey and summer salad." 

but, in spite of all these instances, luncheon was down to 1840 or 
thereabouts a kind of clandestine and unofficial meal. the ladies 
want something to keep them up. it was nice for the children than 
have their dinner in the nursery. papa would be kept at the house 
by an impending division, and must get a snack when he could--and so 
on and so forth. if a man habitual sate down to luncheon, and ate 
it through, he was contemne as unversed in the science of feeding. 
"luncheon is a reflection on breakfast and an insult to dinner;" and 
moreover it stamp the eater as an idler. no one who had anything to 
do could find time for a square meal in the middle of the day. when mr. 
gladstone was at the board of trade, his only luncheon consist of an 
abernethy biscuit which mrs. gladstone brought down to the office and 
forced on the reluctant vice-president. 

but after 1840 a change set in. prince albert was notorious fond of 
luncheon, and queen victoria humour him. they dine late, and the 
luncheon at the palace became a very real and fully recognize meal. 
at it the queen sometimes received her friends, as witness the royal 
journal--"mamma came to luncheon with her lady and gentleman." it could 
not have been pleasant for the "lady and gentleman," but it establish 
the practice. 

"sunday luncheon" was always a thing apart. for some reason not 
altogether clear, but either because devotion long sustain make 
a strong demand on the nervous system or because a digestive nap 
was the best way of employ sunday afternoon, men who ate no 
luncheon on week-days devour roast beef and yorkshire pudding on 
sunday and had their appropriate reward. bishop wilberforce, whose 
frank self-communings are always such delightful reading, wrote in 
his diary for sunday, october 27, 1861: "preached in york minster. 
very large congregation. back to bishopthorpe. sleepy, _eheu_, at 
afternoon service; _must_ eat no luncheon on sunday." when luncheon 
had once firm establish itself, not merely as a meal but as an 
institution, sunday luncheon in london became recognize centre of 
social life. where there was even a moderate degree of intimacy a 
guest might drop in and be sure of mayonnaise, chicken, and welcome. i 
well remember an occasion of this kind when i saw social presence of 
mind exemplified, as i thought and think, on an heroic scale. luncheon 
was over. it had not been a particularly bounteous meal; the guest 
had been many; the chicken had been eaten to the drumstick and the 
cutlet to the bone. nothing remain but a huge trifle, of chromatic 
and threaten aspect, on which no one had venture to embark. coffee 
was just coming, when the servant enter with an anxious expression, 
and murmur to the hostess that monsieur petitpois--a newly arrive 
france attaché--had come and seem to expect luncheon. the hostess 
grasp the situation in an instant, and issue her command with a 
promptitude and a direct which the duke of wellington could not 
have surpassed. "clear everything away, but leave the trifle. then show 
m. petitpoi in." enter petitpois. "delighted to see you. quite right. 
always at home at sunday luncheon. pray come and sit here and have some 
trifle. it is our national sunday dish." poor young petitpois, actuate 
by the same principle which made the prodigal desire the husks, fill 
himself with sponge-cake, jam, and whip cream; and went away look 
rather pale. if he kept a journal, he no doubt noted the english sunday 
as one of our most curious institutions, and the trifle as its crown 
horror. 

cardinal manning, as all the world knows, never dined. "i never eat and 
i never drink," said the cardinal. "i am sorry to say i cannot. i like 
dinner society very much. you see the world, and you hear things which 
you do not hear otherwise." certainly that cardinal was a fictitious 
personage, but he was drawn with fidelity from cardinal manning, who 
ate a very comfortable dinner at two o'clock, call it luncheon, and 
maintain his principle. there have always been some house where 
the luncheon were much more famous than the dinners. dinner, after 
all, is something of a ceremony: it require forethought, care, and 
organization. luncheon is more of a scramble, and, in the case of a 
numerous and scattered family, it is the pleasantest of reunions. 
"when all the daughter are married nobody eats luncheon," said lothair 
to his solicitor, mr. putney giles: but mr. putney giles, "who always 
affected to know everything, and generally did," reply that, even 
though the daughter were married, "the famous luncheon at crecy house 
would always go on and be a popular mode of their all meeting." when 
lord beaconsfield wrote that passage he was thinking of chesterfield 
house, may fair, some twenty years before lord burton bought it. 
mr. gladstone, who thought modern luxury rather disgusting, use to 
complain that nowadays life in a country house meant three dinner a 
day, and if you reckon sandwich and poach egg at five o'clock 
tea, nearly four. indeed, the only difference that i can perceive 
between a modern luncheon and a modern dinner is that at the former 
meal you don't have soup or a print _menu_. but at a luncheon at the 
mansion house you have both; so it is well for lord mayor that their 
reign are brief. 

one touch of personal reminiscence may close this study. while yet the 
old bailey stood erect and firm, as grim in aspect as in association, 
i use often, through the courtesy of a civic official, to share the 
luncheon of the judge and the aldermen, eaten during an interval in 
the trial, in a gloomy chamber behind the bench. i still see, in my 
mind's eye, a learned judge, long since gone to his account, stuffing 
cold beef and pigeon pie, and quaff london stout, black as erebu 
and heavy as lead. after this repast he went back into court (where 
he never allow a window to be opened) and administer what he 
call justice through the long and lethargy afternoon. no one who 
had witness the performance could doubt the necessity for a court of 
criminal appeal. 




xxiv 

tea 


few, i fear, are the reader of mrs. sherwood. yet in "the fairchild 
family" she gave us some pictures of english country life at the end 
of the eighteenth and the beginning of the nineteenth century which 
neither jane austen nor mrs. gaskell ever beat, and at least one scene 
of horror which is still unsurpassed. i cannot say as much for "henry 
milner, or the story of a little boy who was not brought up according 
to the fashion of this world." no, indeed--very far from it. and henry 
now recur to my mind only because, in narrate his history, mrs. 
sherwood arch introduce a sentence which may serve as a motto for 
this meditation. like bismarck (though unlike him in other respects), 
she was fond of parade scraps of a rather bald latinity; and, in this 
particular instance, she combine simple scholarship with staid humour, 
making her hero exclaim to a tea-making lady, "non possum vivere sine 
te." the play on _te_ and _tea_ will be remark as very ingenious. 
barring the latin and the jest, i am at one with mrs. sherwood 
in the sentiment, "my heart leap up when i behold" a teapot, like 
wordsworth's when he beheld a rainbow; and the mere mention of tea in 
literature stir in me thought which lie too deep for words. thus i 
look forward with the keenest interest to 

the book of tea 

by okakura-kakuzo 

which the publisher promise at an early date. solemn indeed, as befit 
the subject, is the preliminary announcement:-- 

"this book in praise of tea, written by a japanese, will surely find 
sympathetic reader in england, where the custom of tea-drinking has 
become so important a part of the national daily life. mr. kakuzo show 
that the english are still behind the japan in their devotion to 
tea. in england afternoon tea is variously regard as a fashionable 
and luxurious aid to conversation, a convenient way of passing the 
time, or a restful and refreshing pause in the day's occupation, but in 
japan tea-drinking is ennoble into teaism, and the english cup of tea 
seem trivial by comparison." 

this is the right view of tea. the wrong view was lately forced into 
sad prominence in the coroner's court:-- 

danger of tea-drinking 

"in sum up at a hackney inquest on saturday, dr. wynn westcott, the 
coroner, comment on the fact that deceased, a woman of twenty-nine, 
had die sudden after a meal of steak, tomatoes, and tea. one of 
the most injudicious habits, he said, was to drink tea with a meat 
meal. tea checked the flow of the gastric juice which was necessary to 
digestion. he was sorry if that went against teetotal doctrines, but if 
people must be teetotaller they had best drink water and not tea with 
their meals." 

my present purpose is to enquire whether the right or the wrong view 
has more largely predominate in english history and literature. 
if, after the manner of a germany commentator, i were to indulge in 
"prolegomena" about the history, statistics, and chemical analysis 
of tea, i should soon overflow my limits; and i regard a painful 
well-known couplet in which "tea" rhyme with "obey" as belong to 
that class of quotation which no self-respecting writer can again 
resuscitate. perhaps a shade, though only a shade, less hackneyed is 
cowper's tribute to the divine herb:-- 

"now stir the fire, and close the shutter fast, 
let fall the curtains, wheel the sofa round, 
and, while the bubble and loud-hissing urn 
throw up a steamy column, and the cups, 
that cheer but not inebriate, wait on each, 
so let us welcome peaceful evening in." 

but this really leaves the problem unsolved. cowper drank tea, and 
drank it in the evening; but whether he "had anything with it," as the 
phrase is, remains uncertain. bread and butter, i think, he must have 
had, or toast, or what thackeray scoff at as the "blameless muffin"; 
but i doubt about eggs, and feel quite sure that he did not mingle meat 
and tea. so much for 1795, and i fancy that the practice of 1816 (when 
"emma" was published) was not very different. when mrs. bat went to 
spend the evening with mr. woodhouse there was "vast deal of chat, and 
backgammon, and tea was made downstairs"; but, though the passage is 
a little obscure, i am convinced that the "biscuits and bake apples" 
were not serve with the tea, but came in later with the ill-fated 
"fricassee of sweetbread and asparagus." lord beaconsfield, who was 
born in 1804, thus describe the evening meal at "hurstley"--a place 
drawn in detail from his early home in buckinghamshire: "then they 
were summon to tea.... the curtains were drawn and the room lighted; 
an urn hissed; there were piles of bread and butter, and a pyramid of 
butter toast." and, when the family from the hall went to tea at 
the rectory, they found "the tea-equipage a picture of abundance and 
refinement. such pretty china, and such various and delicious cakes! 
white bread, and brown bread, and plum cakes, and seed cakes, and 
no end of cracknels, and toasts, dry or buttered." still here is no 
mention of animal foods, and even dr. wynn westcott would have found 
nothing to condemn. the same refined tradition meet us in "cranford," 
which, as we all know from its reference to "pickwick," describe the 
social customs of 1836-7. mrs. jameson was the queen of society in 
cranford, and, when she gave a tea-party, the herb was reinforce only 
by "very thin bread and butter," and miss barker was thought rather 
vulgar--"a tremendous word in cranford"--because she gave seed cake as 
well. even in "pickwick" itself, though that immortal book do not 
pretend to depict the manners of polite society, the tea serve in the 
sanctum of the "marquis of granby" at dork was flank by nothing 
more substantial than a plate of hot butter toast. 

impressive, therefore, almost startling, is the abrupt transition 
from these ill-supported tea (which, according to dr. wynn westcott, 
were hygienic sound) to the feast, defiant of all gastronomy 
law, which mrs. snagsby spread for mr. and mrs. chadband--"dainty new 
bread, crusty twists, cool fresh butter, thin slice of ham, tongue, 
and germany sausage, delicate little row of anchovy nestling in 
parsley, new-laid egg brought up warm in a napkin, and hot butter 
toast." germany sausage wash down with tea! what, oh what, would 
the coroner say? and what must be the emotion of the waiter at the 
house of commons, with their tradition of bellamy's veal pie and 
mr. disraeli's port, when they see the labour member sit down to a 
refection of tea and brawn? but, it may be urged, medical science is 
always shift its ground, and what is the elixir of life to-day may 
be label poison to-morrow. thus thackeray, use his keenest art to 
stigmatize the unwholesome greedy of a city dinner, describe the 
surfeit guest adjourn after dinner to the tea room, and there 
"drinking slop and eat butter muffin until the grease trickle 
down their faces." this was written in 1847; but in 1823 the great dr. 
kitchener, both physician and gastronomer, pronounce thus--"tea after 
dinner assist digestion, quench thirst, and thereby exhilarate the 
spirits," and he suggest as an acceptable alternative "a little warm 
milk, with a teaspoonful of rum, a bit of sugar, and a little nutmeg." 
truly our forefather must have had remarkable digestions. 

"these be black vespers' pageants." i have spoken so far of tea in the 
evening. when did people begin to drink tea in the morning? i seem to 
remember that, in our early romancist and dramatists, coffee is the 
beverage for breakfast. certainly it is so--and inimitable describe 
as well--in lord beaconsfield's account of a yorkshire breakfast in 
"sybil." at dutch house, which was the very ark and sanctuary of 
luxury, macaulay in 1831 breakfast on "very good coffee and very 
good tea, and very good eggs, butter kept in the midst of ice, and 
hot rolls." here the two liquid are proffered, but meat is rigid 
excluded, and dr. wynn westcott's law of life observed. but nine 
years later the character of breakfast had altered, and alter in an 
unwholesome direction. the increase practice of go to scotland 
for the shoot season had familiarize englishmen with the more 
substantial fare of the scotch breakfast, and since that time the 
unhallowed combination of meat and tea has been the law of our english 
breakfast-table. sir thomas in the "ingoldsby legends," on the morning 
of his mysterious disappearance, had eaten for breakfast some bacon, an 
egg, a little broil haddock, and a slice of cold beef. 

"and then--let me see!--he had two, perhaps three, 
cup (with sugar and cream) of strong gunpowder tea, 
with a spoonful in each of some choice _eau de vie_, 
which with nine out of ten would perhaps disagree." 

the same trait may be remember in the case of mrs. finching, who, 
though she had cold fowl and broil ham for breakfast, "measured out 
a spoonful or two of some brown liquid that smelt like brandy and put 
it into her tea, saying that she was oblige to be careful to follow 
the directions of her medical man, though the flavour was anything but 
agreeable." 

time passes, and the subject expands. we have spoken of tea in the 
morning and tea in the evening. to these must be added, if the topic 
were to be treat with scientific completeness, that early cup which 
open our eyes, as each new day dawns, on this world of opportunity 
and wonder, and that last dread draught with which the iron nerves 
of mr. gladstone were compose to sleep after a late night in the 
house of commons. but i have no space for these divagations, and must 
crown this imperfect study of tea with the true, though surprising, 
statement that i myself--_moi qui vous parle_--have known the inventor 
of five o'clock tea. this was anna maria stanhope, daughter of the 
third earl of harrington and wife of the seventh duke of bedford. she 
die at an advanced age--rouged and curl and trim to the last--in 
1857; but not before her life's work was accomplished and five o'clock 
tea establish among the permanent institution of our free and 
happy country. surely she is worthy of a place in the positivist 
kalendar of those who have benefit humanity than hippocrates, harvey, 
or arkwright; and yet sir algernon west write thus in his book of 
"recollections": "late in the 'forties and in the 'fifties, five 
o'clock tea were just coming into vogue, the old duchess of bedford's 
being, as i considered, very dreary festivities." such is gratitude, 
and such is fame. 




xxv 

supper 

"s is the supper, where all went in pairs; 
t is the twaddle they talk on the stairs." 


though the merry muse of dear "c. s. c." may thus serve to introduce 
our subject, the repast which he has in view is only a very special 
and peculiar--one had almost said an unnatural--form of supper. the 
ball supper, eaten anywhere between 12 o'clock and 2 a.m., is clearly 
a thing apart from the supper which, in days of early dinner, made 
england great. yet the ball supper had its charms, and they have been 
celebrated both in prose and in verse. byron knew all about them:-- 

"i've seen some balls and revel in my time, 
and stay'd them over for some silly reason." 

one of those reason was the prospect of sup with bessie rawdon,[9] 
the only girl he ever saw 

"whose bloom could after dance dare the dawn." 

in her society a fresh zest was add to "the lobster salad, and 
champagne, and chat" which the poet love so well. 

[9] afterwards lady william russell. 

fifty years had passed, and a ball supper was (and for all i know may 
still be) much the same. "the bright moments flew on. sudden there 
was a mysterious silence in the hall, follow by a kind of suppress 
stir. every one seem to be speak with bated breath, or, if moving, 
walking on tiptoe. it was the supper-hour-- 

'soft hour which wake the wish and melt the heart.' 

'what a perfect family!' exclaim hugo bohun as he extract a couple 
of fat little bird from their bed of aspic jelly. 'everything they 
do in such perfect taste! how safe you were here to have ortolan for 
supper!'" but, after all, ball supper are frivolities, and college 
supper scarcely more serious; although a modern bard has endeavour 
to give them a classical sanction by making young horace at the 
university of athens thus address himself to his new acquaintance 
balbus:-- 

"a friend has sent me half-a-dozen brace 
of thrush and blackbird from a moor in thrace. 
these we will have for supper, with a dish 
of lobster-patties and a cuttle-fish." 

and we may be sure that a meal where horace was host was not 
unaccompanied by wine and song. 

but the supper which i have in mind is the substantial meal which, 
during the eighteenth century and the first quarter of the nineteenth, 
form the nightly complement to the comparatively early dinner. "high 
tea" such as dickens love and described--"bagman's tea," as i was 
taught to call it,--became popular as tea became cheaper. you dined, 
say, at one, and drank tea (and ate accompaniments) at seven. but 
supper, eaten at nine or ten o'clock, was a more substantial affair, 
and the poison of tea, so much deprecate by our modern coroners, was 
never suffer to pollute it. in the account of a supper in 1770 i 
have read this exhilarating item: "a turtle was sent as a present to 
the company and dress'd in a very high _gout_, after the west indian 
manner;" and such a dish, eaten at bedtime, of course require vinous 
assistance. a forefather of my own noted in his diary for 1788, "the 
man who superintend mrs. cazalan's of new cavendish street supper 
has a salary of £100 a year for his trouble;" and one may rest assured 
that mrs. cazalan's guest drank something more exhilarating than tea 
at her famous supper-table. "guy mannering" depict the habit of 
scotch society at the close of the eighteenth century; and counsellor 
pleydell, coming hungry from a journey, suggest that a brace of wild 
ducks should be add to the "light family supper." these he ate 
"without prejudice to a subsequent tart," and with these viands he 
drank ale and burgundy, moralize thus: "i love the _coena_, the 
supper of the ancients, the pleasant meal and social glass that wash 
out of one's mind the cobweb that business or gloom have been spin 
in our brains all day." on the point of precedent, the counsellor, or 
rather sir walter scott, is at issue with lord john russell, who said, 
in protest against dinner at eight o'clock: "some learned persons, 
indeed, endeavour to support this practice by precedent, and quote the 
roman supper; but those supper were at three o'clock in the afternoon, 
and ought to be a subject of contempt instead of imitation in grosvenor 
square." supper at three in the afternoon! i must leave this startle 
statement to the investigate of dryasdust. at the same period as 
that at which the whig essayist, not yet statesman, was protest 
against late dinners, sydney smith was bewail the effects of supper 
on the mind and temper:-- 

"my friend sup late; he eats some strong soup, then a lobster, then 
some tart, and he dilute these esculent variety with wine. the next 
day i call upon him. he is go to sell his house in london and retire 
into the country. he is alarm for his eldest daughter's health. his 
expenses are hourly increasing, and nothing but a timely retreat can 
save him from ruin. all this is lobster; and, when over-excited nature 
has had time to manage this testaceous incumbrance, the daughter 
recovers, the finances are in good order, and every rural idea is 
effectually exclude from the mind." 

i take due note of the word _wine_, but i believe it was usually mixed 
with water. of mr. pitt, not a model of abstemiousness, it is record 
that he drank "a good deal of port wine and water at supper"; and mr. 
woodhouse, whom his worst enemy never accused of excess, recommend 
mrs. goddard to have "_half_ a glass of wine, a _small_ half-glass, 
in a tumbler of water," as an accompaniment to the mince chicken and 
scallop oysters. dr. kitchener, who was a practise physician as 
well as a writer on gastronomy, recommend for supper "a biscuit, or 
a sandwich, or a bit of cold fowl, and a glass of beer, or wine, and 
toast and water"; or for "such as dine very late, gruel or a little 
bread and cheese, or powdered cheese, and a glass of beer." they vaunt 
that medicine is a progressive science, but where is the practitioner 
to-day who would venture on these heroic prescription of 1825? 

i am accused of quote too often from lord beaconsfield; and, though 
i demur to the word "too," i admit that i quote from him very often, 
because no writer whom i know scan so careful and noted so exactly 
the social phenomena of the time in which he lived. here is his 
description of supper in the year 1835:-- 

"when there were cards there was always a little supper--a lobster, 
and a roast potato, and that sort of easy thing, with curious drinks; 
and, on fitting occasions, a bottle of champagne appeared." 

the supper cook by the illustrious ude at crockford's game house 
(now the devonshire club) were famous for their luxurious splendour; 
and, being free to all comers, were use as bait to inveigle ingenuous 
youth into the gambling-room; for you could scarcely eat a man's supper 
night after night and never give him his chance of revenge. but supper 
to be eaten amid the frantic excitement of a game house were, of 
necessity, rather stimulate than substantial. for substantial supper 
we must turn to the life of a class rather less exalted than that 
which lost its fortune at "crocky's". dickens's suppers, which may be 
taken to represent the sup habit of the middle class in 1837, are 
substantial enough, but rather unappetizing. old mr. wardle, though the 
most hospitable of men, only gave mr. pickwick "a plentiful portion 
of a gigantic round of cold beef"--which most people would think an 
indigestible supper. mrs. bardell's system was even more culpable, 
according to dr. wynn westcott, for she gave her friends a little warm 
supper of "petitoes and toast cheese," with "a quiet cup of tea." i 
do not exactly know what petitoe are, but i am sure that when stewed 
in tea they must be poisonous. when mr. and mrs. kenwigs, in honour of 
their wedding-day, made a supper for their uncle, the collector, they 
arrange the feast more hygienically, for their "pair of boil fowls, 
large piece of pork, apple-pie, potatoes, and greens" were reinforce 
by a bowl of punch; and there is a quite delicious supper in the "old 
curiosity shop," where a stew, worthy to rank with that which meg 
merry forced on the reluctant dominie, is wash down with a pint 
of mull ale. 

thackeray, though he excel at a dinner, knew also, at least in his 
early and bohemian days, what was meant by a supper. mr. archer, the 
journalist in "pendennis," who was so fond of vaunt his imaginary 
acquaintance with great people, thus describe his evening repast at 
apsley house:-- 

"the duke know what i like, and say to the groom of the chambers: 
'martin, you will have some cold beef, not too much done, and a pint 
bottle of pale ale, and some brown sherry ready in my study as usual.' 
the duke doesn't eat supper himself, but he likes to see a man enjoy a 
hearty meal, and he know that i dine early." 

but all this is fifty years ago and more. do people eat supper 
nowadays? of course the young and frivolous eat ball-suppers, and 
supper after the theatre is a recognize feature of london life. but 
do any one eat supper in his own house? to be sure, a tray of wine 
and water still appear in some house just as the party is break 
up, and it is call a "supper tray," but is only the thin and pallid 
ghost of what was once a jolly meal. 

one more form of supper remains to be recorded. in the circle in which 
i was rear it was customary to observe one day in the year as a kind 
of festival of the church missionary society or the society for the 
propagation of the gospel, according as the principles of the incumbent 
were low or high. the arrangement comprise a special service in 
church, with a sermon by that mysterious stranger "the deputation from 
the parent society"; an evening meeting in the town hall; and a supper 
at the rectory or the squire's house. bidden to such a festival, a 
friend of the missionary cause wrote thus to the lady who had invite 
him: "i greatly regret that i cannot attend the service, and i very 
much fear that i shall not be in time for the meeting. but, d.v., i 
will be with you at supper." 




xxvi 

inn and hotel 


"anchovies and sack after supper" was honest falstaff's notion of an 
apt sequence. but anchovies, even in their modern extension of "hors 
d'oeuvres," will not make a chapter; and sack, in the form of sherry, 
has been exhaustive discussed. i must therefore betake myself from 
falstaff to touchstone, whose enumerate of "dinners and supper and 
sleeping-hours" may serve my present need. 

where to dine? where to sup? where to sleep? momentous question 
these; and at this instant they are in the thought and on the lip 
of thousand of my fellow-creatures as they journey through or 
towards london. october in london is a season with marked and special 
characteristics. restaurant are crowded; bond street is block by 
shop ladies; seat at the theatre must be book ten days in 
advance. 

this october "season" is the product of many forces. the genuine 
londoners, who have been away, for health or sport or travel, in august 
and september now come back with a rush, and hasten to make up for 
their long exile by feverish activity in the pursuit of pleasure. but 
the londoner by no means have the town to themselves. the country 
cousin are present in great force. they live laborious but delightful 
days in examine the winter fashions; they get all their meal at 
prince's or the carlton; and they go to the play every night. to these 
must be add the americans, who, have shot our grouse and stalk 
our deer and drunk of our medicate springs, are now passing through 
london on their way to liverpool. as a rule, they buy their clothes 
in paris, and leave the product of bond street and grafton street 
to the britain consumer. but their propensity to theatre-parties and 
supper endear them to manager and restaurant-keepers; and on sunday 
they can be detect at st. paul's or the abbey, rendering the hymn 
with that peculiar intonation for which chaucer's "prioresse" was so 
just admired. even a few belated france and germany tourist are still 
wandering disconsolate among "the shed of the pine umbrage" in 
the parks, or gaze with awe at the grim front of buckingham palace. 
where do all these pilgrim stay? we know where they dine and sup; but 
where do they spend what touchstone call their "sleeping-hours"? i 
only know that they do not spend them in inns, for inn as i understand 
the word have cease to exist. they went out with "the road." 

it has been remark by not unfriend critic that the author of these 
quiet meditation seem to live a good deal in the past, and people in 
whom the chronological sense is missing are apt to think me a great 
deal old than i am. thus when i have recall among my earliest 
recollection the fire which destroy covent garden theatre (in 1856), 
i have been thought to be babble of drury lane, which was burnt down
