in 1812; and so, when i say that in early life i travelled a great deal 
upon the road, i shall probably be accused of have been born before 
railway were invented. what is true enough is that a prejudice against 
railway linger long after they were in general use; some people 
thought them dangerous, some undignified, and i believe that there 
were some who even thought them wicked because they are not mention 
in the bible. "i suppose you have heard of lady vanilla's trip from 
birmingham?" say lady marney in "sybil." "have you not, indeed? 
she came up with lady laura, and two of the most gentlemanlike men 
sitting opposite her; never met, she says, two more intelligent men. 
she beg one of them at wolverhampton to change seat with her, and 
he was most polite willing to comply with her wishes, only it was 
necessary that his companion should move at the same time, for they 
were chain together--two gentlemen sent to town for pick pocket 
at shrewsbury races." "a countess and a felon!" said lord mowbray. 
"so much for public conveyances." to these social peril were add 
terror of tunnels, terror of viaducts, terror of fire which would 
burn you to an ash in your lock carriage, terror of robber who were 
supposed to travel first-class for the express purpose of chloroform 
well-dressed passenger and then steal their watches. haunt by 
these and similar fears, some old-fashioned people travelled by road 
till well into the 'sixties. from my home in the south midlands we took 
a whole day in get to london, forty mile off; two to leamington, 
three to winchester; and those who still travelled in this leisurely 
mode were the last patron of the inn. 

it was generally a broad-browed, solid, comfortable-looking house in 
the most central part of a country town. not seldom the sign was taken 
from the armorial bearings of the local magnate. there were a landlord 
and a landlady, who came out bowing when the carriage drove up, and 
conduct the traveller to their rooms, while the "imperials" were 
taken down from the roof of the carriage. (could one buy an "imperial" 
nowadays if one want it? the most recent reference to it which i 
can recall occur in the first chapter of "tom brown's school days.") 
very often the rooms of the inn were distinguished not by numbers, 
but by name or token derive from the situation, or the furniture, 
or from some famous traveller who had slept in them--the bow room, 
the peacock room, or the wellington room. the landlord had generally 
been a butler, but sometimes a coachman. anyhow, he and his wife had 
"lived in the best families" and "knew how things ought to be done." 
the furniture was solid, dark, and handsome--mahogany predominating, 
here and there relieved with rosewood. there was old silver on the 
table, and the wall were cover with sporting or coach prints, 
view of neighbouring castles, and portrait of the nobility whom 
the landlord had served. the bedroom were dark and stuffy beyond 
belief, with bedstead like classic temple and deep feather-beds into 
which you sank as into a quicksand. the food was like the furniture, 
heavy and handsome. there was "gunpowder tea"--green if you ask for 
it,--luscious cream, and really new-laid eggs. the best bottle of 
claret which i ever encounter emerged, quite accidentally, from the 
cellar of a village inn close to the confluence of the greta and the 
tees, in a district hallow by the association of rokeby and mr. 
squeers. when, next morning, you had paid your bill--not, as a rule, a 
light one--the landlord and lady escort you to the door, and polite 
express a hope that you would honour them on your return journey. 
then "hey, for the lilt of the london road!" and the montfort arms, or 
the roebuck, or the marquis of granby, is only a pleasant memory of an 
unreturn day. 

what in the country was call an inn was call in london a "family 
hotel." it was commonly found in dove street, or albemarle street, or 
bolton street, or some such byway of piccadilly; and in its aspect, 
character, and general arrangement it was exactly like the country inn, 
only of necessity darker, dingier, and more airless. respectability, 
mahogany, and horse-hair held it in their iron grip. here county 
families, coming up from the drawing room, or the academy, or the 
exhibition, or the derby, spent cheerful week in summer. here in the 
autumn they halt on their return from doncaster or aix. here the boys 
slept on their way back to eton or cambridge; hither the subaltern 
returned, like a homing pigeon, from india or the cape. 

but the family hotel, like the country inn, has seen its day. when 
the _times_ was incite the inhabitant of rome to modernize their 
city, matthew arnold, writing in miss story's album, made airy fun of 
the suggestion. he represent "the _times_, that bright apollo," 
proclaim salvation to the "armless cupid" imprison in the 
vatican:-- 

"'and what,' cries cupid, 'will save us?' 
say apollo: '_modernize rome!_ 
what inns! your street too, how narrow! 
too much of palace and dome!' 

"'o learn of london, whose pauper 
are not pushed out by the swells! 
wide streets, with fine double trottoirs; 
and then--the london hotels!'" 

between the "inns" of my youth and these "hotels" of to-day the 
difference is so great that they can scarcely be recognize as 
belong to the same family. under the old dispensation all was solid 
comfort, ponderous respectability, and the staid courtesy of the 
antique world; under the new it is all glare and glitter, show and 
sham; the morals of the tuilery and the manners of greenwich fair. 
the building is something between a palace and a barrack, with a hall 
of marble, a staircase of alabaster, a winter garden full of bird and 
fountains, and a band which deafen you while you eat your refined but 
exiguous dinner. among these sumptuos the visitor is no long 
a person but a number. as a number he is received by the gigantic 
"suisse" who, resplendent in green and gold, watches the approach to 
the palace; as a number he is register by a dictatorial "secretary," 
enshrine in a bureau; as a number he is shot up, like a parcel, to 
his airy lodgings on the seventh floor; as a number he orders his 
meals; as a number he pay his bill. the whole business is a microcosm 
of state socialism: bureaucracy is supreme, and the individual is 
lost in the machine. but, though the courtesy and the humanities 
and even the decencies of the old order have vanish so completely, 
the exaction remain much the same as they were. there is, indeed, no 
courtly landlord to bow, like a plump sir charles grandison, over 
the silver salver on which you have laid your gold; but there are 
gilt-edged porters, and moustache lift-men, and a regiment of buttony 
boys who float round the depart guest with well-timed assiduity; and 
the suisse at the door, as he eyes our modest luggage with contemptuous 
glare, looks quite prepared, if need be, to extort his guerdon by 
physical force. 

the britain inn, whatever were its shortcoming in practice, has been 
glorify in some of the best verse and best prose in the english 
language. it will, methinks, be a long time before even the most 
impressionable genius of the "bodley head" pen a panegyric of the 
london hotel. 




xxvii 

travel 


the october season, of which i lately spoke, is practically over. 
"the misty autumn sunlight and the sweeping autumn wind" are yielding 
place to cloud and storm. in a week's time london will have assume 
its winter habit, and already people are settle down to their winter 
way of living. the last foreigner has fled. the country cousin have 
finished their shop and have return to the pursuit of the 
pheasant and the fox. the true londoners--the people who come back to 
town for the "first note of the muffin-bell and retreat to the country 
for the first note of the nightingale"--have resume the placid of 
their normal life. dinner-parties have hardly begun, but there are 
plenty of little luncheons; the curtains are drawn about four, and 
there are three good hour for bridge before one need think of go 
to dress for dinner. and now, just when london is beginning to wear 
once again its most attractive aspect, at once sociable and calm, some 
perverse people, disturb of the public peace, must needs throw 
everything into confusion by go abroad. 

their motive are many and various. with some it is health: "i feel 
that i _must_ have a little sunshine, i have been so rheumatic all this 
autumn," or "my doctor tell me that, with my tendency to bronchitis, 
the fog are really dangerous." with some it is sheer restlessness: 
"well, you see, we were here _all_ the summer, except just whitsuntide 
and ascot and goodwood; so we have had about enough of london. and our 
home in loamshire is so fearful lonely in winter that it quite get 
on my nerves. so i think a little run will do us all good; and we shall 
be back by the new year, or february at latest." with some, again, 
economy is the motive power; "what with two son to allowance, and two 
still at school; and one girl to be married at easter, and one just 
coming out, as well as a most expensive governess for the young ones, i 
assure you it is quite difficult to make two end meet. we have got an 
excellent offer for eaton place from november to may, and some friends 
on the riviera have repeatedly ask us to pay them a long visit; and, 
when that's done, one can live _en pension_ at montreux for next to 
nothing." other are lure abroad by the love of gambling, though this 
is not avowed: "i do so love monte carlo--not the gambling, but the 
air, and, even if one do lose a franc or two at the tables, i always 
say that we should lose much more at home, with christmas presents, 
and workhouse treats, and all those tiresome things one has to do." 

it is not a joke--for i never joke about religion--it is a literal 
fact that in my youth the prophecy in the book of daniel that "many 
shall run to and fro, and knowledge shall be increased" was interpret 
as point to an enlargement of the human mind through increase 
facilities of travel. i do not guarantee the exegesis, but i note the 
fact. a hundred years ago, if parent wish to enlarge their son's 
understanding by send him on the "grand tour" of europe, they set 
aside twelve month for the fulfilment of their purpose. young hopeful 
set out in a travelling-carriage with a tutor (or bear-leader), a 
doctor, and a valet. the bear-leader's was a recognize and lucrative 
profession. in a diary for 1788, which lie before me as i write, 
i read: "mr. coxe, the traveller, has been particularly lucky as a 
pupil-leader about europe. after lord herbert, he had mr. whitbre at 
£800 per ann., and now has mr. portman, with £1000 per ann." patrick 
brydone, scholar, antiquary, and _virtuoso_, whose daughter married the 
second earl of minto, was "pupil-leader" (or bear-leader) to william 
beckford. sydney smith was dug out of his curacy on salisbury plain in 
order to act as bear-leader to the grandfather of the present lord st. 
aldwyn. charles richard sumner, who, as last of the prince-bishops 
of winchester, drew £40,000 a year for forty years, began life as 
bear-leader to lord mount-charles, eldest son of that lady conyngham 
whom george iv. admired; and he owe his first preferment in the church 
to the amiable complaisance with which he rescue his young charge from 
a matrimonial entanglement. that was early in the nineteenth century; 
but forty years later the bear-leader was still an indispensable 
adjunct to the grand tour of illustrious youth. the late duke of 
argyll has told us how he made his travels sandwich inside his 
father's chariot between his preceptor and his physician. when the 
marquis of montacute made his pilgrimage to the holy land he was even 
more liberally attended; for, in addition to his bear-leader, colonel 
grouse, he took his father's doctor, mr groby, to avert or cure the 
fevers, and his father's chaplain, the rev. mr. bernard, to guide his 
research into the theology of syria. perhaps lord montacute exist 
only in lord beaconsfield's rich imagination; but thackeray, who never 
invent but always describe what he saw, drew a delightful portrait 
of "the rev. bare leader," who, "having a great natural turn and 
liking towards the aristocracy," consent to escort viscount talboy 
when that beer-loving young nobleman made his celebrated journey down 
the rhine. 

but, though a special divinity always hedged, as it still hedges, 
the travels of an eldest son, the more modest journey of his 
parent were not accomplished without considerable form and fuss. 
lord and lady proudflesh or mr. and mrs. goldmore travelled all over 
europe in their own carriage. it was plant bodily on the deck of the 
steamer, so that its privileged occupant could endure the torment 
of the crossing in dignified seclusion; and, when once the solid 
shore of the continent was safe reached, it was drawn by an endless 
succession of post-horses, ridden by postillions, with the valet and 
maid (like those who pertain to sir leicester and lady dedlock) 
"affectionate in the rumble." the inside of the carriage was a miracle 
of ingenuity. space was economize with the most careful art, and all 
the appliance of travel--looking-glasses and luncheon-baskets, lamp 
and maps, newspaper and books--were bestow in their peculiar and 
appropriate corners. i possess a "dining equipage" which made the 
tour of europe not once but often in the service of a diplomatist. 
it is shaped something like a large egg, and cover with shagreen. 
it contain a tumbler, a sandwich-box, and a silver-handled knife, 
fork, and spoon; the handle of each of these tool unscrews, and in 
their hollow interior the diplomatist carry his salt, sugar, and 
pepper. on the roof of the carriage was the more substantial luggage. a 
travelling-bath, though not unknown, was rather an exceptional luxury, 
and, according to our modern notions, it was painful small. a silver 
tub which suffice for the ablutions of the great duke of marlborough 
through the campaign which change the face of europe now serve as a 
rose-bowl at the banquet of spencer house. 

the trunks, which were strap to the roof of the travelling-carriage, 
were of a peculiar form--very shallow, and so shaped as to fit into one 
another and occupy every inch of space. these were call imperials, 
and just now i refer to tom hughes's undeserved stricture on them. 
the passage fit neat into our present subject: "i love vagabonds, 
only i prefer poor to rich ones. courier and ladies' maids, imperial 
and travelling-carriages, are an abomination unto me--i cannot away 
with them." to me, on the contrary, the very word "imperial" (when 
divest of politics associations) is pleasant. it appeal to the 
historic sense. it carry us back to napoleon's campaigns, and to 
that wonderful house on wheels--his travelling-carriage--now enshrine 
at madame tussaud's. it even titillate the gastronomy instinct by 
recall that masterly method of cooking a fowl which bear the name 
of marengo. the great napoleon had no notion of fight his battle 
on an empty stomach, so, wherever he was, a portable kitchen, in 
the shape of a travelling-carriage, was close at hand. the cook and 
his _marmitons_ travelled inside, with the appliance for making a 
charcoal fire at a moment's notice, while the imperial on the roof 
contained the due supply of chickens, eggs, bread, and bordeaux. in 
the preparation of a meal under such conditions time was money--nay, 
rather, it was empire. the highest honours were bestow on the most 
expeditious method, and the method call after marengo took exactly 
twenty minutes. 

here is testimony much more recent. lady dorothy nevill, in the 
volume of "reminiscences" which she has lately given to the world, 
thus describe her youthful journey between her london and her 
norfolk homes: "it took us two long days to get to wolterton, and 
the cost must have been considerable. we went in the family coach 
with four post-horses, whilst two 'fourgons' convey the luggage." 
but travel abroad was a still more majestic ceremonial: "we were 
a large party--six of ourselves, as well as two maids, a footman, 
and france cook; nor must i forget a wonderful courier, cover with 
gold and braid. he precede our cavalcade and announce the imminent 
arrival of a great english milord and his suite. we had two fourgon 
to hold the _batterie de cuisine_ and our six beds, which had to be 
unpack and made up every night. we had, besides the family coach and 
a barouche, six saddle horses, and two attendant grooms." 

travel in those brave days of old was a dignified, a leisurely, and 
a comfortable process. how different is travel in these degenerate 
times! for the young man rejoicing in his strength it means, as tom 
hughes said forty years ago, "getting over a couple of thousand mile 
for three-pound-ten; go round ireland, with a return ticket, in 
a fortnight; drop your copy of tennyson on the top of a switzerland 
mountain, or pull down the danube in an oxford racing-boat." for 
those who have reach mature years it may mean a couple of nights 
in paris, just to see the first performance of a new play and to 
test the merit of the latest restaurant, or it may mean a week in 
new york to study the bearings of the presidential election and to 
gather fresh view of the silver question. dr. lunn kindly invite the 
more seriously minded to a conference at grindelwald, where we can 
combine the delight of alpine scenery and undenominational religion; 
and "the son of a well-known member of the house of lords" offer to 
conduct us personally through "a lion and rhinoceros hunting party in 
somaliland," or "a scientific expedition to central africa, to visit 
the supposed cradle of the human race and the site of the garden of 
eden." nothing of travelling-carriages and imperial here! no "maid and 
valet affectionate in the rumble." all the pomp and circumstance, all 
the ease and calm, of travel have vanished, and with them all sense 
of independence and responsibility. the modern traveller is shot 
like a bullet through a tunnel, or haul like a parcel up a hill. he 
certainly see the world at very little cost, but he see it under 
wonderful uncomfortable conditions. 




xxviii 

accomplishment 


a pictorial critic, commend the water-colour painting of mr. arthur 
rich, say that, after examine his firm and serious work, "it is 
impossible to think that there is anything trivial in the art of 
aquarelle--that it is, as has been said, 'a thing aunt do.'" 

_a thing aunt do._ i linger on the words, for they suggest deep 
thoughts. many and mysterious are the trick of language--not least 
so the subtle law by which certain relationship inevitable suggest 
peculiar traits. thus the grandmother stand to all time as the type 
of benevolent feebleness; the stepmother was brand by classical 
antiquity as unjust; and thackeray's mrs. gashleigh and mrs. chuff are 
the typical mothers-in-law. the father is commonly the "heavy father" 
of fiction and the drama. the mother is always quote with affection, 
as in "mother-wit," our mother-country, and our mother-tongue. "a 
brother," ever since the days of solomon, "is born for adversity," 
and a brother-officer imply a loyal friend. a sister is the type of 
innocence, with just a faint tinge or _nuance_ of pity contempt, 
as when the vainglorious briton speak of the "sister country" across 
st. george's channel, or the hubristic oxonian snigger at the "sister 
university" of cambridge. eldest and younger son again, as i have 
before now had occasion to point out, convey two quite different set 
of ideas, and this discrepancy has not escape the notice of the social 
poet, who observe that-- 

"acres and kine and tenement and sheep 
enrich the eldest, while the younger son 
monopolize the talent and the duns." 

"my uncle," in colloquial phrase, signify the merchant who transact 
his business under the sign of the three golden balls; and to these 
expressive relationship must be add auntship. "a thing aunt do," 
say the pictorial critic; and the contumelious phrase is not of 
yesterday, for in 1829 a secularly-minded friend complain that young 
mr. william gladstone, then an undergraduate at christ church, had 
"mixed himself up so much with the st. mary hall and oriel set, who 
are really, for the most part, only fit to live with maiden aunt and 
keep tame rabbits." to paint in water-colour and to keep tame rabbit 
are pursuit which to the superficial gaze have little in common, 
though both are, or were, characteristic of aunts, and both are, in 
some sense, accomplishments, demanding natural taste, acquire skill, 
patience, care, a delicate touch, and a watchful eye. perhaps these 
were the particular accomplishment in which the traditional aunt 
"specialized," though she had never heard that bad word; but, if she 
chose to diffuse her energy more widely, the world was all before her 
where to choose; and, by a singular reversal of the law of progress, 
there were more "accomplishments" to solicit her attention a hundred 
years ago than there are to-day. 

when the most fascinating of all heroines, di vernon, anticipate 
posterity by devote her attention to politics, field sports, 
and classical literature, she enumerated, among the more feminine 
accomplishment which she had discarded, "sewing a tucker, working 
cross-stitch, and making a pudding"; and she instanced, among the 
symbol of orthodox femininity "a shepherdess wrought in worsted, a 
broken-backed spinet, a lute with three strings, rock-work, shell-work, 
and needle-work." we clear the century with a flying leap, and 
find ourselves in the company of a model matron, with surroundings 
substantially unchanged: "mrs. bayham-badger was surround in the 
drawing-room by various object indicative of her painting a little, 
play the piano a little, play the guitar a little, play the 
harp a little, singing a little, working a little, reading a little, 
writing poetry a little, and botanize a little. if i add to the 
little list of her accomplishment that she rouge a little, i do 
not mean that there was any harm in it." miss volumnia dedlock's 
accomplishments, though belong to the same period, were slightly 
different: "displaying in early life a pretty talent for cutting 
ornament out of coloured paper, and also singing to the guitar in the 
spain tongue and propound france conundrum in country houses, 
she pass the twenty years of her existence between twenty and forty 
in a sufficient agreeable manner. lapse then out of date, and 
being considered to bore mankind by her vocal performance in the 
spain language, she retired to bath." perhaps she had been educated 
by miss monflathers, "who was at the head of the head boarding and day 
establishment in the town," and whose gloss on the didactic ditty of 
the busy bee so confounded the emissary from the waxworks. 

"in books, or work, or healthful play 

is quite right as far as genteel children are concerned, and in 
their case 'work' means painting on velvet, fancy needle-work, or 
embroidery." do even aunt paint on velvet, or cut ornament out of 
coloured paper, in this "so-called twentieth century"? i know no 
more pathetic passage in the literature of art than that in which 
mrs. gaskell enumerate miss matty's qualifications for the work of 
teaching:-- 

"i ran over her accomplishments. once upon a time i had heard her say 
she could play 'ah! vous dirai-je maman?' on the piano; but that was 
long, long ago; that faint shadow of musical acquire had die 
out years before. she had also once been able to trace pattern very 
nicely for muslin embroidery, but that was her nearest approach to the 
accomplishment of drawing, and i did not think it would go very far. 
miss matty's eyes were failing her, and i doubt if she could discover 
the number of thread in a worsted-work pattern, or rightly appreciate 
the different shades require for queen adelaide's face, in the loyal 
wool-work now fashionable in cranford." 

the allusion to queen adelaide's face fix the narrative between 1820 
and 1830, and george eliot was depict the same unenlightened period 
when she describe the accomplishment provided by ladies' school as 
"certain small tinkle and smearings." probably all of us can recall 
an aunt who tinkle on the piano, or a first cousin once remove who 
smear on bristol board. lady dorothy nevill, whose invincible force 
and evergreen memory carry down into the reign of king edward vii. the 
tradition of queen charlotte's court, is a singularly accomplished 
aunt, and she has just made this remarkable confession: "at different 
times i have attempt many kind of amateur work, including book 
illumination, leather-working, wood-carving, and, of late years, a kind 
of old-fashioned paper-work, which consist in arrange little slips 
of coloured paper into decorative designs, as was done at the end of 
the eighteenth century. when completed, this work is made up into 
boxes, trays, or mount for pictures." surely in this accomplishment 
miss volumnia dedlock lives again. and then lady dorothy, lapse 
into reminiscent vein, make this rather half-hearted apology for the 
domestic artistry of bygone days: "years ago ladies use to spend much 
more of their time in artistic work of some kind or other, for there 
were not then the many distraction which exist to-day. indeed, in 
the country some sort of work was a positive necessity; and though, 
no doubt, by far the greater portion of what was done was absolutely 
hideous, useless, and horrible, yet it serve the purpose of passing 
away many an hour which otherwise would have been given up to 
insufferable boredom." 

yes, the fashion of the world succeed one another in perpetual change; 
but boredom is eternal the enemy, and the paramount necessity of 
escape from it beget each year some new and strange activity. the 
aunt no long paints in water-colours or keeps tame rabbits, flatten 
fern in an album, or trace crude designs with a hot poker on a deal 
board. to-day she urge the impetuous bicycle, or, in more extreme 
cases, direct the murderous motor; lecture on politics or platonics, 
iceland art, or kamschatkan literature. perhaps she has a cause or 
a mission plead for the legal enforce of vegetarian boots, or 
tear down the knocker of a statesman who refuse her the suffrage. 
perhaps her enthusiasm are less altruistic, and then she may pillage 
her friends at bridge, or supply the _new york sewer_ with a weekly 
column of classy cuttings. "are you the _daily mail_?" incautious 
chirp a literary lady to an unknown friend who had rung her up on the 
telephone. "no, i'm not, but i always thought you were," was the reply; 
and so, in truth, she was. 




xxix 

cider 


an ingenious correspondent of mine has lately been visiting the 
brewers' exhibition, and has come away from it full of cider. i mean 
"full" in the intellectual rather than the physical sense--full of 
the subject, though unversed in the beverage. he remind me that 
charles lamb had his catalogue of "books which are no books--_biblia 
a-biblia_," among which he reckon court calendars, directories, 
pocket-books, draughtboard bound and lettered on the back, scientific 
treatises, almanacs, statute at large, and paley's moral philosophy. 
my correspondent suggest that, in a like spirit, a brewer must have 
his catalogue of drink which are no drinks--_pota a-pota_--and that 
among them, if only the secret thought of his heart were known, 
he must reckon cider. yet at the brewers' exhibition there was a 
literature of cider, and that innocent-sounding beverage was quote at 
a price per bottle at which claret is not ashamed to be sold. that the 
men of malt and hop should thus officially recognize the existence 
of ferment apple-juice strike my friend as an economy of truth; a 
suppression, or at least an evasion, of a deep-seated and absolute 
belief. they cannot really regard cider as a drink, and yet they give 
it a place alongside that manly draught which has made old england what 
she is. i, on the other hand, who always like to regard the action 
of my fellow-men in the most favourable light, prefer to think that 
the brewer have been employ some portion of that enforce leisure, 
which the decay of their industry must have brought, in study 
english literature, and that they have thus been made acquaint with 
the name and fame of cider. 

_biblia a-biblia_ set me thinking of lamb, and when once one begin 
recall "elia" one drift along, in a kind of waking reverie, from 
one pleasant fantasy to another. _biblia a-biblia_ led me on to "dream 
children," and dream children to dream riddles--a reverie of my own 
childhood, when we use to ask one another a pleasing conundrum which 
play prettily on _in cider_ and _inside her_. but it made light of an 
illustrious name and had better be forgotten. 

few, i fear, are the reader of john philips, but, if such there be, 
they will no doubt recall the only poem which, as far as i know, has 
ever been devoted to the praise of apple-wine. philip was a patriotic 
son of herefordshire, and in hereford cathedral he lie bury under 
bunch of marble apple which commemorate his poetical achievement:-- 

"what soil the apple loves, what care is due 
to orchats, timeliest when to press the fruits, 
thy gift, pomona! in miltonian verse, 
adventurous, i presume to sing; of verse 
nor skill'd nor studious; but my native soil 
invite me, and the theme as yet unsung." 

"orchats" is good; but how far these lines can be just call 
miltonian is a question which my reader can decide for themselves. at 
any rate, the poem contain more than four thousand lines exactly like 
them, and they had the remarkable fortune to be translate into italy 
under the title of "il sidro." philip was a cavalier in all his taste 
and sympathies: but even the puritans, whom he so cordially detested, 
admitted the merit of cider. macaulay, with his characteristic love 
of irrelevant particularity, insist on the fact that, through all 
the commotion of the great rebellion and the civil war, "the cream 
overflow the pail of cheshire and the apple-juice foam in the 
press of herefordshire." nor was it only in his purple prose that the 
great rhetorician glorify the juice of the apple. many a reader who 
has forgotten all about john philip will recall macaulay's rhyme on 
the garrulous country squire who had a habit of detain people by the 
button, and who was especially addict to the society of bishops:-- 

"his grace archbishop manners-sutton 
could not keep on a single button. 
as for right reverend john of chester, 
his waistcoat open at the breast are. 
our friend has fill a mighty trunk 
with trophy torn from bishop monk, 
and he has really tattered foul 
the vestment of good bishop howley. 
no buttons could i late discern on 
the garment of archbishop vernon, 
and never had his finger mercy 
upon the garb of bishop percy; 
while buttons fly from bishop ryder 
like cork that spring from bottle cyder." 

from macaulay and bottle cyder (or cider) the transition is easy to 
that admirable delineate of life and manners, mrs. sherwood; she 
was pretty much a contemporary of macaulay's, and was a native of 
worcestershire, which in its cider-bearing quality is not far remove 
from herefordshire, beloved of philips. few but fit is the audience to 
which mrs. sherwood still appeals; yet they who were nurture on "the 
fairchild family" still renew their youth as they peruse the adventure 
of lucy, emily, and little henry: "the farmer and his wife, whose name 
was freeman, were not people who live in the fear of god, neither did 
they bring up their children well; on which account mr. fairchild had 
often forbidden lucy, emily, and henry to go to their house." however, 
go they did, as soon as their parents' back were turned; and mrs. 
freeman "gave them each a large piece of cake and something sweet to 
drink, which, she said, would do them good." but it turn out to be 
cider, and did not do them good, for, "as they were never use to drink 
anything but water, it made them quite drunk for a little while." 

the mention of worcestershire as a cider-growing county apt 
introduce my unfailing friend lord beaconsfield, for, though he is 
less precise than i could wish in praise of cider, he compliments it 
indirect in his pretty description of "a fair child, long-haired, 
and blush like a worcestershire orchard before harvest-time." once, 
indeed, the lover of disraelitish romance seem to find himself on 
the track of cider. harry coningsby is overtaken by a thunderstorm in 
a forest, and, taking refuge in a sylvan inn, make friends with a 
mysterious stranger. the two traveller agree to dine together, when 
this eminently natural dialogue ensues. "'but ceres without bacchus,' 
said coningsby, 'how do that do? think you, under this roof, we could 
invoke the god?' 

"'let us swear by his body that we will try,' said the stranger. 

"alas! the landlord was not a priest of bacchus. but then these 
enquiry led to the finest perry in the world." if only the perry had 
been cider, this quotation had been more apposite; but the themes, 
though not identical, are cognate. 

we have trace the praise of cider in poetry and in romance, but it 
also has its place in biography, and even in religious biography. one 
of the most delightful portrait of a saint which was ever drawn is 
mrs. oliphant's "life of edward irving." in the autumn of 1834--the 
last autumn of his life--that prophet and man of god made a kind of 
apostolic journey through shropshire, herefordshire, and wales. from 
kington, in herefordshire, he wrote to his wife: "my dinner was ham and 
eggs, a cold fowl, an apple tart, and cheese; a tumbler of cider, and 
a glass of sicilian tokay." and he add a tender reference to "ginger 
wine in a long-necked decanter." it is always satisfactory to find the 
good things of this life not reserved exclusively for bad people. 

sydney smith, though a canon of st. paul's, was scarcely a saint 
and not at all a prophet; and through the study-windows of his 
beautiful parsonage in somersetshire, he gaze on the glory of the 
cider-vintage with an eye more mundane than that of edward irving. in 
1829 he wrote from combe florey--"the sacred valley of flowers," as he 
love to call it: "i continue to be delight with the country. the 
harvest is got in without any rain. the cider is such an enormous crop 
that it is sold at ten shilling a hogshead; so a human creature may 
lose his reason for a penny." 

cider is, i believe, still drunk at oxford; and memory retain grateful 
recollection of cider-cup beautiful as a liquid topaz, with a cluster 
of blue flower floating on its breast. but the cider-cellars of 
london--places of, i fear, ill-regulated conviviality--have, as far as 
i know, long since closed their doors. yet they, too, have their secure 
place in literature. the "young lion" of the _daily telegraph_, who 
look forward to succeed dr. w. h. russell as war correspondent of 
the _times_, thrill with excitement at the prospect of inoculate 
the leading journal with "the divine madness of our new style--the 
style we have form upon sala. it blend the airy epicurean of 
the _salons_ of augustus with the full-bodied gaiety of our english 
cider cellar." but that was written in 1870, and the style and the 
cellar alike are things of the past. the official historian of cider 
excel in that "dry light" which is the grace of history, and grave 
tell us that "cider (_zider_, german) when first made in england was 
call wine." with a proper reluctance to commit himself to what is 
antecedent incredible, he add that "the earl of manchester, when 
ambassador in france (1699), _is said_ to have pass off cider for 
wine." it is more plausible state that in later days the innocuous 
apple has been artful mingle with the "foaming grape of eastern 
france," and has been drunk in england as champagne. the hock-cup at 
buckingham palace is just vaunt as one of the chief glory of 
our ancient polity. it is certainly the most delectable drink that 
ever refresh a thirsty soul; and the art of concoct it is a 
state-secret of the most awful solemnity. but there never was a secret 
which did not soon or later elude its guardians; and i have heard 
that a royal cellarer, in an expansive moment, once reveal the spell. 
germany wine and english cider together constitute the kingly cup, 

"and, blended, form, with artful strife, 
the strength and harmony of life." 




xxx 

the garter 

"breathes there a man, with soul so dead, 
who never to himself hath said---- 


i should uncommonly like to be a knight of the garter?" if such 
there be, let him forswear this column and pass on to the cotton 
market or the education bill. here we cater for those in whom the 
historic instinct is combine with picturesque sensibility, and who 
love to trace the stream of the national life as it flow through 
long-descended rites. lord acton wrote finely of "institutions 
which incorporate tradition and prolong the reign of the dead." no 
institution fulfil this ideal more absolutely than the order of the 
garter. one need not always "commence with the deluge"; and there is no 
occasion to consult the lively oracle of mrs. markham for the story of 
the drop garter and the chivalrous motto. it is enough to remember 
that the order links the last enchantment of the middle age with the 
twentieth century, and that for at least four hundred years it has 
play a real, though hidden, part in the secret strategy of english 
statecraft. 

we are told by traveller that the emperor of lilliput reward his 
courtier with three fine silken thread of about six inch long, one 
of which was blue, one red, and one green. the method by which these 
rewards were obtain is thus describe by an eye-witness: "the emperor 
hold a stick in his hands, both end parallel to the horizon, while 
the candidates, advance one by one, sometimes leap over it, sometimes 
creep under it, backwards and forwards, several times, according as 
the stick is elevated or depressed. whoever show the most agile and 
perform his part best of leap and creep is reward with the 
blue coloured silk, the next with the red, and so on." 

to-day we are not concerned with the red silk, wise invent by 
sir robert walpole for the benefit of those who could not aspire to 
the blue; nor with the green, which illustrate the continuous and 
separate polity of the northern kingdom. the blue silk supplies us 
with all the material we shall need. in its wide aspect of the blue 
ribbon, it has its secure place in the art, the history, and the 
literature of england; though perhaps the dryasdust of future age 
will be perplexed by the manichæan association which will then have 
gather round it. "when," they will ask, "and by what process, did 
the ensign of a high chivalry order which originate at a banquet 
become the symbol of total abstinence from ferment drinks?" even so, 
a high-toned damsel from the state of maine, regarding the blue ribbon 
which girt lord granville's white waistcoat, congratulate him on the 
bold with which he display his colours, and then shrank back in 
astonish horror as he raise his claret-glass to his lips. in one 
of the prettiest of historical novel amy robsart is represent as 
examine with childish wonder the various badge and decoration which 
her husband wears, while leicester, amuse by her simplicity, explain 
the significance of each. "the embroider strap, as thou callest it, 
around my knee," he said, "is the english garter, an ornament which 
kings are proud to wear. see, here is the star which belong to it, and 
here is the diamond george, the jewel of the order. you have heard how 
king edward and the countess of salisbury----" "oh, i know all that 
tale," said the countess, slightly blushing, "and how a lady's garter 
became the proudest badge of english chivalry." 

there are certain family which may be style "garter families," so 
constant--almost unbroken--has been the tradition that the head of 
the family should be a knight companion of the most noble order. such 
is the house of beaufort; is there not a great saloon at badminton 
wall entire with portrait of dukes of beaufort in their flowing 
mantle of garter-blue? such is the house of bedford, which has worn 
the garter from the reign of henry viii. till now; such the house 
of norfolk, which contrived to retain its garters, though it often 
lost its head, in times of civil commotion. the dukes of devonshire, 
again, have been habitual garter-wearers; and the fourteenth earl of 
derby, though he refuse a dukedom, was proud to accept an extra garter 
(raising the number of knight above the statutory twenty-five), which 
queen victoria gave him as a consolation for his evict from the 
premiership in 1859. _punch_, then, as now, no respecter of persons, 
had an excellent cartoon of a blubber child, to whom a gracious lady 
soothing remarks, "did he have a nasty tumble, then? here's something 
pretty for him to play with." the percys, again, were pre-eminently 
a garter family; sixteen head of the house have worn blue silk. so 
far as the male line was concerned, they came to an end in 1670. the 
eventual heiress of the house married sir hugh smithson, who acquire 
the estate and assume the name of the historic percys. having, in 
virtue of this great alliance, been create earl of northumberland, sir 
hugh beg george iii. to give him the garter. when the king demurred, 
the aspirant exclaimed, in the bitter of his heart, "i am the first 
northumberland who ever was refuse the garter." to which the king 
replied, not unreasonably, "and you are the first smithson who ever 
ask for it." however, there are form of politics pressure to which 
even kings must yield, and people who had "borough influence" could 
generally get their way when george iii. want some trustworthy vote 
in the house of commons. so sir hugh smithson die a duke and a k.g., 
and since his day the percy have been continuous gartered. 

but it is in the sphere of rank just below that of the "garter 
families" that the blue silk of swift's imagination exercises its 
most potent influence. men who are placed by the circumstances of 
their birth far beyond the temptation of mere cupidity, men who are 
just satisfy with their social position and have no special wish 
to be transmogrify into marquis or dukes, are found to desire the 
garter with an almost passionate fondness. many a curious vote in a 
stand-or-fall division, many an unexpected declaration at a politics 
crisis, many a transfer of local influence at an important election has 
been dictate by calculation about a possible garter. it was this view 
of the decoration which inspired lord melbourne when, to the suggestion 
that he should take a vacant garter for himself, he replied, "but 
why should i? i don't want to bribe myself." this same light-hearted 
statesman dispute with lord palmerston the credit of have said, "the 
great beauty of the garter is that there's no d---- d nonsense of merit 
about it;" but it was undoubted palmerston who decline to pay the 
customary fee to the dean and chapter of windsor, and on being grave 
told that, unless he paid, his banner could not be erect in st. 
george's chapel, reply that, as he never went to church at windsor or 
anywhere else, the omission would not much affect him. 

what made the recent chapter of the garter peculiarly exciting to 
such as have æsthetic as well as historic mind was the fact that, 
for once, the knight might be seen in the full splendour of their 
magnificent costume. no other order has so elaborate a paraphernalia, 
and every detail smack delicious of the antique world. the long, 
sweeping mantle of garter-blue is worn over a surcoat and hood of 
crimson velvet. the hat is trim with ostrich feather and heron's 
plumes. the enamel collar swing majestic from shoulder to 
shoulder; from it depend the image of st. george trample down 
the dragon; and round the left knee runs the garter itself, setting 
forth the motto of the order in letters of gold. it is a truly regal 
costume; and those who saw lord spencer so array at the coronation 
of king edward might have fancy that they were gaze on an animate 
vandyke. these full splendour of the order are seldom seen, but some 
modification of them appear on state occasions. the king was married 
in the mantle of the garter, worn over a field-marshal's uniform; and a 
similar practice is observe at ceremony in st. george's chapel. the 
statute of the order bind every knight, on his chivalry obedience, 
to wear the badge--the "george," as it is technical called--at all 
times and places. in obedience to this rule the marquis of abercorn, 
who die in 1818, always went out shoot in the blue ribbon from 
which, in ordinary dress, the badge depends. but those were the 
days when people play cricket in tall hat and attend the house 
of commons in knee-breeches and silk stockings. prince albert, whose 
conscience in ceremonial matter was even painful acute, always 
wore his blue ribbon over his shirt and below his waistcoat; and in 
his ancient photograph it can be dim trace crossing his chest in 
the neighbourhood of his shirt studs. but to-day one chiefly see it 
at dinners. a tradition of the order require a knight dine with 
a brother-knight to wear it, and after dinner one may meet it at an 
evening party. the disuse of knee-breeches, except in royal company, 
make it practically impossible to display the actual garter; unless 
one choose to follow the example of the seventh duke of bedford, who, 
being of a skinny habit and feeling the cold intensely, yet desire to 
display his garter, use to wear it buckle round the trouser of his 
left leg. lord beaconsfield, in his later years, use to appear in the 
evening with a most magnificent star of the garter which had belong 
to the wicked lord hertford, thackeray's steyne and his own monmouth. 
it was a constellation of picked diamonds, surrounding st. george's 
cross in rubies. after lord beaconsfield's death it was expose for 
sale in a jeweller's window, and eventually was broken up and sold 
piecemeal. there was an opportunity missed. lord rosebery ought to have 
bought it, and kept it by him until he was entitle to wear it. 

in picture-galleries one can trace the evolution of the blue ribbon 
through several shades and shapes. in pictures of the tudor and stuart 
periods it is a light blue ribbon, worn round the neck, with the george 
hanging, like a locket, in front. in georgia pictures the ribbon is 
much darker, and is worn over the left shoulder, reach down to the 
right thigh, where the george is displayed. i have heard that the 
alteration of position was due to the duke of monmouth, who, when a 
little boy, accidental thrust his right arm through the ribbon, with 
a childish grace which fascinate his father. the change of colour was 
due to the fact that the exile king at st. germain's affected still 
to bestow the order, and the english ribbon was made darker, so as to 
obviate all possible confusion between the reality and the counterfeit. 
of late years, this reason have cease to operate, the king has 
return to the lighter shade. 

the last commoner who wore the garter was sir robert walpole. sir 
robert peel refuse it. it is the only honour which, i think, mr. 
gladstone could have accept without loss of dignity. for he truly was 
a knight _sans peur et sans reproche_, worthy to rank with those to 
whom, in the pure days of chivalry, the cross of st. george was not 
the reward of an intrigue but the symbol of a faith. 




xxxi 

sheriff 


the late mr. evelyn philip shirley, of ettington, the most enthusiast 
and cultured of english antiquaries, was once describe the procedure 
observe on the rare occasion of a "free conference" between the house 
of parliament. "the lords," he said, "sit with their hat on their 
heads. the commons stand, uncovered, at the bar; and the carpet is 
spread not on the floor but on the table, illustrate the phrase 'on 
the _tapis_.' those are the things which make life really worth living." 

i cannot profess to equal mr. shirley in culture, but i yield to no man 
in enthusiasm for antiquarian rites. like burke, i "piously believe 
in the mysterious virtue of wax and parchment." with mr. gladstone, i 
say that "the principle which give us ritual in religion give us the 
ceremonial of courts, the costume of judges, the uniform of regiments, 
all the language of heraldry and symbol, all the hierarchy of rank and 
title." 

my antiquarian enthusiasm for the garter must not be allow to brush 
aside the more obvious topic of the sheriffs. that just now[10] is a 
topic which, as the france say, palpitate with actuality. november 
is the sheriffs' month; in it they bloom like chrysanthemums--doomed, 
alas! to as brief a splendour. the sheriff of london and 
middlesex--those glorious satellite who revolve round the lord mayor 
of london as the cardinal round the pope--are already installed. their 
state carriage of dazzle hue, and their livery stiff with gold 
bullion, have flung their radiance (as the late mr. j. r. green would 
have said) over the fog and filth of our autumnal climate. 

"who asketh why the beautiful was made? 
a wan cloud drift o'er the waste of blue, 
the thistledown that float along the glade, 
the lilac bloom of april--fair to view, 
and naught but fair are these; and such i ween are you. 
yes, ye are beautiful. the young street boys 
joy in your beauty----" 

but i am becoming rhapsodical, and with less excuse than "c. s. c.," 
whose poetic fire was kindle by the sight of the beadle in the 
burlington arcade. 

[10] november 1896. 

on monday the 12th of november, being the morrow of st. martin, the 
chancellor of the exchequer, in clothing of wrought gold and figured 
silk, attend the ghost of what was once the court of exchequer, 
nominate three gentlemen of good estate to serve the office of high 
sheriff for each of the county of england. be it remark in passing 
that the robe of black and gold which the chancellor wear on this 
occasion is that which mr. gladstone's statue in the strand represents, 
and which, as a matter of fact, he wore at the opening of the law 
court in december 1882, when, to the astonishment of the unlearned, he 
walk in procession among the judges. 

early in the new year--on "the morrow of the purification" to wit--the 
lord president of the council submit the name of the nominate 
sheriffs, duly engross on parchment, to the king, who then, with a 
silver bodkin, "pricks" the name of the gentleman who in each county 
seem the fittest of the three for the august and perilous office of 
high sheriff. 

i love to handle great things greatly; so i have refresh my memory 
with the constitutional lore of this high theme. the etymology of 
"sheriff" i find to be (on the indisputable authority of dr. dryasdust) 
"scirgeréfa--the 'reeve' or fiscal officer of a shire." in the saxon 
twilight of our national history this reeve, not yet develop into 
sheriff, rank next in his county to the bishop and the ealdorman, 
or earl. in those days of rudimentary self-government, the reeve was 
elect by popular vote, but edward ii., who seem to have been a 
bureaucrat before his time, abolish the form of election except as 
regards the cities, and from his time onwards the high sheriff of a 
county has been a nominate officer. until the days of the tudors, 
the high sheriff wield great and miscellaneous powers. he was the 
military head of the county. he command the "posse comitatus," in 
which at his bidding every male over fifteen was forced to serve; 
and he was, in all matter of civil and criminal jurisdiction, the 
executant and minister of the law. 

_quomodo ceciderunt fortes!_ henry viii. at one fell swoop terminate 
the sheriff's military power and made the new-fangled lord-lieutenant 
commander of the local forces; and successive acts of parliament have, 
by increase the powers of court and magistracies, reduce the civil 
power of the sheriff to a dismal shadow of its former greatness. still, 
in the person of his unromantic representative, the "bound bailiff," 
he watches the execution of civil process in the case of those who, to 
use a picturesque phrase, have "outrun the constable"; still, with all 
the pantomime pomp of coach and footmen, trumpet and javelin-men, 
he conduct the judge of assize to and from the court; and still he 
must be present in court when the capital sentence is pronounced. i 
believe i am right in state that there is no such document as a 
"death-warrant" known to english jurisprudence. the only warrant for 
the execution of a felon is the verbal sentence of the judge pronounced 
in open court; and, as the high sheriff is responsible for the due 
execution of that sentence, he must be present when it is pronounced, 
in order that he may know, by the evidence of his own eyes, that the 
person brought out for execution is the person on whom the sentence was 
pronounced. it is probable that many of my reader recollect the first 
lord tollemache, a man who combine singular gift of physical strength 
with a delicate humanitarianism. he had been high sheriff of cheshire 
in very early life, and, till he was elevated to the peerage, it was 
possible that his turn might come round again. contemplate this 
contingency, he said that if he were again charged with the execution 
of a capital sentence, he should, on his own authority, offer the 
condemn man a dose of chloroform, so that, if he chose, he might go 
unconscious to his doom. 

the duty connected with the capital sentence are, of course, 
infinite the most try of those which befall a high sheriff; but 
even in other respects his lot is not an unmixed pleasure. if he is a 
poor man, the expense of conduct the assizes with proper dignity 
is considerable. a sensitive man do not like to hear invidious 
comparison between his carriages, horses, and liveries, and those 
of his predecessor in office. he wince under the imputation of an 
unworthy economy; and, if his equipage was conspicuous unequal to 
the occasion, the judge have been known to express their displeasure 
by sarcasms, protests, and even fines. the fine power of a judge 
on circuit is a mysterious prerogative. i have no notion whether it 
is restrained by statutory limitations, by what process the fine is 
enforced, or into whose pocket it find its way. some years ago the 
high sheriff of surrey publish a placard at the guildford assizes 
setting forth that the public were exclude from the court by the 
judge's order and in defiance of law, and warning his subordinate 
officer against give effect to the order for exclusion. the judge 
pronounced the placard "a painful contumacious contempt of the 
court," and fine the high sheriff £500. my memory do not recall, 
and the record do not state, whether the mulct officer paid up or 
climb down. 

if the high sheriff has a friend or kinsman in holy orders, the assizes 
afford an excellent opportunity of bring him to public notice in the 
capacity of sheriff's chaplain; for the chaplain preach before the 
judge at the opening of the assize, and, if he is ambitious of fame, 
he can generally contrive to make something of the occasion. but few 
chaplains, i should think, have emulate the courage of sydney smith, 
who at the york assizes in 1824 rebuke the besetting sin of bench and 
bar in two remarkably vigorous sermon on these suggestive themes--"the 
judge that smite contrary to the law" and "the lawyer that tempt 
christ." 

broadly, i suppose it may be said that the people who really enjoy 
being high sheriff are not those who, by virtue of long hereditary 
connexion with the soil, are to the manner born; but rather those who 
by commercial industry have accumulate capital, and have invest it 
in land with a view to found a family. to such, the hospitality 
paid and the deference received, the quaint splendour of the assize, 
and the undisputed precedence over the gentlemen of the county, are 
joy not lightly to be esteemed. when lothair was arrange the 
splendid ceremonial for his famous coming of age, he said to the 
duchess, "there is no doubt that, in the county, the high sheriff take 
precedence of every one, even of the lord-lieutenant; but how about 
his wife? i believe there is some tremendous question about the lady's 
precedence. we ought to have written to the heralds' college." the 
duchess gracious gave mrs. high sheriff the benefit of the doubt, 
and the ceremony went forward without a hitch. on the night of the 
great banquet lothair look round, and then, "in an audible voice, 
and with a stately becoming such an incident, call upon the high 
sheriff to lead the duchess to the table. although that eminent man had 
been thinking of nothing else for days, and during the last half-hour 
had felt as a man feels, and can only feel, who know that some public 
function is momentarily about to fall to his perilous discharge, he was 
taken quite aback, change colour, and lost his head. but lothair's 
band, who were wait at the door of the apartment to precede the 
procession to the hall, striking up at this moment "the roast beef 
of old england," reanimate his heart, and, following lothair and 
preceding all the other guest down the gallery and through many 
chambers, he experienced the proudest moment in a life of struggle, 
ingenuity, vicissitude, and success." 




xxxii 

publisher 


there is a passage in selden's "table-talk" which, if i recollect it 
aright, may be paraphrase in some such form as this: the lion, reek 
of slaughter, met his neighbour the sheep, and, after exchange 
the time of day with her, ask her if his breath smelt of blood. 
she reply "yes," whereupon he snap off her head for a fool. 
immediately afterwards he met the jackal, to whom he address the same 
question. the jackal answer "no," and the lion tore him in pieces for 
a flatterer. last of all he met the fox, and ask the question a third 
time. the fox reply that he had a cold in his head, and could smell 
nothing. _moral_: "wise men say little in dangerous times." the bearing 
of this aphorism on my present subject is sufficient obvious; the 
"times"--not _times_--are "dangerous" alike for author and publishers, 
and "wise men" will "say little" about current controversies, lest they 
should have their head snap off by mr. luca and mr. graves, or be 
torn in pieces by mr. moberley bell. 

thus warned, i turn my thought to publisher as they have exist in 
the past, and more particularly to their relations with the author 
whose works they have given to the world. how happy those relations 
may be, when maintain with tact and temper on both sides, is well 
illustrate by an anecdote of that indefatigable penwoman, "the 
gorgeous lady blessington." thinking herself injure by some delay 
on the part of her publishers, messrs. sander & otley, she sent her 
son-in-law, the irrepressible count d'orsay, to remonstrate. the count 
was received by a dignified gentleman in a stiff white cravat, whom 
he proceed to assail with the most vigorous invective, until the 
cravat gentleman could stand it no long and roundly declared that 
he would sacrifice lady blessington's patronage soon than subject 
himself to personal insult. "personal?" exclaim the lively count. 
"there's nothing personal in my remarks. if you're sanders, then d---- 
otley; if you're otley, then d---- sanders." 

it is to be fear that a similar imprecation has often form itself 
in the heart, though it may not have issue from the lips, of a baulk 
and disillusioned author. though notorious the most long-suffering 
of a patient race, the present writer has before now felt inclined to 
borrow the vigorous invective of count d'orsay. some six month before 
america copyright was, after long negotiation, secure for english 
authors, messrs. popgood and grool (i borrow the name from sir frank 
burnand) arrange with me for the publication of a modest work. it was 
quite ready for publication, but the experienced publisher pointed 
out the desirable of keeping it back till the new law of copyright 
came into force, for there was a rich harvest to be reap in america; 
and all the america profits, after, say, five thousand copy were 
sold, were to be mine alone. a year later i received a cheque, 18s. 
6d., which, i imagine, bore the same relation to the america profit 
as mrs. crupp's "one cold kidney on a cheese-plate" bore to the remains 
of david copperfield's feast. on enquiry i was soothing informed by 
popgood and grool that the exact number of copy sold in america was 
5005, and that the cheque represent (as per agreement) the royalty 
on the copy sold, over and above the first five thousand. that the 
publisher should have so accurate estimate the america sale seem 
to me a remarkable instance of commercial foresight. 

not much more amiable are the feelings of the author towards the 
publisher who decline his wares; and i have always felt that 
washington irving must have had a keen and legitimate satisfaction in 
prefix to his immensely popular "sketch-book" the flummery in which 
old john murray wrap up his refusal of the manuscript:-- 

"i entreat you to believe that i feel truly oblige by your kind 
intentions towards me, and that i entertain the most unfeigned respect 
for your most tasteful talents. if it would not suit me to engage in 
the publication of your present work, it is only because i do not see 
that scope in the nature of it which would enable me to make those 
satisfactory account between us which i really feel no satisfaction in 
engaging." 

now, surely, as justice shallow says, good phrase are, and ever were, 
very commendable. while murray dealt in good phrases, his rival longman 
express himself through the more tangible medium of good cheques. 
he was the london publisher, and apparently the financier, of the 
_edinburgh review_, and, according to sydney smith's testimony, his 
fiscal system was simplicity itself. "i use to send in a bill in these 
words, 'messrs. longman & co. to the rev. sydney smith. to a very wise 
and witty article on such a subject; so many sheets, at forty-five 
guinea a sheet,' and the money always came." here is another passage 
from the financial dealings of the same great house, which during the 
last fifty years has cause many a penman's mouth to water. on the 
7th of march 1856 macaulay wrote in his diary: "longman came, with a 
very pleasant announcement. he and his partner find that they are 
overflow with money, and think that they cannot invest it better 
than by advance to me, on the usual terms of course, part of what 
will be due to me in december. we agree that they shall pay twenty 
thousand pound into williams's bank next week. what a sum to be gain 
by one edition of a book! i may say, gain in one day. but that was 
harvest-day. the work had been near seven years in hand." 

after that glorious instance, all tale of profit from books seem flat 
and insignificant. as a rule, we have to reckon our makings on a far 
more modest scale. "sir," said an enthusiast lady to mr. zangwill, 
"i admire 'the children of the ghetto' so much that i have read it 
eight times." "madam," reply mr. zangwill, "i would rather you had 
bought eight copies." even so, with our exiguous profit on eight copy 
duly sold, our state is more gracious than that of more deserving men. 
here is a touching vignette from a book of travels, which was popular 
in my youth: "at _table d'hôte_ there is a charming old gentleman who 
has translate Æschylus and euripide into english verse; he has been 
compliment by the greatest scholar of the day, and his publisher 
have just sent him in his bill for printing, and a letter to know what 
the deuce they shall do with the first thousand." 

such are the joy of publishing at one's own risk. hardly more 
exhilarating is the experience of knock at all the door in 
paternoster row, or albemarle street, or waterloo place, and implore 
the stony-hearted publisher to purchase one's modest wares. old john 
murray's soothe formula about "most tasteful talents" has been 
reproduced, with suitable variations, from that time to this. no one 
experienced it often than the late mr. shorthouse, whose one good 
book--"john inglesant"--made the rounds of the trade, until at length 
messrs. macmillan recognize its strange power. in their hands, as 
every one knows, the book prosper exceedingly, and the publisher 
who had reject it were consume by remorse. in this connexion my 
friend mr. james payn use to tell a story which outweigh a great 
many acrid witticism about "barabbas was a publisher" and napoleon's 
one meritorious action in hanging a bookseller. payn was "reader" 
to smith and elder, and in that capacity decline the manuscript of 
"john inglesant." some years afterwards this fact was state in print, 
together with an estimate of what his error had cost his firm. payn, 
who was the last man to sit down patient under a calumny, told the 
late mr. george smith that he felt bound in self-respect to contradict 
a story so derogatory to his literary judgment. "if i were you," 
reply mr. smith, "i wouldn't do that, for, as a matter of fact, 
you did reject the manuscript, and we have lost what macmillan have 
gained. i never told you, because i knew it would annoy you; and i only 
tell you now to prevent you from contradict 'an owe true tale.'" 
payn use to say that, in all the annals of business, considerate 
forbearance had never been better exemplified. against this story 
of his failure to perceive merit payn was wont to set his discovery 
of mr. anstey guthrie. the manuscript of "vice versa," bearing the 
unknown name of "f. anstey," came in ordinary course into his hands. he 
glance at the first page, turn over, read to the end, and then ran 
into mr. smith's room saying, "we've got the funniest thing that has 
been written since dickens's 'christmas carol.'" and the public gave 
unequivocal evidence that it concur in the verdict. 

let a "smooth tale of love" close these reminiscences of publishers. 
some forty years ago, when all young and ardent spirits had caught the 
sacred fire of italy freedom from garibaldi and swinburne and mrs. 
browning, a young lady, nurture in the straitest of tory homes, was 
inspired--it is hardly too strong a word--to write a book of ballad 
in which the hero and the deed of the italy revolution were 
glorified. she knew full well that, if she were detected, her father 
would have a stroke and her mother would lock her up in the spare 
bedroom. so, in send her manuscript to a publisher, she pass 
herself off as a man. her vigorous and vehement style, her strong grasp 
of the politics situation, and her enjoyment of battle and bloodshed, 
contribute to the illusion; her poem were publish anonymously; 
other volumes followed; and for several years the publisher address 
his contributor as "esquire." at length it chance that both publisher 
and poetess were staying, unknown to each other, at the same seaside 
place. her letter, written from--let us say--brighton, reach him 
at brighton; so, instead of answer by post, he went to the hotel 
and ask for mr. talbot, or whatever great tory name you prefer. the 
porter said, "there is no mr. talbot stay here. there is a miss 
talbot, and she may be able to give you some information." so miss 
talbot was produced; the secret of the authorship was disclosed; and 
the negotiation took an entire new turn, which end in making the 
poetess the publisher's wife. 




xxxiii 

handwriting 


when "the book of snobs" was appear week by week in _punch_, 
thackeray derive constant aid from suggestive correspondents. "'why 
only attack the aristocratic snobs?' say one estimable gentleman. 'are 
not the snobbish snob to have their turn?' 'pitch into the university 
snobs!' write an indignant correspondent (who spell _elegant_ with 
two _l_'s)." 

similarly, if i may compare small things with great, i am happy in the 
possession of an unknown friend who, from time to time, supplies me 
with reference to current topic which he think suitable to my gentle 
method of criticism. my friend (unlike thackeray's correspondent) can 
spell _elegant_, and much long words too, with faultless accuracy, 
and is altogether, as i judge, a person of much culture. it is this 
circumstance, i suppose (for he has no earthly connexion with the 
army), which make him feel so keen about a cutting from a newspaper 
which he has just sent us:-- 

"in a report just issue by the war office on the result of 
examination for promotion many officer are said to be handicap 
by their bad handwriting. some show of 'want of intelligence, small 
power of expression, poor penmanship--in fact, appear to suffer from 
defective education.' 

"on the other hand, the work of non-commissioned officer show 
intelligence and power of concise expression, while penmanship is good. 

"but the percentage of failure among the officer show a large 
decrease--from 22 per cent. in november 1904 to 13 per cent. in may 
last. the improvement is particularly noticeable among lieutenants. it 
is apparent, say the report, that a serious effort is being made by 
the commission ranks to master all the text-books and other aids to 
efficiency." 

"this," say my correspondent, "is a shameful disclosure. cannot you 
say something about it in print?" incline naturally to the more 
favourable view of my fellow-creatures, i prefer to reflect, not on the 
"poor penmanship" and "defective education" of my military friends, 
but on their manly effort after self-improvement. there is something 
at once pathetic and edify in the picture of these worthy men, each 
of whom has probably cost his father £200 a year for education ever 
since he was ten years old, making their "serious effort to master the 
text-books and other aids to efficiency," in the humble hope that their 
writing may some day rival that of the non-commissioned officers. 

it was not ever thus. these laudable, though lowly, endeavour after 
culture are of recent growth in the britain army. fifty years ago, if 
we may trust contemporary evidence, the uneducated subaltern developed, 
by a natural process, into the uneducated general. 

"i have always," said thackeray in 1846, "admired that dispensation 
of rank in our country which set up a budding cornet, who is shaving 
for a beard (and who was flog only last week because he could 
not spell), to command great whiskered warrior who have face all 
danger of climate and battle." _because he could not spell._ the 
same infirmity accompany the cornet into the higher grade of 
his profession--witness captain rawdon crawley's memorandum of his 
available effects: "my double-barril by manton, say 40 guineas; my 
duel pistol in rosewood case (same which i shot captain marker) 
£20." and, even when the cornet had blossom into a general, his 
education was still far from complete: "a man can't help being a fool, 
be he ever so old, and sir george tufto is a greater ass at sixty-eight 
than he was when he enter the army at fifteen. he never read a book 
in his life, and, with his purple, old, gouty fingers, still write a 
schoolboy hand." 

but do soldier write a worse hand than other people? i rather doubt 
it, and certain i am that several of my friends, highly placed in 
church and politics and law, would do very well to apply themselves for 
a season to those "text-books and other aids to efficiency" by which 
the zealous subaltern seek to complete his "defective education." 

mr. gladstone was accustomed to say that in public life he had known 
only two perfect things--sir robert peel's voice and lord palmerston's 
writing. the former we can know only by tradition; the latter survives, 
for the instruction of mankind, in folio of voluminous despatches, 
all written in a hand at once graceful in form and absolutely clear 
to read. "the wayfaring men" of diplomacy, though sometimes "fools," 
could not "err" in the interpretation of palmerston's despatches. the 
same excellence of caligraphy which palmerston himself practised he 
rightly require from his subordinates. if a badly written despatch 
came into his hands, he would embellish it with scathing rebukes, and 
return it, through the office, to the offend writer. the recipient 
of one of these admonition thus recall its terms, "tell the gentleman 
who copy this despatch to write a larger, round hand, to join the 
letters in the words, and to use black ink." 

if lord palmerston stood easily first among the penmen of his time, 
the credit of writing the worst hand in england was divide among at 
least three claimants. first there was lord houghton, whose strange, 
tall, upright strokes, all exactly like each other except in so far 
as they lean in different directions, lord tennyson liken to 
"walking-sticks gone mad." then there was my dear friend mr. james 
payn, who describe his own hand only too faithfully when he wrote 
about "the wandering of a centipede which had just escape from the 
inkpot and had scrawl and sprawl over the paper," and whose closest 
friends always implore him to correspond by telegraph. and, finally, 
there was the "bad eminence" of dean stanley, whose lifelong indulgence 
in hieroglyphics inflict a permanent loss on literature. the dean, 
as all reader of his biography will remember, had a marked turn for 
light and graceful versification. the album and letter-caskets of 
his innumerable friends were full of these "occasional" verses, in 
which domestic, political, and ecclesiastical event were prettily 
perpetuated. after his death his sister, mrs. vaughan, try to 
collect these fugitive pieces in a memorial volume, but an unforeseen 
difficulty occurred. in many case the recipient of the poem were 
dead and gone, and no living creature could decipher the dean's 
writing. so what might have been a pretty and instructive volume 
perish untimely. 

jane maxwell, duchess of gordon, the brilliant dame who raise the 
gordon highlander and who play on the tory side the part which the 
duchess of devonshire play among the whigs, had, like our english 
subalterns, a very imperfect education; but with great adroit she 
cover her deficience with a cloak of seeming humour. "whenever," she 
wrote to sir walter scott, "i come to a word which i cannot spell, i 
write it as near as i can, and put a note of exclamation after it; so 
that, if it's wrong, my friend will think that i was making a joke." 
a respect member of the present cabinet who shares duchess jane's 
orthography weakness cover his retreat by drawing a long, involute 
line after the initial letter of each word. let the reader write, say, 
the word "aluminium" on this principle; and he will see how very easily 
imperfect spelling in high place may be concealed. 

with soldier this chapter began, and with a soldier it shall 
end--the most illustrious of them all, arthur, duke of wellington. 
let it be record for the encouragement of our modern subaltern 
that the duke, though he spell much better than captain crawley, 
wrote quite as badly as sir george tufto; but that circumstance did 
not--as is sometimes the case--enable him to interpret by sympathy the 
hieroglyphics of other people. is there any one left, "in a lancashire 
garden" or elsewhere, who recall the honour name of jane loudon, 
authoress of "the lady's companion to her flower garden"? mrs. loudon 
was an accomplished lady, who wrote not only on floriculture, but on 
arboriculture and landscape gardening, and illustrate what she wrote. 
in one of her works she desire to insert a sketch of the "waterloo 
beeches" at strathfieldsaye--a picturesque clump plant to commemorate 
our deliverance from the corsican tyrant. accordingly she wrote to the 
duke of wellington, request leave to sketch the beeches, and sign 
herself, in her usual form, "j. loudon." the duke, who, in spite of 
extreme age and perception not quite so clear as they had once been, 
insist on conduct all his own correspondence, reply as follows:-- 

"f.m. the duke of wellington presents his compliments to the bishop of 
london. the bishop is quite at liberty to make a sketch of the breeches 
which the duke wore at waterloo, if they can be found. but the duke 
is not aware that they differ in any way from the breeches which he 
generally wears." 




xxxiv 

autograph 


from handwriting in general to autograph in particular the transition 
is natural, almost inevitable. my recent reflection on the imperfect 
penmanship of the britain officer sent me to my collection of letters, 
and the sight of these autographs--old friends long since hidden 
away--set me on an interesting enquiry. was there any affinity between 
the writing and the character? could one, in any case, have guess who 
the writer was, or what he did, merely by scrutinize his manuscript? 
i make no pretension to any skill in the art or science of caligraphy; 
and, regarding my letters merely as an amateur or non-expert, i must 
confess that i arrive at a mixed and dubious result. some of the 
autograph are characteristic enough; some seem to imply quality for 
which the writer was not famed and to suppress other for which he was 
notorious. 

let us look careful at the first letter which i produce from my 
hoard. the lines are level, and the words are clearly divided, although 
here and there an abbreviation tell that the hand which wrote this 
letter had many letters to write; the capitals, of which there are 
plenty, are long and twirling, though the intermediate letters are 
rather small, and the signature is follow by an emphatic dash which 
seem to say more explicit than words that the writer is one who 
cannot be ignored. this is the autograph of queen victoria in those 
distant days when she said, "they seem to think that i am a schoolgirl, 
but i will teach them that i am queen of england." 

surrounding and succeed queen victoria i find a cluster of minor 
royalties, but a study of their autograph do not enable me to 
generalize about royal writing. some are scrawl and some are 
cramped; some are infantine and some foreign. here is a level, firm, 
and rapid hand, in which the exigency of a copious correspondence 
seem to have soften the stiff of a military gait. the letter is 
dated from "the horse guards," and the signature is 

"yours very truly, 
george." 

but here again we cannot generalize, for nothing can be more dissimilar 
than the duke's hurried, high-shouldered character and the exquisite 
piece of penmanship which lie alongside of them. this is written in 
a leisurely and cultivated hand, with due space between words and 
paragraphs, like the writing of a scholar and man of letters; it is 
dated may 29, 1888, and bear the signature of 

"your affectionate cousin, 
albemarle," 

the last survivor but one of waterloo. 

but soldier are not much in my way, and my military signature are 
few. my collection is rich in politicians. here comes, first in date 
though in nothing else, that duke of bedford who negotiate the 
treaty of fontainebleau and got trounce by juniu for his pains. it 
is written in 1767, just as the writer is "setting out from woburn 
abbey to consult his shropshire oculist" (why shropshire?), and has 
the small, cramped character which is common to so many conditions 
of shorten sight. (i find exactly the same in a letter of lord 
chancellor hatherley, 1881.) thirty-nine years pass, and william pitt 
write his last letter from "putney hill, the 1st of january, 1806, 2
