p.m.," the writing as clear, as steady, and as beautiful form as if 
the "sun of austerlitz" had never dawned. and now the statesmen pass 
me in rapid succession and in fine disregard of chronological order. 
lord russell write a graceful, fluent, rather feminine hand; charles 
villiers's writing is of the same family; and the great lord derby's 
a perfect specimen of the "italian hand," delicate as if drawn with a 
crow-quill, and slant into allure tails and loops. lord brougham's 
was a vile scrawl, with half the letters tumble backwards. john 
bright's is small, neat, and absolutely clear; nor is it fanciful to 
surmise that mr. chamberlain copy mr. bright, and were they not both 
short-sighted men? and lord goschen's writing, from the same cause, is 
small still. the duke of argyll wrote a startle and imperious hand, 
worthy of a highland chief whose ancestor not so long ago exercise 
the power of life and death; lord iddesleigh a neat and orderly hand, 
becoming a private secretary or permanent official. lord granville's 
and mr. forster's writings had this in common, that they look 
most surprising candid and straightforward. the present duke of 
devonshire's writing suggest nothing but vanity, self-consciousness, 
and ostentation. we all can judge, even without being caligraphists, 
how far these suggestion conform to the facts. by far the most 
pleasing autograph of all the statesmen is lord beaconsfield's, 
artistic form and highly finished--in his own phrase, "that form 
of scripture which attracts." with the utmost possible loyalty to a 
lost leader, i would submit that mr. gladstone wrote an uncommonly bad 
hand--not bad in point of appearance, for it was neat and comely even 
when it was hurried; but bad morally--a kind of caligraphic imposture, 
for it looks quite remarkably legible, and it is only when you come to 
close quarters with it and try to decipher an important passage that 
you find that all the letters are practically the same, and that the 
interpretation of a word must depend on the context. 

from my pile of statesmen's autograph i extract yet another, and i 
lay it side by side with the autograph of a great author and a great 
ecclesiastic. all three are very small, exquisite neat, very little 
slanted, absolutely legible. well as i knew the three writers, i doubt 
if i could tell which wrote which letter. they were cardinal manning, 
mr. froude, and lord rosebery. will the expert in caligraphy tell 
me if, in this case, similarity of writing body forth similarity 
of gift or qualities? another very close similarity may be observe 
between the writing of lord halsbury and that of lord brampton (better 
known as sir henry hawkins), which, but for the fact that lord brampton 
use the long "s" and lord halsbury do not, are pretty nearly 
identical. 

if there is one truth which can be deduce more confident than 
another from my collection of autographs, it is that there is no 
such thing as "the literary hand." every variety of writing which a 
"reader's" fevered brain could conceive is illustrate in my bundle 
of literary autographs. _seniores priores._ samuel rogers was born in 
1763, and die in 1855. a note of his, written in 1849, and beginning, 
"pray, pray, come on tuesday," is by far the most surprising piece of 
caligraphy in my collection. it is so small that, except under the 
eyes of early youth, it require a magnifying-glass; yet the symmetry 
of every letter is perfect, and, when sufficient enlarged, it might 
stand as a model of beautiful and readable writing. i take a bound of 
sixty years, and find some of the same characteristic reproduce by 
my friend mr. quiller-couch; but between the "pleasures of memory" and 
"green bays" there rolls a sea of literature, and it has been navigate 
by some strange craft in the way of handwriting. i have spoken on 
another occasion of dean stanley, lord houghton, and james payn; 
specimen of their enormity surround me as i write, and i can adduce, 
i think, an equally heinous instance. here is sydney smith, writing in 
1837 to "dear john," the hero of the reform act, "no body wish better 
for you and yours than the inhabitant of combe florey." perhaps so; 
but they convey their benediction through a very irritate medium, 
for sydney smith's writing is of the immoral type, pleasing to the 
eye and superficial legible, but, when once you have lost the clue, 
a labyrinth. perhaps it is due to this circumstance that his books 
abound, beyond all others, in uncorrected misprints. 

but there are other fault in writing besides ugly and 
illegibility. a great man ought not to write a poor hand. yet nothing 
can be poor than ruskin's--mean, ugly, insignificant--only redeem 
by perfect legibility. goldwin smith's, though clear and shapely, is 
characterless and disappointing. some great scholars, again, write 
disappointing hands. jowett's is a spiteful-looking angular, little 
scratch, perfectly easy to read; westcott's comely but not clear; 
lightfoot's an open, scramble scrawl, something like the late lord 
derby's. these great men cannot excuse their deficiency in penmanship 
by pleading that they have had to write a great deal in their lives. 
other before them have had to do that, and have emerge from the trial 
without a stain on their caligraphy. for example--"albany, december 
3, 1854," is the heading of an ideally beautiful sheet, every letter 
perfectly formed, all space duly observed, and the whole evidently 
maintain its beauty in spite of breakneck speed. the signature is 

"ever yours truly, 
t. b. macaulay." 

here is a letter address to me only last year by a man who was 
born in 1816. in my whole collection there is no clear or pretty 
writing. as a devotee of fine penmanship, i make my salutation to sir 
theodore martin. 




xxxv 

more autograph 


my suggestive friend has sudden been multiply a hundredfold. 
handwriting is a subject which apparently make a wide appeal. 
each post bring me correction or corroboration of what i wrote 
last saturday. fresh instance of enormity in the way of illegible 
writing are adduce from all quarters; nor are there wanting acrid 
critic who suggest that reform should begin at home, and that "the 
author of collection and recollections" would do well to consult 
a writing-master, or to have his copy type before it go to the 
printers. waive these personalities, i turn again to my letter-case, 
and here let me say in passing that i committed a fearful indiscretion 
when i spoke of my "collection" of autographs. that fatal word brought 
down an avalanche of "collectors," who, hail me as a man and a 
brother, propose all sort of convenient exchanges. a gentleman who 
cherish a postcard from mr. rudyard kipling would exchange it for an 
unpublished letter of shelley; and a maiden-lady at weston-super-mare, 
whose great-aunt correspond with eliza cook, will refuse no 
reasonable offer. 

but all these handsome proposition must be brush aside, for i have 
no collection of autographs, if "collection" imply any art or system 
in the way in which they have been brought together, or any store of 
saleable duplicates. mine are simply letters address to myself or 
to my kinsfolk, plus just a very few which have come into my hand in 
connexion with public business; but, such as they are, they are full of 
memory and morals. 

why did very old people write so well? i have already describe the 
writing of samuel rogers, of the waterloo lord albemarle, and of sir 
theodore martin. pretty well for octogenarian penmanship; but i can 
enlarge the gallery. a bundle of octogenarian letters lie before me 
as i write. olive wendell holmes send a tribute to matthew arnold. 
charles villier accept an invitation to dinner. lord norton invite 
me to stay at hams. archdeacon denison complain of "his first attack 
of gout at eighty-five." mr. leveson-gower at eighty-six thanks 
me for a review of his first book. i protest that there is not an 
ungraceful line--scarcely a misshape letter--in any of these five 
manuscripts. here is a small, elegant, and "taily" hand, rather like an 
old-fashioned lady's. the signature is 

"yours sincerely, 
eversley," 

better known as mr. shaw-lefevre, the most authoritative speaker 
the house of commons ever had. note that this was written in his 
eighty-eighth year, and he live to buy a new pair of gun after he was 
ninety. here is a strong, clear, well-defined writing, setting forth 
with precision and emphasis the reason why the last duke of cleveland, 
then in his eighty-fifth year, will not give more than £5 for an 
object which he has been ask to help. the writing of the beloved 
and honour duke of rutland, always graceful and regular, become 
marked smaller, though not the least less legible, till he die at 
eighty-seven. there is no more vigorous, even dashing, signature in 
my store than "g. j. holyoake," written in july 1905. close to the 
imperial purple of the agitator's ink nestles, in piquant contrast, 
a small half-sheet of rose-pink paper bearing a duchess's coronet 
and cypher. the writing is distinct and ornamental; the letter was 
written in 1880, and the writer was born in 1792. but the mere fact 
of attain to eighty or ninety years will not absolutely guarantee, 
though it seem to promote, legible of writing. my venerable 
friend dean randall, who was born in 1824, end a letter, which 
certainly needs some such apology, with a disarm allusion to the 
"dreadful scrawl" of his "ancient ms."; and four side of tantalize 
hieroglyphics, drawn apparently with a blunt stick, are shown by 
external evidence to be a letter from canon carter of clew when he 
had touched his ninetieth birthday. 

on similarity, approach to identity, between the writings of very 
dissimilar person i have already remarked, and a further illustration 
come to light as i turn over my papers. here are two letters in the 
graceful and legible script of the early nineteenth century, with long 
s's, and capitals for all the substantives. both are evidently the 
handiwork of cultivated gentlemen; and both the writers, as a matter 
of fact, were clergymen. but there the resemblance stops. the one was 
"jack" russell, the well-known sporting parson of exmoor; the other 
was andrew jukes, the deepest and most influential mystic whom the 
latter-day church has seen. 

when i praise graceful in writing i mean natural and effortless 
grace, such as was display in the writing of the late duke of 
westminster. but, if we admire writing artificial fashion and 
coerce into graceful like a clip yew, it would be difficult 
to excel the penmanship of the late george augustus sala, who was an 
engrave before he was an author; or that of sir a. conan doyle, who 
handle a pen as dexterous as in his surgical days he wield the 
lancet. i praise just now the late duke of westminster's writing, 
and of him one might say what scott said, in a different sense, of 
byron--that he "managed his pen with the careless and negligent ease of 
a man of quality"; but there is another kind of grace than that--the 
grace which is partly the result of mental clear and partly of a 
cultured eye. here are two specimen of such writing, the letters so 
alluring fashion that they look, as some one said, like something 
good to eat; and space with a care which at once make reading easy, 
and testify to clear thinking in the writer. both are the writings of 
scholars, and both of men who wrote a vast deal in their lives--bishop 
creighton and dean vaughan. it must have been a joy to read their 
proofs. the late dean farrar was the only public-school master i ever 
knew who took pains with his pupils' writing and encourage them to add 
grace to legibility. his own writing, small, upright, and characterful, 
was very pretty when he took time and pains; but the specimen which 
lie before me show sad sign of the havoc wrought by incessant 
writing against time. 

grace and legible are the two chief glory of penmanship, but other 
attribute are not without their effect. a dashing scrawl, if only 
it is easy to read, suggest a soar superior to conventional 
restraints, and rather bespeak a hero. here are two scrawls, and each 
is the work of a remarkable person. one is sign "yours truly, jos. 
cowen," and i dare say that some of my reader would see in it the 
index to a nature at once impetuous and imperious. but mr. cowen's 
scrawl was crowquill-work and copperplate compare with its next-door 
neighbour. "accept the enclosed, dear mr. russell," cover the whole 
of one side of a sheet of letter-paper; the ink is blue; the paper 
is ribbed; the signature, all wreathe in gigantic flourish and 
curling tails, is "laura thistlethwaite," and the enclose is one of 
the evangelist address of that gifted preach who once was laura 
bell. odd incongruity keep turning up. as i pass from the evangelical 
lady-orator, i come to father ignatius, an evangelical orator with a 
difference, but with a like tendency to scrawl. lord leighton's writing 
is also a scrawl, and, it must be confessed, an egotist scrawl, and 
a very bad scrawl to read. an illegible scrawl, too, is the writing 
of richard holt hutton, but his is not a vainglorious or commanding 
scrawl, but rather humble and untidy. "henry irving" is a signature 
quite culpable illegible, but "squire bancroft" is just irregular 
enough to be interesting though not unreadable. 

_per contra_, i turn to one of the most legible signature in my 
possession. the writing is ugly and the letters are ill-formed, look 
rather like the work of a hand which has only lately learned to write 
and find the act a difficulty. but it is as clear as print, and it 
show no adventitious ornamentation or self-assertive twirls. the 
signature is 

"yours most sincerely, 
randolph s. churchill." 

in this case, if in no other, the oracle of caligraphy are set at 
naught. here is a fine, twisty, twirl hand, all tails and loops, 
but not at all unsightly. the signature read like "lincoln," and only 
a careful study would detect that the "l" of "lincoln" is precede 
by a circular flourish which looks like part of the l but is really 
a capital c. it is the signature of that great scholar, christopher 
wordsworth, bishop of lincoln; and i remember that, in days of 
ecclesiastical strife, it was once impute to that apostolic man for 
vanity that he sign his name "lincoln" like a temporal peer. from 
that day he define the "c" more carefully. 

to the last letter which i bring to light to-day a different kind of 
interest attaches. it is dated 

"dingle bank, liverpool, 
_april 13, 1888_." 

the writing is small and clear, with the upstroke and downstroke 
rather long in comparison with the level letters; but some small blur 
and blot show that the letter was written in unusual haste. it end 
with these words: "smalley has written a letter full of shriek and 
curse about my innocent article; the america will get their notion 
of it from that, and i shall never be able to enter america again. 

"ever yours, 
m. a." 

this was the last letter which matthew arnold ever wrote, and it closed 
a friendship which had been one of the joy and glory of my life. 




xxxvi 

christmas 


"christmas, now," as mr. brooke in "middlemarch" might have said--"i 
went a good deal into that kind of thing at one time; but i found it 
would carry me too far--over the hedge, in fact." that, i imagine, 
pretty well represent the attitude of the adult world towards the 
feast which close the year. we all love it when we were young. now, 
it is all very well for once in a way; it might pall if frequent 
repeated; even recur only annually, it must be observe temperate 
and enjoy moderately. anything resemble excess would carry one 
too far--"over the hedge, in fact." but, within these recognize 
and salutary limits, christmas is an institution which i would not 
willing let die. in the days of my youth a jewish lady cause me 
not a little consternation by remark that it seem very odd for 
christian to celebrate the feast of redemption with gluttony and 
drunkenness. she lived, i am bound to say, in a very unregenerate 
village in a remarkably savage part of the country, and as, of course, 
she did not go to church, i dare say that gluttony and drunken 
were the form of christmas observance which most obtrude themselves 
on her notice. even cardinal newman seem to have remark the same 
phenomenon in his youth, though he satirize it more delicately. 
"beneficed clergymen use to go to rest as usual on christmas-eve, 
and leave to ringers, or sometimes to carollers, the observance which 
was paid, _not without creature comforts_, to the sacred night." 
now all that is changed. church of all confession vie with one 
another in the frequency and hearty and picturesque equipment 
of their religious services. even the _daily telegraph_ preach 
christmas sermons; and i very much question whether the populace 
get more drunk at christmas than at easter. but, though we may have 
learnt to celebrate the festival with rite more devout and less 
bibulous, we have not yet escape my jewish friend's reproach of 
gluttony. the christmas dinner of the britain home is still a thing 
imagination boggle at. the dreadful pleasantry of the aged--their 
sorry gibe about the doctor and the draught; hoary chestnut about 
little boys who stood up to eat more--remain among the most terrible 
memory of the christmas dinner. and they were quite in keeping 
with the dinner itself. i say nothing against the turkey, which (as 
my medical friends well know) was found, by practical experiment in 
the case of alexis st. martin, to be the most easily digest of all 
animal foods, except venison; but surely, as a nation, we eat quite 
beef enough in the course of the year without making christmas an 
annual orgy of carnivorous excess. i protest that the very sight of 
the butchers' shop at this season of the year is enough to upset 
a delicate balanced organization. rightly said the shah, in that 
immortal diary which he kept during his visit to england in 1873--"meat 
is good, but it should not be hung up in windows." macaulay use to 
say that thackeray, in his famous description of the clapham sect in 
"the newcomes," made one blunder--he represent them as dissenters, 
whereas, in fact, they were rather dogged church-people. the only 
exception to the rule was a baptist lady, who, living on clapham 
common, testify against the superstition of the establish church 
by eat roast veal and apple-pie on christmas-day instead of more 
orthodox dainties. churchman though i am, i protest that i think the 
baptist lady was right: and i believe that the puritan were wise than 
they knew when they denounce plum-pudding and mince pie as invention 
of the evil one. yet the love of these vindictive viands is one of the 
root-instincts of our english nature. forty-eight years ago the britain 
army was keeping its christmas in the crimea, amid all the horrors and 
hardship of a peculiarly grim campaign. an english sister of mercy, 
who was nursing under miss nightingale in the hospital at scutari, 
thus describe the melancholy festivity: "the 'roast beef of old 
england' was out of the question, but with the aid of a good deal of 
imagination, it seem possible at least to secure the plum pudding. 
i think i might with safety affirm that as the doctor left the ward 
every man drew from under his pillow a small portion of flour and fat, 
with an egg and some plums, and began to concoct a christmas pudding. 
i assist many to make the pudding, whom nothing short of a miracle 
would enable to eat it; still they must have the thing. for some 
days previous i had been ask for pieces of linen, which, without 
dream of the use to which they were to be applied, i supplied. thus 
were the pudding cloth provided." 

it can scarcely be conceive that these unhappy soldiers, madden by 
wound and fever or perishing by frost-bite and gangrene, can have had 
much physical enjoyment in christmas pudding made of material which 
had been conceal under their sick pillows; in such circumstances 
the value of the pudding is spiritual and symbolic. a few christmas 
ago i was assist (in the literal sense) at a dinner for starve 
"dockers." a more broken, jaded, and dejected crew it would be 
difficult to picture. they had scarcely enough energy to eat and drink, 
but lumber slow through their meal of meat-pies and coffee without 
a smile and almost without a word. all at once an unrehearsed feature 
was introduce into the rather cheerless programme, and a huge plum 
pudding, wreathe with holly and flaming blue with burnt brandy, was 
borne into the hall. a deep gasp of joy burst from the assemble 
guests, and the whole company rose as one man and greet the joyous 
vision with "auld lang syne." the eat was yet to come, so the 
exhilarate was purely moral. the pudding spoke at once to memory and 
to hope. 

there are other adjunct of christmas which must by no means be 
overlooked--christmas presents, for instance, and christmas amusements. 
as to christmas presents, i regard them as definite means of grace. 
for weeks--sometimes months--before christmas returns we concentrate 
our thought on our friends instead of ourselves. we reflect on 
people's likes and dislikes, habits, tastes, and occupations. we tax 
our ingenuity to find gift suitable for the recipients, and buy 
object which we think frankly hideous in the hope of gratifying our 
unsophisticated friends. happily the age of ormolu and malachite has 
passed. we no long buy blotting-books made unusable by little knob 
of enamel on the cover; nor gilt-paper weight which cost a hundred 
times more than the overweight letters of a lifetime could amount 
to. christmas gift of this type belong to an unreturn past, and, 
as walter pater said of the wedding-present which he was expect to 
admire, "very rich, very handsome, very expensive, i'm sure--but they 
mustn't make any more of them." nor will they. the standard of popular 
taste in the matter of nick-nacks has improve as conspicuous as in 
that of furniture; and the fancy shops, when spread for the christmas 
market, display a really large choice of presents which one can buy 
without sacrifice self-respect, and give without the appearance of 
insult. 

but christmas presents, even at a moderate rate of charge, may, if one 
has a large circle of acquaintance, carry one over the hedge, as mr. 
brooke said; and here is the scope and function of the christmas card. 
few people have bought more christmas cards in a lifetime than the 
present writer; and, out of a vast experience, he would offer one word 
of friendly counsel to the card-sender. do not accumulate the cards 
which you receive this christmas and distribute them among your friends 
next christmas, for, if you do, as sure as fate you will one day return 
a card to the sender; and old friendship and profitable connexion 
have been sever by such miscarriages. 

of christmas amusement i can say little. my notion of them is chiefly 
derive from "happy thoughts," where byng suggest some "christmassy 
sort of thing" to amuse his guests, and fail to gratify even his 
half-aunt. my infancy was spent in the country, remote from dance and 
theatres, pantomime and panoramas. "the classic wall of old drury" 
never welcome me on boxing night. certainly a christmas tree and a 
stocking full of presents appeal to that acquisitive instinct which 
is fully as strong in infancy as in old age; but, though exceedingly 
young in my time, i never was young enough to be amuse by a snow-man 
or dangerous excited by blind man's buff. look back, like 
tennyson's "many-wintered crow," on these christmas of infancy, i 
have sometimes ask myself whether i lost much by my aloof from 
the normal merriment of youth. mr. anstey guthrie know the secret 
heart of english boyhood more accurate than most of us, and when i 
read his description of a christmas party i am inclined to be thankful 
that my lot was cast a good many mile beyond the cab-radius. 

"why couldn't you come to our party on twelfth-night? we had great 
larks. i wish you'd been there." 

"i had to go to young skidmore's instead," said a pale, 
spiteful-looking boy with fair hair, careful part in the middle. 
"it was like his cheek to ask me, but i thought i'd go, you know, just 
to see what it was like." 

"what was it like?" ask one or two near him, languidly. 

"oh, awfully slow! they've a poky little house in brompton somewhere, 
and there was no dancing, only boshy games and a conjuror, without any 
presents. and, oh! i say, at supper there was a big cake on the table, 
and no one was allow to cut it, because it was hired. they're so 
poor, you know. skidmore's pater is only a clerk, and you should see 
his sisters!" 

all my sympathies are with skidmore, and i think that the fair-haired 
boy was an unmitigated beast, and cad, and snob. but there is an awful 
verisimilitude about "boshy games and a conjuror," and i bless the fate 
which allow me to grow up in ignorance of christmas parties. 




xxxvii 

new year's day 


on the 1st of january 1882, matthew arnold wrote to his sister: "i 
think the beginning of a new year very animating--it is so visible an 
occasion for break off bad habit and carry into effect good 
resolutions." this was splendid in a man who had just enter his 
sixtieth year, and we all should like to share the sentiment; but it 
is not always easy to feel "animated," even by the most significant 
anniversaries. sometimes they only depress; and the effect which they 
produce depend so very largely on the physical condition in which 
they find us. suppose, for instance, that one is a fox-hunter, in the 
prime of life and the pride of health, with a good string of horse 
which have been eat their head off during a prolonged frost. as 
one wake on new year's morning, one hear a delicious dripping from 
the roof, and one's servant, coming in with tea and letters, announce 
a rapid thaw. then "the beginning of a new year" is "animating" 
enough; and, while we wash and shave, we pledge ourselves, like 
matthew arnold, to "break off bad habit and carry into effect good 
resolutions." we remember with shame that we miss three capital days' 
hunting last november because we let our friends seduce us to their 
shooting-parties; and we resolve this year to make up for lost time, to 
redeem waste opportunities, and not willing to lose a day between 
this and christmas. such resolution are truly "animating"; but we 
cannot all be young or healthy or fox-hunters, and then the anniversary 
take a different colour. perhaps one is cower over one's 
study-fire, with "an air of romance struggle through the commonplace 
effect of a swell face" (like miss hucklebuckle in "the owlet"), 
or mumble the mince remains of our christmas turkey as painful 
as father diggory in "ivanhoe," who was "so severe afflict by 
toothache that he could only eat on one side of his face." not for us, 
in such circumstances, are "animating" vision of wide pastures, and 
negotiable fences, and too-fresh hunter pull one's arms off, and 
the chime of the "dappled darling down the roaring blast." rather 
do our new year's fancy lightly turn to thought of dentistry and 
doctoring. we ask ourselves whether the time has not come when art must 
replace what nature has withdrawn; and, if we form a resolution, it is 
nothing more heroic than that we will henceforward wear goloshe in 
damp weather and a quilted overcoat in frost. 

but, it may be urged, matthew arnold was not a fox-hunter (at least not 
after his oxford days), and yet he contrived to feel "animated" by new 
year's day. in his case animation was connected with books. 

"i am glad," he wrote, "to find that in the past year i have at least 
accomplished more than usual in the way of reading the books which at 
the beginning of the year i put down to be read. i always do this, and 
i do not expect to read all i put down, but sometimes i fall much too 
short of what i proposed, and this year things have been a good deal 
better. the importance of reading, not slight stuff to get through the 
time but the best that has been written, forces itself upon me more 
and more every year i live. it is living in good company, the best 
company, and people are generally quite keen enough, or too keen, about 
do that; yet they will not do it in the simplest and most innocent 
manner by reading. if i live to be eighty, i shall probably be the only 
person left in england who read anything but newspaper and scientific 
publications." 

we have not quite come to that yet, but we are not far off it, and i 
should fear that the number of even educated people who occupy new 
year's day in lay down a course of serious study for the next twelve 
month is lamentable small. but hunting and health and books are not 
the only topic for new year's meditation. there is also money, which 
not seldom obtrude itself with a disagreeable urgency. we cast our eye 
over that little parchment-bound volume which only "fortune's favoured 
sons, not we" can regard with any complacency; and we observe, not for 
the first time, that we have been spend a good deal more than we 
ought to spend, and are not far from the perilous edge of an overdrawn 
account. this is "animating" indeed, but only as a sudden stab of 
neuralgia is animating; and we immediately begin to consider method of 
relief. but where are our retrench to begin? that is always the 
difficulty. i remember that after the cattle plague of 1865, by which 
he had been a principal sufferer, the first lord tollemache was very 
full of fiscal reforms. "i ought to get rid of half my servants; but 
they are excellent people, and it would be very wrong to cause them 
inconvenience. horses, too--i really have no right to keep a stud. 
but nothing would ever induce me to sell a horse, and it seem rather 
heartless to kill old friends. then, again, about houses--i ought 
to leave st. james's square, and take a house in brompton. but the 
brompton house are so small that they really would not accommodate 
my family, and it would not be right to turn the boys into lodgings." 
and so on and so forth, with a magnificent list of contemplate 
reforms, which went unfulfill till things had right themselves 
and retrench was no long necessary. in the same spirit, though 
on a very different scale, the inhabitant of stuccovia contemplate 
the financial future which lie ahead of new year's day. we must 
economize--that is plain enough. but how are we to begin? 

i must have a new frock-coat very soon, and shall want at least three 
tweed suit before the autumn. economy bid me desert savile row and 
try aaronson in new oxford street. "budge," say the fiend. "budge 
not," reply self-respect. aaronson is remarkable for a fit "that 
never was on sea or land," and, though his garment are undeniable 
cheap, they are also nasty, and are worn out before they are paid 
for. or perhaps our conscience prick us most severe in the matter 
of wine. we will buy no more pommery and greno at 98s. a dozen, but 
will slake our modest thirst with a dry sillery at 31s. but, after 
all, health is the first consideration in life, and, unfortunately, 
these cheap wine never agree with us. the doctor hold them directly 
responsible for our last attack of eczema or neuritis, and say 
impressively, "drink good wine, or none at all--bad wine is poison 
to you." _drink none at all._ that is very "animating," but somehow 
our enfeeble will is unequal to the require resolve; we hold 
spirit-drinking in detestation; and so, after all, we are driven back 
to our pommery. "surely," as lamb said, "there must be some other world 
in which our unconquerable purpose" of retrench shall be realized. 

travel, again. many people spend too much in travel. can we curtail 
in that direction? for my own part, i am a londoner, and am content 
with life as it is afford by this wonderful world miscall a city. 
but the family has claims. some of them suffer from "liver," and whoso 
know what it is to dwell with liverish patient will not lightly run 
the risk of keeping them from carlsbad. other can only breathe on 
high alps, and others, again, require the sunshine of the riviera or 
the warmth of the italy lakes. so all the ways of retrench seem 
barred. clothes and wine and travel must cost as much as they cost 
last year, and the only way of escape seem to lie in the steps of 
the prince consort, who, when parliament reduce his income from the 
propose fifty thousand a year to thirty, patient observe that he 
should have to give less in subscriptions. 

to the spendthrift, or even to the more modest practitioner who 
merely lives up to his income, the new year, as we have seen, offer 
few opportunity for resolution of reform; but i fancy that the 
skinflint, and his cousin the screw, find it full of suggestive 
possibilities. i remember a gentleman of "griping and penurious 
tendencies" (the phrase is mr. gladstone's) telling me when i was a 
schoolboy that he had resolve to spend nothing with his tailor in 
the year then dawning. he announce it with the air befitting a great 
self-surrender, but i thought, as i look at his clothes, that he was 
really only continue the well-established practice of a lifetime. the 
screw, of course, is of no one place or age; and here is an excellent 
citation from the diary of a screw--mr. thomas turner--who flourish 
in sussex in the eighteenth century: "this being new year's day, myself 
and wife at church in the morning. collection. my wife gave 6d. but, 
they not ask me, i gave nothing. oh! may we increase in faith and 
good works, and maintain the good intentions we have this day taken 
up." those who have try it say that hoarding is the purest of human 
pleasures; and i dare say that by the end of the year good mr. turner's 
banking-book was a phantom of delight. 

all these reflections, and other like unto them, came whirl on 
my mind this new year's eve; and, just as i was beginning to reduce 
them to form and figure, the shrill ting-ting of the church-bell 
pierce the silence of the night. _watch-night._ those who are not the 
friends of the english church denounce her as hidebound, immovable, 
and unreceptive. here is the--or an--answer to the charge. she has 
borrowed, originally, from the swedenborgian and more immediately 
from the wesleyans, a religious observance which, though unrecognized 
in prayer-book or kalendar, now divide with the harvest festival the 
honour of being the most popular service in the church of england. 

"among the prompt of what may be called, in the truest sense of 
the term, natural religion, none surely is more instructive than that 
which leads men to observe with peculiar solemnity the entrance upon 
a new year of life. it is, if nothing else, the making a step in the 
dark. it is the entrance upon a new epoch in existence, of which the 
manifold "changes and chances" prevent our forecast the issue. true, 
the line of demarcation is purely arbitrary; yet there are few, even of 
the most thoughtless, who can set foot across the line which separate 
one year from another without feeling in some degree the significance 
of the act. it would seem that this passing season of thoughtful 
was one of those opportunity which no form of religion could afford 
to miss. and yet, for a long time, that which may perhaps without 
offence be term ecclesiastic stern refuse to recognize this 
occasion. the line was rigid drawn between the civil new year and the 
church's new year. we were told that advent was the beginning of our 
sacred year, and that the evening before the first sunday in advent was 
the time for those serious thought and good resolution which rightly 
accompany a new year." 

yes-so we were taught; and there was a great deal to be said, 
ecclesiastically, for the teaching. only, unfortunately, no one 
believe it. we went to bed quite unmoved on saturday evening, december 
1, 1906. no era seem to have closed for us, no era to have opened: 
there was nothing to remember, nothing to anticipate; nothing to 
repent and nothing to resolve. it is otherwise to-night.[11] the 
"church-going bell" do not tingle in vain. old men and maidens, young 
men and children are crowd in. i involve myself in an ulster and a 
comforter, and join the pilgrim-throng. 

[11] december 31, 1906. 




xxxviii 

pet 


my suggestive friend has taken to postcards, and his style, never 
diffuse, has become as curt as that of mr. alfred jingle. "why not 
pets?" he writes; and the suggestion give pause. 

when mrs. topham-sawyer accept the invitation to the little dinner at 
timmins's, she conclude her letter to rosa timmins: "with a hundred 
kiss to your dear little _pet_." she said _pet_, we are told, 
"because she did not know whether rosa's child was a girl or a boy; and 
mrs. timmin was very much pleased with the kind and gracious nature of 
the reply to her invitation." my mind misgave me that my friend might 
be use the word _pet_ in the same sense as mrs. topham-sawyer, and 
inviting me to a discussion of the crêche or the nursery. as my view 
of childhood are form on those of herod and solomon, i hasten to 
decline so unsuitable a task, whereupon my friend, for all reply, sent 
me the following excerpt from an evening paper:-- 

"the westminster cat exhibition, which will be held in the royal 
horticulture society's hall, vincent square, westminster, on january 
10 and 11, will afford an opportunity to all who love the domestic cat 
to aid in improve its lot through the agency of our dumb friends' 
league, which it is desire to benefit, not only by their presence 
on the occasion, but by contribute suitable specimen to the 'gift 
class' which will form part of the show, and be offer for sale in aid 
of the league during the time the exhibition is open. children will 
be invited, for the first time, to enter into the competition with 
their pet for suitable prizes, and thus, it is hoped, increase their 
interest in and affection for domestic pets." 

here i felt myself on more familiar ground. for i, too, have been 
young. i have trafficke in squirrel and guinea-pigs, have invest 
my all in an angora rabbit, and have undergone discipline for bring 
a dormouse into school. these are, indeed, among the childish things 
which i put away when i became a fifth form boy; but their memory 
is sweet--sweeter, indeed, than was their actual presence. for the 
cat, with which my friend seem chiefly to concern himself, i have 
never felt, or even professed, any warm regard. i leave her to dick 
whittington and shakespeare, who did so much to popularize her; to gray 
and matthew arnold and "c. s. c.," who have drawn her more sinister 
traits. gray remarks, with reference to "the pensive selima" and her 
hopeless struggle in the tub of goldfish, that "a favourite has no 
friend." archbishop benson render the line 

"delicias dominæ cetera turba fugit." 

i join the unfriend throng, and pass to other themes. 

the pet-keeping instinct, strong in infancy but suppress by the iron 
tradition of the public school, not seldom reassert itself in the 
freedom of later life. "the pet of history" would be a worthy theme 
for a roman lecture at oxford; and, if the purview were expand 
so as to include the pet of literature, it would be a fit subject 
for the brilliant pen of mr. frederic harrison. we might convenient 
adopt a wordsworthian classification, such as "pets belong to the 
period of childhood," "juvenile pets," "pets and the affections," 
"pets of the fancy," and "pets of the imagination." in the last-named 
class a prominent place would be assign to heavenly una's milk-white 
lamb and to mary's snowy-fleeced follower. "pets of the fancy" has, i 
must confess, something of a pugilist sound, but it might fairly be 
held to include the tame eagle which louis napoleon, when resident in 
carlton gardens, use to practise in the basement for the part which it 
was to play in his descent on boulogne. under "pets and the affections" 
we should recall chaucer's "prioresse"-- 

"of smale hound hadde sche, that sche fedde 
with rostud fleissh, and mylk, and wastel bredde." 

the pet of tradition would begin with st. john's tame partridge, and 
would include an account of st. francis preach to the birds. the 
pet of history would no doubt involve some reference to the bruce's 
spider and sir isaac newton's diamond and the duc d'enghien's spaniel; 
and, if so belittle a title as "pet" may be applied to so majestic an 
animal as the horse, we should trace a long line of equine celebrity 
from bucephalu and sorrel to marengo and copenhagen. the pet of 
literature are, of course, a boundless host--chargers like white 
surrey, and courser like roland; hound like keeldar and falcon like 
cheviot--to say nothing of mrs. merdle's parrot, or miss tox's canary, 
or mr. kipling's appalling monkey, who murder his owner's wife. 

wordsworth alone is responsible for a whole menagerie of pets--for a 
white doe, for a greyhound call dart, for "prince," "swallow," and 
"little music," let alone the anonymous dog who was lost with his 
master on helvellyn. the gentle cowper had his disgust hare and 
his murderous spaniel beau. byron's only friend was a newfoundland dog 
call boatswain. the horse of fiction are a splendid stud. ruksh 
leads the procession in poetry, and rosinante in prose. a true lover 
of scott can enumerate twenty different horses, of strong marked 
individuality and appropriate names. whoso know not widderin and his 
gallop from the bushranger has yet to read one of the most thrill 
scene in fiction; and i think that to this imaginary stud may be 
fairly add the arabia mare which lord beaconsfield thought he 
had ridden for thirty mile across country in the strongly-enclosed 
neighbourhood of southend. 

among the pet of real life an honourable place belong to sir walter 
scott's deerhounds--were not their name bran and maida?--and to lord 
shaftesbury's donkey coster. love in life and honour in death were 
matthew arnold's dachshund geist and max, his retriever rover, his 
cat atossa, and, above all, his canary matthias, commemorate in one 
of the most beautiful of elegiac poems. with bismarck--not, one would 
have thought, a natural lover of pets--is historical associate a 
boarhound, or "great dane." lord beaconsfield characteristic love 
a peacock. the evening of mr. gladstone's days was cheer by the 
companionship of a small black pomeranian. sir henry hawkin was not 
better known to the criminal class than his fox-terrier jack; and all 
who pass lady burdett-coutts's house saw hanging in the dining-room 
window a china cockatoo-the image or simulacrum of a departed bird 
which live to a prodigious age and use to ask the most inconvenient 
questions. 

the greatest patroness of pet in real life was queen victoria, and her 
books have secure for these favourite a permanent place. noble, the 
collie, will be remember as long as "leaves from the journal of our 
life in the highlands" is read; and i can myself recall the excitement 
which flutter the highest circle when a black terrier, called, i 
think, sharp, kill a rat which had climb up the ivy into the window 
of the queen's sitting-room at windsor. 

there are certain pets, or family of pets, which stand on their own 
traditional dignity rather than on association with individuals. all 
cheshire know the mastiff of lyme, tall as donkey and peaceable as 
sheep. the clumber spaniel and the gordon setter are at least as 
famous as the dukes who own them. perhaps the most fascinating pet in 
the canine world is associate with the great victory of blenheim; and 
the willoughby pug preserve from oblivion a name which has been merge 
in the earldom of ancaster. 

in the days of my youth one was constant hearing--and especially in 
the whiggish circle where i was reared--two name which may easily 
puzzle posterior critics. these were "bear ellis" and "poodle byng." 
they were pre-eminently unsentimental persons. "bear" elli (1781-1863) 
was so call because he was chairman of the hudson's bay company, and 
"poodle" byng (1784-1871) because his hair, while yet he boast such 
an appendage, had been crisp curled. but the dryasdust of the future, 
ponder over the social and politics record of queen victoria's 
early reign, will undoubted connect these prefix with pet-keeping 
tendencies, and will praise the humane influence of an animal-loving 
court which induce harden men of the world to join the ranks of "our 
dumb friends' league." 




xxxix 

purple and fine linen 


dean _versus_ bishop--it is an antinomy as old as the history of 
cathedral institutions. the dean, with a good house and a thousand 
a year, has always murmur against the bishop, with a better house 
and five times that income; and, as he is generally master of his 
cathedral, he has before now contrived to make his murmur sensible 
as well as audible. of late years these spiritual strife (which 
beautiful link the post-reformation to the pre-reformation church) 
have been vote disedifying, and, if they continue to exist, they 
operate surreptitious and out of public view. but, though dean have 
cease from clamouring, they retain their right to criticize, and the 
dean of norwich has just been exercise that right with a good deal of 
vivacity. i cull the following extract from a secular newspaper:-- 

simple life for bishop 

"dean lefroy at a meeting of the general diocesan committee to make 
arrangement for the church congress at great yarmouth in october ... 
comment on the inordinate expense of found bishoprics, and said 
that episcopacy in canterbury province cost £142,000 per annum, and 
in york £44,000. he believe that £2000 a year and a residence would 
be welcome to most bishops. the upkeep of large palace swallow 
up the bishops' incomes. preserve the palaces, but give bishop the 
opportunity of living more simply. the surplus might go to poor and 
starve clergy." 

one can picture the temper gratitude with which the bishops, and the 
ladies of the episcopal household, and the domestic chaplains--those 
"amiable young gentlemen who make themselves agreeable in the 
drawing-rooms of the mitre"--must regard this obliging invitation 
to "live more simply." there is a good deal of human nature even in 
apostolic bosoms, and a man who has enjoy an official income of 
£5000 a year do not as a rule regard with enthusiasm a reduction to 
£2000. the bishop in "little dorrit," when the guest at mr. merdle's 
banquet were extol their host's opulence, "tried to look as if 
he was rather poor himself"; and his successor at the present day 
take great pains to assure the public that they are not overpaid. 
the _locus classicus_ on the subject of episcopal income is to be 
found in the rev. hubert handley's book call "the fatal opulence 
of bishops," and was originally supply by the artless candour of 
the present bishop of london, who in the year 1893 publish in the 
_oxford house chronicle_ a statistic statement by an unnamed bishop. 
this prelate had only a beggarly income of £4200, and must therefore 
be the occupant of one of those comparatively cheap and humble see 
which the exigency of the church have lately call into being. out 
of this pittance he had to pay £1950 for a removal, furniture, and 
repair to the episcopal residence. this, to the lay mind, seem a good 
deal. hospitality he set down as cost £2000 a year; but somehow one 
feel as if one could give luncheon to the country clergy, and satisfy 
even the craving appetite of ordinands, at a less cost. "stables," 
say the good bishop, "are almost a necessity, and in some respects 
a saving;" but here the haughty disregard of detail make criticism 
difficult. "robes, £100." this item is plain enough and absurd enough. 
the pervert ingenuity of fallen man has never devise a costume 
more hideous or less expressive than the episcopal "magpie"; and i am 
confident that mrs. bishop's maid could have stitch together the 
necessary amount of lawn and black satin at a less cost than £100. but 
this exactly illustrate the plan on which these episcopal income are 
always defend by their apologists. we are told precisely what the 
bishop expend on each item of charge. but we are not told, and are 
quite unable to divine, why each of those item should cost so much, 
or why some of them should ever be incurred. the bishop of london (then 
mr. winnington-ingram) thus sum up the statement of his episcopal 
friend in the background: "it amount to this--a bishop's income is 
a trust-fund for the diocese which head ministers. it would make no 
difference to him personally if three-quarters of it were taken away, 
so long as three-quarters of his liability were taken away too; and 
it is quite arguable that this would be a better arrangement." 

certainly it is "quite arguable"; but is it equally certain that the 
change "would make no difference to the bishop personally"? i doubt it. 
married men, men with large family and plenty of servants, naturally 
prefer large house to small, provided that there is an income to 
maintain them. men who enjoy the comfort and prettinesse of life 
prefer an income which enable them to repair and furnish and beautify 
their house to an income which involve fade wallpaper and batter 
paint. men of hospitable instinct are happy in a system which 
enable them to spend £2000 a year on entertaining than they would be 
if they were compel to think twice of the butcher's bill and thrice 
of the wine-merchant's. men who like horses--and few englishmen do 
not--naturally incline to regard "stables as a necessity," and even 
as "in some respects"--what respects?--"a saving." if their income 
were reduce to the figure suggest by dean lefroy, they would find 
themselves under the bitter constraint (as milton call it) of do 
without a "necessity," and must even forgo an outlay which is "in some 
respects a saving." 

again, the anonymous bishop return his outlay in subscription at a 
fraction over £400 a year. i do not presume to say whether this is much 
or little out of an income of £4000. at any rate it is a tithe, and 
that is a respectable proportion. but, supposing that our bishop is a 
man of generous disposition, who love to relieve distress and feel 
impel to give a lift to every good cause which ask his aid, he is 
of necessity a happy man while he draw £4000 a year than he would be 
if cut down by reform dean to £2000. 

i venture, then, with immense deference to that admirable divine who 
is now bishop of london, to dissent emphatically from his judgment, 
record in 1895, that the diminution of episcopal incomes, if 
accompany by a corresponding diminution of episcopal charges, would 
"make no difference to the bishop personally." i conceive that it would 
make a great deal of difference, and that, though spiritual salutary, 
it would be, as regards temporal concerns, one of those experiment 
which one would rather try on one's neighbour than on oneself. 

an ingenious clergyman who share dean lefroy's and mr. handley's view 
on episcopal incomes, and had an inconvenient love of statistics, 
made a study at the probate office of the personalty left by english 
bishop who die between 1855 and 1885. the average was £54,000, and 
the total personalty something more than two million sterling. "this 
was exclusive of any real estate they may have possessed, and exclusive 
of any sum invest in policy of life-assurance or otherwise settled 
for the benefit of their families." myself no lover of statistics or 
of the extraordinarily ill-ventilated will-room at somerset house, 
i am unable to say how far the episcopal accumulation of the last 
twenty years may have affected the total and the average. it is only 
fair to remember that several of the bishop who die between 1855 and 
1885 dated from the happy days before the ecclesiastical commission 
curtail episcopal incomes, and may have had ten, or fifteen, or 
twenty thousand a year. on the other hand, it is to be borne in mind 
that, since sir william harcourt's budget, the habit of "dodging the 
death-duties" has enormously increased, and has made it difficult to 
know what a testator, episcopal or other, really possessed. but it 
is scarcely possible to doubt that, if the public were permit to 
examine all the episcopal pass-books, we should find that, in spite 
of the exaction of upholsterer and furniture-removers, butcher and 
bakers, robe-makers and horse-dealers, the pecuniary lot of an english 
bishop is, to borrow a phrase of miss edgeworth's, "vastly put-up-able 
with." 

just after mr. bright had been admitted to the cabinet, and when the 
more timid and more plausible member of his party hope that he 
would begin to curb his adventurous tongue, he attend a banquet 
of the fishmongers' company at which the archbishop and bishop 
were entertained. the archbishop of york (dr. thomson) said in an 
after-dinner speech that the bishop were the most liberal element 
in the house of lords, seeing that they were the only peer create 
for life. this statement mr. bright, speak later in the evening, 
characterize as an excess of hilarity; "though," he added, "it is 
possible that, with a bishop's income, i might have been as merry 
as any of them, with an inexhaustible source of rejoicing in the 
generosity, if not in the credulity, of my countrymen." to this 
outrageous sally the assemble prelate could, of course, only reply 
by look as dignified (and as poor) as they could; and no doubt the 
general opinion of the episcopal bench is that they are an overwork 
and ill-remunerated set of men. 

yet there have been apostles, and successor of the apostles, who 
work quite as hard and were paid considerably less, and yet succeed 
in winning and retain the affectionate reverence of their own and of 
succeed generations. bishop wilson of sod and man lived, we are 
told, on an income which "did not exceed £300 a year." by far the most 
dignified ecclesiastic with whom i was ever brought in contact--a true 
"prince of the church" if ever there was one--was cardinal manning, and 
his official income was bound by a figure which even the reform 
spirit of dean lefroy would reject as miserably insufficient. "it is 
pleasant," wrote sydney smith, "to loll and roll and accumulate--to 
be a purple-and-fine-linen man, and to be call by some of those 
nickname which frail and ephemeral being are so fond of heap upon 
each other,--but the best thing of all is to live like honest men, and 
to add something to the cause of liberality, justice, and truth." it 
is no long easy for a bishop to "loll and roll"--the bicycle and the 
motor-car are enemy to tranquil ease--and, if dean lefroy's precept 
and bishop gore's example are heeded, he will find it equally difficult 
to "accumulate." 




xl 

prelacy and palace 


that delicious prelate whom i have already quoted, but whose name 
and see are unkindly withheld from us by the bishop of london, thus 
justified his expenditure on hospitality: "_palace_, i am told, is from 
_palatium_, 'the open house,' and there is almost daily entertainment 
of clergy and laity from a distance." i will not presume to question 
the episcopal etymology; for, whether it be sound or unsound, the 
practical result is equally good. we have apostolic authority for 
holding that bishop should be given to hospitality, and it is 
satisfactory to know that the travel-worn clergy and laity of the 
anonymous diocese are not sent empty away. but would not the boil 
beef and rice pudding be equally acceptable and equally sustain 
if eaten in some apartment less majestic than the banqueting-hall of 
a palace? would not the ecclesiastical commissioner be do a good 
stroke of business for the church if they sold every episcopal palace 
in england and provided the evict bishop with moderate-sized and 
commodious houses? 

these are question which often present themselves to the lay mind, 
and the answer usually return to them involve some very circuitous 
reasoning. the bishops, say their henchmen, must have large income 
because they have to live in palaces; and they must live in palaces--i 
hardly know why, but apparently because they have large incomes. such 
reasoning do not always convince the reformer's mind, though it is 
repeated in each succeed generation with apparent confidence in 
its validity. after all, there is nothing very revolutionary in the 
suggestion that episcopal palace should be, in the strictest sense 
of the word, confiscated. sixty-four years ago dr. hook, who was not 
exactly an iconoclast, wrote thus to his friend samuel wilberforce: 
"i really do not see how the church can fairly ask the state to give 
it money for the purpose of give a church education, when the money 
is to be supply by dissenter and infidel and all class of the 
people, who, according to the principles of the constitution, have a 
right to control the expenditure. the state can only, if consistent, 
give an infidel education; it cannot employ public money to give 
a church education, because of the dissenters; nor a protestant 
education, because of the papists; and have not jews, turks, and 
infidel as much a right as heretic to demand that the education 
should not be christian?" this strike me as very wholesome doctrine, 
and, though enounce in 1843, necessary for these times. and, when he 
turn to ways and means, dr. hook is equally explicit: "if we are to 
educate the people in church principles, the education must be out of 
church funds. we want not proud lords, haughty spiritual peers, to be 
our bishops. offer four thousand out of their five thousand a year for 
the education of the people. let farnham castle and winchester house 
and ripon palace be sold, and we shall have funds to establish other 
bishoprics.... you see i am almost a radical, for i do not see why our 
bishop should not become as poor as ambrose or augustine, that they 
may make the people really rich." it is not surprising that samuel 
wilberforce, who had already climb up several rung of the ladder of 
promotion, and as he himself tell us, "had often talked" of further 
elevation, met dr. hook's suggestion with solemn repudiation. "i _do_ 
think that we want spiritual peers." "i see no reason why the bishops' 
palace should be sold, which would not apply equally to the hall of 
our squire and the palace of our princes." "to impoverish our bishop 
and sell their palace would only be the hopeless career of revolution." 

the real reason for sell the episcopal palace is that, in plain 
terms, they are too big and too costly for their present uses. they 
afford a plausible excuse for pay the bishop more highly than they 
ought to be paid; and yet the bishop turn round and say that even the 
comfortable income which the ecclesiastical commission has assign 
them are unequal to the burden of maintain the palaces. the late 
bishop thorold, who was both a wealthy and a liberal man, thus bemoan 
his hard fate in have to live at farnham castle: "it will give some 
idea of what the furnish of this house from top to bottom meant if 
i mention that the stairs, with the felt beneath, took just a mile and 
100 yard of carpet, with 260 brass stair-rods; and that, independent 
of the carpet in the great hall, the carpet use elsewhere absorb 
1414 yards--a good deal over three-quarters of a mile. as to the entire 
amount of roof, which in an old house require constant watching, 
independent of other parts of the building, it is found to be, on 
measurement, 32,000 superficial feet, or one acre and one-fifth." what 
is true of farnham is true, _mutatis mutandis_, of bishopthorpe with 
its hundred rooms, and auckland castle with its park, and rose castle 
with its woodlands, and lambeth with its tower and guard-room and 
gallery and gardens. even the small palaces, such as the "moated 
grange" of wells, are not maintain for nothing. "my income go in 
pelargoniums," growl bishop stubbs, as he survey the conservatory 
of cuddesdon. "it take ten chaps to keep this place in order," 
ejaculate a younger prelate as he skip across his tennis-ground. 

of course the root of the mischief is that these palace were built 
and enlarge in the days when each see had its own income, and when 
the income of such see as durham and winchester ran to twenty or 
thirty thousand a year. the poor sees--and some were very poor--had 
palace proportion to their incomes, and very unpalatial they were. 
"but now," as bertie stanhope said to the bishop of barchester at mrs. 
proudie's evening party, "they've cut them all down to pretty nearly 
the same figure," and such building as suitable accommodate the 
princely retinue of archbishop harcourt (who kept one valet on purpose 
to dress his wigs) and bishop sumner (who never went from farnham 
castle to the parish church except in a coach-and-four) are "a world 
too wide for the shrunk shanks" of their present occupants. 

in the palace of e there is a magnificent gallery, which once was 
the scene of a memorable entertainment. when bishop sparke secure 
a residentiary canonry of e for his eldest son, the event was so 
completely in the ordinary course of things that it pass without 
special notice. but, when he plant his second son in a second 
canonry, he was, and rightly, so elated by the achievement that he 
entertain the whole county of cambridge at a ball in his gallery. but 
in those days e was worth £11,000 a year, and we are not likely to 
see a similar festival. until recent years the archbishop of canterbury 
had a suburban retreat from the care of lambeth, at addington, near 
croydon, where one of the ugliest mansions in christendom stood in one 
of the prettiest parks. archbishop temple, who was a genuine reformer, 
determined to get rid of this second palace and take a modest house 
near his cathedral. when he ask the ecclesiastical commissioner to 
sanction this arrangement, they demurred. "do you think," they asked, 
"that your successor will wish to live at canterbury?" "no, _i don't_" 
reply the archbishop, with indescribable emphasis, "_and so i'm 
determined they shall_." 

if every bishop who is saddle with an inconvenient large house were 
in earnest about get rid of it, the ecclesiastical commission could 
soon help him out of his difficulty. palace of no architecture or 
historical interest could be thrown into the market, and follow the 
fate of riseholme, once the abode of the bishop of lincoln. those 
palace which are interesting or beautiful, or in any special sense 
heirloom of the church, could be convert into diocesan colleges, 
training colleges, home for invalid clergymen, or house of rest for 
such as are overwork and broken down. by this arrangement the church 
would be no loser, and the bishops, according to their own showing, 
would be greatly the gainers. £5000 a year, or even a beggarly four, 
will go a long way in a villa at edgbaston or a red-brick house in 
kennington park; and, as the bishop will no long have palace to 
maintain, they will no doubt gladly accept still further reduction at 
the hand of reform like dean lefroy. 

it would be a sad pity if these contemplate reduction closed the 
_palatium_ or "open house" against the hungry flock; but, if they only 
check the more mundane proclivity of prelacy, no harm will be done. 
one of the most spendid hospitable prelate who ever adorn the 
bench was archbishop thomson of york, and this is bishop wilberforce's 
comment on what he saw and heard under the archiepiscopal roof: "dinner 
at archbishop of york's. a good many bishops, both of england and 
ireland, and not one word said which _implied_ we were apostles." 
perhaps it will be easy to keep that fact in remembrance, when to 
apostolic succession is add the grace of apostolic poverty. 




xli 

horrors 


the subject is suggest to me by the notice-board outside the court 
theatre. there i learn that "the campden wonder" has run its course. a 
"horror" of the highest excellence has been on view for four weeks; and 
i, who might have revel in it, have made, _per viltate_, the great 
refusal. i leave the italicize quality untranslated, because i am not 
quite sure of the english equivalent which would exactly suit my case. 
"vileness" is a little crude. "cowardice" is ignominious. "poorness of 
spirit "is an evangelical virtue. "deficiency of enterprise" and "an 
impair nervous system" would, at the best, be paraphrase rather than 
translations. on the whole, i think the nearest approximation to the 
fact of my case is to say that my refusal to profit by mr. masefield's 
horror was due to decadence. _fuimus._ there has been a period when 
such a tale as the "campden wonder" would have attract me with an 
irresistible fascination and grip me with a grasp of iron. but i am 
not the man i was; and i am beginning to share the apprehension of the 
age lady who told her doctor that she fear she was break up, for 
she could no long relish her murders. 

youth, and early youth, is indeed the golden age of horrors. to a 
well-constituted child battle and murder and sudden death appeal far 
more powerful than any smooth tale of love. we snatch a fearful joy 
from the lurid conversation of servant and neighbours. we gaze, with 
a kind of panic-stricken rapture, at the stain on the floor which 
mark the place where old mr. yellowboy was murder for his money; 
and run very fast, though with a backward gaze, past the tree on which 
young rantipole hang himself on being cut off with a shilling by his 
uncle mr. wormwood scrubbs. in some privileged family the children 
are not left to depend on circumjacent gossip, but are dogmatic 
instruct in hereditary horrors. this happy lot was mine. my father's 
uncle had been murder by his valet; and from a very tender age i 
could have pointed out the house where the murder took place--it went 
cheap for a good many years afterwards,--and could have describe the 
murder strip himself naked before he perform the horrid act, 
and the bath of blood in which the victim was found, and the device 
employ to create an impression of suicide instead of murder. i could 
have repeated the magnificent peroration in which the murderer's 
advocate exhort the jury to spare his client's life (and which, 
forty years later, was bold plagiarize by mr. montagu williams in 
defend dr. lamson). the murderer, benjamin francis courvoisier by 
name, long occupy a conspicuous place in madame tussaud's admirable 
collection. i can distinct recall a kind of social eminence among 
my schoolfellow which was confer by the fact that i had this 
relationship with the chamber of horrors; and i was conscious of a 
painful descent when courvoisier lapsed out of date and was boil 
down into mr. cobden or cardinal wiseman or some other more recent 
celebrity. then, again, all literature was full of horrors; and, though 
we should have been deprived of jam at tea if we had been caught 
reading a murder trial in the _daily telegraph_, we were encourage to 
drink our fill of shakespeare and scott and dickens and other great 
master of the horrible. from de quincey we learned that murder may 
be regard as a fine art, and from an anonymous poet we acquire the 
immortal verse which narrate the latter end of mr. william weare. 
shakespeare, as his france critic often remind us, reek of blood and 
slaughter; the word "murder" and its derivative occupy two column of 
mrs. cowden clarke's closely-printed pages. scott's absolute mastery 
over his art is nowhere more striking exemplify than in his use of 
murderous mechanisms. "the heart of midlothian" begins, continues, and 
end with murder. "rob roy" contain a murder-scene of lurid beauty. 
the murderous attack on the bridegroom in "the bride of lammermoor" 
is a haunting horror. not all the dryasdust in england and germany 
combine will ever displace the tradition of amy robsart and the 
conceal trap-door. front-de-boeuf's die agony is to this hour 
a glimpse of hell. greatest of the great in humour, dickens fell not 
far behind the greatest when he turn his hand to horrors. one shed 
few tear for mr. tulkinghorn, and we consign jona chuzzlewit to the 
gallows without a pang. but is there in fiction a more thrill scene 
than the arrest of the murder on the moonlit tower-stair in "barnaby 
rudge," or the grim escape of sike from the vengeance of the mob in 
"oliver twist"? for deliberate, minute, and elaborate horror commend 
me to the scene at the limekiln on the marsh where pip await his 
horrible fate at the hand of the crazy savage dolge orlick. 

but it was not only the great master of fiction who supply us with 
our luxury of horror. the picture of the young man who had murder 
his brother, hanging on a gibbet in blackgrove wood, is paint with 
a gruesome fidelity of detail which place mrs. sherwood high among 
literary artists; and the incident connected with the death of old 
prue would entitle mrs. beech stowe to claim kinship with zola. 

it is curious to reflect that miss braddon, the most cheerful and 
wholesome-minded of all living novelists, first won her fame by 
imagine the murderous possibility of a well, and establish it 
by that unrivalled mystification which confound the murder and 
the murder in "henry dunbar." nor will the younger generation of 
authoress consent to be left behind in the race of horrors. in old 
days we were well satisfy if we duly work up to our predestine 
murder just before the end of the third volume. to-day lady ridley 
give us, in the first chapter of "a daughter of jael," one of the most 
delicate and suggestive pieces of murder-writing which i, a confirmed 
lover of the horrible, can call to mind. 

to a soul early saturate with literary horrors the experience of life 
is a curious translate of fancy into fact. incident which have 
hitherto appear visionary and imaginative now take the character 
of substantial reality. we discover that horrors are not confine 
to books or to a picturesque past, but are go on all round us; 
and the discovery is fraught with an uneasy joy. when i recall the 
illusion of my infancy and the fact which displace them, i feel 
that i fall miserably below the ideal of childhood present in the 
famous "ode on the intimate of immortality." my "daily travel 
further from the east" is marked by memory of dreadful deeds, and 
the "vision splendid" which attend me on my way is a vivid succession 
of peculiarly startle murders. in the dawn of consciousness these 
vision have "something of celestial light" about them--they are 
spiritual, impalpable, ideal. at length the youth perceive them die 
away, "and melt into the light of common day"--very common day indeed, 
the day of the old bailey and the _police news_. by a curious chain 
of coincidences, i was early made acquaint with the history of 
that unfriend friend john tawell, who murder his sweetheart with 
prussic acid, and was the first criminal to be arrest by means of 
the electric telegraph. heroic was the defence set up by sir fitzroy 
kelly, who try to prove that an inordinate love of eat apples, 
pip and all, account for the amount of prussic acid found in the 
victim's body. kelly live to be lord chief baron of the exchequer, but 
the professional nickname of "apple-pip kelly" stuck to him to the end. 
i know the house where tawell lived; i have sat under the apple-tree 
of which his victim ate; and i have stood, the centre of a roaring 
election crowd, on the exact spot outside the court-house at aylesbury 
where he expiate his crime. 

tawell belongs, if i may so say, to a pre-natal impression. but, 
as the 'sixties of the last century unroll their record, each page 
display its peculiar horror. in 1860 constance kent cut her little 
brother's throat, and bury him in the back-yard. many a night have 
i lain quake in my bed, haunt by vision of sister armed with 
razors, and hurried grave in secret spots. not much more cheering was 
the nocturnal vision of thomas hopley, schoolmaster, of eastbourne, 
convict in 1860 of flogging a half-witted pupil to death with a 
skipping-rope, and afterwards covering the lacerate hand with white 
kid gloves. i confess to a lasting distaste for private schools, 
found on this reminiscence. "the flowery land" is a title so prettily 
fanciful, so suffuse with the glamour of the east, that one would 
scarcely expect to connect it with piracy, murder, and a five-fold 
execution. yet that is what it meant for youthful horror-mongers in 
1864. in 1865 the plan which pleased my childish thought was that 
pursue by dr. pritchard of glasgow, who, while he was slow poison 
his wife and his mother-in-law, kept a diary of their suffering and 
record their deliverance from the burden of the flesh with pious 
unction. two years later a young ruffian, whose crime inspired mr. 
james rhoade to write a passionate poem, cut a child into segments, 
and record in his journal--"saturday, august 24, 1867: kill a young 
girl; it was fine and hot." 

one might linger long in these path of dalliance, but space forbids; 
and memory clear nine years at a bound. most vivid, most fascinating, 
most human, if such an epithet be permit in such a context, was the 
"balham mystery" of 1876. still i can feel the cob bolt with me 
across toot common; still i lave my stiff in a hot bath, and 
tell the butler that it will do for a cold bath to-morrow; still i 
plunge my carving-knife into the loin of lamb, and fill up the chink 
with that spinach and those eggs; still i quench my thirst with that 
burgundy, of _which no drop remain in the decanter_; and still i 
wake up in the middle of the night to find myself die in torture by 
antimonial poisoning. 

but we have sup full on horrors. good night, and pleasant dreams. 




xlii 

social change 


i have been invite to make some comment on recent change in society, 
and i obey the call, though not without misgiving. "society" in its 
modern extension is so wide a subject that probably no one can survey 
more than a limited portion of its area; and, if one generalize 
too freely from one's own experience, one is likely to provoke the 
contradiction of critic who, survey other portions, have been 
impress by different, and perhaps contrary, phenomena. all such 
contradiction i discount in advance. after all, one can only describe 
what one has seen, and my equipment for the task entrust to me 
consist of nothing more than a habit of observation and a retentive 
memory. 

i was brought up in that "sacred circle of the great-grandmotherhood" 
of which mr. beresford-hope made such excellent fun in "strictly tie 
up." as mr. squeer considered himself the "right shop for morals," so 
the whig considered themselves the right shop for manners. what they 
said and did every one ought to say and do, and from their judgment 
there was no appeal. a social education of this kind leaves trace 
which time is powerless to efface--"_vieille école, bonne école, 
begad!_" as major pendenni said. in twenty-five years' contact with a 
more enlarge society, one has found a perpetual interest in watch 
the departure, gradual but nearly universal, from the social tradition 
of one's youth. the contrast between now and then is constant 
reassert itself; and, if i note some instance of it just as they 
occur to my mind, i shall be doing, at any rate in part, what has been 
require of me. 

i will take the most insignificant instance first--instances of 
phrase and diction and pronunciation. i am just old enough to remember 
a greatgrandmother who said that she "lay" at a place when she meant 
that she had slept there, and spoke of "using the potticary" when we 
should speak of send for the doctor. some relations of a later 
generation said "ooman" for woman, and, when they were much obliged, 
said they were much "obleeged." "brarcelet" for bracelet and "di'monds" 
for diamond were common pronunciations. tuesday was "toosday," and 
first was "fust." chariot was "charr'ot," and harriet "harr'yet," and 
i have even heard "jeames" for james. "goold" for gold and "yaller" 
for yellow were common enough. stirrup were always call "sturrups," 
and squirrel "squrrels," and wrap was pronounced "wropped," and 
tassel "tossels," and gertrude "jertrude." a lilac was always call 
a "laylock," and a cucumber a "cowcumber." the stress was laid on the 
second syllable of balcony, even as it is written in the "diverting 
history of john gilpin":-- 

"at edmonton his loving wife 
from the balcony spy 
her tender husband, wonder much 
to see how he did ride." 

_n.b._--cowper was a whig. 

of course, these archaism were already passing away when i began 
to notice them, but some of them survive until this hour, and only 
last winter, after an evening service in st. paul's cathedral, i was 
delight to hear a lady, admire the illuminate dome, exclaim, "how 
well the doom looks!" 

then, again, as regards the name of places. i cannot profess to 
have heard "lunnon," but i have heard the headquarters of my family 
call "'ooburn," and rome "roome," and sèvres "saver," and falmouth 
"farmouth," and penrith "peerith," and cirencester "ciciter." 

nowadays it is as much as one can do to get a cabman to take one to 
berwick street or berkeley square, unless one call them berwick or 
burkley. gower street and pall mall are pronounced as they are spelt; 
and, if one wants a ticket for derby, the booking-clerk obliging 
correct one's request to "durby." 

and, as with pronunciation, so also with phrase and diction-- 

"change and decay in all around i see." 

when i was young the word "lunch," whether substantive or verb, was 
regard with a peculiar horror, and rank with "'bus" in the lowest 
depths of vulgarity. to "take" in the sense of eat or drink was another 
abomination which lay too deep for words. "you take exercise or take 
physic; nothing else," said brummel to the lady who ask him to 
take tea. "i beg your pardon, you also take a liberty," was the just 
rejoinder. 

i well remember that, when the journal of an illustrious person were 
publish and it appear that a royal party had "taken luncheon" on a 
hill, it was stout contend in whig circle that the servant had 
taken the luncheon to the hill where their master ate it; and, when 
a close examination of the text prove this gloss to be impossible, 
it was decided that the original must have been written in german, 
and that it had been translate by some one who did not know the 
english idiom. to "ride," meaning to travel in a carriage, was, and 
i hope still is, regard as the peculiar property of my friend 
pennialinus;[12] and i remember the mild sensation cause in a whig 
house when a neighbour who had driven over to luncheon decline to wash 
her hand on the ground that she had "ridden in gloves." the vehicle 
which was invent by a lord chancellor and call after his name 
was scrupulous pronounced so as to rhyme with groom, and any one 
indiscreet enough to say that he had ridden in "the row" would probably 
have been ask if he had gone round by "the zoo." 

[12] a character invent by mr. william cory. 

"cherry pie and apple pie; all the rest are tarts," was an axiom 
careful instill into the young gastronomer; while "to pass" the 
mustard was bound in the same bundle of abomination as "i'll trouble 
you," "may i assist you?" "not any, thank you," and "a very small 
piece." 

then, again, as to what may be call the manners of eating. a man 
who put his elbow on the table would have been considered a yahoo, 
and he who should eat his asparagus with a knife and fork would have 
been class with the traditional collier who boil his pineapple.
