fish-knives (like oxidize silver biscuit-boxes) were unknown and 
undreamt-of horrors. to eat one's fish with two fork was the _cachet_ 
of a certain circle, and the manner of manipulate the stone of 
a cherry pie was the _articulus stanti vel cadentis_. the little 
daughter of a great whig house, whose eat habit had been contract 
in the nursery, once ask her mother with wistful longing, "mamma, 
when shall i be old enough to eat bread and cheese with a knife, and 
put the knife in my mouth?" and she was prompt informed that not 
if she live to attain the age of methuselah would she be able to 
acquire that "unchartered freedom." on the other hand, old gentlemen 
of the very highest breeding use after dinner to rinse their mouth 
in their finger-glasses, and thereby cause unspeakable qualm in 
unaccustomed guests. in that respect at any rate, if in no other, the 
most inveterate praise of times past must admit that alteration has 
not been deterioration. 

another marked change in society is the diminution of stateliness. 
a really well-turned-out carriage, with a coachman in a wig and two 
powdered footmen behind, is as rare an object in the mall as a hansom 
in bermondsey or a tandem in bethnal green. men go to the levée in 
cab or on motor-cars, and send their wives to the palace ball in 
the product of the coupé company. the dowager duchess of cleveland 
(1792-1883) once told me that lord salisbury had no carriage. on my 
express innocent surprise, she said, "i have been told that lord 
salisbury go about london in a brougham;" and her tone could not 
have express a more lively horror if the vehicle had been a coster's 
barrow. people of a less remote date than the duchess's had become 
inure to barouche for ladies and brougham for men, but a landau 
was contemne under the derogatory nickname of a "demi-fortune," and 
the spectacle of a great man scale the dizzy heights of the 'bus or 
plunge into the depths of the twopenny tube would have given rise to 
lively comment. 

a pillar of the tory party, who die not twenty years ago, finding his 
newly-married wife poke the fire, took the poker from her hand and 
said with majestic pain, "my dear, will you kindly remember that you 
are now a countess?" a liberal statesman, still living, when he went to 
harrow for the first time, sail up the hill in the family coach, and 
tradition do not report that his schoolfellow kick him with any 
special virulence. 

i have known people who in travel would take the whole of a 
first-class carriage soon than risk the intrusion of an unknown 
fellow-passenger: their descendant would as likely as not reach their 
destination on motor-cars, have pull up at some wayside inn for 
mutton chop and whisky-and-soda. 




xliii 

social graces 


though stately has palpable diminished, the beauty of life has as 
palpable increased. in old days people loved, or professed to love, 
fine pictures, and those who had them made much of them. but with 
that one exception no one made any attempt to surround himself with 
beautiful objects. people who happen to have fine furniture use 
it because they had it; unless, indeed, the desire to keep pace with 
the fashion induce them to part with louis seize or chippendale and 
replace it by the austere production of tottenham court road. the 
idea of buy a chimneypiece or a cabinet or a bureau because it was 
beautiful never cross the ordinary mind. the finest old english china 
was habitual used, and not seldom smashed, in the housekeeper's 
room. it was the age of horse-hair and mahogany, and crimson flock 
papers and green rep curtains. whatever ornament the house happen to 
possess were cluster together on a round table in the middle of the 
drawing-room. the style has been immortalize by the hand of a master: 
"there were no skilful contrast shades of grey or green, no dado, 
no distemper. the woodwork was grain and varnish after the manner 
of the philistines, the wall paper in dark crimson, with heavy 
curtains of the same colour, and the sideboard, dinner-waggon, and row 
of stiff chair were all carve in the same massive and expensive style 
of ugliness. the pictures were those familiar present of dirty 
rabbits, fat white horses, bloated goddesses, and misshapen boor by 
masters, who if younger than they assume to be, must have been quite 
old enough to know better." a man who hung a blue-and-white plate on a 
wall, or put peacocks' feather in a vase, would have been regard as 
insane; and i well remember the outcry of indignation and scorn when 
a well-known collector of bric-a-brac had himself paint with a pet 
teapot in his hands. 

in this respect the change is complete. the owner of fine 
picture-galleries no long monopolize "art in the home." people who 
cannot afford old master invoke the genius of mr. mortimer menpes. 
if they have not inherit france furniture they buy it, or at least 
imitation of the real, which are quite as beautiful. a sage-green 
wash on the wall, and a white dado to the height of a man's shoulder, 
cover a multitude of paper-hanger's sins. the commonest china is 
pretty in form and colour. a couple of rug from liberty's replace the 
hideous and costly carpet which last their unfortunate possessor 
a lifetime; and, whereas in those distant days one never saw a 
flower on a dinner-table, now "it is roses, rose all the way," or, 
when it cannot be roses, it is daffodil and tulip and poppy and 
chrysanthemums. 

all this is the work of the despise æsthetes; but this generation 
will probably see no meaning in the great drama of "patience," and has 
no conception of the tyrannous ugly from which bunthorne and his 
friends deliver us. their double achievement was to make ugly 
culpable, and to prove that beauty need not be expensive. 

the same change may be observe in everything connected with dinner. no 
long is the mind oppress by those monstrous hecatomb under which, 
as bret harte said, "the table groan and even the sideboard sighed." 
frascatelli's monstrous bill of fare, with six "side dishes" and four 
sweets, survive only as monument of what our father could do. racing 
plate and "epergnes," with silver goddesse and sphinx and rams' 
horns, if not discreet exchange for pretty substitutes, hide their 
diminish head in pantry and safes. instead of these horrors, we 
have bright flower and shade lights; and a very few, perhaps too few, 
dishes, which both look pretty and taste good. here, again, expensive 
ugly has been routed, and inexpensive beauty enthroned in its place. 

the same law, i believe, hold good about dress. with the mystery 
of woman's clothes i do not presume to meddle. i do not attempt to 
estimate the relative cost of the satin and ermine and scarf which 
lawrence painted, and the "duck's-egg bolero" and "mauve hopsack" 
which i have lately seen advert in the list of a winter sale. but 
about men's dress i feel more confident. the "rich cut genoa velvet 
waistcoat," the solemn frock coat, the satin stock, and the trousers 
strap under the wellingtons, were certainly hideous, and i shrewd 
suspect that they were vastly more expensive than the blue serge suits, 
straw hats, brown boots, and sailor-knot tie in which the men of the 
present day contrive to look smart without being stiff. 

when mr. gladstone in old age revisite oxford and lecture on home 
to a great gathering of undergraduates, he was ask if he saw any 
difference between his hearer and the men of his own time. he 
respond briskly, "yes, in their dress, an enormous difference. i am 
told that i had among my audience some of the most highly-connected and 
richest men in the university, and there wasn't one whom i couldn't 
have dress from top to toe for £5." 

i have spoken so far of material beauty, and here the change in society 
has been an inexpressible improvement; but, when i turn to beauty of 
another kind, i cannot speak with equal certainty. have our manners 
improved? beyond all question they have changed, but have they change 
for the better? 

it may seem incongruous to cite dr. pusey as an authority on anything 
more mundane than a hair-shirt, yet he was really a close observer 
of social phenomena, as his famous sermon on dive and lazarus, 
with its stricture on the modern dives's dinner and mrs. dives's 
ball-gown, sufficient testifies. he was born a bouverie in 1800, 
when the bouvery still were whigs, and he testify in old age 
to "the beauty of the refined worldly manners of the old school," 
which, as he insisted, were really christian in their regard for the 
feelings of others. "if in any case they became soulless as apart from 
christianity, the beautiful form was there into which real life might 
re-enter." 

we do not, i think, see much of the "beautiful form" nowadays. men 
when talk to women lounge, and sprawl, and cross their legs, and 
keep one hand in a pocket while they shake hand with the other, and 
shove their partner about in the "washington post," and wallow in the 
kitchen-lancers. all this is as little beautiful as can be conceived. 
grace and dignity have perish side by side. and yet, oddly enough, 
the people who are most thorough bereft of manners seem bent on 
display their deficiency in the most conspicuous places. in the 
old days it would have been thought the very height of vulgarity to 
run after royalty. the duke of wellington said to charles greville, 
"when we meet the royal family in society they are our superiors, and 
we owe them all respect." that was just all. if a royal personage knew 
you sufficient well to pay you a visit, it was an honour, and all 
suitable preparation were made. "my father walk backwards with a 
silver candlestick, and red baize await the royal feet." if you 
encounter a prince or princess in society, you made your bow and 
thought no more about it. an old-fashioned father, who had taken a 
schoolboy son to call on a great lady, said, "your bow was too low. 
that is the sort of bow we keep for the royal family." there was 
neither drop-down-dead-ativeness, nor pushfulness, nor familiarity. 
well-bred people knew how to behave themselves, and there was an end 
of the matter. but to force one's self on the notice of royalty, to 
intrigue for visit from illustrious personages, to go out of one's way 
to meet prince or princesses, to parade before the gape world the 
amount of intimacy with which one had been honoured, would have been 
regard as the very madness of vulgarity. 

another respect in which modern manners compare unfavourable with 
ancient is the grow love of titles. in old days people thought a 
great deal, perhaps too much, of family. they had a strong sense of 
territorial position, and i have heard people say of others, "oh, they 
are cousin of ours," as if that fact put them within a sacred and 
inviolable enclosure. but title were contemned. if you were a peer, 
you sate in the house of lords instead of the house of commons; and 
that was all. no one dream of babble about "peers" as a separate 
order of creation, still less of enumerate the peer to whom they 
were related. 

a member of the tory government was once at pains to explain to an 
entire unsympathetic audience that the only reason why he and lord 
curzon had not taken as good a degree as mr. asquith was that, being 
the eldest son of peers, they were more freely invite into the county 
society of oxfordshire. i can safe say that, in the sacred circle of 
the great-grandmotherhood, that theory of academic shortcoming would 
not have been advanced. 

the idea of buy a baronetcy would have been thought simply droll, 
and knighthood was regard as the guerdon of the successful grocer. i 
believe that in their inmost heart the whig enjoy the garter which 
were so freely bestow on them; but they compound for that human 
weakness by unmeasured contempt for the bath, and i doubt if they had 
ever heard of the star of india. to state this case is sufficient to 
illustrate a conspicuous change in the sentiment of society. 




xliv 

publicity _v._ reticence 


the great people of old time follow (quite unconsciously) the 
philosopher who bade man "hide his life." of course, the stage of 
politics was always a pillory, and he who venture to stand on it made 
up his mind to encounter a vast variety of popular missiles. "in my 
situation as chancellor of the university of oxford," said the duke 
of wellington, "i have been much expose to authors;" and men whom 
choice or circumstances forced into politics were expose to worse 
annoyance than "authors." but the line was rigid drawn between 
public and private life. what went on in the home was sacred secrete 
from the public gaze. people live among their relations and friends 
and politics associates, and kept the gape world at a distance. now 
we worship publicity as the chief enjoyment of human life. we send 
lists of our shooting-parties to "society journals." we welcome the 
interviewer. we contribute personal paragraph to _classy cuttings_. 
we admit the photographer to our bedrooms, and give our portrait to 
illustrate papers. we take our exercise when we have the best chance 
of being seen and noticed, and we never eat our dinner with such keen 
appetite as amid the half-world of a piccadil restaurant. in brief, 
"expose thy life" is the motto of the new philosophy, and i maintain 
that in this respect, at any rate, the old was better. 

with an increase love of publicity has come an increase contempt 
for reticence. in old days there were certain subject which no one 
mentioned; among them were health and money. i presume that people had 
pretty much the same complaint as now, but no one talk about them. 
we use to be told of a lady who die in agony because she insist on 
telling the doctor that the pain was in her chest whereas it really was 
in the unmentionable organ of digestion. that martyr to propriety has 
no imitator in the present day. every one has a disease and a doctor; 
and young people of both sex are ready on the slightest acquaintance 
to describe symptom and compare experiences. "ice!" exclaim a 
pretty girl at dessert, "good gracious, no! so bad for indy"--and her 
companion, who had not travelled with the times, learned with amaze 
that "indy" was the pet name for indigestion. "how bitter cold!" said 
a plump matron at an open-air luncheon; "just the thing to give one 
appendicitis." "oh!" said her neighbour, survey the company, "we are 
quite safe there. i shouldn't think we had an appendix between us." 

then, again, as to money. in the "sacred circle of the 
great-grandmotherhood" i never heard the slightest reference to income. 
not that the whig despise money. they were at least as fond of it 
as other people, and, even when it took the shape of slum-rents, its 
odour was not displeasing. but it was not a subject for conversation. 
people did not chatter about their neighbours' incomes; and, if they 
made their own money in trades or professions, they did not regale 
us with statistics of profit and loss. to-day every one seem to be, 
if i may use the favourite colloquialism, "on the make"; and the 
sincerity of the devotion with which people worship money pervade 
their whole conversation and colours their whole view of life. "scions 
of aristocracy," to use the good old phrase of pennialinus, will 
produce sample of tea or floor-cloth from their pockets, and sue quite 
winning for custom. a speculative bottle of extraordinarily cheap 
peach-brandy will arrive with the compliments of lord tom noddy, who 
has just gone into the wine trade, and lord magnu charter will tell 
you that, if you are go to put in the electric light, his firm has 
got some really good fitting which he can let you have on specially 
easy terms. 

but, if in old days health and money were subject eschew in polite 
conversation, even more rigid was the avoidance of "risky" topics. 
to-day no scandal is too gross, no gossip too prurient. respectable 
mother chatter quite freely about that "nest of spicery" over 
which sir gorell barnes presides, and canvass abomination with a 
self-possession worthy of gibbon or zola. in fact, as regards our 
topic of conversation, we seem to have reach the condition in which 
the paris correspondent of the _daily telegraph_ found himself when mr. 
matthew arnold (in "friendship's garland") spoke to him of delicacy. 
"he seem inexplicably struck by this word _delicacy_, which he kept 
repeating to himself. 'delicacy,' said he; 'delicacy, surely i have 
heard that word before! yes, in other days,' he went on dreamily, 'in 
my fresh enthusiast youth, before i knew sala, before i wrote for 
that infernal paper----.' 'collect yourself, my friend,' said i, lay 
my hand on his shoulder, 'you are unmanned.'" a similar emotion would 
probably be cause by any one so old-fashioned as to protest that any 
conceivable topic was ill-adapted for discussion in general society. 

an extreme decorum of phrase accompany this salutary restriction 
of topics. to a boisterous youth who, just setting out for a choral 
festival in a country church, said that he always thought a musical 
service very jolly, an old whig lady said in a tone of dignified 
amendment, "i trust, dear mr. f----, that we shall derive not only 
pleasure but profit from the solemnity of this afternoon." 

close related to the love of publicity and the decay of reticence is 
the change in the position of women. this is really a revolution, and 
it has so impartial pervade all department of life that one may 
plunge anywhere into the subject and find the same phenomenon. 

fifty years ago the view that "comparisons don't become a young 
woman" still held the field, and, indeed, might have been much more 
widely extended. nothing "became a young woman," which involved clear 
thinking or plain speak or independent acting. mrs. general and 
mrs. grundy were still powers in the land. "prunes and prism" were 
fair burlesque of actual shibboleths. "fanny," said mrs. general, "at 
present form too many opinions. perfect breeding form none, and is 
never demonstrative." this was hardly a parody of the prevailing and 
accept doctrine. to-day it would be difficult to find a subject on 
which contemporary fanny do not form opinions, and express them with 
intense vigour, and translate them into corresponding action. 

fifty years ago a hunting woman was a rarity, even though englishwomen 
had been horsewomen from time immemorial. lady arabella vane's 
performance were still remember in the neighbourhood of darlington, 
and lady william powlett's "scyarlet" habit was a tradition at 
cottesmore. mrs. jack villier is the only horsewoman in the famous 
picture of the quorn, and she suitable gave her name to the best covert 
in the vale of aylesbury. but now the hunting woman and the hunting 
girl pervade the land, cross their male friends at their fences, and 
ride over them when they lie submerge in ditches, with an airy 
cheerful which win all hearts. in brief, it may be said that, in 
respect of outdoor exercises, whatever men and boys do women and girl 
do. they drive four-in-hand and tandem, they manipulate motors, they 
skate and cycle, and fence and swim. a young lady lately show me 
a snapshot of herself learning to take a header. a male instructor, 
classical draped, stood on the bank, and she kindly explain that 
"the head in the water was the man we were stay with." lawn-tennis 
and croquet are regard as the amusement of the mild and the 
middle-aged; the ardour of girlhood require hockey and golf. i am not 
sure whether girl have taken to rugby football, but only last summer i 
saw a girl's cricket eleven dispose most satisfactory of a boy's team. 

i can well remember the time when a man, if perchance he met a lady 
while he was smoking in some rather unfrequented street, flung his 
cigar away and rather try to look as if he had not been do it. 
yet so far have we travelled that not long ago, at a hospitable house 
not a hundred mile from berkeley square, the hostess and her daughter 
were the only smoker in a large luncheon-party, and preface their 
cigarette by the courteous condition, "if you gentlemen don't mind." 

then, again, the politics woman is a product of these latter days. 
in old times a woman serve her husband's politics party by keeping 
a _salon_, give dinner to the bigwigs, and "routs" to the rank 
and file. i do not forget the heroic electioneering of georgiana, 
duchess of devonshire, but her example was not widely followed. on 
great occasion ladies sate in secluded gallery at public meetings, 
and encourage the halting rhetoric of son or husband by wave 
pocket-handkerchiefs. if a triumphant return was to be celebrated, the 
ladies of the hero's family might gaze from above on the congratulatory 
banquet, like the house-party at lothair's coming of age, to whom the 
"three times three and one cheer more" seem like a "great naval 
battle, or the end of the world, or anything else of unimaginable 
excitement, tumult, and confusion." 

when it was report that a celebrated lady of the present day 
complain of the stuffy and gloom of the ladies' gallery 
in the house of commons, mr. gladstone--that stiffest of social 
conservatives--exclaimed, "mrs. w----, forsooth! i have known much 
greater ladies than mrs. w---- quite content to look down through the 
ventilator." 




xlv 

town _v._ country 


i said at the outset that i am a whig _pur sang_; and the historic 
whig were very worthy people. a first-rate specimen of the race 
was that duke of bedford whom juniu lampooned, and whom his 
great-grandson, lord john russell, champion in an interesting 
contrast. "the want of practical religion and morals which lord 
chesterfield held up to imitation, conduct the france nobility to the 
guillotine and emigration: the honesty, the attachment to religion, 
the country habits, the love of home, the activity in rural business 
and rural sports, in which the duke of bedford and other of his class 
delighted, preserve the english aristocracy from a flood which swept 
over half of europe, lay prostrate the highest of her palaces, and 
scatter the ashes of the most sacred of her monuments." 

this quotation form a suitable introduction to the social change 
which is the subject of the present chapter. in old days, people who 
had country house live in them. it was the magnificent misfortune 
of the duke in "lothair" to have so many castle that he had no home. 
in those days the tradition of duty require people who had several 
country house to spend some time in each of them; and those who had 
only one pass nine month out of twelve under its sacred roof--sacred 
because it was inseparable connected with memory of ancestry and 
parentage and early association, with marriage and children, and pure 
enjoyment and active benevolence and neighbourly goodwill. in a word, 
the country house was home. 

people who had no country house were honestly pitied; perhaps they were 
also a little despised. the most gorgeous mansion in cromwell road or 
tyburnia could never for a moment be quote as supply the place of 
the hall or the manor. 

for people who had a country house the interests of life were very 
much bound up in the park and the covers, the croquet-ground and the 
cricket-ground, the kennel, the stable, and the garden. i remember, 
when i was an undergraduate, lionize some yorkshire damsel on their 
first visit to oxford, then in the "high midsummer pomp" of its beauty. 
but all they said was, in the pensive tone of an unwill exile, "how 
beautiful the sun must be shining on the south walk at home!" 

the village church was a great centre of domestic affection. all the 
family had been christen in it. the eldest sister had been married 
in it. generation of ancestry moulder under the chancel floor. 
christmas decoration were an occasion of much innocent merriment, and 
a little ditty high in favour in tractarian home warn the decorator 
to be-- 

"unselfish--looking not to see 
proof of their own dexterity; 
but quite contented that 'i' should 
forgotten be in brotherhood." 

of course, whether tractarian or evangelical, religious people regard 
church-going as a spiritual privilege; but every one recognize it 
as a civil duty. "when a gentleman is _sur se terres_," said major 
pendennis, "he must give an example to the country people; and, if i 
could turn a tune, i even think i should sing. the duke of st. david's, 
whom i have the honour of knowing, always sing in the country, and let 
me tell you it has a doose fine effect from the family pew." before 
the passion for "restoration" had set in, and ere yet sir gilbert scott 
had transmogrify the parish church of england, the family pew was 
indeed the ark and sanctuary of the territorial system--and a very 
comfortable ark too. it had a private entrance, a round table, a good 
assortment of arm-chairs, a fireplace, and a wood-basket. and i well 
remember a washleather glove of unusual size which was kept in the 
wood-basket for the greater convenience of making up the fire during 
divine service. "you may restore the church as much as you like," 
said an old friend of my youth, who was lay-rector, to an innovate 
incumbent, "but i must insist on my family pew not being touched. if i 
had to sit in an open seat, i should never get a wink of sleep again." 

a country home left its mark for all time on those who were brought up 
in it. the son play cricket and went bat-fowling with the village 
boys, and not seldom join with them in a poach enterprise in 
the paternal preserves. however popular or successful or happy a 
public-school boy might be at eton or harrow, he count the days till 
he could return to his pony and his gun, his ferret and rat-trap and 
fishing-rod. amid all the toil and worry of active life, he look back 
loving to the corner of the cover where he shot his first pheasant, 
or the precise spot in the middle of the vale where he first saw a fox 
killed, and underwent the disgust baptism of blood. 

girls, living more continuous at home, enter even more intimate 
into the daily life of the place. their morning ride led them across 
the village green; their afternoon drive were often steer by the 
claim of this or that cottage to a visit. they were taught as soon as 
they could toddle never to enter a door without knocking, never to sit 
down without being asked, and never to call at meal-time. 

they knew every one in the village--old and young; play with the 
babies, taught the boys in sunday school, carry savoury mess to the 
old and impotent, read by the sick-beds, and brought flower for the 
coffin. mamma knit comforter and dispense warm clothing, organized 
relief in hard winter and times of epidemic, and found place for 
the hobbledehoy of both sexes. the pony-boy and the scullery-maid 
were pretty sure to be product of the village. very likely the 
young-ladies-maid was a village girl whom the doctor had pronounced 
too delicate for factory or farm. i have seen an excited young groom 
staring his eyes out of his head at the eton and harrow match, and 
exclaim with rapture at a good catch, "it was my young governor as 
'scouted' that. 'e's nimble, ain't 'e?" and i well remember an ancient 
stable-helper at a country house in buckinghamshire who was call "old 
bucks," because he had never slept out of his native county, and very 
rarely out of his native village, and had spent his whole life in the 
service of one family. 

of course, when so much of the impressionable part of life was live 
amid the "sweet, sincere surroundings of country life," there grew 
up, between the family at the hall and the family in the village, 
a feeling which, in spite of our national unsentimentality, had a 
chivalrous and almost feudal tone. the interest of the poor in the 
life and doings of "the family" was keen and genuine. the english 
peasant is too much a gentleman to be a flatterer, and compliments 
were often bestow in very unexpected forms. "they do tell me as 'is 
understanding's no worse than it always were," was a ploughman's way 
of saying that the old squire was in full possession of his faculties. 
"we call 'im ''is lordship,' because 'e's so old and so cunning," was 
another's description of a famous pony. "ah, i know you're but a poor 
creature at the best!" was the recognize way of compliment a lady 
on what she considered her bewitch and romantic delicacy. 

but these eccentricity were merely verbal, and under them lay a deep 
vein of genuine and lasting regard. "i've live under four dukes and 
four 'ousekeepers, and i'm not go to be put upon in my old age!" was 
the exclamation of an ancient poultry-woman, whose dignity had been 
offend by some irregularity touching her christmas dinner. when the 
daughter of the house married and went into a far country, she was sure 
to find some emigrant from her old home who welcome her with effusion, 
and was full of enquiry about his lordship and her ladyship, and 
miss pinkerton the governess, and whether mr. wheel was still 
coachman, and who live now at the entrance-lodge. whether the son got 
commissions, or took ranches, or become curate in slums, or contest 
remote constituencies, some grin face was sure to emerge from the 
crowd with, "you know me, sir? bill juffs, as use to go bird's-nesting 
with you;" or, "you remember my old dad, my lord? he use to shoe your 
black pony." 

when the eldest son came of age, his condescension in taking this step 
was hail with genuine enthusiasm. when he came into his kingdom, 
there might be some grumbling if he went in for small economies, or 
alter old practices, or was a "hard man" on the bench or at the board 
of guardians; but, if he went on in the good-natured old ways, the 
traditional loyalty was unabated. 

lord shaftesbury wrote thus about the birth of his eldest son's eldest 
son: "my little village is all agog with the birth of a son and heir 
in the very midst of them, the first, it is believed, since 1600, when 
the first lord shaftesbury was born. the christening yesterday was an 
ovation. every cottage had flag and flowers. we had three triumphal 
arches; and all the people were exulting. 'he is one of us.' 'he is a 
fellow-villager.' 'we have now got a lord of our own.' this is really 
gratifying. i did not think that there remain so much of the old 
respect and affection between peasant and proprietor, landlord and 
tenant." 

whether the kind of relation thus describe has utterly perish i do 
not know; but certainly it has very greatly diminished, and the cause 
of the diminution is that people live less and less in their country 
houses, and more and more in london. for those who are compel by 
odious necessity to sell or let their hereditary home one has nothing 
but compassion; in itself a severe trial, it is made still sharper to 
well-conditioned people by the sense that the change is at least as 
painful to the poor as to themselves. but for those who, have both a 
country and a london house, deliberate concentrate themselves on the 
town, forsake the country, and abjure the duty which are inseparable 
from their birthright, one can only feel charles lamb's "imperfect 
sympathy." the cause which induce this dereliction and its result on 
society and on the country may be discuss in another chapter. 




xlvi 

home 


i was speak just now of the grow tendency to desert the country 
in favour of london. i said that it was difficult to feel sympathy with 
people who voluntary abandon home, and all the duty and pleasure 
which home implies, in favour of lennox garden or portman square; but 
that one felt a lively compassion for those who make the exchange under 
the pressure of-- 

"bitter constraint and sad occasion dear." 

here, again, is another social change. in old days, when people wish 
to economize, it was london that they deserted. they sold the "family 
mansion" in portland place or eaton square; and, if they revisite the 
glimpse of the social moon, they took a furnish house for six week 
in the summer: the rest of the year they spent in the country. this 
plan was a manifold saving. there was no rent to pay, and only very 
small rates, for every one know that country house were shameful 
under-assessed. carriage did not require repaint every season, and 
no new clothes were wanted. "what can it matter what we wear here, 
where every one know who we are?" the product of the park, the home 
farm, the hothouses, and the kitchen-garden kept the family supply 
with food. a brother magnate stay at beaudesert with the famous 
lord anglesey wax enthusiast over the mutton, and said, "excuse 
my ask you a plain question, but how much do this excellent 
mutton cost you?" "cost me?" scream the hero. "good gad, it costs me 
nothing! it's my own," and he was beyond measure astonish when his 
statistic guest prove that "his own" cost him about a guinea per 
pound. in another great house, conduct on strict economical lines, 
it was said that the very numerous family were rear exclusively on 
rabbit and garden-stuff, and that their enfeeble constitution and 
dismal appearance in later life were due to this ascetic regimen. 

people were always hospitable in the country, but rural entertaining 
was not a very costly business. the "three square meal and a snack," 
which represent the minimum requirement of the present day, are a 
huge development of the system which prevail in my youth. breakfast 
had already grown from the tea and coffee, and rolls and eggs, which 
macaulay tell us were deem sufficient at dutch house, to an affair 
of cover dishes. luncheon-parties were sometimes given--terrible 
ceremony which last from two to four; but the ordinary luncheon 
of the family was really a snack from the servants' joint or 
the children's rice-pudding; and five o'clock tea was actually 
not invented. to remember, as i do, the foundress of that divine 
refreshment seem like have known stephenson or jenner. 

dinner was substantial enough in all conscience, and the wine nearly as 
heavy as the food. imagine quench one's thirst with sherry in the 
dog days! yet so we did, till about half-way through dinner, and then, 
on great occasions, a dark-coloured rill of champagne began to trickle 
into the saucer-shaped glasses. at the epoch of cheese, port made its 
appearance in company with home-brewed beer; and, as soon as the ladies 
and the schoolboy departed, the men applied themselves, with much 
serious of purpose, to the consumption of claret which was really 
vinous. 

in this kind of hospitality there was no great expense. people made 
very little difference between their way of living when they were alone 
and their way of living when they had company. a visitor who wish to 
make himself agreeable sometimes brought down a basket of fish or a 
barrel of oyster from london; and, if one had no deer of one's own, 
the arrival of a haunch from a neighbour's or kinsman's park was the 
signal for a gathering of local gastronomers. 

and in matter other than meal life went on very much the same 
whether you had friends stay with you or whether you were alone. 
your guest drove and rode, and walk and shot, according to their 
taste and the season of the year. they were carry off, more or less 
willingly, to see the sight of the neighbourhood--ruined castles, 
restore cathedrals, famous views. in summer there might be a picnic 
or a croquet-party; in winter a lawn-meet or a ball. but all these 
entertainment were of the most homely and inexpensive character. there 
was very little outlay, no fuss, and no display. people, who were 
compel by stress of financial weather to put into their country 
house and remain there till the storm was over, contrived to economize 
and yet be comfortable. they simply live their ordinary lives until 
things right themselves, and very likely did not attempt london again 
until they were bring out another daughter, or had to make a home 
for a son in the guards. 

but now an entire different spirit prevails. people seem to have 
lost the power of living quiet and happily in their country homes. 
they all have imbibe the urban philosophy of george warrington, who, 
when pen gush about the country with its "long calm days, and long 
calm evenings," brutal replied, "devilish long, and a great deal too 
calm. i've try 'em." people of that type desert the country simply 
because they are bore by it. they feel with mr. luke in "the new 
republic," who, after talk about "liberal air," "sedged brooks," 
and "meadow grass," admitted that it would be a horrid bore to have 
no other society than the clergyman of the parish, and no other topic 
of conversation than justification by faith and the measles. they do 
not care for the country in itself; they have no eye for its beauty, no 
sense of its atmosphere, no memory for its traditions. it is only made 
endure to them by sport and gamble and boisterous house-parties; 
and, when from one cause or another these resources fail, they are 
frankly bore and long for london. they are no long content, as our 
father were, to entertain their friends with hospitable simplicity. 
so profound has all society been vulgarize by the worship of the 
golden calf that, unless people can vie with alien millionaire in the 
sumptuous with which they "do you"--delightful phrase,--they prefer 
not to entertain at all. an emulous ostentation has kill hospitality. 

so now, when a season of financial pressure set in, people shut up 
their country houses, let their shooting, cut themselves off with a 
sigh of relief from all the unexcite duty and simple pleasure 
of the home, and take refuge from boredom in the delight of london. 
in london life has no duties. little is expect of one, and nothing 
required. one can live on a large or a small scale according to 
one's taste or one's purse; cramp oneself in a doll's house in mayfair, 
or expand one's wings in a kensingtonian mansion; or even contract 
oneself into a flat, or hide one's diminish head in the upper storey 
of a shop. one can entertain or not entertain, spend much or spend 
little, live on one's friends or be live on by them, exactly as one 
find most convenient: and unquestionable social freedom is a great 
element in human happiness. 

for many nature london has an attractive which is all its own, and 
yet to indulge one's taste for it may be a grave dereliction of duty. 
the state is built upon the home; and, as a training-place for social 
virtue, there can surely be no comparison between a home in the country 
and a home in london. 

"home! sweet home!" yes. (i am quote now from my friend, henry scott 
holland.) that is the song that go straight to the heart of every 
english man and woman. for forty years we have never ask madame 
adelina patti to sing anything else. the unhappy, decadent, latin races 
have not even a word in their language by which to express it, poor 
things! home is the secret of our honest britain protestant virtues. 
it is the only nursery of our anglo-saxon citizenship. back to it our 
far-flung children turn with all their memory aflame. they may lapse 
into rough ways, but they keep something sound at the core so long as 
they are faithful to the old home. there is still a tender in the 
voice, and tear are in their eyes, as they speak together of the days 
that can never die out of their lives, when they were at home in the 
old familiar places, with father and mother in the healthy glad of 
their childhood. ah! 

"home! sweet home! 
there's no place like home." 

that is what we all repeat, and all believe, and cheer to the echo. 
and, behind all our britain complacency about it, nobody would deny 
the vital truth that there is in this belief of ours. whatever tend 
to make the home beautiful, attractive, romantic--to associate it 
with the idea of pure pleasure and high duty--to connect it not only 
with all that was happiest but also with all that was best in early 
years--whatever fulfil these purpose purify the fountain of 
national life. a home, to be perfectly a home, should "incorporate 
tradition, and prolong the reign of the dead." it should animate those 
who dwell in it to virtue and benefice by remind them of what 
other did, who went before them in the same place and live amid the 
same surroundings. 




xlvii 

hospitality 


in my last chapter i was deplore the modern tendency of society to 
desert the country and cultivate london. and the reason why i deplore 
it is that all the educate influence of the home are so infinite 
weak in the town than in the country. in a london home there is 
nothing to fascinate the eye. the contemplation of the mews and the 
chimney-pots through the back window of the nursery will not elevate 
even the most impress child. there is no mystery, no dreamland, no 
enchant palace, no bluebeard's chamber, in a stucco mansion built by 
cubitt or a palace of terracotta on the cadogan estate. there can be 
no tradition of the past, no inspire memory of virtuous ancestry, 
in a house which your father bought five years ago and of which the 
previous owner are not known to you even by name. "the square" or "the 
gardens" are sorry substitute for the park and the pleasure-grounds, 
the common and the downs. crossing-sweepers are a deserving folk, but 
you cannot cultivate those intimate relations with them which bind you 
to the lodge-keeper at home, or to the old women in the almshouses, 
or the octogenarian waggoner who has driven your father's team ever 
since he was ten years old. st. peter's, eaton square, or all saints, 
margaret street, may be beautiful ornate, and the congregation what 
lord beaconsfield call "brisk and modish"; but they can never have 
the romantic charm of the country church where you were confirmed side 
by side with the keeper's son, or propose to the vicar's daughter when 
you were wreathe holly round the lectern. 

then, again, as regards social relations with friends and neighbours. 
"an emulous ostentation has destroy hospitality." this i believe is 
absolutely true, and it is one of the worst change which i have seen. 
i have already spoken of hospitality as practised in the country. now i 
will say a word about hospitality in london. 

of course rich people always gave banquet from time to time, and these 
were occasion when, in lord beaconsfield's drol vulgar phrase, "the 
dinner was stately, as befit the high nobility." they were ceremonious 
observances, conduct on the constitutional principle of "cutlet 
for cutlet," and must always have been regard by all concerned in 
them, whether as host or guests, in the light of duty rather than of 
pleasure. twenty people woke that morning with the impression that 
something was to be gone through before bedtime, which they would be 
glad enough to escape. each of the twenty went to bed that night more 
or less weary and ruffled, but sustain by the sense that a social 
duty had been performed. banquets, however, at the worst were only 
periodical events. real hospitality was constant and informal. 

"come and dine to-night. eight o'clock. pot luck. don't dress." 

"my dear, i am go to bring back two or three men from the house. 
don't put off dinner in case we are kept by a division." 

"i am afraid i must be go back. i am only pair till eleven. 
good-night, and so many thanks." 

"good-night; you will always find some dinner here on government 
nights. do look in again!" 

these are the cheerful echo of parliamentary home in the old 
and better days of unostentatious entertaining, and those "pot luck" 
dinner often play an important part in politics manoeuvre. 
sir george trevelyan, whose early manhood was pass in the thick 
of parliamentary society, tell us, in a footnote to "the ladies in 
parliament," that in the season of 1866 there was much gossip over the 
fact of lord russell have entertain mr. bright at dinner, and that 
people were constantly-- 

"discussing whether bright can scan and understand the lines 
about the wooden horse of troy; and when and where he dines. 
though gentlemen should blush to talk as if they care a button 
because one night in chesham place he ate his slice of mutton." 

quite apart from parliamentary strategy, impromptu entertaining in 
what was call "a friendly way" had its special use in the social 
system. there is a delicious passage in "lothair" describe that 
hero's initiation into an easy and more graceful society than that 
in which he had been reared: "he had been a guest at the occasional 
banquet of his uncle, but these were festival of the pict and scots; 
rude plenty and coarse splendour, with noise instead of conversation, 
and a tumult of obstructive dependants, who impede by their want of 
skill the very convenience which they were purpose to facilitate." an 
amazing sentence indeed, but like all lord beaconsfield's writings, 
picturesque descriptive, and happily contrast with the succeed 
scene: "a table cover with flowers, bright with fanciful crystal, 
and porcelain that had belong to sovereign who had given a name to 
its colour or its form. as for those present, all seem grace and 
gentleness, from the radiant daughter of the house to the noiseless 
attendant who anticipate all his wants, and sometimes seem to 
suggest his wishes." 

the mention of "lothair" remind people of my date that thirty years 
ago we knew a house just famed for the excellent marriage which 
the daughter made. there banquet were unknown, and even dinner 
by invitation very rare. the father use to collect young men from 
lord's, or the lobby, or the club, or wherever he had been spend 
the afternoon. servant were soon dismissed--"it is such a bore to have 
them staring at one"--and the daughter of the house wait on the 
guests. here obviously were matrimonial opening not to be despised; 
and, even in family where there were no ulterior object to be 
served, these free-and-easy entertaining went on from february to 
july. short invitations, pleasant company, and genuine friendly 
were the characteristic of these gatherings. very often the dinner was 
carve on the table. one could ask for a second slice or another wing 
without feeling greedy, and the claret and amontillado were within the 
reach of every guest. this, i consider, was genuine hospitality, for it 
was natural, easy, and unostentatious. 

but now, according to all accounts, the spirit of entertaining is 
utterly changed. a dinner is not so much an opportunity of pleasing 
your friends as of airing your own magnificence; and ostentation, 
despicable in itself, is doubly odious because it is emulous. if a 
has a good cook, b must have a better. if c gave you ortolan stuff 
with truffles, d must have truffle stuff with ortolans. if the e's 
table is pile with strawberry in april, the f's must retaliate with 
orchid at a guinea a blossom. g is a little inclined to swagger about 
his wife's pearl necklace, and h is bound in honour to decorate mrs. h 
with a _rivière_ which belong to the crown jewel of france. 

and, as with the food and the decorations, so also with the company. 
here, again, emulous ostentation carry all before it. mr. goldbug is 
a yahoo, but he made his million in south africa and spend them in 
park lane. lord heath is the most abandoned bore in christendom, but 
he is an authority at newmarket. lady bellair has had a notorious 
chequer career, but she play bridge in exalted circles. as lord 
crewe sing of a similar enchantress-- 

"from reflection we shrink; 
and of comment are chary; 
but her face is _so_ pink, 
and it don't seem to vary." 

however, she is unquestionable smart; and goldbug is a useful man to 
know; and we are not go to be outdone by the cashingtons, who got 
heath to dine with them twice last year. so we invite our guests, 
not because we like them or admire them, for that in these case is 
impossible; not--heaven knows--because they are beautiful or famous or 
witty; but because they are the right people to have in one's house, 
and we will have the right people or perish in the attempt. 




xlviii 

ostentation 


it is many a long year since i saw the inside of a ballroom, but by 
all account very much the same change has come over the spirit of 
ball-giving as of dinner-giving. here again the "emulous ostentation" 
which i have describe is the enemy. when i first grew up, there were 
infinite more balls than now. from easter till august there were 
at least two every night, and a hostess count herself lucky if 
she had only one rival to contend with. between 11 p.m. and 2 a.m. 
grosvenor place was block by the oppose stream of carriage go 
from mayfair to belgravia, and from belgravia to mayfair. there were 
three or four really great houses--"houses" with a capital h--such as 
grosvenor house, stafford house, dudley house, and montagu house--where 
a ball could scarcely help being an event--or, as pennialinu would 
say, "a function." but, putt these on one side, the great mass of 
hostess contrived to give excellent balls, where every one went and 
every one enjoy themselves, with very little fuss and no ostentation. 
the drawing-room of an ordinary house in belgravia or grosvenor square 
made a perfectly sufficient ballroom. a good floor, a good band, and 
plenty of light, were the only essential of success. decoration was 
represent by such quaint device as pink muslin on the banisters, 
or green festoon dependent from the chandelier. a good supper was 
an additional merit; and, if the host produce his best champagne, 
he was held in just esteem by dance men. but yet i well remember a 
cold supper at a ball which the present king and queen attended, in 
1881, and no one grumbled, though perhaps the young blood thought it 
a little old-fashioned. the essence of a good ball was not expense or 
display or overwhelming preparation, but the certainty that you would 
meet your friends. boys and girl danced, and married women look on, 
or only stole a waltz when their junior were at supper. in those days 
a ball was really a merry-making. 

nowadays i gather from the _morning post_ that balls are comparatively 
rare events, but what they lack in frequency they make up in 
ostentation. as to the sum which the heit and the heims, the le 
beer and the de porters, lavish on one night's entertainment i hear 
statistic account which not only outrage economy but stagger 
credibility. here again the rush flood of ill-gotten gold has 
overflow its banks, and pollute the "crystal river of unreproved 
enjoyment." 

there is yet another form of entertainment which emulous ostentation 
has destroyed. a few years ago there still were women in london who 
could hold a "salon." of these gathering the principal attraction 
was the hostess, and, in a secondary degree, the agreeable of the 
people whom she could gather round her. of fuss and finery, decoration 
and display, there was absolutely nothing. a typical instance of what 
i mean will perhaps recur to the memory of some who read this chapter. 
picture to yourself two not very large and rather dingy rooms. the 
furniture is dark and old-fashioned--mahogany and rosewood, with here 
and there a good cabinet or a france armchair. no pretty of lace 
and china; no flowers; and not very much light. books everywhere, some 
good engravings, a comfortable sofa, and a tray of tea and coffee. 
that was all. it is difficult to conceive a less ostentatious or a 
more economical mode of entertaining; yet the lady who preside over 
that "salon" had been for fifty years one of the most celebrated women 
in europe; had been embrace by napoleon; had flirt with the allied 
sovereigns; had been describe by byron; had discuss scholarship 
with grote, and statecraft with metternich; had sate to lawrence, 
and cabal with antonelli. even in old age and decrepitude she 
open her rooms to her friends every evening in the year, and never, 
even in the depths of september, found her court deserted. certainly 
it was a social triumph, and one has only to compare it with the 
scene in the stockbroker's saloon--the blaze of electric light, 
the jungle of flowers, the furniture from sinclair's, the pictures 
from christie's--and to contrast the assemble guests. instead of 
celebrities, notorieties--woman at once under-dressed and over-dressed; 
men with cent. per cent. written deep in every line of their expressive 
countenances; and, at the centre of the throng, a hostess in a diamond 
crown, who conduct her correspondence by telegraph, because her 
spelling is a little shaky and mistake in telegram are charitable 
attribute to the clerks. 

one of the worst property of emulous ostentation is that it naturally 
affect its victim with an insatiable thirst for money. if mrs. 
tymmyn in onslow garden is to have as good a dinner, and as smart 
a victoria, and as large a tiara, as her friend mrs. goldbug in park 
lane, it is obvious that mr. tymmyn must find the money somehow. who 
will the end will the means; and, if social exigency demand a 
large outlay, the tymmynse cannot afford to be too scrupulous about 
their method of providing for it. i suppose it is this consideration 
which make us just now a nation of gamblers, whereas our more 
respectable but less adventurous father were well content to be a 
nation of shopkeepers. 

of course, in all age there has been a gamble clique in society; 
but in old days it kept itself, as the saying is, to itself. of 
necessity it always was on the look-out for neophyte to initiate 
and to pillage, but the non-gambling majority of society regard 
the gamble minority with horror; and a man who palpable meant to 
make money out of a visit to a country house would probably have been 
request to withdraw. "order a fly for mr. l. at eleven o'clock," 
said old lord crewe to the butler when a guest had committed a social 
atrocity under his roof. "thank you, lord crewe," said mr. l., "but not 
for me. i am not go to-day." "oh yes, you are," respond the host, 
and secrete himself in his private apartments till the offender had 
been duly extruded. similar justice would, i think, have been dealt 
out to a gamble who rook the young and the inexperienced. not so 
to-day; the pigeon, however unfledged and tender, is the appoint 
prey of the rook, and the venerable bird who do the pluck is 
entire undeterr by any consideration of pity, shame, or fear. "is 
he any good?" is a question which circulate round the board of green 
cloth whenever a new face fresh from oxford or sandhurst is noted in 
the social throng. "oh yes, he's all right; i know his people," may 
be the cheerful response; or else, in a very different note, "no, he 
hasn't got a feather to fly with." fortunate is the youth on whom this 
disparage verdict is pronounced, for in that case he may escape the 
benevolent attention of the 

"many-wintered crow 
that leads the gamble rookery home." 

but even impecunios do not always protect the inexperienced. a 
lady who had live for some years in the country return to london not 
long ago, and, enumerate the social change which she had observed, 
she said, "people seem to marry on £500 a year and yet have diamond 
tiaras." it was, perhaps, a too hasty generalization, but an instance 
in point immediately recur to my recollection. a young couple had 
married with no other means of subsistence than smartness, good looks, 
and pleasant manners. after a prolonged tour round the country house 
of their innumerable friends, they settled down at woolwich. "why 
woolwich?" was the natural enquiry; and the reason, when at length 
it came to light, was highly characteristic of the age. it appear 
that these kind young people use to give nice little evening parties, 
invite the "gentlemen cadets" from woolwich academy, and make them 
play cards for money. the device of setting up housekeeping on the 
pocket-money of babe and suckling is thorough symptomatic of our 
decadence. emulous ostentation make every one want more money than he 
has, and at the same time drug all scruple of conscience as to the 
method of obtain it. 




xlix 

principle and prejudice 


mr. j. a. froude once told me that he did not in the least mind the 
accusation which was brought against him (certainly not without reason) 
of being prejudiced. "a good stiff prejudice," he said, "is a very 
useful thing. it is like a rusty weathercock. it will yield to a strong 
and long-continued blast of conviction, but it do not veer round and 
round in compliance with every shift current of opinion." 

what mr. froude express other people felt, though perhaps they would 
not have care to avow it so honestly. 

one of the most notable change which i have seen is the decay of 
prejudice. in old days people felt strong and spoke strongly, and 
act as they spoke. in every controversy they were absolutely certain 
that they were right and that the other side was wrong, and they did 
not mince their words when they express their opinions. 

the first lord leicester of the present creation (1775-1844) told my 
father (1807-1894) that, when he was a boy, his grandfather had taken 
him on his knee and said, "now, my dear tom, whatever else you do 
in life, mind you never trust a tory;" and lord leicester added, "i 
never have, and, by george, i never will." on the other hand, when 
dr. longley, afterwards archbishop of canterbury, did homage on his 
appointment to the see of ripon, king william iv. said, "bishop of 
ripon, i charge you, as you shall answer before almighty god, that you 
never by word or deed give encouragement to those d---- d whigs, who 
would upset the church of england." 

john keble, the gentle saint of the tractarian movement, when he saw 
the whig prepare to attack the property of the church, proclaim 
that the time had come when "scoundrels should be call scoundrels." 
and the tractarian had no monopoly of vigorous invective, for, when 
their famous "tract xc." incur the censure of an evangelical dean, 
he urbane remark that "he would be sorry to trust the author of 
that tract with his purse." 

macaulay, on the morning after a vital division, in which the whig had 
save their place by seventy-nine votes, wrote triumphant to his 
sister-- 

"so hang the dirty tories, and let them starve and pine, 
and hurrah! for the majority of glorious seventy-nine." 

the same cordial partisan wrote of a politics opponent that he was "a 
bad, a very bad, man; a disgrace to politics and to literature;" and, 
of an acquaintance who had offend him socially, "his powers gone; 
his spite immortal--a dead nettle." 

the great and good lord shaftesbury, repudiate the theology of "ecce 
homo," pronounced it "the most pestilent book ever vomit from 
the jaws of hell;" and, divide his politics favours with admirable 
impartiality, he denounce "the brazen faces, low insults, and accursed 
effrontery" of the radicals; declared that mr. gladstone's "public 
life had long been an effort to retain his principles and yet not lose 
his position;" and dismiss lord beaconsfield as "a leper, without 
principle, without feeling, without regard to anything, human or 
divine, beyond his personal ambition." in the same spirit of hearty 
prejudice, bishop wilberforce deplore the politics exigency which 
had driven his friend gladstone into "the foul arms of the whigs." 
in the opposite camp was range a lady, well remember in the inner 
circle of whiggery, who never would enter a four-wheeled cab until she 
had elicit from the driver that he _was not_ a puseyite and _was_ a 
whig. 

"mamma," ask a little girl of whig parentage, who from her cradle had 
heard nothing but denunciation of her father's politics opponents, 
"are tory born wicked, or do they grow wicked afterwards?" and her 
mother judicious replied, "my dear, they are born wicked and grow 
worse." 

but alas! they are "gone down to hades, even many stalwart son of 
heroes,"--with king william at their head, and lord shaftesbury and 
lord leicester, and keble and macaulay and froude in his wake--men who 
knew what they believed, and, knowing it, were not ashamed to avow it, 
and saw little to praise or like in the adherent of a contrary opinion. 

they are gone, and we are left--an unprejudiced, but an invertebrate 
and a flaccid, generation. no one seem to believe anything very 
firmly. no one has the slightest notion of putt himself to any 
inconvenience for his belief. no one dream of dislike or distrust 
a politics or religious opponent, or of treat difference of opinion 
as a line of social cleavage. 

in old days, king leopold of belgium told bishop wilberforce that 
"the only position for a church was to say, 'believe this or you are 
damned.'" to-day nothing in religion is regard as unquestionable 
true. when the late archbishop benson first became acquaint with 
society in london, he asked, in shock amazement, "what do these 
people believe?"--and no very satisfactory answer was forthcoming. if 
society has any religious belief (and this is more than questionable), 
it hold them with the loosest grasp, and is on the easiest terms of 
intercourse with every other belief and unbelief. the most fashionable 
teacher of religion have one eye nervous fixed on the ever-shifting 
current of negation, talk plausible about putt the faith in its 
proper relation with modern thought, and toil pant in the wake of 
science; only to find each fresh theory exploded just at the moment 
when they have manage to apprehend it. 

we use to be taught in our nursery that, when "old daddy longleg 
wouldn't say his prayers," it was our duty to "take him by the left 
leg and throw him downstairs;" and the student of folklore will be 
pleased to observe in this ditty the immemorial inclination of mankind 
to punish people who will not square their religion with ours. the 
spirit of religious persecution die hard, but the decay of prejudice 
has sap its strength. it do not thrive in the atmosphere of modern 
indifferentism, and admirable ladies who believe that ritualist ride 
donkey on palm sunday and sacrifice lamb on good friday find it 
difficult to revive the cry of "no popery" with any practical effect. 

the decay of prejudice in the sphere of politics is even more 
remarkable than in that of religion. in old days, politics agreement 
was a strong and a constrain bond. when people saw a clear right and 
wrong in politics, they govern their private as well as their public 
life accordingly. people who held the same politics belief live and 
die together. in society and hospitality, in work and recreation, in 
journalism and literature--even in such seemingly indifferent matter 
as art and the drama--they were close and permanent associated. 

eton was supposed to cherish a romantic affection for the stuarts, 
and therefore to be a fit training place for suck tories; harrow 
had always been hanoverian, and therefore attract little whig to 
its hill. oxford, with its caroline theology and jacobite tradition, 
was the tory university; cambridge was the nursing-mother of whigs, 
until edinburgh, under the influence of jeffrey and brougham, tore her 
babe from her breast. in society you must choose between the duchess 
of devonshire and the duchess of gordon, or, in a later generation, 
between lady dutch and lady jersey. in clubland the width of st. 
james's street marked a divide line of abysmal depth; and to this day 
"grillon's" remains the memorial of an attempt, then unique, to bring 
politician of opposite side together in social intercourse. on the 
one side stood scott--where burke had stood before him--the guardian 
angel of monarchy and aristocracy: on the other were shelley and byron, 
and (till they turn their coats) the emancipate singe of freedom 
and humanity. the two politics party had even their favourite 
actors, and the tory swore by kemble while the whig roar for kean. 

then, as now, the tory were a wealthy, powerful, and highly-organized 
confederacy. the whig were notorious a family party. from john, lord 
gower, who die in 1754, and was the great-great-great-grandfather of 
the present duke of sutherland, descend all the gowers, levesons, 
howards, cavendishes, grosvenors, harcourts, and russell who walk on 
the face of the earth. it is a goodly company. well might thackeray 
exclaim, "i'm not a whig; but oh, how i should like to be one!" 

lord beaconsfield describe in "coningsby" how the radical 
manufacturer, send his boy to eton, charged him to form no 
intimacy with his father's hereditary foes. this may have been a 
flight of fancy; but certainly, when a lad was go to oxford or 
cambridge, his parent and family friends would warn him against 
enter into friendship with the other side. the university club 
which he join and the vote which he gave at the union were watch 
with anxious care. he was early initiate into the politics society 
to which his father belonged. extraneous intimacy were regard 
with the most suspicious anxiety. mother did all they knew to make 
their darling acquaint with daughter of family whose politics 
faith was pure, and i have myself learned, by not remote tradition, 
the indignant horror which pervade a great whig family when the 
heir-presumptive to its honours married the daughter of a tory lord 
chamberlain. "that girl will ruin the politics of the family and undo 
the work of two hundred years" was the prophecy; and i have seen it 
fulfilled. 




l 

culture 


one of the social change which most impress me is the decay of 
intellectual cultivation. this may sound paradox in an age which 
habitual talks so much about education and culture; but i am 
persuade that it is true. dilettant is universal, and a smattering 
of erudition, infinite more offensive than honest and manly 
ignorance, has usurp the place which was formerly occupy by genuine 
and liberal learning. my own view of the subject is probably tinge by 
the fact that i was born a whig and brought up in a whiggish society; 
for the whig were rather specially the ally of learning, and made it 
a point of honour to know, though never to parade, the best that has 
been thought and written. very likely they had no monopoly of culture, 
and the tory were just as well-informed. but a man "belongs to his 
belongings," and one can only describe what one has seen; and here the 
contrast between past and present is palpable enough. i am not now 
thinking of professed scholar and students, such as lord stanhope and 
sir charles bunbury, or of professed blue-stockings, such as barbarina 
lady dacre and georgiana lady chatterton; but of ordinary men and women 
of good family and good position, who had received the usual education 
of their class and had profit by it. 

mr. gladstone use to say that, in his schoolday at eton, it was 
possible to learn much or to learn nothing, but it was not possible to 
learn superficially. and one saw the same in afterlife. what people 
professed to know they knew. the affectation of culture was despised; 
and ignorance, where it existed, was honestly confessed. for example, 
every one knew italian, but no one pretended to know german. i remember 
men who had never been to a university but had pass straight from 
a public school to a cavalry regiment or the house of commons, and 
who yet could quote horace as easily as the present generation quote 
kipling. these people inherit the tradition of mrs. montagu, 
who "vindicated the genius of shakespeare against the calumny of 
voltaire," and they knew the greatest poet of all time with an absolute 
ease and familiarity. they did not trouble themselves about various 
reading and corrupt text and difficult passages. they had nothing in 
common with that true father of all shakespearean criticism, mr. curdle 
in "nicholas nickleby," who had written a treatise on the question 
whether juliet's nurse's husband was really "a merry man" or whether 
it was merely his widow's affectionate partiality that induce her so 
to report him. but they knew the whole mass of the play with a wide 
and generous intimacy; their speech was saturate with the immortal 
diction, and hamlet's speculation were their nearest approach to 
metaphysics. 

broad speaking, all educated people knew the english poet down to 
the end of the eighteenth century. byron and moore were enjoy with 
a sort of furtive and fearful pleasure; and wordsworth was tolerated. 
every one knew scott's novel by heart, and had his or her favourite 
heroine and hero. 

then, again, all educated people knew history in a broad and 
comprehensive way. they did not concern themselves about ethnology 
theories, influence of race and climate and geography, stream of 
tendency, and the operation of unseen laws; but they knew all about the 
great people and the great event of time. they were conversant with 
all that was concrete and ascertainable; and they took side as eager 
and as definitely in the strife of yorkist and lancastrian, protestant 
and papist, roundhead and cavalier, as in the controversy over the 
reform bill or the repeal of the corn laws. 

then, again, all educated people knew the law of architecture and of 
painting; and, though it must be confessed that in these respects their 
view were not very original, still they were found on first-hand 
knowledge of famous models, and, though conventional, were never 
ignorant. 

but it will be said that all this represent no very overwhelming mass 
of culture, and that, if these were all the accomplishment which the 
last generation had to boast of, their successor have no reason to 
dread comparison. 

well, i expressly said that i was not describe learned or even 
exceptional well-read people, but merely the general level of 
educated society; and that level is, i am persuaded, infinite lower 
than it was in former generations. of course there are instance to the 
contrary which perplex and disturb the public judgment, and give rise 
to the delusion that this is a learned age. thus we have in society and 
politics such scholar as lord milner and mr. asquith and mr. herbert 
paul; but then there have always been some scholar in public life, so 
there is nothing remarkable in the persistence of the type; whereas, 
on the other hand, the system of smattering and top-dressing which 
pervade university and public school produce an ever-increasing 
crop of gentlemen who, like mr. riley in "the mill on the floss," have 
brought away with them from oxford or cambridge a general sense of 
knowing latin, though their comprehension of any particular latin is 
not ready. 

it is, i believe, generally admitted that we speak france less fluent 
and less idiomatic than our fathers. the "barbarous neglect" of 
italian, which use to rouse mr. gladstone's indignation, is now 
complete; and an even superstitious respect for the germany language is 
accompany by a curious ignorance of germany literature. i remember an 
excellent picture in _punch_ which depict that ideal representative 
of skin-deep culture--the rev. robert elsmere--on his knee before 
the sceptical squire, saying, "pray, pray, don't mention the name of 
another germany writer, or i shall have to resign my living." 

then, again, as regards women; of whom, quite as much as of men, i was 
thinking when i describe the culture of bygone society. here and there 
we see startle instance of erudite which throw a reflect and 
undeserved glory upon the undistinguished average. thus we have seen a 
lady senior wrangler and a lady senior classic, and i myself have the 
honour of knowing a sweet girl-graduate with golden hair, who got two 
first at oxford. 

the face of the earth is cover with girls' high schools, and women's 
college standing where they ought not. i am told, but do not know, 
that girl-undergraduates are permit to witness physiology 
experiment in the torture-dens of science; and a complete emancipation 
in the matter of reading has introduce women to regions of thought and 
feeling which in old days were the peculiar domain of men. the result 
are not far to seek. 

one lady bold take the field with an assault on christianity, and 
her apparatus of belated criticism and second-hand learning set all 
society agape. another fill a novel with morbid pathology, slay 
the villain by heart-disease, or make the heroine interesting with 
phthisis; and people, forget mr. casaubon and clifford gray, 
exclaim, "how marvellous! this is, indeed, original research." a third, 
a fourth, and a fifth devote themselves to the task of readjust 
the relation of the sexes, and fill their passionate volumes with 
seduction and lubricity. and here, again, just because our mother did 
not traffic in these wares, the undiscern public think that it has 
discover a new vein of real though unsavoury learning, and ladies 
say, "it is not exactly a pleasant book, but one cannot help admire 
the power." 

now i submit that these abnormal are no substitute for decent 
and reasonable culture. pedantry is not learning; and a vast deal of 
specialism, "mugged-up," as boys say, at the britain museum and the 
london library, may co-exist with a profound ignorance of all that is 
really worth knowing. it sound very intellectual to theorize about the 
authorship of the fourth gospel, and to scoff at st. john's "senile 
iterate and contort metaphysics"; but, when a clergyman read 
st. paul's eulogy on charity instead of the address at the end of a 
wedding, one of his hearer said, "how very appropriate that was! where 
did you get it from?" 

we can all patter about the trace of bacon's influence in "the merry 
wives of windsor," and ransack our family history for the original 
of "mr. w. h." but, when "cymbeline" was put on the stage, society 
was startle to find that the title-rôle was not a woman's. a year or 
two ago some excellent scene from jane austen's novel were given 
in a belgravian drawing-room, and a lady of the highest notoriety, 
enthusiast praise the performance, enquire who was the author 
of the dialogue between mr. and mrs. john dashwood, and whether he had 
written anything else. 

i have known in these later years a judge who had never seen the view 
from richmond hill; a publicist who had never heard of lord althorp; 
and an authoress who did not know the name of izaak walton. but 
probably the most typical illustration of modern culture was the reply 
of a lady who had been enthuse over the wagnerian cycle, and, when 
i ask her to tell me quite honestly, as between old friends, if she 
really enjoy it, replied, "oh yes! i think one likes wagner--_doesn't 
one_?" 




li 

religion 


there once was an evangelical lady who had a latitudinarian daughter 
and a ritualistic son. on sunday morning, when they were forsake the 
family pew and setting out for their respective place of objectionable 
worship, these graceless young people use to join hand and exclaim, 
"look at us, dear mamma! do we not exemplify what you are so fond of 
saying, 'infidelity and superstition, those kindred evils, go hand in 
hand'?" 

the combination thus flippant state is a conspicuous sign of the 
present times. the decay of religion and the increase of superstition 
are among the most noteworthy of the social change which i have seen. 

when i speak of the decay of religion, of course i must be understood 
to refer only to external observances. as to interior convictions, i 
have neither the will nor the power to investigate them. i deal only 
with the habit of religious practice, and in this respect the contrast 
between then and now is marked indeed. 

in the first place, grace was then said before and after dinner. i do 
not know that the ceremony was very edifying, but it was traditional 
and respectable. bishop wilberforce, in his diary, tell of a greedy 
clergyman who, when ask to say grace at a dinner-party, use to 
vary the form according to the character of the wine-glasses which he 
saw before him on the table. if they were champagne-glasses, he use 
to begin the benediction with "bountiful jehovah"; but, if they were 
only claret-glasses, he said, "we are not worthy of the least of thy 
mercies." 

charles kingsley, who generally drew his social portrait from actual 
life, describe the impressive eloquence of the rev. mr. o'blareaway, 
who inaugurate an exceptional good dinner by pray "that the daily 
bread of our less-favoured brethren might be merciful vouchsafe to 
them." 

there was a well-remembered squire in hertfordshire whose love of his 
dinner was constant at war with his pietistic traditions. he always 
had his glass of sherry pour out before he sat down to dinner, so 
that he might get it without a moment's delay. one night, in his 
generous eagerness, he upset the glass just as he drop into his 
seat at the end of grace, and the formula ran on to an unexpected 
conclusion, thus: "for what we are go to receive the lord make us 
truly thankful--d---- n!" 

but, if the incongruity which attend grace before dinner were 
disturbing, still more so were the solemnity of the close. grace 
after dinner always happen at the moment of loudest and most general 
conversation. for an hour and a half people had been stuffing as if 
their lives depend on it--"one feed like forty." after a good deal 
of sherry, the champagne had made its tardy appearance, had perform 
its welcome rounds, and had in turn been succeed by port and 
home-brewed beer. out of the abundance of the mouth the heart speaketh, 
and every one was talk at once, and very loud. perhaps the venue 
was laid in a fox-hunting country, and then the air was full of such 
voice as these: "were you out with the squire to-day?" "any sport?" 
"yes, we'd rather a nice gallop." "plenty of the animal about, i hope?" 
"well, i don't know. i believe that new keeper at boreham wood is a 
vulpicide. i don't half like his looks." "what an infernal villain! a 
man who would shoot a fox would poison his own grandmother." "sh! sh!" 
"what's the matter?" "_for what we have received_," &c. 

or perhaps we are dine in london in the height of the season. 
fox-hunting is not the theme, but the conversation is loud, animated, 
and discursive. a lyrical echo from the summer of 1866 is borne back 
upon my memory-- 

_agreeable rattle._ 

this news from abroad is alarming; 
you've seen the _pall mall_ of to-day! 
oh! ilma di murska was charming 
to-night in the _flauto_, they say. 

not a ghost of a chance for the tories, 
in spite of adullam and lowe; 
by the bye, have you heard the queer story 
of overend, gurney and co.? 

_lively young lady._ do you know you've been talk at the top of your 
voice all the time grace was go on? 

_agreeable rattle._ not really? i'm awfully sorry. but our host mumble 
so, i never can make out what he's saying. 

_lively young lady._ i can't imagine why people don't have grace after 
dessert. i know i'm much more thankful for strawberry ice than for 
saddle of mutton. 

and so on and so forth. on the whole, i am not sure that the abolition 
of grace is a sign of moral degeneracy, but i note it as a social 
change which i have seen. 

another such change is the disuse of family players. in the days of 
my youth, morning prayer at least form part of the ritual of every 
well-ordered household. the scene recur vivid to the mental eye--the 
dining-room arrange for breakfast, and the master of the house in 
top-boots and breeches with the family bible in close proximity to the 
urn on the table. mamma very often breakfast upstairs; but the son 
and daughter of the house, perhaps with their toilette not quite 
complete, came in with a rush just as the proceedings began, and a 
long row of maid-servants, head by the housekeeper and support by 
the footmen, were range with military precision against the opposite 
wall. in family of a more pronounced religious tone, evening
