prayer were frequent superadded; and at ten o'clock the assemble 
guest were arouse from "squails" or "consequences" by the entrance 
of the butler with "thornton's family prayers" on a silver salver. in 
one very evangelical house which i knew in my youth, print prayer 
were supersede by extempore devotions, and, as the experiment seem 
successful, the servant were invite to make their contribution in 
their own words. as long as only the butler and the housekeeper voice 
the aspiration of their fellows, all was well; but, in an evil moment, 
a recalcitrant kitchenmaid utter an unlooked-for petition for her 
master and mistress--"and we pray for sir thomas and her ladyship. oh! 
may they have now heart given them." and the bare suggestion that 
there was room for such an improvement cause a prompt return to the 
lively oracle of henry thornton. 

i note the disappearance of the domestic liturgy; and here again, as in 
the matter of grace, i submit that, unless the rite can be decently, 
reasonably, and reverent performed, it is more honour in the breach 
than in the observance. 

much more significant is the secularize of sunday. this is not 
merely a change, but a change conspicuous for the worse. the amount 
of church-going always differ in different circles; religious people 
went often and careless people went seldom, but almost every one went 
sometimes, if merely from a sense of duty and decorum. mr. gladstone, 
whose tradition were evangelical, thought very poorly of what he 
call a "once-er," _i.e._ a person who attend divine service only 
once on a sunday. he himself was always a "twice-er," and often a 
"thrice-er"; but to-day it would puzzle the social critic to discover a 
"twice-er," and even a "once-er" is sufficient rare to be noticeable. 

but far more serious than the decay of mere attendance at church is 
the complete abolition of the day of rest. people, who have nothing 
to do but to amuse themselves, work at that entrance occupation 
with redouble energy on sundays. if they are in london, they whirl 
off to spend the "week-end" amid the meretricious splendour of the 
stockbroker's suburban paradise; and, if they are entertaining friends 
at their country houses, they play bridge or tennis or croquet; they 
row, ride, cycle, and drive, spend the afternoon in a punt, and wind up 
the evening with "the washington post." 

all this is an enormous change since the days when the only decorous 
amusement for sunday was a visit after church to the stables, or a 
walk in the afternoon to the home farm or the kitchen garden; and, of 
course, it entail a corresponding amount of labour for the servants. 
maid and valet spend the "week-end" in a whirl of packing and 
unpacking, and the whole staff of the kitchen is continuous employed. 

in old days people use to reduce the meal on sunday to the narrowest 
dimensions, in order to give the servant their weekly due of rest and 
recreation, and in a family with which i am connected the traditional 
bill of fare for sunday's dinner, drawn by a cook who live before the 
school board, is still affectionate remembered-- 

soup. 

cold beef. 

salad. 

_cold sweats._ 

in brief, respectable people use to eat and drink sparing on sunday, 
cause no unnecessary work, went a good deal to church, and fill up 
their leisure time by visiting sick people in the cottage or teaching 
in the sunday school. no doubt there was a trace of puritan strict 
about the former practice, and people too generally forgot that the 
first day of the week is by christian tradition a feast. society 
has rediscovere that great truth. it observe the weekly feast by 
over-eating itself, and honours the day of rest by over-working its 
dependants. 




lii 

superstition 


"superstition and infidelity usually go together. professed atheist 
have trafficke in augury, and men who do not believe in god 
will believe in ghosts." to-day i take up my parable concerning 
superstition, to which, time out of mind, the human spirit has betaken 
itself as soon as it part company with faith. 

i once ask a lady who, in her early life, had live in the very 
heart of society, and who return to it after a long absence, what was 
the change which struck her most forcibly. she prompt replied, "the 
growth of superstition. i hear people seriously discuss ghosts. in 
my day people who talk in that way would have been put in bedlam; 
their relations would have require no other proof that they were mad." 

my own experience entire confirm this testimony as to the 
development of superstition, and i have had some peculiarly favourable 
opportunity of observing its moral effect upon its votaries. the 
only superstition tolerate in my youth was table-turning, and that 
was always treat as more than half a joke. to sit in a darken 
room round a tea-table, secret join hand under the mahogany, and 
"communicate a revolving motion" to it (as mr. pickwick to his fists) 
was not bad fun when the company was mainly young and larky, but 
contained one or two serious people who desire to probe the mystery to 
its depths. or, perhaps, our psychic force would cause the respectable 
piece of furniture to rear itself upon one leg, and deal out with a 
ponderous foot mysterious raps, which the serious people interpret 
with their own admirable solemnity. i well remember a massive gentleman 
with an appalling stammer who proclaim that some lost document which 
we had ask the table to discover would be found in the vatican 
library, "wrapped in a ragged palimpsest of tertullian;" and the 
quaint of the utterance dissolve the tables, or at least the 
table-turners, in laughter. this particular form of superstition became 
discredit among respectable people when sharper got hold of it and 
use it as an engine for rob the weak-minded. it died, poor thing, 
of exposure, and its epitaph was written by brown in "mr. sludge, 
the medium." 

it was the same with ghost-stories. people told them--partly to fill 
gap when reasonable conversation failed, and partly for the fun of 
making credulous hearer stare and gasp. but no one, except ladies as 
weak-minded as byng's half-aunt in "happy thoughts," ever thought of 
taking them seriously. bishop wilberforce invent a splendid story 
about a priest and a slide panel and a conceal confession; and i 
believe that he habitual use it as a foolometer, to test the mental 
capacity of new acquaintances. but the bishop belong to that old 
generation which despise superstition, and during the last few years, 
twaddle of this kind has risen to the dignity of a pseudo-science. 

necromancy is a favourite substitute for religion. it supplies the 
element of mystery without which the human spirit cannot long subsist; 
and, as it do not require its adherent to practise self-denial, 
or get up early on sunday, or subscribe to charities, or spend their 
leisure in evil-smelling slums, it is a cult particularly well adapt 
to a self-indulgent age. i vivid remember a scene which occur 
just before the coronation. a luxurious luncheon had been prolonged by 
the aid of coffee, kümmel, and cigarette till four o'clock; and the 
necromancers--surfeited, flushed, and a little maudlin--were loll 
round the drawing-room fire. a whisper colloquy in a corner was heard 
through the surrounding chatter, and the hostess saw her opportunity. 
"dear lady de spook, do let us hear. i know you are such a wonderful 
medium." 

_lady de spook._ really, it was nothing at all out of the common. i had 
come home dead tired from the opera, and just as i was go to bed i 
heard that rap--you know what i mean? 

_mr. sludge_ (enthusiastically). oh yes, indeed i do! no one who has 
ever heard it can ever forget it. 

_lady de spook_ (resuming). well, and do you know it turn out to be 
poor dear lord de spook. it was wonderful how energetic he rapped, 
for you know he was quite paralyse years before he died; and the 
curious thing was that i couldn't make out what he said. it seem 
to be, "don't buy. sarah. search." i was too tired to go on talk 
to him, so i went to bed; but next day, do you know, my maid found 
the coronet which his first wife, whose name was sarah, had worn at 
the last coronation. i was just go to order a new one. wasn't it a 
wonderful interposition!--such a saving! 

_chorus_ (sentimentally). ah, wonderful indeed! our dear one are never 
really lost to us. 

close connected with necromancy is clairvoyance. a man whom i knew 
well was taken sudden and seriously ill, and his relations, who were 
enthusiast spookists, telegraph for the celebrated clairvoyante 
mrs. endor. she duly arrived, threw herself into a trance, declared 
that the patient would die, came to, and declared that there was 
nothing much the matter, and that he would be about again in two or 
three days. then, have pocket her cheque, she return to london. 
the patient grew rapid worse, and died; and his relations, though i 
am sure they sincerely mourn him, were much sustain in the hour 
of bereavement by the thought that the opinion which mrs. end had 
given in her trance had prove to be the right one, and that spiritual 
science was justified by the result. 

but, after all, necromancy and clairvoyance are a little old-fashioned. 
crystal-gazing is more modish. 'tis as easy as lying. you gather 
open-mouthed round a glass ball, and the gifted gaze report that 
which he or she can see, but which is invisible to gross eyes. there 
are no bounds to the fascinating range of a crystal-gazer's fancy, nor 
to the awestruck credulous with which his revelation are received. 

but crystal is not the only medium through which a purge eye can 
discern the mysterious future. coffee-grounds, though less romantic, 
are very serviceable. our hostess is an expert in this form of science, 
and, being a thorough amiable woman, she make the coffee say pretty 
much what we should like to hear. "dear mr. taper, this is delightful. 
you will be prime minister before you die. it is true that your party 
will not be in office again just yet; but 'hope on, hope ever,' and 
trust your star." 

"oh! mr. garbage, i have such good news for you. your next book will be 
an immense success, and, after that, messrs. skin & flint will be more 
liberal, and, what with the america copyright and the acting rights, 
you will make quite a fortune." 

close akin to the science of coffee-grounds is that of palmistry. 
a wretched gipsy who "tells fortunes" at a race-meeting is sent to 
prison; but, when st. berengaria's get up a bazaar for its new vestry, 
a bejewell lady sit in a secret chamber (for admission to which an 
extra half-crown is charged), and, after scrutinize your line of 
life, tell you that you have had the influenza; and, project her 
soul into futurity, predict that the next time you have it you will 
get pneumonia unless you are very careful. 

of course, these minor superstition are mainly ridiculous, and to 
get up moral indignation over them would be a waste of force. but 
one cannot speak so lightly of the degrade cult which are group 
together under the name of spiritualism. i have known a "spiritual 
wife" who was highly commend in spookish circle because she left her 
husband, family, and home in one continent and cross the world to 
find her "affinity" in another. i have known a most promising boy whose 
health was destroy and his career ruin by a hypnotic experiment 
perform on him without his parents' knowledge. i have known a 
mesmeric clergyman who cozen the women of his congregation out of 
money, character, and in some case reason. where occult is pursued, 
all veracity and self-respect disappear; prurient find a congenial 
lodgment, and the issue is--well--what we sometimes see exhibit in 
all its uncome at the central criminal court. 

the wisest lawgiver who ever live said, "thou shalt not suffer a 
witch to live." and a great judge act on the rule. but that was a 
long time ago. we have improve upon the jurisprudence of moses and the 
method of sir matthew hale. stone and hanging are a little out of 
date, but boycott is a remedy still within our reach. whoso is wise 
will ponder these things, and will give occultists, male and female, an 
uncommonly wide berth. 




liii 

the remnant 


some recent observations of mine on the deteriorate of society have 
drawn this interesting response from an eminent clergyman in the north 
of london:-- 

"is it possible that in 'society' itself there is a point of resistance 
which may be touched by an effective appeal coming from the wholesome 
elements in english life? belong as i do to that section of english 
life which is a stranger to society in the technical sense, i am deep 
impress with the taint which come to all circle of society from 
the contamination of the circle at the top. to elicit a strong opinion 
and a resolute determination from what i may call the puritan side of 
english life, may be perhaps the first step towards the correction 
of the evil which mr. russell describes. are there not in society 
itself some men and women who retain the high ideal and the strenuous 
purpose of their ancestry? can they be induce to raise their protest, 
to assert their principles, and open the way to a better--because a 
purer--future? i venture to make this appeal because it is my fixed 
conviction that even in the worst and most degrade society there are 
men who sigh for better things, just as in the worst and most degrade 
men there remains a desire, however overlaid, for regeneration." 

well, frankly i think that an amiable insanity delude my reverend 
friend if he expect a moral reformation in the sort of society which 
i have been describing. it would tax the combine energy of st. john 
the baptist, savonarola, the two wesleys, and george whitefield, all 
roll into one, to convince the people whom i have in my mind of their 
ethical shortcomings. they have made their own beds, in every sense of 
that expressive phrase, and must lie on them till the cataclysm come 
which will bring us all to our senses. 

but i am remind that i promise to write not exclusively about 
deteriorate in society, but about change of all kinds. that there 
has been some change for the better i readily admit, as well as an 
enormous number of change for the worse. "all things are double," say 
the son of sirach, "one against the other," and in this close chapter 
i will try to balance our gain and our losses. 

that there has always been a mixture of good and bad in society is 
only another way of saying that society is part of mankind; but, if i 
am right in my survey, the bad just now is flagrant and ostentatious 
to a degree which we have not known in england since 1837. there was 
once a moralist who spoke of the narrow path which lay between right 
and wrong, and similarly there use to be a debatable land which lay 
between the good and evil district of society. it was inhabit by the 
people who, have no ethical conviction of their own, go very much as 
they are led. it was written of them long ago that-- 

"they eat, they drink, they sleep, they plod, 
they go to church on sunday; 
and many are afraid of god, 
and more of mrs. grundy." 

as long as mrs. grundy was a real, though comical, guardian of social 
propriety--as long as the highest influence in the social system 
tend towards virtue and decorum--the inhabitant of the debatable 
land were even painful respectable. but now that the "trend" (as 
pennialinu call it) is all the other way, and mrs. grundy has been 
depose as a bore and an anachronism, they willing follow the "smart" 
multitude to do evil; and so the area cover by social wicked is 
much large than in former times. in other words, the evil of society 
is both worse in quality and large in quantity than it was fifty--or 
even twenty--years ago. 

now if this be true--and i hold it to be unquestionable--what have we 
to set against it? i reply, the greatly increase activity of those 
who are really good. in old days the good were good in a quiescent 
and lethargy way. they were punctual in religious observances, public 
and private; exemplary in the home and the family, and generous to the 
poor. but their religion could scarcely be call active, except in 
so far as potter about among the cottages, or teaching a class of 
well-washed children in the sunday school, can be reckon as active 
employments; and even such activity as these were as a rule confine 
to women. 

sir walter scott believe that "there were few young men, and those 
very sturdy moralists, who would not rather be tax with some moral 
peccadillo than with want of horsemanship." and, in days much more 
recent than the beloved sir walter's, men, if they were religious, 
studious kept their light under a bushel, and took the utmost pains 
to avoid being detect in acts of charity or devotion. 

nowadays all this is changed, and changed, in my opinion, much for the 
better. religious people are ready to let the world know what they 
believe, and are active in the pursuit of the things which are pure 
and lovely and of good report. well-dressed young men combine dance 
with slumming. untidy and dul are no long the necessary 
concomitant of virtue. officer of the guard sing in the choir and 
serve the altar. men whose name are written in the book of the peerage 
as well as the book of life conduct bible-classes and hand round the 
hymn-books at mission-services. the group of young m.p.'s who were 
nickname "hughligans" show the astonish house of commons that 
religion is as practical a thing as politics, and (as one of them 
lately said) they cheerful encounter that hot water which is the 
modern substitute for boil oil. the university send their best 
athlete and social favourite to curacy in the slum or martyrdom 
in the mission-field. the example set by mr. james adderley, when he 
left christ church and found the oxford house at bethnal green, has 
been follow in every direction. both the universities, and most of 
the colleges, run "settlements," where laymen, in the interval of 
professional work and social enjoyment, spread religion, culture, and 
physical education amid the "dim, common populations" of camberwell and 
stratford and poplar. 

the public schools, formerly denounce as "the seat and nursery 
of vice," make their full contribution to active religion. eton and 
winchester and harrow have their mission in crowded quarters of great 
towns. at one school, the boys have a guild of devotion; at another, a 
voluntary bible-class with which no master inter-meddles. and so the 
young citizen of the privileged order gain their first lesson in 
religious and social service, and carry the idea with them to the army 
or the bar or the stock exchange or the house of commons. all this is, 
in my eyes, a social change which is also a clear and enormous gain. 

but, if what i say is true of men, it is even more conspicuous true 
of women. they are no long content with the moderate church-going 
at comfortable hours, and the periodical visit to particularly clean 
cottages, which at one time were the sum-total of their activities. 
every well-organized parish has its staff of woman-workers, who 
combine method with enthusiasm and piety with common sense. belgravia 
and mayfair send army of district-visitors to hoxton and poplar. 
girl from fashionable homes, pretty and well dressed, sacrifice 
their evenings to club and social gathering for factory-hands and 
maids-of-all-work. beneath the glittering surface of social life, there 
is a deep current of wise and devoted effort for those unhappy being 
who are least able to help themselves. and all this philanthropic 
energy is distinctive and avowed christian. it is the work of 
men and women, young and old, widely differentiate from one another 
in outward circumstances of wealth and accomplishment and social 
influence, but all agree about "the one thing needful," and all keen 
to confess their faith before a hostile world. 

what, then, is the conclusion of the whole matter? society, during 
the years in which i have known it, has change enormously, alike in 
its exterior characteristic and, as far as i can judge, in its inner 
spirit. while some of the change have been simply innocuous, and a 
few even beneficial, the great majority have been gross and palpable 
deteriorations. an onlooker who knew society well thus describe its 
present condition: "we are living in an age of decadence, and we 
pretend not to know it. there is not a feature wanting, though we 
cannot mention the worst of them. we are roman of the worst period, 
given up to luxury and effeminacy, and care for nothing but money. we 
care no more for beauty in art, but only for a brutal realism. sport 
has lost its manliness, and is a matter of pigeon from a trap, or a 
mountain of crush pheasant to sell to your own tradesmen. religion 
is coming down to juggle and table-turnings and philander with 
cult brought, like the rite of isis, from the east; and as for 
patriotism, it is turn on like beer at election times, or work like 
a mechanical doll by wire-pullers. we belong to one of the most corrupt 
generation of the human race. to find its equal one must go back to 
the worst times of the roman empire, and look devilish close then. 
but it's uncommonly amuse to live in an age of decadence; you see 
the funniest sight and you get every conceivable luxury, and you die 
before the irruption of the barbarians." 

this is, i believe, a true indict against the age in which our lot 
is cast, although the utterance has just that touch of exaggeration 
which secure a hearing for unpalatable truth. but the man who wrote 
it left out of account that redeem element in our national life 
which i have discuss in this close chapter. after all, there is a 
world-wide difference between the "majority" and the "remnant,"--and 
the ten righteous men may yet save the guilty city.
